COSTLIEST YEAR FOR DISASTERS; RESEARCHERS CITE HUMANS: [NORTH SPORTS FINAL EDITION]

Author: Associated Press.

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/costliest-year-disasters-researchers-cite-humans/docview/418750359/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Violent weather has cost the world a record $89 billion this year, more money than was lost from weather-related disasters in all of the 1980s, and, in a study released Friday, researchers blame human meddling for much of it. Preliminary estimates by the Worldwatch Institute, an environmental research group, and Munich Re, the world's largest reinsurer, put total losses from storms, floods, droughts and fires for the first 11 months of the year 48 percent higher than the previous one-year record of more than $60 billion in 1996. This year's damage also far surpassed the $55 billion in losses for the entire decade of the 1980s.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=COSTLIEST+YEAR+FOR+DISASTERS%3B+RESEARCHERS+CITE+HUMANS&amp;author=Associated+Press.&amp;issn=10856706&amp;title=Chicago+Tribune&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-11-28&amp;spage=20&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Violent weather has cost the world a record $89 billion this year, more money than was lost from weather-related disasters in all of the 1980s, and, in a study released Friday, researchers blame human meddling for much of it.
Preliminary estimates by the Worldwatch Institute, an environmental research group, and Munich Re, the world's largest reinsurer, put total losses from storms, floods, droughts and fires for the first 11 months of the year 48 percent higher than the previous one-year record of more than $60 billion in 1996.
This year's damage also far surpassed the $55 billion in losses for the entire decade of the 1980s.
Even when adjusted for inflation, the 1980s losses, at $82.7 billion, still fall short of the first 11 months of this year.
In addition to the material losses, the disasters have killed an estimated 32,000 people and displaced 300 million, according to the report.
Munich Re writes policies that protect insurance companies from the risk of massive claims that might put them out of business.
The report says a combination of deforestation and climate change has caused this year's most severe disasters, among them Hurricane Mitch, the flooding of China's Yangtze River and Bangladesh's most extensive flood of the century.
Dunn said that, when hillsides are left bare, rainfall will rush across the land or into rivers without being slowed by trees and allowed to be absorbed by the soil or evaporate back into the atmosphere.
This leads to floods and landslides that are strong enough to wipe out roads, farms and fisheries far downstream.
"In a sense, we're turning up the faucets . . . and throwing away the sponges, like the forests and the wetlands," said Dunn.
Another element that has contributed to this year's losses is the growing population pressures that have led many people to settle on vulnerable flood plains and hillsides.
The most severe 1998 disasters listed in the report include Hurricane Mitch, the deadliest Atlantic storm in 200 years, which has caused more than 10,000 deaths in Honduras, Nicaragua, Guatemala and El Salvador.
Mitch caused damage estimated at $4 billion in Honduras and $1 billion in Nicaragua.
The study said Mitch hit an ecologically vulnerable region. Central American nations have experienced some of the highest rates of deforestation in the world, said the study.
The costliest disaster of 1998, according to the report, was the flooding of the Yangtze River in the summer.
It killed more than 3,000 people, dislocated about 230 million people and incurred $30 billion in losses.

'98 WEATHER IS COSTLIEST EVER: [BROWARD METRO EDITION]

Author: DONNA ABU-NASR The Associated Press

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/98-weather-is-costliest-ever/docview/388334683/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Violent weather has cost the world a record $89 billion this year, more money than was lost from weather-related disasters in all of the 1980s, and researchers in a study released on Friday blame human meddling for much of it. Preliminary estimates by the Worldwatch Institute and Munich Re, the world's largest reinsurer, put total losses from storms, floods, droughts and fires for the first 11 months of the year 48 percent higher than the previous one-year record of more than $60 billion in 1996. This year's damage was also far ahead of the $55 billion in losses for the entire decade of the 1980s. Even when adjusted for inflation, the 1980s losses, at $82.7 billion, fall short of the first 11 months of this year.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=%2798+WEATHER+IS+COSTLIEST+EVER&amp;author=DONNA+ABU-NASR+The+Associated+Press&amp;issn=&amp;title=Sun+Sentinel&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-11-28&amp;spage=22.A&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Violent weather has cost the world a record $89 billion this year, more money than was lost from weather-related disasters in all of the 1980s, and researchers in a study released on Friday blame human meddling for much of it.
Preliminary estimates by the Worldwatch Institute and Munich Re, the world's largest reinsurer, put total losses from storms, floods, droughts and fires for the first 11 months of the year 48 percent higher than the previous one-year record of more than $60 billion in 1996.
This year's damage was also far ahead of the $55 billion in losses for the entire decade of the 1980s. Even when adjusted for inflation, the 1980s losses, at $82.7 billion, fall short of the first 11 months of this year.
In addition to the material losses, the disasters have killed an estimated 32,000 people and displaced 300 million -- more than the population of the United States -- according to the report.
The study is based on estimates from Worldwatch, an environmental research group, and Munich Re, the Frankfurt, Germany-based reinsurer, which writes policies that protect insurance companies from the risk of massive claims that might put them out of business.
The report says a combination of deforestation and climate change has caused this year's most severe disasters, among them Hurricane Mitch, the flooding of China's Yangtze River and Bangladesh's most extensive flood of the century.
Another element that has contributed to this year's losses is the growing population pressures that have led many people to settle on vulnerable flood plains and hillsides.
The most severe 1998 disasters listed in the report include Hurricane Mitch, the deadliest Atlantic storm in 200 years, which caused more than 10,000 deaths in Honduras, Nicaragua, Guatemala and El Salvador, and damage estimated at $4 billion in Honduras and $1 billion in Nicaragua.
The study said Mitch hit an ecologically vulnerable region. Central American nations have experienced some of the highest rates of deforestation in the world.
The costliest disaster of 1998, the report said, was the flooding of the Yangtze River. It killed more than 3,000 people, dislocated about 230 million people and caused $30 billion in losses.

Natural disaster costs soar to world record Study blames deforestation and human meddling for 1998's $130-billion in damages

Author: Abu-Nasr, Donna

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/natural-disaster-costs-soar-world-record-study/docview/384471090/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: "More and more, there's a human fingerprint in natural disasters in that we're making them more frequent and more intense and we're also . . . making them more destructive," said Seth Dunn, research associate and climate-change expert at the institute.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Natural+disaster+costs+soar+to+world+record+Study+blames+deforestation+and+human+meddling+for+1998%27s+%24130-billion+in+damages&amp;author=Abu-Nasr%2C+Donna&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-11-28&amp;spage=A.25&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Natural disaster costs soar to world record Study blames deforestation and human meddling for 1998's $130-billion in damages
Saturday, November 28, 1998
Associated Press
Washington -- Violent weather has cost the world a record $130-billion this year -- more money than was lost from weather-related disasters in all of the 1980s -- and researchers in a study released yesterday blame human meddling for much of the loss.
Preliminary estimates by the Worldwatch Institute and Munich Re, the world's largest reinsurer, put total losses from storms, floods, droughts and fires for the first 11 months of the year 48 per cent higher than the previous one-year record of more than $90-billion in 1996.
This year's damage was also far ahead of the $82-billion in losses for the entire decade of the 1980s. Even when adjusted for inflation, the 1980s losses, at $120-billion, still fall short of the first 11 months of this year.
In addition to the material losses, the disasters have killed an estimated 32,000 people and displaced 300 million -- more than the population of the United States -- the report says.
The study is based on estimates from Worldwatch, an environmental research group, and Munich Re, a German-based reinsurer, which writes policies that protect insurance companies from the risk of massive claims that might put them out of business.
The report says a combination of deforestation and climate change has caused this year's most severe disasters, among them Hurricane Mitch, the flooding of China's Yangtze River and Bangladesh's most extensive flood of the century.
"More and more, there's a human fingerprint in natural disasters in that we're making them more frequent and more intense and we're also . . . making them more destructive," said Seth Dunn, research associate and climate-change expert at the institute.
Mr. Dunn said when hillsides are left bare, rainfall will rush across the land or into rivers without being slowed by trees and allowed to be absorbed by the soil or evaporate back into the atmosphere. This leads to floods and landslides that are strong enough to wipe out roads, farms and fisheries far downstream.
"In a sense, we're turning up the faucets . . . and throwing away the sponges, like the forests and the wetlands," Mr. Dunn said.
The most severe 1998 disasters listed in the report include Hurricane Mitch, which has caused more than 10,000 deaths in Honduras, Nicaragua, Guatemala and El Salvador and has caused damage estimated at $6-billion in Honduras and $1.5-billion in Nicaragua.

Title: Natural disaster costs soar to world record Study blames deforestation and human meddling for 1998's $130-billion in damages

World faces record year for disasters

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/world-faces-record-year-disasters/docview/379510205/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The report says a combination of deforestation and climate change has caused this year's most severe disasters, among them Hurricane Mitch, the flooding of China's Yangtze River and Bangladesh's most extensive flood of the century. The most severe 1998 disasters listed in the report include Hurricane Mitch, the deadliest Atlantic storm in 200 years, which has caused more than 10,000 deaths in Honduras, Nicaragua, Guatemala and El Salvador, and caused damage estimated at $4 billion in Honduras and $1 billion in Nicaragua. Bangladesh suffered its most extensive flood of the century in the summer. Two-thirds of the low-lying country located at the mouth of the Ganges and Brahmaputra Rivers was flooded for months, 30 million people were left temporarily homeless, and 10,000 miles of roads were heavily damaged. Damage estimates exceed $3.4 billion.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=World+faces+record+year+for+disasters&amp;author=&amp;issn=0898168X&amp;title=Northwest+Florida+Daily+News&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-11-28&amp;spage=A.1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   A study finds "human fingerprints" on weather conditions that caused $89 billion in damage.
WASHINGTON (AP)-- Violent weather has cost the world a record $89 billion this year, more money than was lost from weather-related disasters in all of the 1980s, and researchers in a study released Friday blame human meddling for much of it.
Preliminary estimates by the Worldwatch Institute and Munich Re, the world's largest reinsurer, put total losses from storms, floods, droughts and fires for the first 11 months of the year 48 percent higher than the previous one-year record of more than $60 billion in 1996.
This year's damage was also far ahead of the $55 billion in losses for the entire decade of the 1980s. Even when adjusted for inflation, the 1980s losses, at $82.7 billion, still fall short of the first 11 months of this year.
In addition to the material losses, the disasters have killed an estimated 32,000 people and displaced 300 million-- more than the population of the United States-- according to the report.
The study is based on estimates from Worldwatch, an environmental research group, and Munich Re, the Frankfurt, Germany-based reinsurer, which writes policies that protect insurance companies from the risk of massive claims that might put them out of business.
The report says a combination of deforestation and climate change has caused this year's most severe disasters, among them Hurricane Mitch, the flooding of China's Yangtze River and Bangladesh's most extensive flood of the century.
"More and more, there's a human fingerprint in natural disasters in that we're making them more frequent and more intense and we're also ... making them more destructive," said Seth Dunn, research associate and climate change expert at the institute.
Dunn said when hillsides are left bare, rainfall will rush across the land or into rivers without being slowed by trees and allowed to be absorbed by the soil or evaporate back into the atmosphere. This leads to floods and landslides that are strong enough to wipe out roads, farms and fisheries far downstream.
"In a sense, we're turning up the faucets ... and throwing away the sponges, like the forests and the wetlands," said Dunn.
Another element that has contributed to this year's losses is the growing population pressures that have led many people to settle on vulnerable flood plains and hillsides.
The most severe 1998 disasters listed in the report include Hurricane Mitch, the deadliest Atlantic storm in 200 years, which has caused more than 10,000 deaths in Honduras, Nicaragua, Guatemala and El Salvador, and caused damage estimated at $4 billion in Honduras and $1 billion in Nicaragua.
The study said Mitch hit an ecologically vulnerable region. Central American nations have experienced some of the highest rates of deforestation in the world, losing some 2 percent to 4 percent of their remaining forest cover each year, said the study.
The costliest disaster of 1998, according to the report, was the flooding of the Yangtze River in the summer. It killed more than 3,000 people, dislocated about 230 million people and incurred $30 billion in losses.
The study said that while heavy summer rains are common in southern and central China, the Yangtze Basin has lost 85 percent of its forest cover to logging and agriculture in recent decades, wetlands have been drained, and the river heavily dammed.
Bangladesh suffered its most extensive flood of the century in the summer. Two-thirds of the low-lying country located at the mouth of the Ganges and Brahmaputra Rivers was flooded for months, 30 million people were left temporarily homeless, and 10,000 miles of roads were heavily damaged. Damage estimates exceed $3.4 billion.
Logging upriver in the Himalayas of north India and Nepal exacerbated the disaster, as did the fact that the region's rivers and flood plains have been filled with silt and constricted by development, according to the report.
"Climate change and rising sea levels are projected to make Bangladesh even more vulnerable to flooding in the future," said the study.
The study said governments are beginning to recognize the role of human activities in worsening natural disasters. It noted that China has banned logging in the upper Yangtze watershed, prohibited additional land reclamation projects in the river's flood plain and earmarked $2 billion to reforest the watershed.
"Unless ravaged nations rebuild along a path of sustainable development that emphasizes restoring and maintaining healthy ecosystems, they risk even greater exposure to the devastation of unnatural disasters in the future," said the report.

People: Dunn, Seth

Title: World faces record year for disasters

Report: Weather caused record $89 billion damage in '98 // Losses top all disasters of the 1980s combined

Author: Abu-Nasr, Donna

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/report-weather-caused-record-89-billion-damage-98/docview/375183068/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: WASHINGTON - Violent weather has cost the world a record $89 billion this year, more money than was lost from weather-related disasters in all of the 1980s, and researchers in a study released Friday blame human meddling for much of it. Full text: Associated Press
WASHINGTON - Violent weather has cost the world a record $89 billion this year, more money than was lost from weather-related disasters in all of the 1980s, and researchers in a study released Friday blame human meddling for much of it.
Preliminary estimates by the Worldwatch Institute and Munich Re, the world's largest reinsurer, put total losses from storms, floods, droughts and fires for the first 11 months of the year 48 percent higher than the previous one-year record of more than $60 billion in 1996.
This year's damage was also far ahead of the $55 billion in losses for the entire decade of the 1980s. Even when adjusted for inflation, the 1980s losses, at $82.7 billion, still fall short of the first 11 months of this year.
In addition to the material losses, the disasters have killed an estimated 32,000 people and displaced 300 million - more than the population of the United States - according to the report.
The study is based on estimates from Worldwatch, an environmental research group, and Munich Re, the Frankfurt, Germany-based reinsurer, which writes policies that protect insurance companies from the risk of massive claims that might put them out of business.
The report says a combination of deforestation and climate change has caused this year's most severe disasters, among them Hurricane Mitch, the flooding of China's Yangtze River and Bangladesh's most extensive flood of the century.
"More and more, there's a human fingerprint in natural disasters in that we're making them more frequent and more intense and we're also ... making them more destructive," said Seth Dunn, research associate and climate change expert at the institute.
Dunn said when hillsides are left bare, rainfall will rush across the land or into rivers without being slowed by trees and allowed to be absorbed by the soil or evaporate back into the atmosphere. This leads to floods and landslides that are strong enough to wipe out roads, farms and fisheries far downstream.
"In a sense, we're turning up the faucets ... and throwing away the sponges, like the forests and the wetlands," said Dunn.
Another element that has contributed to this year's losses is the growing population pressures that have led many people to settle on vulnerable flood plains and hillsides.
The most severe 1998 disasters listed in the report include Hurricane Mitch, the deadliest Atlantic storm in 200 years, which has caused more than 10,000 deaths in Honduras, Nicaragua, Guatemala and El Salvador, and caused damage estimated at $4 billion in Honduras and $1 billion in Nicaragua.
The study said Mitch hit an ecologically vulnerable region. Central American nations have experienced some of the highest rates of deforestation in the world, losing some 2 percent to 4 percent of their remaining forest cover each year, said the study.
The costliest disaster of 1998, according to the report, was the flooding of the Yangtze River in the summer. It killed more than 3,000 people, dislocated about 230 million people and incurred $30 billion in losses.
The study said that while heavy summer rains are common in southern and central China, the Yangtze Basin has lost 85 percent of its forest cover to logging and agriculture in recent decades, wetlands have been drained, and the river heavily dammed.
Bangladesh suffered its most extensive flood of the century in the summer. Two-thirds of the low-lying country located at the mouth of the Ganges and Brahmaputra Rivers was flooded for months, 30 million people were left temporarily homeless, and 10,000 miles of roads were heavily damaged. Damage estimates exceed $3.4 billion.
Logging upriver in the Himalayas of north India and Nepal exacerbated the disaster, as did the fact that the region's rivers and flood plains have been filled with silt and constricted by development, according to the report.
"Climate change and rising sea levels are projected to make Bangladesh even more vulnerable to flooding in the future," said the study.
The study said governments are beginning to recognize the role of human activities in worsening natural disasters. It noted that China has banned logging in the upper Yangtze watershed, prohibited additional land reclamation projects in the river's flood plain and earmarked $2 billion to reforest the watershed.
""Unless ravaged nations rebuild along a path of sustainable development that emphasizes restoring and maintaining healthy ecosystems, they risk even greater exposure to the devastation of unnatural disasters in the future," said the report.

The study is based on estimates from Worldwatch, an environmental- research group, and Munich Re, the Frankfurt, Germany-based reinsurer, which writes policies that protect insurance companies from the risk of massive claims.
Locally, tornadoes swept across Central Florida on Feb. 22 and 23, killing 42 and causing more than $87 million in damage.
Florida's July wildfires cost about $150 million to extinguish. Property and business damages totaled an additional $550 million.
The Worldwatch report says a combination of deforestation and climate change contributed to this year's most severe disasters, among them Hurricane Mitch, the flooding of China's Yangtze River and Bangladesh's most extensive flood of the century.
"More and more, there's a human fingerprint in natural disasters in that we're making them more frequent and more intense, and we're also ... making them more destructive," said Seth Dunn, climate- change expert at the institute.
Another element that has contributed to this year's losses is the population pressure that has led many people to settle on vulnerable flood plains and hillsides.
The most severe 1998 disasters listed in the report include Hurricane Mitch, the deadliest Atlantic storm in 200 years, which caused more than 10,000 deaths in Honduras, Nicaragua, Guatemala and El Salvador, and caused damage estimated at $4 billion in Honduras and $1 billion in Nicaragua.

Heat is on in attempt to combat climate change: [1 Edition]

Author: Watson, Craig

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/heat-is-on-attempt-combat-climate-change/docview/332493132/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: aGLOBAL climate change is likely to cause floods, droughts and other natural disasters in Scotland and should be dealt with by a distinctly Scottish group of experts, a Government-sponsored report recommends. The report by the UK Climate Impacts Programme (UKCIP), due to be published next week, highlights the need for more political leadership and an integrated research programme. UKCIP is funded by the Department of Environment, Transport and the Regions and already carries out some of those functions for the whole of the UK, but a distinctly Scottish advisory group is seen as necessary to advise Holyrood leaders.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Heat+is+on+in+attempt+to+combat+climate+change&amp;author=Watson%2C+Craig&amp;issn=&amp;title=The+Herald&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-11-25&amp;spage=4&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: aGLOBAL climate change is likely to cause floods, droughts and other natural disasters in Scotland and should be dealt with by a distinctly Scottish group of experts, a Government-sponsored report recommends.
The report by the UK Climate Impacts Programme (UKCIP), due to be published next week, highlights the need for more political leadership and an integrated research programme.
The Scottish Office is understood to have acted on the latter recommendation and invited tenders from academic groups to carry out the work.
The bids were received this week and an announcement about the successful tender is expected on Monday at a climate change workshop in Edinburgh.
Ministers are also expected to agree to set up a climate change forum - which could include academics, campaigners and public bodies - to advise the Government on Scottish issues.
UKCIP is funded by the Department of Environment, Transport and the Regions and already carries out some of those functions for the whole of the UK, but a distinctly Scottish advisory group is seen as necessary to advise Holyrood leaders.
The recommendations are contained in a report from the first workshop, which examined the impact of climate change.
The report says: "Evidence of increasing global temperatures is irrefutable. The world is now 0.6C hotter than it was 100 years ago. Warming has proceeded at a rapid rate and nine of the 10 hottest years on record have occurred since 1980.
"Last year, was the warmest since records began in 1860 and each month so far has re-written the record books.
"Scotland may become warmer and wetter and possibly windier, particularly in the autumn. Spring and summer temperatures could become a little more variable and winter temperatures are likely to be milder. Heavy downpours and severe winter gales are likely to increase."
It says temperatures in Scotland could have risen by as much as 2.6C by 2080, with 17% more rainfall over the same period. By 2050, sea levels around Scotland could have risen by up to 69cm, with the east coast slightly worse off than the west.
Plant and wildlife habitats could shift northwards by up to 50 miles a decade, threatening rare alpine species, but perhaps encouraging the spread of the midge and other creatures.
There are also fears that the North Atlantic Drift, which is often wrongly referred to as the Gulf Stream, could shift and cause big temperature drops in Scotland, although that is currently not thought likely.
The report highlights the dangers to the Scottish economy, warning of reduction of salmon stocks because of warmer waters and a decline in the winter sports industry due to a lack of snow.
Tourism and agriculture could also be hit, it says.
The cost of flood damage could increase six-fold to #60m over the next 50 years and insurance premiums may rise, the report warns.
Failure to tackle greenhouse gases, principally carbon dioxide, will also contribute to global warming, it adds, although so-called carbon sinks in Scotland, such as forests and peat bogs, help to soak up such emissions.
At the workshop, which involved a range of organisations, Scottish Environment Minister Lord Sewel asked if there was a gap in the UK approach to climate change which could be filled by a distinctly Scottish group.
The report says: "Whilst there is considerable expertise already existing in Scotland, there is a need for leadership and policy support and motivation.
"Many organisations would be reluctant to participate in a concerted approach unless encouraged to do so by Government policy at a high level. It would be important to foster collaborative and integrated research.
"There was agreement that a new forum should advise the Ministers and the Scottish Parliament on climate change."
The paper says the forum could: report every five years; review research and produce an integrated assessment of impacts on Scotland; and make recommendations about policy.
Illustration Caption: It gets worse: the reports predicts environmental change could bring warmer but wetter weather to Scotland with heavy downpours and severe gales expected in winter. Picture: BRIAN LOGUE

Global heat mounts for US to cut pollution Former environment secretary JOHN GUMMER at the Buenos Aires world climate conference says it is vital for the United States to curb emissions before they cause floods here: [A Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-heat-mounts-us-cut-pollution-former/docview/329291059/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: It's not just the worldwide increase in storms and hurricanes nor even the rising sea levels which are menacing Britain's East coast from Yorkshire to Essex. Scientists are increasingly united in the belief that it is our industrial and traffic pollution which is changing the world's climate. What is at stake in Buenos Aires is the world's climate. We can't reverse the damage we've already done. The earth's temperature will rise by about two degrees centigrade whatever we do, because of the greenhouse gases already up in the atmosphere. What we can do is to stop it getting worse. Fear of those overwhelming effects starting within our children's lifetimes has brought ministers from almost every country in the world to Buenos Aires to try to make last year's agreement, hammered out in Kyoto, actually work. The Honduran floods ought to be concentrating the minds of delegates on the dangers of failure. Sadly, the situation is constantly distorted by intense lobbying by the worst of American big business. A photograph in the Conference Bulletin sums it up. The lugubrious Don Pearlman, longtime paid advocate for the United States' coal and oil industry, was seen on camera passing information to the Saudi Arabian delegate.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Global+heat+mounts+for+US+to+cut+pollution+Former+environment+secretary+JOHN+GUMMER+at+the+Buenos+Aires+world+climate+conference+says+it+is+vital+for+the+United+States+to+curb+emissions+before+they+cause+floods+here&amp;author=&amp;issn=14725215&amp;title=Evening+Standard&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-11-13&amp;spage=21&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   YOU can't ignore global warming any more.
It's not just the worldwide increase in storms and hurricanes nor even the rising sea levels which are menacing Britain's East coast from Yorkshire to Essex. Scientists are increasingly united in the belief that it is our industrial and traffic pollution which is changing the world's climate.
Even if you don't rate the scientists, listen to the insurance industry, where the hardheaded money men, from the Guardian Royal to the Munich Re, are busy taking global warming for granted in calculating our premiums.
What is at stake in Buenos Aires is the world's climate. We can't reverse the damage we've already done. The earth's temperature will rise by about two degrees centigrade whatever we do, because of the greenhouse gases already up in the atmosphere. What we can do is to stop it getting worse.
We can just about cope with the present degree of change but we can't manage catastrophic changes which will interrupt the monsoons, turn the whole of Spain and southern France into desert, and may even mean that Britain ceases to benefit from the Gulf Stream.
Fear of those overwhelming effects starting within our children's lifetimes has brought ministers from almost every country in the world to Buenos Aires to try to make last year's agreement, hammered out in Kyoto, actually work. The Honduran floods ought to be concentrating the minds of delegates on the dangers of failure. Sadly, the situation is constantly distorted by intense lobbying by the worst of American big business. A photograph in the Conference Bulletin sums it up. The lugubrious Don Pearlman, longtime paid advocate for the United States' coal and oil industry, was seen on camera passing information to the Saudi Arabian delegate.
Throughout these negotiations, ministers from Britain and the rest of Europe are battling, not just against the entrenched and opposing positions of the United States and China, but against hundreds of well-financed American lobbyists moving freely among Third World delegations, stiffening their resistance to any agreement.
Britain has been at the centre of all this ever since Margaret Thatcher became convinced of the scientists' warnings of impending disaster. Britain's Hadley research centre has done much of the key research.
Tony Blair's Government has taken on John Major's consistently tough line and Deputy Prime Minister John Prescott is playing a central part in forging the deal.
He has spelled it out in words of one syllable. Either we act now or it will be too late and our children will never forgive us. "We've got to start taking action at home in all our countries and we've got to convince people that change is possible," he said.
The trouble remains centred in the American fossil fuel industry. They are, in a real sense, dinosaurs. They cannot see that modern industry throughout the world is accepting the facts of climate change and turning them to commercial and technological advantage. The US will be left behind while British companies have set the pace. When BP left the industry lobbying group, the Global Climate Coalition, it made a crucial change. Shell followed, and Amoco will go before the end of the year. An increasingly important group of companies has come out of the closet to fight climate change. Monsanto, Toyota, Allied Signal, United Technologies - even General Motors has taken the first tentative steps.
Yet the battle is not over.
There are still some huge interests fighting to stop any agreement.
Reactionary companies such as Exxon (Esso in the UK) used all their strength to stop the US even signing up to an agreement which they had already made in Kyoto. Now that those tactics have failed, they are trying to make the signature inoperative. One of their friendly senators, Chuck Hagel, claimed yesterday, immediately after the US had announced it would sign, that the protocol is already dead. His industry backers will certainly spend vast sums to make that statement come true.
On the other side, American environmentalists have been putting pressure on the Clinton administration. A huge hot-air balloon floated over the conference yesterday morning, emblazoned with the words "more hot air from the US". The National Environmental Trust has sponsored huge advertisements contrasting Vice-President Al Gore's fine words on the environment with his lack of any effective action. The White House is so annoyed that it has telephoned those who fund this respected green pressure group, threatening them unless they withdraw their money.
SO BUENOS Aires is a frenzy of activity, of charge and countercharge. Some time early tomorrow morning I believe they will reach some sort of agreement.
It will not be enough but it will begin the process of turning Kyoto into a reality. The real problem will continue to be the US and its fear of industry lobbyists. The trouble is that, with 25 per cent of the world's emissions, the US is single-handedly affecting everyone's climate. So it is Britain's climate they are changing. This is therefore not just a matter for the US Senate alone.
Somehow we need to bring home to our American allies that glob- alisation demands global responsibility. If they want the global trading advantages, they will need to take tough measures to protect that globe from disaster.
Buenos Aires is a start but there's a very long way still to go.

Title: Global heat mounts for US to cut pollution Former environment secretary JOHN GUMMER at the Buenos Aires world climate conference says it is vital for the United States to curb emissions before they cause floods here:   [A Edition ]

A corporate agenda for global warming: [Final Edition]

Author: Foster, Peter

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/corporate-agenda-global-warming/docview/329252528/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The Buenos Aires follow-up to the Kyoto protocol on climate change ground to a blessedly inconclusive halt this week, leaving inhabitants of the U.N. wonkasphere to plan further exotically- located conflabs. Global warming hysteria has cooled since Kyoto, despite allegations that the hand of man is behind every extreme weather phenomenon, including the tragic devastation wrought by Hurricane Mitch. Nevertheless, one unlikely place in which the issue seems to have established a firmer foothold is the corporate boardroom. Journalists whose research goes no further than the clippings files maintain that only "loonies" could fail to acknowledge that global warming is a reality. But if that is the case, then Imperial Oil -- Canada's largest and most respected oil company -- is in need of a strait jacket. In a new study, "The High Costs of Kyoto," Imperial not only points out the uncomfortable fact that the science of global warming remains unproven, it clearly lays out the enormous costs of meeting last December's commitments, which for Canada means reducing greenhouse gas emissions for the period 2008-2012 to a target level of 6% below the level of 1990. To criticize those who -- unlike Imperial -- do not take a firm public stand on global warming is not to suggest that they should refuse to take part in the debate. The problem is that some observers appear to believe that the level of corporate interest in the goings on at Buenos Aires gives credence to the science of global warming. It rather reflects a growing realization of the potential costs of unwise policies.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=A+corporate+agenda+for+global+warming&amp;author=Foster%2C+Peter&amp;issn=14868008&amp;title=National+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-11-13&amp;spage=C.7&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The Buenos Aires follow-up to the Kyoto protocol on climate change ground to a blessedly inconclusive halt this week, leaving inhabitants of the U.N. wonkasphere to plan further exotically- located conflabs. Global warming hysteria has cooled since Kyoto, despite allegations that the hand of man is behind every extreme weather phenomenon, including the tragic devastation wrought by Hurricane Mitch. Nevertheless, one unlikely place in which the issue seems to have established a firmer foothold is the corporate boardroom.
The vast majority of corporations in developed countries are already acutely sensitive to green issues. Many have made environmental responsibility an integral part of their corporate culture. Managements realize that an environmentally responsible stance is valued both by their employees and their customers, and thus has an impact on the bottom line. But this is not the same as claiming -- on the basis that "the customer is always right" -- that they should cater to every public misconception.
Many corporations -- including some of the world's biggest oil companies -- apparently regard it as a mark of public relations sophistication to adopt an almost Neville Chamberlain approach to global warming. Some now emphasize the "business opportunities" presented by the drastically lower energy consumption that slashing carbon dioxide emissions implies. Some have enthusiastically signed onto "solutions," such as emissions trading, noting not merely that these methods would be "least cost," but also that the U.S. favours them (which means of course that most of the rest of the world doesn't, as became very clear at Buenos Aires).
The media largely acquiesces in this apparent sell-out.
Journalists whose research goes no further than the clippings files maintain that only "loonies" could fail to acknowledge that global warming is a reality. But if that is the case, then Imperial Oil -- Canada's largest and most respected oil company -- is in need of a strait jacket. In a new study, "The High Costs of Kyoto," Imperial not only points out the uncomfortable fact that the science of global warming remains unproven, it clearly lays out the enormous costs of meeting last December's commitments, which for Canada means reducing greenhouse gas emissions for the period 2008-2012 to a target level of 6% below the level of 1990.
"In our view," says the report, "the issue of climate change and what to do about it, if anything, is the most important economic policy issue facing Canada today."
Imperial points out that the cumulative cost to the Canadian economy of meeting Kyoto commitments could, by 2010, amount to half the total public and private education budget, with a cumulative cost by that date as high as $140 billion. Energy prices could double, large numbers of jobs would be lost, family incomes would be significantly reduced. It explodes a number of the spurious rationales concocted by the act-first-think-later environmental school. Perhaps the most prominent is that draconian action now represents a worthwhile "insurance policy." But, as Imperial points out, taking out an insurance policy is a rational decision. Nobody in her right mind would take out a cripplingly expensive insurance policy with unspecified benefits against a risk that may not even exist.
Part of the problem is that the public has not absorbed the enormous costs of meeting emissions targets. In the proper sense of the term, the customer is only right when the customer is actually buying something, and that doesn't include environmental hysteria.
Direct consumer judgment will only come when Canadians discover they are paying -- among many other higher charges -- up to 25 cents a liter extra for gasoline, which Imperial notes is a possibility if Kyoto commitments are to be met.
To criticize those who -- unlike Imperial -- do not take a firm public stand on global warming is not to suggest that they should refuse to take part in the debate. The problem is that some observers appear to believe that the level of corporate interest in the goings on at Buenos Aires gives credence to the science of global warming. It rather reflects a growing realization of the potential costs of unwise policies.
No scientist denies the greenhouse phenomenon as a critical factor in the existence of life on earth, nor that global warming as a result of human activity is a possibility. But a possibility is not a probability, and the alleged solutions are more costly and damaging than the original problem, if there is one at all.
Meanwhile "natural" climate change of the sort that created historical phenomena such as the "Little Ice Age" in Medieval times is likely to be much more important than anything humans might precipitate.
Above all, it is critical to remember that the global warming debate is at least as much an ideological issue as a scientific one.
Global warming is embraced and ceaselessly promoted by the undead left, for whom it represents an apocalyptic nightmare, proving -- yet again -- the inherent evils of capitalism, and establishing that what we really need is more central control at a global level.

Subject: Insurance policies; Costs; Global warming; Medieval period; Emissions; Climate change; Energy consumption

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=WORLD%2FNATION+BRIEFS&amp;author=COMPILED+FROM+NEWS+DISPATCHES&amp;issn=&amp;title=Newsday&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-11-12&amp;spage=A.21&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Sniper Witness Spotted
A man sought as a witness in the sniper killing of an abortion provider near Buffalo has been spotted in at least two locations in New Jersey, the FBI said yesterday.
James Charles Kopp looks different now from photographs distributed by authorities, an FBI spokeswoman said.
"He is wearing a bushier beard, and he also has gained approximately 10 to 15 pounds," said the spokeswoman, in Newark. "He may additionally be wearing large, reading-type glasses."
Kopp, 44, has also been spotted in the central part of the state, in the Red Bank area, she said. Kopp is wanted on a federal material witness warrant in the Oct. 23 shooting of Dr. Barnett Slepian of Amherst, N.Y.
Additional Gacy Victims? A ground radar survey and a tip from a former detective may lead police to dig behind a house for more victims of John Wayne Gacy, who was executed in 1994 for the killings of 33 boys and young men.
Police in Chicago said yesterday that it was too soon to determine if digging was warranted behind the house on the city's Northwest Side. The radar indicates, but does not prove, the presence of as many as four bodies buried almost 3 1/2 feet deep, the Chicago Tribune reported, citing unnamed sources.
Holocaust Fund Set Up Six European insurance firms agreed yesterday to set up a $90 million humanitarian fund to aid Holocaust victims and to conduct an audit of their books to identify unpaid Holocaust-era claims.
The agreement followed a meeting of an international commission set up with the insurance companies, the U.S. National Association of Insurance Commissioners and the World Jewish Congress.
The WJC has estimated that pre-war life insurance policies held by European Jews were valued at $2.5 billion at today's prices. The commission will now investigate the archives of the insurance companies and establish a mechanism for repaying claims.
Argentina to Curb Gases Argentina will become the first developing country to announce targets to curb its emissions of heat-trapping greenhouse gases to help fight global warming, President Carlos Menem told a UN climate change conference yesterday.
Argentina's announcement was hailed by its close ally, the United States, which is refusing to ratify the Kyoto climate treaty and cut its own emissions without "meaningful participation" from key developing countries.
But it could enrage China and India, which argued that they are too poor to limit their burning of fossil fuels which emit gases like carbon dioxide and that rich nations which contribute most to global warming should pay to clean up.
The fight over developing-world participation has threatened to stop all progress at the Buenos Aires conference, which ends tomorrow. It was meant to find ways of implementing the promise by rich countries in Kyoto, Japan, last December, to cut their emissions by 5.2 percent from 1990 levels by 2008 through 2012.
Pinochet `Immunity' Cited
Britain's highest court is expected to finish hearing testimony today on whether former Chilean dictator Augusto Pinochet is immune from trial.
Yesterday, as Britain received Spain's formal request for Pinochet's extradition, lawyers for Pinochet told the five judges from the House of Lords that as a former head of state he had "absolute immunity" from prosecution.
The Law Lords are considering an appeal by the Crown Prosecution Service, on behalf of Madrid, against a lower court ruling that the ex-dictator was unlawfully arrested on Oct. 16 because he has sovereign immunity.

Our task is to steer a course of stability Autumn Pre-Budget statement: [CITY Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/our-task-is-steer-course-stability-autumn-pre/docview/326455742/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: We expect the current surplus to be pounds 1 billion pounds next year, pounds 3 billion pounds in 2000-2001, pounds 8 billion pounds the year after that, pounds 10 billion pounds and pounds 11 billion pounds in the two years to follow. For the sameyears , net borrowing is expected to be pounds 4 billion pounds and successively pounds 5 billion, pounds 2 billion, pounds 2 billion and pounds 1 billion. Figures better in every year of this Parliament than in any year of the last Parliament.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Our+task+is+to+steer+a+course+of+stability+Autumn+Pre-Budget+statement&amp;author=&amp;issn=&amp;title=Birmingham+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-11-04&amp;spage=18&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Chancellor's
statement n
Mr Gordon Brown told the Commons:
"In this year's Pre-Budget Report we seek to steer a course of stability amid a world economic downturn. We set in place measures to increase productivity and provide support for enterprise. We introduce new measures to make work pay for all our peoplea nd we show how we will invest in health, education and our infrastructure, to provide the modern services on which people rely, in each case long term decisions that will equip our country for the future.
The background to this report is the global downturn, which started in Asia and which has reverberated throughout every continent. It has not only shifted the balance of risks in the world economy from fears about inflation to fears about growth, buthas forced every country, every continent, every international financial institution to cut their estimates for growth.
World trade growth is set to fall by two thirds.
Forecasts for world growth as a whole have now been virtually halved.
One quarter of the world is in recession.
And my objective, the objective for Britain, is that in this uncertain world, Britain not only steers a stable course but that, by building up our long term strength, is more than equal to any and every challenge the global economy presents.
Because in the last 18 months British inflation has been brought down to its target of 2.5 per cent, and because Britain has set in place a new long term monetary framework, with the independence of the Bank of England, Britain is better placed thanin the past to face these global difficulties.
And because, too, Britain has tackled its structural deficit in the public finances we are more able to steer that stable course.
But as all forecasts round the world have made clear there is acute uncertainty about the eventual outcome of the global financial instability.
So we are conscious of the balance of risks - the risk on the one hand of a sharper slowdown in the world economy as a result of instability and its effect on confidence, the risk on the other that inflationary pressures might persist.
The Pre-Budget Report is set with this firmly in mind, no denial of short term difficulties, no diversion from policies for long term strength.
And our challenge in the year ahead is to strengthen the three essential foundations for that long term strength and success.
"First, Britain now has, for the first time, a consistent long term framework for both monetary and fiscal policy - one that has an inbuilt capacity to respond credibly to short term pressures and one from which we will not be deflected.
Second, with business, we are putting in place a strategy to tackle a fundamental long term economic weakness, the 40 per cent productivity gap with our most successful competitors - and these measures include investment in education and innovation,andnew encouragement for enterprise and competition.
And third, with our Welfare to Work programme and by ensuring that work pays, we are extending opportunity to all, creating a Britain where no one is excluded, and everyone has a contribution to make, a Britain that is both enterprising and fair.
Stability n
First, the foundation of long term economic stability.
Official figures now show that by spring, 1997, with consumer spending growing at an unsustainable pace, inflation was heading way above the country's 2.5 per cent target, to twice that of our competitors, four per cent and above, and that Britain wasse t to repeat the boom-bust cycle that had led to 15 per cent interest rates for a whole year in 1990.
Because immediate action was taken - making the Bank of England independent, and tackling the inflationary pressures - inflation is today at our target of 2.5 per cent. And I can announce that for future years our forecast is that it will stay ontarget.
As a result, Britain's long term interest rates have come down from over seven per cent to five per cent; the differential between Britain and Germany has narrowed by nearly one per cent; long term interest rates are now the lowest for 35 years, thelowest since Britain's boom-bust cycle became entrenched.
And because Britain has brought inflation under control it has been possible, as the world has turned downwards, for interest rates to be able to respond more quickly and in a more forward-looking way than in past British economic cycles.
In the previous economic cycle, interest rates remained in double figures for over four years. In contrast, the Bank of England has already been able to reduce interest rates to respond to a changed international environment.
Long term monetary stability is a pre-condition of economic success and I reaffirm my support for the Bank of England's independence - its remit and membership - and I do not believe that any political party - putting the long term interests of Britainf irst, will, on reflection, bring party politics and short-termism back into interest rate decisions.
It is also because Britain now has a new long term fiscal framework - with clear disciplines set out in the Code for Fiscal Stability that we are laying before Parliament today - that, as world growth slows, fiscal policy is able to make itscontribution to stability and future growth in Britain.
"The official figures published today confirm that in our first year we cut the budget deficit from pounds 28 billion to pounds 8 billion pounds.
This tightening has continued throughout our second year, a total fiscal tightening in two years of 3.75 per cent of national income. So fiscal policy has played its part with interest rate policy in tackling inflationary pressures.
I can tell the House that, because we have concentrated on our priorities and cut waste, spending will be a total of pounds 2 billion pounds below the ceilings we inherited.
As a result, our current budget this year is expected to be pounds 5.5 billion pounds in surplus, and what was prudently projected to be net borrowing of pounds 1 billion pounds is now expected to be a debt repayment of pounds 1.5 billion pounds.
The golden rule is that over the cycle, we balance the current budget, in other words on current spending that we eliminate the structural deficit. I can report to the House that because of the tough action we have taken since we came to Government thisis exactly what we are now achieving.
Having broken with short termism and created, for the long term, a stable monetary and fiscal framework, with an inbuilt capacity to be more responsive to the economic cycle, fiscal and monetary policy can both together contribute to stability andgrowth in the coming years.
My forecast for 1999 - of one to 1.5 per cent growth - will see Britain steering a stable course even when one quarter of the world is in recession.
And as the economy returns to its sustainable growth path, we expect growth in 2000 to be between 2.25 and 2.75 per cent. And in 2001 between 2.75 and 3.25 per cent.
It is because we are cautious about the balance of risks in the economy that we have based public finance forecasts on deliberately more prudent assumptions than before.
First, our public finances are planned on an estimate of 2.25 per cent trend rate of growth, 0.25 per cent lower than the assumption that we inherited.
Second, our forecasts have revised downwards the ratio of VAT receipts to projected consumer spending, reducing estimated revenues by nearly pounds 4 billion over the next five years.
Third, revenues from tackling fraud have been set at a more cautious level than in the last Parliament.
Fourth, we have discontinued the imprudent practice of assuming revenues from privatisations that have not even been agreed.
In each case our assumptions have been independently audited by the National Audit Office.
And, because of the experience of the early 1990s, we have adopted a more prudent approach to forecasting income tax and corporation tax revenues, including a cautious estimate of revenue from self assessment.
So, even after making most prudent assumptions and then taking into account the world downturn, we meet our first rule - to balance our current budget over the cycle.
We expect the current surplus to be pounds 1 billion pounds next year, pounds 3 billion pounds in 2000-2001, pounds 8 billion pounds the year after that, pounds 10 billion pounds and pounds 11 billion pounds in the two years to follow. For the sameyears , net borrowing is expected to be pounds 4 billion pounds and successively pounds 5 billion, pounds 2 billion, pounds 2 billion and pounds 1 billion. Figures better in every year of this Parliament than in any year of the last Parliament.
An estimated current surplus for the coming five years of pounds 33 billion pounds, a margin that shows that we are equipped to cope with further uncertainties.
This pounds 33 billion pounds contrasts with, under the last Government, a deficit of pounds 149 billion pounds over the economic cycle, as national debt doubled.
Our second rule, the sustainable investment rule, requires that, as we borrow for investment, debt is set at a prudent and stable level.
In the last year of the last Government, that debt ratio was 45 per cent. In the next three years, to meet the needs of a modern infrastructure, public investment will double.
As a result of our overall prudence, debt as a proportion of GDP is set to fall below 40 per cent to 39 per cent next year, to 38 and then to 36.5 per cent.
And members who take a special interest in the Maastricht criteria will want to know that in each year of the next five years Britain is comfortably within the Maastricht guidelines.
Productivity n
Long-term economic stability, from which we will not be diverted, is the foundation for future success.
But we will only achieve our long term goals for growth and employment through an even more radical modernisation of our economic policy in favour of opportunity, enterprise and work.
We need to push ahead with modernisation in every area - improving productivity, expanding opportunity and investing in our future. And in every one of these areas our economic policy is based on getting the best out of all our people and all theirpoten tial - in other words, on maximising economic opportunity for all. What makes for a good economy also makes for a good society - one that is fair and cohesive.
So I now turn to the first conclusions from the review we have conducted with British business into removing the barriers to productivity.
As our seminars with business have revealed, the only way for Britain to be at the forefront of the new knowledge-based economies of the future is by modern policies for education, employee participation, small business development and science andinnova tion.
Our first recommendations concern the quality of education. With our pounds 19 billion pound investment in educational reform, we have set out demanding targets for literacy, numeracy, teaching standards and qualifications. And with my Rt Hon friendtheSecretary of State for Education and Employment's announcement today of a pounds 250 million pound investment in a new traineeship programme for teenagers, we have now made it possible for every young person after 16 to stay on in part-time or fulimeedu cation, to get qualifications and to have the opportunity of a job.
But to meet the productivity challenge we must do far more to encourage the ambitions of all our children, not least by bringing the world of education and the world of work into closer contact.
Over the next year we plan to enlist business leaders to take the world of work and business into our classrooms.
And to encourage businesses to offer expertise and management help to our schools and colleges, I can announce that the Budget will allow businesses to claim tax relief when they second staff to schools and colleges.
But as our productivity discussions with business have also revealed, Britain can do more to remove the barriers to opportunity and to ambition.
The opportunity not just to acquire the best skills but the ambition to work your way up and use your creative talents, the ambition to start and build a successful business, the ambition to see the firm in which you work succeed and you succeed withit.
Today, only a fraction of British employees and an even smaller minority of those outside senior management own shares in the companies that they work in and yet the evidence is that employee commitment is a vital strength for companies competing andsuc ceeding in the global economy. And I want, through targeted tax reform, to reward long term commitment by employees and I want to remove, once and for all, the old "them and us" culture in British industry. I want to encourage the new enterpriseculture of team work in which everyone contributes and everyone benefits from success. So, in the Budget we will make it easier for all employees - and not just a few - to become stakeholders in their company.
I want to double the number of firms in which all employees have the opportunity to own shares.
Many employees already hold shares through their pension funds. So I will propose to pensions funds and other institutions that they should provide better and more direct public information to their members and investors.
In future, more of our wealth and jobs will come from small and growing businesses.
In just 18 months we have announced cuts in the rate of corporation tax on large companies twice to 30 pence, and for small companies twice to 20 pence - its lowest level ever.
In the Budget we will consider a further tax reduction for small businesses. In particular we will consider converting the temporary investment allowance we introduced last year into a permanent tax cut.
But I have in mind a bigger reform to cut the burden of tax and red tape. Small businesses getting started and growing, lack not only the resources to pay tax but the back up to administer national insurance, income tax, their VAT payments and theirpayroll systems.
From April, 1999, the Inland Revenue and the Contributions Agency will be merged. And we propose to roll out, on a nationwide basis, a new comprehensive service for new businesses, helping replace time- consuming book-keeping by offering a one stopadvice service administered by a national helpline, backed up by local offices.
So from now on every department of government will have an obligation to encourage enterprise and entrepreneurs.
And I can confirm to businesses also that we will re-examine planning regulations and building control to identify barriers to productivity and job creation and how the planning system can be speeded up in Britain to help us emulate the success ofhi-tec h clusters and corridors like America's Silicon Valley.
Access to bank finance is critical to the success of every single small business. I have therefore asked the banks to work with Mr Don Cruickshank to assess what steps can be taken to more effectively serve the needs of businesses in the economy.
And to open up and enhance competition we will ensure, with a 20 per cent increase in new funding, that the Office of Fair Trading has the necessary resources to break down barriers which prevent new firms entering markets and keep prices high forconsum ers. Further announcements on how the new arrangements will proceed will come from my Right Honourable Friend the Secretary of State for Trade and Industry when he publishes his Competitiveness White Paper.
Our policy is pro-small business, pro-share ownership, pro-tax simplification and pro-competition.
Our policy is also pro-skills and pro-science.
To turn scientific inventions in Britain into jobs for Britain we need to do more to honour the spirit of invention, facilitate the exploitation of invention and encourage the commercialisation of invention.
More than ever innovation is the key to higher productivity. So w must see that inventions which are created in Britain are developed and manufactured in Britain.
The first step is a higher quality and quantity of research and development. So the Government will consult small business on supplementing the current tax relief for R&D with a more effective tax credit for small business based on the volume of R&Dinvestment.
Three months ago we announced a partnership with the Wellcome Trust to invest over one billion pound to re-equip university science in Britain, the largest ever investment in Britain's science base.
Having received an overwhelming response from universities to our new University Challenge Fund - the private public partnership for commercialising scientific inventions - we are now inviting further private sector involvement.
I now want to complete the path that takes inventions from the science lab through to high tech venture capital and then to the national and global marketplace.
So I am announcing today that to develop business expertise in science and to transfer technology from the science lab to the marketplace, we will endow up to eight new Institutes of Enterprise in British universities. A further signal of ourdeterminati on that the genius of British invention will once again become an engine for British growth and jobs.
A strong venture capital industry supporting high tech, high risk investments is critical to the future of Britain.
Today we have only six per cent of the early stage high tech venture capital of the United States. So we will consult on and consider new incentives, including how to encourage our most successful companies to invest in start-ups, and how we can providenew sources of venture capital and management expertise for entrepreneurs.
The productivity challenge is one that must be met by the public sector and not just the private sector.
Next month, for the first time, the Government will publish Public Service Agreements which set, for all departments, clear targets for improved performance. And to help them meet these targets and to monitor performance we are establishing an advisoryp anel from business and management.
Absenteeism costs the public sector up to pounds 6 billion pounds a year. Specific targets are being set for each department to reduce absence rates by 20 per cent by 2001 and 30 per cent by 2003.
We are working with business, not just to promote higher productivity, but to secure policies for a sustainable environment.
I am publishing today the Report of Lord Marshall, formerly president of the CBI, into the role of economic instruments and the business use of energy. I thank him for his work, which substantially moves forward the debate, and the Government will givef ull consideration to his recommendations as part of its wider strategy on climate change.
Today I am also publishing a consultation document, containing proposals for a pounds 50 pound reduction in Vehicle Excise Duty for the smallest and most environmentally efficient cars.
Welfare to Work n
Just as the Government is modernising our industrial economy so, too, we are modernising employment policy.
The House will be pleased to know that by next April 300,000 people will have benefited from the New Deal. That is now giving men and women previously left out and excluded a new sense of hope.
Already, 29,000 companies have signed up to the New Deal and 30,000 have found jobs with employers, jobs they would not have had without the New Deal.
We are now ready to extend the New Deal. From November 30, 60,000 opportunities will be created for the long term unemployed in 28 areas of the country.
In Northern Ireland, in support of the peace process, we will now guarantee new opportunities to all those men and women who have been unemployed for more than 18 months.
We will also extend nationwide the New Deal skills shortage programme, and offer up-front support for training to help fill the vacancies that come on-stream every month.
And by March next year I can confirm that to tackle skill shortages there will be 120 centres for IT and high tech training in every part of the country.
Side-by-side with the New Deal there will be new guarantees that work will pay more than benefits.
From April, as a result of abolishing the entry fee to national insurance, all employees will receive a tax cut of pounds 66.
And business will be pleased that I can announce that from April, employers will not pay national insurance on earnings below pounds 83 pounds a week, the 1999 level for the personal tax allowance.
When it is economically right to do so - and so that work pays more - we will introduce the ten pence starting rate of income tax.
From April 1, 1.9 million employees, including 1.3 million women, will benefit from the minimum wage.
And I can also confirm that the minimum income guarantee for a low paid family, which as a result of the working families tax credit was announced at pounds 180 pounds will, as a result of the minimum wage and tax and benefit changes we are making, bera ised to pounds 190 pounds a week from October next year.
So that work pays - a guaranteed minimum of pounds 190 pounds - with no income tax to pay on incomes below pounds 220 pounds a week.
This will guarantee a minimum income for a lone parent with one child of at least pounds 5.50 pounds an hour, and for an adult with two children pounds 6.37 pounds an hour.
To enable parents and carers to balance work and family responsibilities, I want to provide extra help for child care. We have provided resources for up to one million new child care places over the next four years.
And today, consistent with our national child care strategy we will extend our new childcare tax credit to cover all children up to age 14. And in the case of children who are disabled, and who have greater needs, until the school leaving age of 16.
We will also match the working families tax credit with a new disabled person's tax credit that will ensure that work pays for a disabled man or woman who takes a job.
A disabled person, with one child, moving from benefit to full time employment, will be guaranteed a minimum income of pounds 220 pounds a week, with no income tax payable on income below pounds 274 pounds a week.
Disabled men and women taking up work will be as much as pounds 78 pounds a week better off.
So under this Government, disabled people, who want to work, will now be guaranteed fair treatment at work and work will pay.
And matching the minimum income guarantee for disabled persons in work there will be a new disability income guarantee of pounds 128 a week for the severely disabled out of work.
And at the same time, we are guaranteeing pensioner couples a new minimum income of pounds 117 a week. And this will be followed in the Budget with a guarantee that pensioners will have no income tax to pay unless their income rises above a newspecified level.
And as a result of the measures we have taken - by following up our cut in VAT on fuel with tougher regulation and our new winter fuel payments - I can say that pensioners are saving pounds 108 pounds on their fuel bills and the poorest pensioners aresa ving pounds 140 pounds.
So our task as a country for the coming year, steering a course of stability, is to meet the productivity challenge, to extend fairness and welfare to work, and to make the modern investment we need in our future.
Investing in our future n
And I am pleased to announce that following the modernisation of the private finance initiative there will be new investment in our infrastructure - in over 1,000 schools, in over 25 hospitals and in dozens of public transport projects - over the comingthree years an additional pounds 11 billion pounds.
And I can also confirm that over the three years from next April, public investment itself will double and do so at the right time - the right decision, the right course of action for our country.
I have one further announcement:
Because of our prudence in the management of public finances, Government spending is well within the limits we set.
Our prudence is for a purpose.
Therefore, to ensure in every part of the United Kingdom the health care that people - and especially elderly people - need this winter, I am today making an additional and immediate winter cash allocation, to be spent in the next five months, of pounds250 million pounds more for our National Health Service.
And I can confirm that because this Government believes in the best health and the best education, not just for a few but for all our people, we will invest an additional pounds 40 billion pounds in the modernisation of education and our health serviceo ver the next three years.
Public services that in the months and the years ahead are safe in this Government's hands.
A Government that is steering a stable course, prudently investing in our future, proudly building strong public services and consistently keeping its promises to the British people.
And I commend this statement to the House.

Title: Our task is to steer a course of stability Autumn Pre-Budget statement:   [CITY Edition ]
