'Seeking to steer a course of stability amid world downturn' CHANCELLOR'S SPEECH: [London edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/seeking-steer-course-stability-amid-world/docview/248691606/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Because in the last 18 months British inflation has been brought down to its target of 2.5 per cent, and because Britain has set in place a new long-term monetary framework, with the independence of the Bank of England, Britain is better placed than in the past to face these global difficulties. Official figures now show that by spring 1997, with consumer spending growing at an unsustainable pace, inflation was heading way above the country's 2.5 per cent target, to twice that of our competitors, 4 per cent and above, and that Britain was set to repeat the boom-bust cycle that had led to 15 per cent interest rates for a whole year in 1990. Because immediate action was taken - making the Bank of England independent, and tackling the inflationary pressures - inflation is today at our target of 2.5 per cent. And I can announce that for future years our forecast is that it will stay on target. As a result, Britain's long-term interest rates have come down from over 7 per cent to 5 per cent; the differential between Britain and Germany has narrowed by nearly 1 per cent; long-term interest rates are now the lowest for 35 years, the lowest since Britain's boom-bust cycle became entrenched.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=%27Seeking+to+steer+a+course+of+stability+amid+world+downturn%27+CHANCELLOR%27S+SPEECH%3A&amp;author=&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-11-04&amp;spage=15&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: In this year's pre-Budget report we seek to steer a course of stability amid a world economic downturn. We set in place measures to increase productivity and provide support for enterprise. We introduce new measures to make work pay for all our people and we show how we will invest in health, education and our infrastructure, to provide the modern services on which people rely, in each case long-term decisions that will equip our country for the future.
The background to this report is the global downturn, which started in Asia and which has reverberated throughout every continent. It has not only shifted the balance of risks in the world economy from fears about inflation to fears about growth, but has forced every country, every continent, every international financial institution to cut their estimates for growth.
World trade growth is set to fall by two thirds. Forecasts for world growth as a whole have now been virtually halved. One quarter of the world is in recession.
And my objective, the objective for Britain, is that in this uncertain world, Britain not only steers a stable course but that, by building up our long-term strength, is more than equal to any and every challenge the global economy presents.
Because in the last 18 months British inflation has been brought down to its target of 2.5 per cent, and because Britain has set in place a new long-term monetary framework, with the independence of the Bank of England, Britain is better placed than in the past to face these global difficulties.
And because too, Britain has tackled its structural deficit in the public finances we are more able to steer that stable course.
But as all forecasts round the world have made clear there is acute uncertainty about the eventual outcome of the global financial instability.
So we are conscious of the balance of risks - the risk on the one hand of a sharper slowdown in the world economy as a result of instability and its effect on confidence, the risk on the other that inflationary pressures might persist.
The pre-Budget report is set with this firmly in mind, no denial of short-term difficulties, no diversion from policies for long-term strength.
And our challenge in the year ahead is to strengthen the three essential foundations for that long-term strength and success.
First, Britain now has, for the first time, a consistent long-term framework for both monetary and fiscal policy - one that has an inbuilt capacity to respond credibly to short-term pressures and one from which we will not be deflected.
Second, with business, we are putting in place a strategy to tackle a fundamental long-term economic weakness, the 40 per cent productivity gap with our most successful competitors - and these measures include investment in education and innovation, and new encouragement for enterprise and competition.
And third, with our Welfare-to-Work programme and by ensuring that work pays, we are extending opportunity to all, creating a Britain where no one is excluded, and everyone has a contribution to make, a Britain that is both enterprising and fair. Stability
First, the foundation of long-term economic stability.
Official figures now show that by spring 1997, with consumer spending growing at an unsustainable pace, inflation was heading way above the country's 2.5 per cent target, to twice that of our competitors, 4 per cent and above, and that Britain was set to repeat the boom-bust cycle that had led to 15 per cent interest rates for a whole year in 1990.
Because immediate action was taken - making the Bank of England independent, and tackling the inflationary pressures - inflation is today at our target of 2.5 per cent. And I can announce that for future years our forecast is that it will stay on target. As a result, Britain's long-term interest rates have come down from over 7 per cent to 5 per cent; the differential between Britain and Germany has narrowed by nearly 1 per cent; long-term interest rates are now the lowest for 35 years, the lowest since Britain's boom-bust cycle became entrenched.
And because Britain has brought inflation under control it has been possible, as the world has turned downwards, for interest rates to be able to respond more quickly and in a more forward-looking way than in past British economic cycles.
In the previous economic cycle, interest rates remained in double figures for over four years. In contrast, the Bank of England has already been able to reduce interest rates to respond to a changed international environment.
Long-term monetary stability is a pre-condition of economic success and I reaffirm my support for the Bank of England's independence - its remit and membership - and I do not believe that any political party - putting the long-term interests of Britain first, will, on reflection, bring party politics and short-termism back into interest rate decisions.
It is also because Britain now has a new long-term fiscal framework - with clear disciplines set out in the Code for Fiscal Stability that we are laying before parliament today - that, as world growth slows, fiscal policy is able to make its contribution to stability and future growth in Britain.
The official figures published today confirm that in our first year we cut the budget deficit from £28bn to £8bn.
This tightening has continued throughout our second year, a total fiscal tightening in two years of 3{3/4} per cent of national income. So fiscal policy has played its part with interest rate policy in tackling inflationary pressures.
I can tell the House that, because we have concentrated on our priorities and cut waste, spending will be a total of £2bn below the ceilings we inherited.
As a result, our current budget this year is expected to be £5{1/2}bn in surplus, and what was prudently projected to be net borrowing of £1bn is now expected to be a debt repayment of £1{1/2}bn.
The golden rule is that over the cycle, we balance the current budget, in other words on current spending that we eliminate the structural deficit. I can report to the House that because of the tough action we have taken since we came to government this is exactly what we are now achieving.
Having broken with short termism and created, for the long term, a stable monetary and fiscal framework, with an inbuilt capacity to be more responsive to the economic cycle, fiscal and monetary policy can both together contribute to stability and growth in the coming years.
My forecast for 1999 - of 1 to
1{1/2} per cent growth for 1999 - will see Britain steering a stable course even when one-quarter of the world is in recession.
And as the economy returns to its sustainable growth path, we expect growth in 2000 to be between 2{1/4} per cent and 2{3/4} per cent. And in 2001 between 2{3/4} and 3{1/4} per cent.
It is because we are cautious about the balance of risks in the economy that we have based public finance forecasts on deliberately more prudent assumptions than before.
First, our public finances are planned on an estimate of 2{1/4} per cent trend rate of growth, {1/4} per cent lower than the assumption that we inherited.
Second, our forecasts have revised downwards the ratio of VAT receipts to projected consumer spending, reducing estimated revenues by nearly £4bn over the next five years.
Third, revenues from tackling fraud have been set at a more cautious level than in the last parliament.
Fourth, we have discontinued the imprudent practice of assuming revenues from privatisations that have not even been agreed.
In each case our assumptions have been independently audited by the National Audit Office.
And, because of the experience of the early 1990s, we have adopted a more prudent approach to forecasting income tax and corporation tax revenues, including a cautious estimate of revenue from self-assessment.
So, even after making most prudent assumptions and then taking into account the world downturn, we meet our first rule - to balance our current budget over the cycle.
We expect the current surplus to be £1bn next year, £3bn in 2000-2001, £8bn the year after that, £10bn and £11bn in the two years to follow. For the same years, net borrowing is expected to be £4bn and successively 5, 2, 2 and 1. Figures better in every year of this parliament than in any year of the last parliament.
An estimated current surplus for the coming 5 years of £33bn, a margin that shows that we are equipped to cope with further uncertainties.
This £33bn contrasts with, under the last government, a deficit of £149bn over the economic cycle, as national debt doubled.
Our second rule, the sustainable investment rule, requires that, as we borrow for investment, debt is set at a prudent and stable level.
In the last year of the last government, that debt ratio was 45 per cent. In the next three years, to meet the needs of a modern infrastructure, public investment will double.
As a result of our overall prudence, debt as a proportion of GDP is set to fall below 40 per cent to 39 per cent next year, to 38 and then to 36{1/2} per cent.
And members who take a special interest in the Maastricht criteria will want to know that in each year of the next five years Britain is comfortably within the Maastricht guidelines. Productivity
Long-term economic stability, from which we will not be diverted, is the foundation for future success.
But we will only achieve our long-term goals for growth and employment through an even more radical modernisation of our economic policy in favour of opportunity, enterprise and work.
We need to push ahead with modernisation in every area - improving productivity, expanding opportunity and investing in our future. And in every one of these areas our economic policy is based on getting the best out of all our people and all their potential - in other words, on maximising economic opportunity for all. What makes for a good economy also makes for a good society - one that is fair and cohesive.
So I now turn to the first conclusions from the review we have conducted with British business into removing the barriers to productivity.
As our seminars with business have revealed, the only way for Britain to be at the forefront of the new knowledge-based economies of the future is by modern policies for education, employee participation, small business development and science and innovation.
Our first recommendations concern the quality of education. With our £19bn investment in educational reform, we have set out demanding targets for literacy, numeracy, teaching standards and qualifications. And with my Rt Hon Friend the Secretary of State for Education and Employment's announcement today of a £250m investment in a new traineeship programme for teenagers, we have now made it possible for every young person after 16 to stay on in part-time or full-time education, to get qualifications and to have the opportunity of a job.
But to meet the productivity challenge we must do far more to encourage the ambitions of all our children, not least by bringing the world of education and the world of work into closer contact.
Over the next year we plan to enlist business leaders to take the world of work and business into our classrooms.
And to encourage businesses to offer expertise and management to help to our schools and colleges, I can announce that the Budget will allow businesses to claim tax relief when they second staff to schools and colleges.
But as our productivity discussions with business have also revealed, Britain can do more to remove the barriers to opportunity and to ambition.
The opportunity not just to acquire the best skills but the ambition to work your way up and use your creative talents, the ambition to start and build a successful business, the ambition to see the firm in which you work succeed and you succeed with it.
Today, only a fraction of British employees and an even smaller minority of those outside senior management own shares in the companies that they work in and yet the evidence is that employee commitment is a vital strength for companies competing and succeeding in the global economy.
And I want, through targeted tax reform, to reward long-term commitment by employees and I want to remove, once and for all, the old 'them and us' culture in British industry. I want to encourage the new enterprise culture of team-work in which everyone contributes and everyone benefits from success. So, in the Budget we will make it easier for all employees - and not just a few - to become stakeholders in their company.
I want to double the number of firms in which all employees have the opportunity to own shares.
Many employees already hold shares through their pension funds. So I will propose to pensions funds and other institutions that they should provide better and more direct public information to their members and investors.
In future, more of our wealth and jobs will come from small and growing businesses.
In just 18 months we have announced cuts in the rate of corporation tax on large companies twice to 30 pence, and for small companies twice to 20 pence - its lowest level ever.
In the Budget we will consider a further tax reduction for small businesses. In particular we will consider converting the temporary investment allowance we introduced last year into a permanent tax cut.
But I have in mind a bigger reform to cut the burden of tax and red tape. Small businesses getting started and growing, lack not only the resources to pay tax but the back up to administer national insurance, income tax, their VAT payments and their payroll systems.
From April 1999, the Inland Revenue and the Contributions Agency will be merged. And we propose to roll out, on a nationwide basis, a new comprehensive service for new businesses, helping replace time consuming bookkeeping by offering a one-stop advice service administered by a national helpline, backed up by local offices.
So from now on every department of government will have an obligation to encourage enterprise and entrepreneurs.
And I can confirm to businesses also that we will re-examine planning regulations and building control to identify barriers to productivity and job creation and how the planning system can be speeded up in Britain to help us emulate the success of high-tech clusters and corridors like America's silicon valley.
Access to bank finance is critical to the success of every single small business. I have therefore asked the banks to work with Mr Don Cruickshank to assess what steps can be taken to more effectively serve the needs of businesses in the economy.
And to open up and enhance competition we will ensure, with a 20 per cent increase in new funding, that the Office of Fair Trading has the necessary resources to break down barriers which prevent new firms entering markets and keep prices high for consumers. Further announcements on how the new arrangements will proceed will come from my Right Honourable Friend the Secretary of State for Trade and Industry when he publishes his Competitiveness white paper.
Our policy is pro-small business, pro-share ownership, pro-tax simplification and pro-competition. Our policy is also pro-skills and pro-science.
To turn scientific inventions in Britain into jobs for Britain we need to do more to honour the spirit of invention, facilitate the exploitation of invention and encourage the commercialisation of invention.
More than ever innovation is the key to higher productivity. So we must see that inventions which are created in Britain are developed and manufactured in Britain.
The first step is a higher quality and quantity of research and development. So the government will consult small business on supplementing the current tax relief for R&D with a more effective tax credit for small business based on the volume of R&D investment.
Three months ago we announced a partnership with the Wellcome Trust to invest over £1bn to re-equip university science in Britain, the largest ever investment in Britain's science base.
Having received an overwhelming response from universities to our new University Challenge Fund - the private-public partnership for commercialising scientific inventions - we are now inviting further private sector involvement.
I now want to complete the path that takes inventions from the science lab through to high-tech venture capital and then to the national and global marketplace.
So I am announcing today that to develop business expertise in science and to transfer technology from the science lab to the marketplace, we will endow up to eight new Institutes of Enterprise in British universities. A further signal of our determination that the genius of British invention will once again become an engine for British growth and jobs.
A strong venture capital industry supporting high-tech, high-risk investments is critical to the future of Britain.
Today we have only 6 per cent of the early stage high-tech venture capital of the US. So we will consult on and consider new incentives, including how to encourage our most successful companies to invest in start-ups, and how we can provide new sources of venture capital and management expertise for entrepreneurs.
The productivity challenge is one that must be met by the public sector and not just the private sector.
Next month, for the first time, the government will publish Public Service Agreements which set, for all departments, clear targets for improved performance. And to help them meet these targets and to monitor performance we are establishing an advisory panel from business and management.
Absenteeism costs the public sector up to £6bn a year. Specific targets are being set for each department to reduce absence rates by 20 per cent by 2001 and 30 per cent by 2003.
We are working with business, not just to promote higher productivity, but to secure policies for a sustainable environment.
I am publishing today the Report of Lord Marshall, formerly president of the CBI, into the role of economic instruments and the business use of energy. I thank him for his work, which substantially moves forward the debate, and the government will give full consideration to his recommendations as part of its wider strategy on climate change.
Today I am also publishing a consultation document, containing proposals for a £50 reduction in Vehicle Excise Duty for the smallest and most environmentally efficient cars. Welfare to Work
Just as the government is modernising our industrial economy, so too we are modernising employment policy.
The House will be pleased to know that by next April 300,000 people will have benefited from the New Deal. That is now giving men and women previously left out and excluded a new sense of hope.
Already, 29,000 companies have signed up to the New Deal and 30,000 have found jobs with employers, jobs they would not have had without the New Deal.
We are now ready to extend the New Deal. From 30 November, 60,000 opportunities will be created for the long-term unemployed in 28 areas of the country.
In Northern Ireland, in support of the peace process, we will now guarantee new opportunities to all those men and women who have been unemployed for more than 18 months.
We will also extend nationwide the New Deal skills shortage programme, and offer up-front support for training to help fill the vacancies that come on-stream every month.
And by March next year I can confirm that to tackle skill shortages there will be 120 centres for IT and high-tech training in every part of the country.
Side-by-side with the New Deal there will be new guarantees that work will pay more than benefits.
From April, as a result of abolishing the entry fee to national insurance, all employees will receive a tax cut of £66 a year.
And business will be pleased that I can announce that from April, employers will not pay national insurance on earnings below £83 a week, the 1999 level for the personal tax allowance.
When it is economically right to do so - and so that work pays more - we will introduce the 10 pence starting rate of income tax.
From April 1, 1.9m employees, including 1.3m women, will benefit from the minimum wage.
And I can also confirm that the minimum income guarantee for a low paid family, which as a result of the working families tax credit was announced at £180 will, as a result of the minimum wage and tax and benefit changes we are making, be raised to £190 a week from October next year.
So that work pays - a guaranteed minimum of £190 - with no income tax to pay on incomes below £220 a week.
This will guarantee a minimum income for a lone parent with one child of at least £5.50 an hour, and for an adult with 2 children £6.37 an hour.
To enable parents and carers to balance work and family responsibilities, I want to provide extra help for childcare. We have provided resources for up to 1m new childcare places over the next four years. And today, consistent with our national childcare strategy we will extend our new childcare tax credit to cover all children up to age 14. And in the case of children who are disabled, and who have greater needs, until the school leaving age of 16.
We will also match the working families tax credit with a new disabled person's tax credit that will ensure that work pays for a disabled man or woman who takes a job.
A disabled person, with one child, moving from benefit to full time employment, will be guaranteed a minimum income of £220 a week, with no income tax payable on income below £274 a week.
Disabled men and women taking up work will be as much as £78 a week better off.
So under this government, disabled people, who want to work, will now be guaranteed fair treatment at work and work will pay.
And matching the minimum income guarantee for disabled persons in work there will be a new disability income guarantee of £128 a week for the severely disabled out of work.
And at the same time, we are guaranteeing pensioner couples a new minimum income of £117 a week. And this will be followed in the Budget with a guarantee that pensioners will have no income tax to pay unless their income rises above a new specified level.
And as a result of the measures we have taken - by following up our cut in VAT on fuel with tougher regulation and our new winter fuel payments - I can say that pensioners are saving £108 on their fuel bills and the poorest pensioners are saving £140.
So our task as a country for the coming year, steering a course of stability, is to meet the productivity challenge, to extend fairness and welfare to work, and to make the modern investment we need in our future. Investing in our Future
And I am pleased to announce that following the modernisation of the private finance initiative there will be new investment in our infrastructure - in over 1,000 schools, in over 25 hospitals and in dozens of public transport projects - over the coming three years an additional £11bn.
And I can also confirm that over the three years from next April, public investment itself will double and do so at the right time - the right decision, the right course of action for our country.
I have one further announcement.
Because of our prudence in the management of public finances, government spending is well within the limits we set.
Our prudence is for a purpose.
Therefore, to ensure in every part of the United Kingdom the healthcare that people - and especially elderly people - need this winter, I am today making an additional and immediate winter cash allocation, to be spent in the next five months, of £250m more for our National Health Service.
And I can confirm that because this government believes in the best health and the best education, not just for a few but for all our people, we will invest an additional £40bn in the modernisation of education and our health service over the next three years.
Public services that in the months and the years ahead are safe in this government's hands.
A government that is steering a stable course, prudently investing in our future, proudly building strong public services and consistently keeping its promises to the British people.
And I commend this Statement to the House. Copyright Financial Times Limited 1998. All Rights Reserved.

UNITED STATES CONGRESS: [METRO EDITION]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/united-states-congress/docview/279243142/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: IMMIGRATION: The fundamental immigration problem today is with illegal immigrants, not legal immigrants. To address this problem, I have supported efforts to control our borders. In terms of legal immigration, I agree with the current policy, which prioritizes family reunification. In addition, I have supported other forms of legal immigration that serve a specific purpose. I introduced a bill that will provide Florida's farmers with a legal method to meet their seasonal labor requirements if adequate numbers of U.S. workers are not available. I also support legislation that would allow some high- skilled workers to legally work in the United States if there are not U.S. workers available.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=UNITED+STATES+CONGRESS&amp;author=&amp;issn=&amp;title=Orlando+Sentinel&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-11-01&amp;spage=G.4&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   United States Congress
A United States senator must be at least 30 years old, must have been a U.S. citizen for at least nine years and must be a resident of the state he or she represents. Salary: $137,700. Term: six years. Duties include voting on legislation in Congress and helping constituents deal with the federal government.
A U.S. representative must be at least 25 years old, must have been a U.S. citizen for at least seven years and must be a resident of the state he or she represents. Salary: $133,600. Term: two years. Duties include voting on legislation in Congress and helping constituents deal with the federal government.
Here are the questions that the candidates were asked by the Orlando Sentinel:
LEGISLATION: If you could push through only one peice of legislation, what would it be?
SOCIAL SECURITY: Which approaches to ensuring the long-term survival of Social Security do you favor?
- Raise Social Security taxes.
- Raise the retirement age.
- Tax a larger share of benefits.
- Invest Social Security money in the stock market.
- Reduce benefits.
If none of the above, how would you deal with government projections that, without action, the program will go broke?
MEDICARE: What steps do you favor to ensure the long-term survival of Medicare?
- Raise Medicare premiums.
- Reduce benefits.
- Cut payments to health-care providers.
- Means test the program so that affluent retirees pay higher premiums than those with lower incomes.
If none of the above, how would you deal with government projections that, without action, the program will go broke?
PUERTO RICO: Do you favor giving Puerto Ricans the right to vote on whether the commonwealth should become the 51st state? If they vote yes, should Congress automatically accept the results and vote for statehood?
IMMIGRATION: Should the level of legal immigration be raised, held steady or cut? Should immigration law give priority to reuniting families or allowing immigration to those people with skills and education?
TOBACCO: Should taxes on cigarettes be raised to finance a tobacco settlement? And, if so, by how much?
TAXES: Would you replace the current federal income-tax system with either a flat tax or national sales tax? If so, what would you do to ensure that the new system would bring in enough revenue to keep the government functioning and would not disrupt the economy?
ENVIRONMENT: Should the Senate ratify the global-warming treaty, which would require the United States to reduce greenhouse gases by 7 percent from 1990 levels?
Charlie Crist
Republican St. Petersburg Age: 42 Occupation: Lawyer Education: Bachelor's degree from Florida State University, law degree from Cumberland School of Law LEGISLATION: Eliminate the IRS code and replace it with either a flat tax or a national sales tax.
SOCIAL SECURITY: I will never vote to take away benefits owed to our seniors. I view Social Security as a contract that can never be broken. Seniors deserve 100 percent of their benefits. Using some of the budget surplus to make Social Security stable will fix any difficulties.
MEDICARE: The best way to ensure long-term survival of Medicare is to attack fraud and waste in the system. I would not vote to raise premiums, reduce benefits or use means testing. Where appropriate, I would favor cutting payments to health-care providers.
PUERTO RICO: No.
IMMIGRATION: The current level of immigration should be held steady. Priority should be given to both people with skills and those who are trying to reunite with their families. We should focus our attention on controlling our borders and illegal immigration.
TOBACCO: No, the tobacco companies should pay. The consumers should not.
TAXES: I want to abolish the IRS code and institute either a flat tax or a national sales tax. I would encourage a great national debate on the new system. A transition must be done responsibly.
ENVIRONMENT: Not until the theory has been proven that we really have a problem.
Bob Graham
Democrat Miami Lakes Age: 62 Occupation: Senator Education: B.A., University of Florida, J.D., Harvard Law School LEGISLATION: The one piece of legislation that I would enact increases the quality of care in HMOs, strengthens Medicare and expands access to preventative health-care services. Patients who are enrolled in HMOs should expect and demand quality care from their physicians without interference from insurance bureaucrats. They should have comparative information about the care they are receiving, access to an independent appeals process, and a choice of doctors. Medicare should be strengthened by eliminating fraud and encouraging long-term care. Finally, this legislation increases access to preventative-care services, such as screenings for osteoporosis, and breast and colon cancer.
SOCIAL SECURITY: We should resist the temptation to spend today's Social Security surplus on tax breaks. Any reform of Social Security should be a part of a larger context of retirement security, which has three key components: pensions, personal savings and Social Security. Of those three, Social Security is a safety net for retirement security and, therefore, should feature the least risk to participants.
MEDICARE: The Medicare program should be refocused to reflect the realities of older Americans. First, more emphasis should be placed on preventing illnesses. Second, access to long-term care should be expanded by merging similar programs within the Medicare and Medicaid programs. Third, Medicare recipients who use HMOs should be guaranteed high-quality care and access to emergency services. Finally, reforms should be made to eliminate fraud in Medicare.
PUERTO RICO: I have sponsored bipartisan legislation that will give the 3.8 million U.S. citizens in Puerto Rico the right to determine their political future: continued commonwealth status, statehood or independence. If this legislation is enacted and the citizens of Puerto Rico vote for statehood, I will support their inclusion as the 51st state in our union.
IMMIGRATION: The fundamental immigration problem today is with illegal immigrants, not legal immigrants. To address this problem, I have supported efforts to control our borders. In terms of legal immigration, I agree with the current policy, which prioritizes family reunification. In addition, I have supported other forms of legal immigration that serve a specific purpose. I introduced a bill that will provide Florida's farmers with a legal method to meet their seasonal labor requirements if adequate numbers of U.S. workers are not available. I also support legislation that would allow some high- skilled workers to legally work in the United States if there are not U.S. workers available.
TOBACCO: The United States should commit to a program that will reduce underage smoking. Each day, 3,000 children begin smoking - 1,000 of whom will die as a result. The tobacco industry should pay on a national basis, as it has already done in Florida, to reimburse the government for health-care costs related to smoking and for education programs to warn children about the dangers of tobacco. Earlier this year, I sponsored legislation to increase the price of cigarettes by $1.50 a pack. It is estimated that such an increase would reduce teenage smoking by more than 60 percent.
TAXES: I support a simpler, flatter tax system. The current code is complicated and confusing. But I do not support the risky notion of throwing out the entire tax code at once, which could disrupt families and businesses. We took a step in the right direction this year, with the bipartisan reform of the IRS, to make the IRS more consumer-oriented. We should simplify the tax code by tackling a section at a time.
ENVIRONMENT: The Senate should not ratify the global-warming treaty until we receive commitments from developing countries that they will reduce their greenhouse-gas emissions. As currently written, the treaty is not balanced. It requires the United States and other developed countries to carry the burden of meeting a worldwide challenge. I supported a resolution that called for scheduled commitments from developing nations as a prerequisite for Senate ratification of this treaty. Meaningful participation from these nations is critical for reducing the impacts of climate change, while retaining a level playing field for international trade.

AWAY FROM THE FRAY, A SPACE OASIS: THE COSTLY INTERNATIONAL SPACE STATION IS READY TO BE BUILT - 220 MILES ABOVE ITS MANY CRITICS.

Author: Flam, Faye

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/away-fray-space-oasis/docview/1842479074/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=AWAY+FROM+THE+FRAY%2C+A+SPACE+OASIS&amp;author=Flam%2C+Faye&amp;issn=08856613&amp;title=Philadelphia+Inquirer&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-10-29&amp;spage=H.5&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   The International Space Station is so big and complicated it will have to be launched in 43 parts and then put together like a giant set of Tinker Toys.
Astronauts will ride the space shuttle to the 220-mile-high construction site, where, working outside in temperatures that can plunge to minus 170 degrees Fahrenheit, they will assemble the various power modules, laboratory spaces and living quarters.
The space station is the most expensive and ambitious planned project since the moon landing. The first piece is scheduled to go up on an unmanned rocket launched from Russia late next month, followed by a connecting module to be ferried aboard the shuttle in early December.
When construction is done, about 2004 in NASA's estimate, the space station will house six astronauts who will stay for periods of three to six months. With them will be a menagerie of experimental creatures - rats, mice, rabbits, insects and zebra fish. The astronauts will also conduct experiments by growing crystals and tending to plants, and will act as guinea pigs for medical experiments.
Before humans can venture farther into space, the thinking goes, scientists need to learn more about the way people fare when exposed to the radiation, weightlessness and possible hazards out there. Some information was gathered from the Russian space station Mir, launched in 1986. Spaceflight throws off balance, degrades the organs, thins the blood, wastes the muscles, and dissolves the skeleton.
Critics say the International Space Station, a 16-country project, will not do enough science to justify the cost to the United States of $30 billion or more. The station won't ever venture out into the solar system from its permanent height of 220 miles, and the astronauts on board will make it too jiggly to be a platform for telescopes looking out into the heavens.
``For NASA to set its sights at 220 miles above the Earth is a failure of leadership and imagination,'' said Robert Zubrin, founder of a grassroots movement, Mars Direct, promoting human travel to Mars.
Since NASA began seriously promoting the idea of a space station in the early 1980s, prominent scientists and whole scientific societies have waged battle against it.
The General Accounting Office and outside panels of scientists have tabulated costs above $90 billion - far greater than NASA's estimate. And recently NASA has acknowledged that the Russians failed to produce many of the parts they promised, forcing the United States to absorb more of the costs.
Critics also point out that NASA launched a space station called Skylab in 1973, and by 1974 had already abandoned it. ``They couldn't think of anything to do with it,'' said physicist Robert Park of the American Physical Society. In 1979, Skylab fell from its orbit and landed in bits and pieces over the Indian Ocean.
The controversy over the currently planned station sits at the center of a wider debate on the merits of exploring the universe with astronauts, who are fragile and vulnerable, versus robotic probes, which cost less and can venture farther.
Those in favor of manned spaceflight argue that it is humankind's destiny to explore and colonize space, building civilizations on the moon and Mars. They compare it to the settling of the New World.
NASA does not emphasize conquering the universe as the primary motivation behind the space station. But colonization is a quasi-religious sentiment that pervades support for the station both inside and outside of the agency.
The purpose of a space station was much clearer during the 1980s, when the Russians were planning Mir. They had already launched a station, Salyut 1, in 1971. The notion of Soviet dominance in space was considered a threat to our national security.
Early plans for a U.S. station - which would be called Freedom - focused on international competition, both military and industrial. When President Ronald Reagan officially announced Space Station Freedom in 1984, he promised that it would promote America's industrial spirit.
NASA set up a vision of dozens of companies rushing to invest in the space station as a base of space manufacturing.
Martin Glicksman, a materials researcher at Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute in Troy, N.Y., said he remembered when NASA was trying to get people excited about ball bearings.
NASA engineers were promoting the idea that, in space, you could make ball bearings of heavenly perfection.
``They overlooked the reality of the way metals solidify,'' Glicksman said. Space wouldn't yield perfect ball bearings after all - and even if it did, he said, ``at $10,000 a kilogram, who cares?''
Congress nearly killed funding for the space station several times, and the existing plan was completely scrapped for a slimmed-down version drawn up in 1993. By that time, more than $10 billion had been spent on the project and not a single piece had been built.
Soon, however, contractors around the country began to put hammer to metal, and now about half of the million some-odd pounds of space-station components are waiting for further assembly.
With the Cold War over, NASA shifted the proposed aim of the project to biology and materials science. NASA says space-station research will lead to better materials of all sorts and medical breakthroughs. NASA spokesman Dwayne Brown estimates ``we'll get $2 back for every $1 on the investment.''
Many scientists don't buy it.
``The space station has nothing to do with materials science,'' said Rustum Roy, a Pennsylvania State University materials researcher. His dim view of space research is based on experience. In the 1980s, he flew an experiment on the space shuttle for growing crystals of triglycine sulfate, useful for infrared-light detection, a project he now calls ``a waste of money.''
``I have never seen one honest example of an advance in materials'' developed from space, he said. ``You can do things expensively that would be done much more cheaply from the ground.''
Another area of research touted by NASA is a branch of biology known as protein crystallography. In the body, proteins come in thousands of varieties, each one with a unique shape determining its function. Using crystallized proteins, scientists can study how the body works at the molecular level.
Not all proteins are easy to crystallize, and for a time, some proteins could be crystallized better in space.
But recent breakthroughs in molecular biology have rendered space-based research much less useful. Last summer, the American Society for Cell Biology reported that space-based crystal experiments had ``made no serious contributions to scientific knowledge'' and concluded that there was no justification for continuing them.
Other scientists argue that the space station is a good project, but that NASA is stressing the wrong purpose. The space station should not be thought of as a research facility at all, but rather as a step toward human space exploration, where some limited science can piggyback.
``You wouldn't want to pay $30 billion for a space laboratory,'' Rensselaer's Glicksman said, ``but if you're already going to have a space station, then, yes, there are some wonderful experiments that can be done on it.''
He is optimistic about continuing a line of research he began on the space shuttle - the study of microscopic treelike growths called dendrites that form in metals and influence the strength and durability of alloys. Results that he has gotten so far from space research, he said, have been incorporated into casting codes used in foundries for making steel and aluminum.
Other experiments would dovetail more directly into the space-exploration dream. Martin Fettman, a veterinarian from Colorado State University, has done shuttle experiments on the loss of red blood cells in rats.
Rats in space, he said, suffer some of the same physical hardships that humans do, so animals can be useful for experimenting on drugs and other techniques to counteract the medical hazards of space.
Fettman is particularly excited about a huge centrifuge to be flown on the space station and designed to spin, thereby simulating gravity. Inside the centrifuge, scientists can create any fraction of Earth's gravity.
By seeing how animals fare inside the centrifuge, Fettman said, scientists will learn whether astronauts would need simulated gravity to go to Mars - a flight that would take at least six months.
Though NASA has no official plan to send humans to Mars, the agency works under the assumption people will eventually explore and colonize the planet and then venture beyond.
``When you take out the political aspect of this, the real purpose of human spaceflight is to lay the groundwork for one day colonizing space,'' said Jonathan McDowell, a Harvard astrophysicist who writes a newsletter about the space program.
He argues for space colonization as a sort of insurance plan. ``A single planet is a fragile place,'' he said, subject to asteroid bombardment, global climate change, and even nuclear war.
Others say space exploration, including the space station, fulfills a human need to reach new places - not by way of robots or cyberspace but in person.
``It's not about space, but about Earth and people's ability to do things,'' said Bruce Murray, a planetary scientist and former director of NASA's Jet Propulsion Laboratory. We should have astronauts in space not for the science, he said, but for the ``drama and adventure . . . and the need for the rest of us to vicariously share it.''

Credit: By Faye Flam, INQUIRER STAFF WRITER
Illustration PHOTO
A digital image depicts the finished International Space Station, powered by solar panels, as it is expected to appear in about six years. The 16-nation project, which will cost the United States at least $30 billion, has been decried as ''a failure of leadership and imagination.'' Others see it as the next step in expanded space travel. (NASA)
The U.S. section of the station is moved for tests at Kennedy Space Center. Called the Unity Node, the connecting module is scheduled to be launched Dec. 3 and will link living and working areas.

Solar Power For The Millennium

Author: Dr Keith

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/solar-power-millennium/docview/1012302736/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: At present on a world average this costed154 energy is made up of about 76 per cent fossil fuels, 14 per cent biomass (wood etcetera a form of solar energy), 6 per cent hydroelectricity (also a form of solar energy) 4 per cent nuclear and tiny fraction from other solar sources. There are demonstrated solar technologies for chemically storing solar energy to allow 24-hour electricity generation, to produce chemical commodities, to detoxify hazardous waste, to split water to make hydrogen and to make fuels for motor vehicles.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Solar+Power+For+The+Millennium&amp;author=Dr+Keith&amp;issn=01576925&amp;title=The+Canberra+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-10-29&amp;spage=9&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: IT SEEMS that increasingly these days the public agenda is dominated by endless discussion of obscure intangible human inventions such as interest rates, share price indices and so on. In this environment it is easy to lose sight of some rather important fundamentals. Perhaps the most fundamental is energy.
To the human animal as any other, energy means food to keep us alive, heat to keep us warm and energy is also what makes all the trappings of our civilisation possible, transport of goods, information and ourselves and the manufacture of every single object we take for granted.
In the global view 99.99 per cent of the energy that makes life possible is coming from the sun. Turn off the sun and pretty soon we would be trying to live on a lump of rock at close to absolute zero (around -273`C). However the 0.01 per cent which is the stuff that we actually pay154 for in some way is the bit that is causing the world so much angst at the moment. At present on a world average this costed154 energy is made up of about 76 per cent fossil fuels, 14 per cent biomass (wood etcetera a form of solar energy), 6 per cent hydroelectricity (also a form of solar energy) 4 per cent nuclear and tiny fraction from other solar sources.
Much of the debate on the greenhouse issue is about uncertainty. It is worth repeating a few facts which are definitely not uncertain: There is a greenhouse effect without it the average temperature of the planet would be around -19`C. Carbon dioxide, methane and many other gases contribute to the effect.
The concentration of carbon dioxide (and other gases) has beyond doubt increased the proportion of human emissions. The uncertainty arises in trying to determine what the effect of these changes is likely to be on the world's weather systems. The rational approach, when faced with the possibility of an extremely bad outcome with an unknown probability, is to take out some form of insurance. Obvious insurance is to look at ways of reducing gas emissions without compromising standards of living and energy. This leads to the search for renewable (solar) energy technologies.
Greenhouse is not the only reason for pursuing solar energy technologies. Even without it we are faced with a considerable burden of pollution from fossil fuel use and the certainty that some day this will be exhausted. Recent events on the Indian subcontinent serve to remind those who might have forgotten about the Chernobyl accident that a nuclear-based future has major drawbacks.
So what can solar energy do?
The short answer is, everything. There already exist, either commercially or in the laboratory, technologies which can replace every single application of fossil fuel. Everyone knows solar energy can heat water, and many people would already realise that solar energy is sufficient to keep a carefully designed house warm. Most people also know that solar energy can generate electricity.
About 18 per cent of the world's electricity is generated by hydroelectricity a form of solar energy. In 1997 the Danish wind-turbine industry sold enough turbines to generate electricity at a rate of 1 gigawatt (about three times what Canberra uses).
There are in southern California, large solar thermal power systems that together can generate at a rate of 350 megawatts, and 800 megawatts of photovoltaic panels has been installed in small systems around the world.
What many people would not realise is that technologies exist for solar energy to replace other uses of fossil fuel. The key to many of these technologies is solar-driven chemical processes. There are demonstrated solar technologies for chemically storing solar energy to allow 24-hour electricity generation, to produce chemical commodities, to detoxify hazardous waste, to split water to make hydrogen and to make fuels for motor vehicles.
Won't solar energy systems require too much space?
Hardly; on an average of 25 times our yearly costed154 energy needs lands on the surface of the continent. To provide for Australia's consumption of primary costed energy of 5000 petajoules per year in a place like Canberra where about 18 megajoules of sun falls on every square metre on an average day, would require 20 per cent efficient collectors only covering an area of 62km square.
This could really be done with a sensible combination of existing technologies.
What about the energy needed to manufacture solar equipment in the first place?
Making any piece of equipment requires energy, which in turn can result in the emission of greenhouse gases, if fossil fuels are used in the process.
However, commercial solar equipment more than pays for its energy debt154 during its operating life. For example, a solar hot water heater will repay its energy debt in six to 18 months, depending on location as will wind turbines and solar thermal power systems. Isn't Solar energy too expensive?
In its simplest form, solar energy is free. You don't have to pay for the warmth of a summer day, or for the sunlight needed to make the vegetables in your garden grow. There are many ways to utilise solar energy which will pay for themselves.
In other words, the money you will save in the long term on reduced energy bills will more than make up for the initial outlay. Building a solar passive house for example, which is designed to capture the heat from the sun effectively in winter, but keep it out in summer, need not cost any more than a poorly designed house which requires much more energy, and therefore money, to keep comfortable all year round.
Adding insulation into the walls and ceiling of an existing house, too, will pay for itself over a period of a few years as will a solar hot water heater in many circumstances.
The hard nut to crack is large- scale electricity production. While solar technologies are already competitive for electricity production in remote areas, current estimates for existing technology used on a reasonable scale, place them as being two or three times more expensive to the consumer than Australia's current cheap coal-fired electricity. Considering the potential for cost-effective efficiency improvements and future technology developments it is quite plausible to consider making the switch with very little discomfort.
The extra cost154 must also be seen in the context of the potential costs of global climate change. Estimating these is just as uncertain as predicting the changes themselves, but attempts to do so produce numbers that show that the solar technologies are actually cheaper.
In the greenhouse debate much is made of the possible negative effect on the economy that such an increase in costs would have. In considering this issue it should be remembered that this is not money somehow lost from the economy, rather it is money that is directed into major highemployment initiatives in manufacturing new products.
So what is happening in the ACT?
In the research and development arena, a lot is happening in Canberra. At the Australian National University, research groups within the Department of Engineering's Centre for Sustainable Energy Systems are working on solar thermal and photovoltaic power systems, the university's commercial arm, ANUTECH is developing and marketing the world's largest paraboloidal dish and a group within the department of Geology is investigating hot dry rock geothermal power systems.
Work on solar cells promises to significantly reduce the cost of these cells by reducing the amount of high quality silicon needed. Researches are working on two concepts to do this. In the first approach, cells are made from a film of silicon only 30 to 50 thousandth of a millimetre thick using less than a tenth of the silicon of standard cells.
In the second approach, the ANU team is perfecting a system that focuses sunlight from a large area on to a narrow line of solar cells, using mirrors that are much cheaper than solar cells. The solar thermal group within the CSES has pioneered a themochemical energy storage system based on dissociating and re-synthesising ammonia. This system will allow solar thermal systems such as those based around the ANUTECH dish to generate power continuously day and night.
Politically speaking, the ACT Government has led other states and territories in making a minimum energy efficiency (modest though the requirement is) mandatory for new house designs and has adopted a decent target for greenhouse emission reductions of achieving emissions at 1990 levels by 2008 and 20 per cent by 2018. Exactly how this is to be implemented is being decided, hopefully by measures that have a real prospect of achieving the target.
The new millennium
The past 100 years have given us cars, computers, television, space travel and more. The next 100 are certain to be just as exciting and unpredictable. If we take a positive view, it will see mankind taking increasing concern for the quality of life on the only inhabitable planet at our disposal. There will be big changes in the way we provide ourselves with extra energy and solar technology will definitely play an increasing part. As usual though the Bill Gates" of each generation will not be those who wait for someone else to go first, but rather those with the foresight to pick winners at the right time. Solar technologies could well be at the point quite soon. This article was prepared by members of the ACT branch of the ANZ Solar Energy Society. The society exists to promote social and economic development through the environmentally sound use of solar energy. Its activities include the publication of the quarterly journal Solar Progress. For more information call 02 9311003. As part of the Newspaper in Education program, there are activity sheets to accompany this article for schools in the Canberra Region. If your school is not receiving these resources or you require more information, contact Linda Fraser in the promotions department of The Canberra Times on 6280 2106.

Subject: Solar energy; Fossil fuels; Electricity generation

Title: Solar Power For The Millennium

Publication date: Oct 29, 1998

Publisher: Rural Press Pty Ltd Australian Community Media (ACM)

Are you prepared for a disaster?: [Final Edition]

Author: Haddrall, Lynn

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/are-you-prepared-disaster/docview/352997810/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: During the first days of the ice storm, our reporters and photographers received vastly different treatment within the newly amalgamated city and the outlying municipalities. In Loyalist and South Frontenac townships, for example, we were invited into meetings and accepted as part of the solution. But in the city, we were made to sit outside closed-door meetings. When officials emerged, no media spokesperson had been appointed. At one point, we were told a press release would be faxed to us. That would have been great - if we had power or a fax machine. The pamphlet is a project co-ordinated by the Kingston, Frontenac Lennox and Addington Health Unit and funded by the Ministry of Community and Social Services. It is a timely publication, as is The Ice Storm book, which The Whig-Standard helped produce. The hard-cover book is a collaboration among nine newspapers. Award-winning Whig-Standard photographers Ian MacAlpine and Michael Lea are among the many contributors. (Ian receives special thanks in the opening pages.) Their dramatic photographs are among more than 200 that capture the fury and the perverse beauty of the storm. The book is now available for sale and we will contribute The Whig's share of the proceeds to local charities.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Are+you+prepared+for+a+disaster%3F&amp;author=Haddrall%2C+Lynn&amp;issn=11974397&amp;title=Kingston+Whig+-+Standard&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-10-24&amp;spage=8&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: As we head into another winter, I've been reminded of the need to be prepared for natural disasters. Three recent events had me thinking about my emergency plans at work and at home.
The first was a conference to which I was invited. The second was the arrival of a pamphlet from the local health unit. And the third is a book about the ice storm last January.
My invitation to the conference was unexpected. The one-day event in Ottawa was sponsored by the Canadian National Committee for the International Decade for Natural Disaster Reduction. I had not heard of the committee or its work and I was unaware that the United Nations General Assembly had declared the 1990s as the international decade for natural disaster reduction.
I was keen to attend, however, because the conference came at a time when Kingston officials had just released a new emergency plan curiously void of any news media involvement. I believe the media provides a vital link to the community in times of crisis, so I was surprised that our input hadn't been sought. I was also disappointed to see that there was no section dealing with the media.
During the first days of the ice storm, our reporters and photographers received vastly different treatment within the newly amalgamated city and the outlying municipalities. In Loyalist and South Frontenac townships, for example, we were invited into meetings and accepted as part of the solution. But in the city, we were made to sit outside closed-door meetings. When officials emerged, no media spokesperson had been appointed. At one point, we were told a press release would be faxed to us. That would have been great - if we had power or a fax machine.
When I attended the conference in Ottawa, I recounted some of our frustrations and learned that other media had encountered similar difficulties, especially with Quebec Hydro. I also heard, however, about success stories where television crews and newspapers in the Ottawa region were given continuous updates and information.
Scientists and insurance representatives at the meeting suggested there could be more natural disasters. The insurance industry is so concerned it has created a group called the Institute for Catastrophic Loss Reduction. Paul Kovacs, executive director, told us that prior to 1987 there had never been a natural disaster causing insured losses of more than $1 billion (US) anywhere in the world. But during the past decade there have been 18 natural disasters of this magnitude or greater.
Factors he attributed to the trend: population growth, urbanization, economic expansion, climate change. His group is interested in ways that loss of life and damage to property can be mitigated.
The local health unit is certainly taking steps to help us be prepared. I was happy this week to receive in my mailbox a pamphlet called Making the Connection. It has useful information about ways to stay healthy during an emergency. There are tips about water, food, carbon monoxide, cold weather conditions, generators and evacuation.
The pamphlet is a project co-ordinated by the Kingston, Frontenac Lennox and Addington Health Unit and funded by the Ministry of Community and Social Services. It is a timely publication, as is The Ice Storm book, which The Whig-Standard helped produce.
The hard-cover book is a collaboration among nine newspapers. Award-winning Whig-Standard photographers Ian MacAlpine and Michael Lea are among the many contributors. (Ian receives special thanks in the opening pages.) Their dramatic photographs are among more than 200 that capture the fury and the perverse beauty of the storm. The book is now available for sale and we will contribute The Whig's share of the proceeds to local charities.
One quotation in the book exemplifies the pride we can take in how we responded last year, but also the need to be more vigilant in our emergency preparation: "The storm and its aftermath reminded us of some immutable truths - of our fragility, of nature's strength and of our remarkable ability to pull together in times of need."
Mayor Gary Bennett designated last Thursday as Emergency Preparedness Day. It's a good time for all of us to ask how prepared we are.

Call for more El Nino funding: [2 Edition]

Author: BARNAO, Pete

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/call-more-el-nino-funding/docview/315029103/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: MORE Government funding was needed to help minimise the future impact of the El Nino weather system, which is estimated to have cost New Zealand more than $1 billion this year, scientists and industry figures said yesterday. Representatives of the science, farming, energy, and environmental sectors were taking part in a workshop in Wellington on how New Zealand can better prepare for future El Nino events.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Call+for+more+El+Nino+funding&amp;author=BARNAO%2C+Pete&amp;issn=0113941X&amp;title=Dominion&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-10-22&amp;spage=6&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: MORE Government funding was needed to help minimise the future impact of the El Nino weather system, which is estimated to have cost New Zealand more than $1 billion this year, scientists and industry figures said yesterday.
Representatives of the science, farming, energy, and environmental sectors were taking part in a workshop in Wellington on how New Zealand can better prepare for future El Nino events.
Participants called for greater government involvement in long- term climate forecasting. Some wanted an official climate forecasting agency to help farmers and other stakeholders prepare for El Nino, which this year caused devastating draughts along much of the east coast.
Some suggested that initially only a few hundred thousand dollars were needed, but others said greater funding was needed. Such funding would be an investment in the future as a wide range of sectors, not just farmers, suffered the effects of El Nino.
Participants also called for better communication between climate experts and farmers and members of other affected sectors. These sectors could include insurance, biosecurity and health.
The ideas were discussed at El Nino: Lessons and Opportunities for the Future, a workshop organised by the Royal Society's New Zealand climate committee.
Judy Lawrence, of the National Science Strategy Committee on Climate Change, said the economy "totally and utterly depends" on the climate. Policymakers and scientists needed to work more closely together to manage climatic risks.
Rob Davison, of the Economic Service of Meat and Wool Boards, said climatic forecasting was crucial to farmers. About 32,000 farms were potentially affected by El Nino and reliable information was needed to help farmers minimise their losses.
ECNZ Spokesman Russell Howie said the 1992 El Nino event had carved $100 million from the company's bottom-line profit.

Energy, environment ministers act on acid rain

Author: Duffy, Andrew; Southam News

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/energy-environment-ministers-act-on-acid-rain/docview/423061597/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Since 1980, Canada has cut its acid rain emissions - primarily sulphur dioxide - by 54 per cent.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Energy%2C+environment+ministers+act+on+acid+rain&amp;author=Duffy%2C+Andrew%3BSoutham+News&amp;issn=17066204&amp;title=New+Brunswick+Telegraph+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-10-20&amp;spage=&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Canada's energy and environment ministers have agreed to a renewed attack in the new millennium on the pollutants that cause acid rain.
As part of the agreement, four provinces - Ontario, Quebec, Nova Scotia and New Brunswick - will negotiate new targets and timetables for further reductions in acid rain emissions over the next year.
The commitment was announced yesterday in Halifax at a federal-provincial meeting of energy and environment ministers.
The new acid rain program is being developed in response to a task force report that suggested further cuts of up to 75 per cent are needed to stem the damaging effects of acid rain on Canada's lakes and forests.
The report found that while both Canada and the United States are ahead of their current targets, acidity levels remain stubbornly high in lakes in Ontario, Quebec and Eastern Canada. Acid rain can stunt tree growth, damage buildings and destroy the ecological balance of lakes, making it difficult for waterfowl like loons to feed and lay eggs.
The new reduction targets will take effect after 2000 when Canada's current acid rain program expires.
"Today's signature by all provincial governments is a formal commitment that we in Canada will do our share to solve the acid rain problem," said Environment Minister Christine Stewart.
"We consider this an important opportunity to put our own house in order to take advantage of the current interest in the United States in achieving further emission reductions.
Since 1980, Canada has cut its acid rain emissions - primarily sulphur dioxide - by 54 per cent. Yet the damage to lakes and wildlife caused by acid rain has not subsided, in part because the U.S. has been slower to reduce its emission levels. The U.S., which is responsible for half of the acid rain that falls in Canada, has so far reduced its emissions by 30 per cent.
Its current program calls for a total reduction of 40 per cent by 2010, although current revisions to the U.S. Clean Air Act could trigger further cuts.
Meanwhile, the federal government also unveiled yesterday details of its three- year, $150-million action plan on climate change.
More than one-third of the funds ($56-million) will be used to support the development of technology that enhances energy efficiency.
The rest of the money will go toward improving climate science ($15-million), assessing the potential for emission reductions in Canada ($34-million) and educating Canadians on the need for action ($30-million). A reserve of $15- million has also been established.
"Our message to Canadians is this: Acting now is like investing in an insurance policy to protect the environment and the economy, for ourselves and our future generations," said Natural Resources Minister Ralph Goodale.
Environmentalists, however, complain that the government's climate insurance policy is too meagre.
"This isn't a plan to achieve the Kyoto protocol, it's a minimal first step," said Robert Hornung of the Pembina Institute for Appropriate Development.
Most scientists believe the earth's atmosphere is slowly being warmed as man- made gases like carbon dioxide absorb heat that would otherwise escape into outer space. The gases, produced largely through the burning of coal, oil and gasoline, were the subject of a treaty negotiated last year in Kyoto, Japan.
The treaty, which must still be ratified by Canada and other industrialized nations, commits the developed world to reducing its collective emissions of greenhouse gases by at least 5 per cent. Canada agreed to cut its emissions by 6 per cent over 1990 levels between 2008 and 2012.

Subject: Acid rain; Kyoto Protocol; Insurance policies; Environmentalists; Energy efficiency; Clean Air Act-US

Federal gov't sets up fund to fight climate change

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/federal-govt-sets-up-fund-fight-climate-change/docview/348520556/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The Climate Change Action Fund will also go to technology to cut greenhouse emissions ($56 million), analysis of options to cut emissions ($34 million), research into impacts of climate change and adaptation ($15 million). Scientists say it will be much more difficult to cut greenhouse emissions than it has been to curb acid rain, because greenhouse gases are produced by a huge number of sources -- from cars and factories to garbage dumps.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Federal+gov%27t+sets+up+fund+to+fight+climate+change&amp;author=&amp;issn=08324174&amp;title=Star+-+Phoenix&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-10-20&amp;spage=D.7&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: OTTAWA (CP) -- The federal government will spend $30 million during the next three years to persuade people climate change is real and to tell them what they can do about it.
The public education program announced Monday is part of $150 million in new spending related to global warming. The money was allocated in the last federal budget without details on how it would be spent.
The Climate Change Action Fund will also go to technology to cut greenhouse emissions ($56 million), analysis of options to cut emissions ($34 million), research into impacts of climate change and adaptation ($15 million).
"Acting now is like investing in an insurance policy to protect the environment and the economy, for ourselves and for future generations," Natural Resources Minister Ralph Goodale said Monday in statement from Halifax where he was attending a meeting of environment and energy ministers.
The ministers also agreed on a strategy for further action on acid rain after 2000. The latest reports show acid-causing emissions have been dramatically reduced during the last 15 years, but are still causing significant damage, especially in Eastern Canada.
Scientists say it will be much more difficult to cut greenhouse emissions than it has been to curb acid rain, because greenhouse gases are produced by a huge number of sources -- from cars and factories to garbage dumps.
Advertising and classroom materials will be used to educate people about "what climate change is, what its impacts are, and what people can do in their individual lives to make a difference," Goodale added in an interview.

Outlook for Scots is wet and windy: [1 Edition]

Author: Brown, Amanda

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/outlook-scots-is-wet-windy/docview/326703003/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: WETTER winters in Scotland and drier summers in the south-east are more likely as the UK experiences a three degree temperature rise by the end of the next century. A climate change report from scientists at the University of East Anglia and experts at the Hadley Centre, part of the Meteorological Office, also warns of a greater risk of flooding, sea levels rising between two to ten centimetres per decade and extreme weather patterns with gales and very hot days sending the thermometer over 25C. There is also the possibility of a complete absence of frost in coastal areas.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Outlook+for+Scots+is+wet+and+windy&amp;author=Brown%2C+Amanda&amp;issn=03075850&amp;title=The+Scotsman&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-10-17&amp;spage=22&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: WETTER winters in Scotland and drier summers in the south-east are more likely as the UK experiences a three degree temperature rise by the end of the next century.
The impact of greenhouse gases on the atmosphere will lead to droughts, flooding and storms with cities such as Glasgow and Manchester- already famous for high rainfall - likely to get more downpours.
A climate change report from scientists at the University of East Anglia and experts at the Hadley Centre, part of the Meteorological Office, also warns of a greater risk of flooding, sea levels rising between two to ten centimetres per decade and extreme weather patterns with gales and very hot days sending the thermometer over 25C. There is also the possibility of a complete absence of frost in coastal areas.
The report by the UK Climate Impacts Programme for the first time enables water companies, insurance firms, local authorities, industry and farmers, to make plans for dealing with the changes.
Dr Mike Hulme, of the University of East Anglia, said winter rainfall would increase, particularly in the north and with greater intensity. "Manchester is going to get wetter and we anticipate more downpours. This is an evolution of the science we have been reporting for some years."
Michael Meacher, the environment minister, said it showed the potential for UK climate to change and the need to prepare for it.
"These scenarios illustrate the very real threat that climate change represents to us in the UK and underlines the importance that the Government attaches to meeting its targets and tackling the threat of climate change in the long run."
Earlier he said that a three degree rise may not sound much but "when I say that at the last Ice Age, the temperature fell by only five degrees Centigrade, clearly a rise in temperature of three degrees can have an enormous effect".
Anna Stanford, energy campaigner at Friends of the Earth, said: "Mr Meacher is right to draw attention to the catastrophic impacts of climate change and the need to take action to cut greenhouse gas emissions. But we've known about this problem for some time. Now we need to see actiona"
