Changes to climate will pose 'very real threat' WEATHER STUDY WARNS OF FLOODS, GALES: [London edition]

Author: Houlder, Vanessa

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/changes-climate-will-pose-very-real-threat/docview/248726605/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Temperatures in the UK could rise by as much as 3{0}C over the next century, increasing the frequency of floods, severe winter gales and unusually hot days, scientists said yesterday. Michael Meacher, the environment minister, said the findings of a government-commissioned study of future UK weather patterns "illustrate the very real threat that climate change poses to the UK". Researchers working for the UK Climate Impacts Programme, based at Oxford University, drew up four scenarios for the UK's climate, using differing projections of greenhouse gases and climate sensitivities.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Changes+to+climate+will+pose+%27very+real+threat%27+WEATHER+STUDY+WARNS+OF+FLOODS%2C+GALES%3A&amp;author=Houlder%2C+Vanessa&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-10-17&amp;spage=08&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Temperatures in the UK could rise by as much as 3{0}C over the next century, increasing the frequency of floods, severe winter gales and unusually hot days, scientists said yesterday.
Michael Meacher, the environment minister, said the findings of a government-commissioned study of future UK weather patterns "illustrate the very real threat that climate change poses to the UK".
Researchers working for the UK Climate Impacts Programme, based at Oxford University, drew up four scenarios for the UK's climate, using differing projections of greenhouse gases and climate sensitivities.
Under all the possibilities considered by the scientists, the UK's climate would change significantly. "We no longer believe past weather statistics are an adequate guide to the future," said Dr Mike Hulme of the University of East Anglia who is one of the report's authors.
The likely impact of climate change will differ between the north and south. By 2080, temperatures are likely to increase between 1.5{0}C and 3.2{0}C from 1961-90 levels in the south-east, compared with 1.2{0}C to 2.6{0}C in Scotland.
Winters and autumns will become up to 20 per cent wetter over the whole UK. In spring and summer, the south-east will become drier and the north-west wetter.
"Extreme" events will become more common. Intense downpours will become more frequent, especially in winter. Summer gales and severe winter gales will become a little more frequent. Under one scenario, an August as hot as 1997 - the second hottest on record - will occur on average four times a decade by the 2080s.
Dr Hulme said some of the study's findings, such as increased frequency of severe gales, had serious implications for the construction and insurance industries.
Changing weather patterns also posed a challenge to water management in the south-east. Agriculture will also be affected. In parts of southern England, for example, frosts might cease by the end of the next century.
The researchers believe the report represents "a balanced account of the best information currently available on the future climate of the UK". But they acknowledge the possibility that changes to the UK climate could be more rapid than any of the scenarios they studied.
For example, although their models do not predict dramatic changes to the strong ocean currents that transport heat around the world, the possibility must be taken seriously, they say.
The work was based on climate-modelling experiments by the Met Office's Hadley Centre and studies of greenhouse gas emissions by the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change.
Mr Meacher said the government would soon publish a consultation paper addressing climate change, as a follow-up to the greenhouse gas reduction targets agreed at last year's Kyoto summit. Measures would include improved energy efficiency in industry and the home, reduced transport emissions and greater use of renewable energy. Copyright Financial Times Limited 1998. All Rights Reserved.

____________________________________________________________

UN can cure 'global contagion'

Author: Lynch, Claudy

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/un-can-cure-global-contagion/docview/412638039/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Mr. Annan has described the role of the UN in business in clear and powerful terms which bear repeating in these troubled times: As national economies become more and more interdependent through information, trade, investment and financial ties, the UN is helping to fill a growing need for international cooperation and regulatory consistency to spread the benefits of globalization and to avoid chaos and backlash. It prepares the ground for investment in emerging economies, by promoting political stability and good governance, battling corruption and human rights abuses, urging sound economic policies and business-friendly legislation, and working to improve health, education and social well-being.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=UN+can+cure+%27global+contagion%27&amp;author=Lynch%2C+Claudy&amp;issn=08216983&amp;title=Daily+Gleaner&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-10-16&amp;spage=&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: We wanted it so badly, we strong- armed nations into going for it. Now, we are all standing around, some scratching our heads, others rubbing them to make the pain go away. Globalization. "Market contagion." That's what nation after nation called the current mess during the debate of the UN Second Committee (Economic and Financial) on the world economic situation.
In Canada, Finance Minister Paul Martin made an economic statement which some say was heavily inspired by Prime Minister Jean Chretien. Like a good pater familia, the PM is battening down the hatches for bad times ahead by ordering that all of the "first-in-a-generation-surplus" go to reducing the national debt. This will still appeal to Mr. Martin's constituency, however, since it is one of the policy decisions that big business and the investment community recommended, instead of spending on repairing our slashed safety net.
Mr. Martin appears not to get all that he wanted, but, as he is fond of saying himself, "make no mistake," he will manage to get the rest somehow. He will allocate funds to health care so that it does not make the Liberals lose seats in the next election. This might even be helpful in Quebec, where Jean Charest is running on criticism of Lucien Bouchard's government's handling of the health file. It would not do for Mr. Bouchard to point out that it was the federal cuts which put the health system in Quebec where it is, which is in a mess, as it is in every other province and territory in Canada.
Mr. Martin will get to reduce taxes on consumers so that more money is available to kick the economy in the slats and make it turn over a bit. He will be able to do this, because he appropriated the Unemployment Insurance program's funds to fashion his so-called surplus.
He at least honestly admits that the world economic situation is threatening all the good results of our sacrifices. But, he contends that Canada is in a better situation than most countries to weather the storm, because of these sacrifices. I hope so, but I doubt it.
Canada will not be taking up its newly won seat on the UN Security Council until the beginning of 1999. However, there is much it can do even now, as an in-coming member of that chamber of decision to deal with the millennium plague -- this market contagion.
Foreign Affairs Minister Lloyd Axworthy said that Canada would push social issues to the fore, on the Security Council. With Kofi Annan at the helm of the United Nations, Canada has the greatest ally to enable it to make another world class contribution (as with peacekeeping) to deal with what ails the planet. We are not used to thinking of the UN as playing this role. But, as part of his "quiet revolution" to renew the United Nations for the 21st century, Secretary- General Annan is building a stronger relationship with the business community. "Thriving markets and human security go hand in hand; without one, we will not have the other," he has repeatedly said.
Mr. Annan has described the role of the UN in business in clear and powerful terms which bear repeating in these troubled times: "As national economies become more and more interdependent through information, trade, investment and financial ties, the UN is helping to fill a growing need for international cooperation and regulatory consistency to spread the benefits of globalization and to avoid chaos and backlash.
"It prepares the ground for investment in emerging economies, by promoting political stability and good governance, battling corruption and human rights abuses, urging sound economic policies and business-friendly legislation, and working to improve health, education and social well-being."
The UN addresses the down side of globalization by fighting transnational crime, the traffic in drugs, arms and people, and other "problems without passports." Much of its operational work in over 170 countries is aimed at combating poverty. These efforts reduce tensions, prevent backlash and help build future markets
"The UN is working to solve global environmental problems, something which free markets, left to themselves, cannot do. It is tackling transboundary problems like climate change, ozone depletion, toxic waste, loss of forests and species, and air and water pollution.
Unless these problems are addressed, markets and economies will not be sustainable in the long term -- they are depleting the natural "capital" on which growth, and human survival, are based.
Globalizing markets rely on contractual and trust-based relationships built on shared values. Since its inception, the UN's primary mission has been to advocate universal values: freedom, justice and the peaceful resolution of disputes; social progress and better standards of living; equality, tolerance and dignity. The global acceptance and spread of these values is forging a common understanding that is paving the way for markets to expand and take root.
"As a place where business can make its voice heard and engage in dialogue with policy-makers and other stakeholders from all countries, the UN can bring the private sector to the table to solve global problems as partners rather than adversaries."
In Canada, both friends and foes are faulting NDP leader Alexa McDonough for courting small business. They feel it is a betrayal of the principles of social democracy. How wrong they are. Just look at what the UN's promotion of development programs, which extend credit to poor people to start their own businesses, has accomplished.
The MicroStart project so far is up and running in 15 countries. The UN Industrial Development Organization, acting as a broker between technology suppliers and developing countries, has brought over a billion dollars worth of investment and clean technologies to more than 80 countries in the last four years.
So, go baby, go, as U.S. anchorman Walter Cronkite said to John Glenn on his first loop into space and repeated for Mr. Glenn's second foray at the age of 72. Canada will be right there at the table, ready to provide a second Mike Pearson-class contribution to the world. This time it would not only bring glory to the nation, but direct relief and protection from the millennium plague.
(Claudy Lynch writes from Albert, N.B. Her column appears each Friday.)

Clinton celebrates environmental gains; others unsure: [Final Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/clinton-celebrates-environmental-gains-others/docview/260813873/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: In the end, the environmental deals were sweetened with money for both sides. The White House said it won an additional $476 million for favored programs involving climate change, water quality and conservation lands. At the same time, Republican lawmakers won provisions to spend millions more on items that include logging and transportation projects in Alaska and an expansion of federal flood insurance and probably development for 75 acres of coastal Florida.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Clinton+celebrates+environmental+gains%3B+others+unsure&amp;author=&amp;issn=10828850&amp;title=Milwaukee+Journal+Sentinel&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-10-16&amp;spage=9&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: President Clinton claimed victory Thursday in a last-minute budget battle over proposed changes in environmental policy.
But many environmental activists were in no mood to celebrate. They felt they lost major fights across the country.
Based on what budget negotiators told activists, wins and losses followed geographic lines, with environmentalists mostly prevailing on proposals involving the Pacific Northwest and Republicans and their business allies prevailing in most other regions.
In the end, the environmental deals were sweetened with money for both sides. The White House said it won an additional $476 million for favored programs involving climate change, water quality and conservation lands. At the same time, Republican lawmakers won provisions to spend millions more on items that include logging and transportation projects in Alaska and an expansion of federal flood insurance and probably development for 75 acres of coastal Florida.
Congress agreed to increase spending on the president's clean water proposals, including controls on polluted runoff from farms, to $1.7 billion, $150 million more than Congress had proposed.
Spending from the federal trust fund that pays for land acquisition would increase to $325 million, $55 million more than last year.
Activists said they were disappointed that provisions in the budget would delay by a year environmental regulations on mining. Also, it will delay by eight months an increase in the royalties oil companies pay for drilling on public lands.
Credit: Knight Ridder News Service, New York Times

Long-term weather forecast: havoc: Ice Storm '98, history's costliest natural disaster per-capita, just tip of the iceberg: insurance industry: [Final Edition]

Author: Butler, Don

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/long-term-weather-forecast-havoc-ice-storm-98/docview/240198765/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Mr. [Paul Kovacs] said the ice storm triggered insurance claims of $1.75 billion, $1.44 billion of which were in Canada. That puts the damage in the same relative league as history's costliest storm, Hurricane Andrew, which generated claims totalling $15 billion in the United States, a nation with 10 times Canada's population. Between the ice storm, the Red River flood of 1997 and the Saguenay flood of 1996, the past three years have been the worst ever for weather-related insurance claims in Canada, Mr. Kovacs said. "We've simply never seen anything like the last few years. The trend is extremely disturbing." Before 1998, Mr. Kovacs said Canadian insurers had never paid out more than $500 million for natural disaster claims in any single year. Costs related to natural disasters are 65 per cent higher in the past five years than in the previous five-year period. And though there had been just one natural disaster causing insured losses of more than $1 billion prior to 1987, Mr. Kovacs said that there have more than 20 such storms worldwide in the past decade.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Long-term+weather+forecast%3A+havoc%3A+Ice+Storm+%2798%2C+history%27s+costliest+natural+disaster+per-capita%2C+just+tip+of+the+iceberg%3A+insurance+industry&amp;author=Butler%2C+Don&amp;issn=08393222&amp;title=The+Ottawa+Citizen&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-10-15&amp;spage=A.1.FRO&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: On a per-capita basis, last January's ice storm was one of the most costly natural disasters in recorded history, the executive director of the Institute for Catastrophic Loss Reduction said yesterday.
And the insurance industry expects that severe weather-related events will occur even more often in coming years, Paul Kovacs told an Ottawa breakfast marking International Decade for Natural Disaster Reduction Day.
Mr. Kovacs said the ice storm triggered insurance claims of $1.75 billion, $1.44 billion of which were in Canada. That puts the damage in the same relative league as history's costliest storm, Hurricane Andrew, which generated claims totalling $15 billion in the United States, a nation with 10 times Canada's population.
"Adjusted for the size of the country, on a per-capita basis, the ice storm was one of the biggest events any society has had to go through," he said. Insurance companies received an unprecedented 700,000 claims for storm-related damage.
Between the ice storm, the Red River flood of 1997 and the Saguenay flood of 1996, the past three years have been the worst ever for weather-related insurance claims in Canada, Mr. Kovacs said. "We've simply never seen anything like the last few years. The trend is extremely disturbing."
Before 1998, Mr. Kovacs said Canadian insurers had never paid out more than $500 million for natural disaster claims in any single year. Costs related to natural disasters are 65 per cent higher in the past five years than in the previous five-year period. And though there had been just one natural disaster causing insured losses of more than $1 billion prior to 1987, Mr. Kovacs said that there have more than 20 such storms worldwide in the past decade.
Mr. Kovacs said there are four main reasons why the insurance industry expects this trend to continue and worsen.
- Demographics. "There are simply more people living close together in large urban communities," Mr. Kovacs said.
That raises the potential for damage in the event of a weather- related disaster.
- Economic prosperity. With the population generally wealthier than it has been in the past, "we've got more things to be damaged."
- Aging infrastructure. Deficit reduction by governments at all levels means that there has been little re-investment in things such as sewer systems. As a result, many systems are deteriorating and inadequate to meet present demands.
- Climate change. "There is no question that the weather is changing, and that some aspects of that change are leading to more severe weather than we've ever seen," said Mr. Kovacs.
While the full implications of climate change are still unclear, Environment Canada modelling supports the view that Canadians can expect more episodes of extreme weather in the next few decades, said John Stone, Environment Canada's director general of Policy, Program and International Affairs.
Historically, weather-related claims have accounted for only about two per cent of all insurance claims, said Mr. Kovacs. That figure has now risen to 10 per cent.
Mr. Kovacs said weather-related claims "are not big enough yet that we've had to seriously look at the cost of insurance," but warned that that could change if costs continue to rise.
The insurance industry is worried enough that it established the Institute for Catastrophic Loss Reduction earlier this year. Its mission is to explore ways to reduce the loss of life and property caused by severe weather and earthquakes.
In co-operation with Emergency Preparedness Canada, the institute has spent the last few months holding workshops across the country with representatives of government, the private sector, non-profit groups and academics to develop a framework to mitigate natural disaster risks.
"We don't have a co-ordinated overall effort to focus on being ready for crises in this country," said Mr. Kovacs. While there is no agreement on what to do, he said there was broad consensus among workshop participants that investments are needed to mitigate the damage caused by natural disasters when they occur.
Mr. Kovacs said these investments can be very cost effective. "A small investment initially can lead to major savings down the road."
For example, the insurance industry has spent $1.5 million a year for the past three years to divert hail-storms away from Calgary, using cloud-seeding techniques. As a result, he said, "the damage caused by hail in Calgary has gone down remarkably."
The consultations will culminate in a national conference in Toronto on Dec. 2, at which the results of the workshops will be reported to federal and provincial ministers. Emergency Preparedness Canada is expected to be empowered to investigate measures to reduce the damage caused by natural disasters, something not currently within its mandate.

Their special interest is ignoring warmer, weather-beaten globe: [City Edition]

Author: Epstein, Paul R

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/their-special-interest-is-ignoring-warmer-weather/docview/405268307/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Paul R. Epstein, MD, MPH, is associate director of the Center for Health and the Global Environment at Harvard Medical School. The center will host a New England Town Meeting on climate change Saturday at Faneuil Hall. As a November meeting in Buenos Aires on climate approaches, some members of Congress are trying to block any energy-saving solution that echoes the principles of the agreement hashed out in Kyoto last year to reduce global warming -- even if these measures are good for our well-being and our economy. For example, the House Appropriations Committee has blocked the administration from promoting energy-saving measures and incentives for new technologies. Meanwhile, the Senate Appropriations Committee wants to have veto power over any money the White House spends to get developing countries to participate in the Kyoto protocol. That's certainly a Catch-22, for the Senate voted last year in Kyoto to sign the treaty unless developing nations participate.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Their+special+interest+is+ignoring+warmer%2C+weather-beaten+globe&amp;author=Epstein%2C+Paul+R&amp;issn=07431791&amp;title=Boston+Globe&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-10-11&amp;spage=D.2&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Paul R. Epstein, MD, MPH, is associate director of the Center for Health and the Global Environment at Harvard Medical School. The center will host a New England Town Meeting on climate change Saturday at Faneuil Hall.
A ferocious but little-publicized policy war looms between the White House and the Republican Congress over the future of the earth's environment.
As a November meeting in Buenos Aires on climate approaches, some members of Congress are trying to block any energy-saving solution that echoes the principles of the agreement hashed out in Kyoto last year to reduce global warming -- even if these measures are good for our well-being and our economy.
For example, the House Appropriations Committee has blocked the administration from promoting energy-saving measures and incentives for new technologies. Meanwhile, the Senate Appropriations Committee wants to have veto power over any money the White House spends to get developing countries to participate in the Kyoto protocol. That's certainly a Catch-22, for the Senate voted last year in Kyoto to sign the treaty unless developing nations participate.
This obstruction comes just as the world is experiencing a period of the most unhealthy and costly harsh weather in decades. Accumulating research indicates that these changes in the earth's climate are attributable to the burning of fossil fuels, rather than to natural variability.
Predictably, the congressmen who are blocking efforts to reduce global warming are those who favor the interests of the fossil fuel industries, which are intent on extracting and selling all the buried oil and coal they can well into the next century.
The petroleum and coal industries -- and those who represent their interests -- want voters to distrust their own instincts and experiences about climate changes. They want voters to doubt clear explanations accepted by a consensus of the world's leading scientists.
There is plenty of empirical evidence that something odd is happening with our weather. Areas such as Florida and Texas and Mexico and India have experienced prolonged heat waves, droughts, and fires. Meanwhile, China and Bangladesh (not to mention many of the same places that were hit with droughts, including Texas, Mexico, and India) are deluged by intense rains and flooding.
In many regions, extreme weather has spawned clusters of water- and mosquito-borne diseases, such as cholera and malaria. Heat is building up in ocean surfaces, and there is evidence of deep ocean warming, which may explain the intensity of recent El Nino-related weather events.
More atmospheric water vapor also means warmer nights, as clouds block escaping heat. Insects like these changes, but humans, animals, and plants need relief at night. Warmer winters and warming at high elevations have turned traditional winter snows into winter rains -- leaving sheets of ice when a cold snap hits.
The warmer winters and earlier springs also spawn more molds and pollens. And higher temperatures and humidity increase the photochemical smog formation fed by burning fossil fuels. More allergens and more smog -- from air pollution and climate change acting together -- could be contributing to the rising rates of asthma.
As our experience suggests, extreme weather events are on the rise. Data from the US National Climatic Data Center indicate that in this century in the United States and most other parts of the world, dry spells and heat waves have become longer, and sudden, intense downpours (more than two inches a day of rain) and flash floods also have increased.
New England has already experienced its share of floods and ice storms, and -- with it's extended coastline -- is vulnerable to the long-term impact of global warming, such as rising sea level and greater storm surges.
But perhaps the most troubling consequence of global warming is the instability of the climate. Ice core records near the end of the last ice age 10,000 years ago indicate that such instability has, in the past, preceded rapid and profound climate changes.
Lately, some elements of the fossil fuel lobby have acknowledged that global warming is occurring. But they insist -- contrary to the weight of scientific opinion -- that the impact will be minimal. Instead, they focus on what they call the "uncertainty" in the science. But common sense suggests that there is far more uncertainty about our future than about the science.
The costs of unstable and severe weather are already mounting. Insurance losses and Federal Emergency Management Agency payouts from weather-related events quadrupled this decade. Even higher costs are in store if warmer temperatures do, as some predict, give rise to more weather extremes and associated disease outbreaks that can disrupt trade, transport, and tourism.
Fossil fuel dependence is an addiction from which we must wean ourselves. Looking at the full "life-cycle" of fossil fuels -- their extraction, mining, refining, transport, and combustion -- it is clear they are neither healthy for the environment nor for its inhabitants.
Granted, transition to clean energy sources will not be easy; money will be needed to jump-start infant industries and accelerate markets for new technologies. But the change is ultimately necessary. Greater energy efficiency and new technologies can generate eventual economic growth in the United States and in the global economy. The cost of inaction could be enormous.
While Washington politicians debate the meaning of climate changes, New England can lead the way in this transition. Powering utility plants in Massachusetts with natural gas, rather than coal or oil -- an initiative by the Massachusetts Public Interest Research Group, Clean Water Action, and other groups -- will contribute to a cleaner environment.
Meanwhile, the Clean Buses for Boston Coalition is urging this and other cities to switch from polluting diesel buses to those powered with natural gas (emitting minimal greenhouse gases), electric batteries, or fuel cells. Such buses would decrease soot and smog and possibly asthma -- a disease affecting poor communities most dramatically -- while also decreasing the threat of climate change.
Massachusetts also has a number of solar businesses. Contrary to the fossil fuel industry's contentions, enterprises devising new technologies stand to gain an edge in future global markets.
Farsighted leadership is needed to drive clean development. It is possible to stabilize the climate system, but it will require a 60 to 70 percent reduction in fossil fuel emissions. The money wisely invested in that effort would constitute a global insurance policy.
We face an increasingly uncertain future, with climatic disruption compounded by advancing economic and political instabilities. The obstructionist moves by industry lobbyists and recalcitrant legislators represent a blind path, ignoring the most fundamental scientific, social, and economic challenges we face today. The question before us is not whether but how to develop a transition to clean energy and use it to help stimulate equitable global economic growth.

Subject: Greenhouse effect; Climate; Environmental policy; Fossil fuels; Energy conservation; Pollution control; LDCs; Developing countries--LDCs

LETTERS TO THE EDITOR: [SECOND EDITION]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/letters-editor/docview/270482290/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Here is what I want to be reading about: I have just heard that, worldwide, the insurance industry spent an average of $2 billion a year covering severe weather-related damage during the 1980s. And that from 1990 to 1995 it spent an average of $30 billion a year. And it is worried about its future. I want to know more about this and what has been happening since 1995 as severe weather patterns have worsened. I also hear that all of us have been duped by oil- and coal-industry representatives into believing that the impact of global warming on our climate is still a mystery. I now understand that 2,500 research scientists of the United Nations Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change agreed in 1995 that this extreme weather was the result of human activity, and principally from our use of fossil fuels. Is this true? This was a lady of great words whose good heart, warm smiles and inner beauty enriched us all. Godspeed, Caroline [Drewes]. With you up there, heaven will be a lovelier place. Tom Eastham Vice president & Western director William Randolph Hearst Foundation (Former Examiner executive editor) San Francisco

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=LETTERS+TO+THE+EDITOR&amp;author=&amp;issn=2574593X&amp;title=San+Francisco+Examiner&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-10-08&amp;spage=A.22&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Too focused on scandal, we miss a serious climatic
threat
I was happy to read the letters to the editor (Oct. 1) lamenting the extensive coverage given to President Clinton and Monica Lewinsky and I agreed with them all. I noticed, though, that the writers did not tell you what they actually did want to see covered by your newspaper.
Here is what I want to be reading about: I have just heard that, worldwide, the insurance industry spent an average of $2 billion a year covering severe weather-related damage during the 1980s. And that from 1990 to 1995 it spent an average of $30 billion a year. And it is worried about its future. I want to know more about this and what has been happening since 1995 as severe weather patterns have worsened.
I also hear that all of us have been duped by oil- and coal-industry representatives into believing that the impact of global warming on our climate is still a mystery. I now understand that 2,500 research scientists of the United Nations Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change agreed in 1995 that this extreme weather was the result of human activity, and principally from our use of fossil fuels. Is this true?
If so, it would seem to me that there is a coverup we have been missing by spending so much time investigating Clinton. And the results have been catastrophic.
I am not interested in blaming the coal and oil industry for covering up the truth about global warming. They, like all of us, are trying to survive. I am interested in what we are going to do now. Who will the heroes be? How will we make it through? How will we transform our fossil-fuel economy into a global system of renewable energy? Let's hear this story! Tina Estes Oakland
Caroline Drewes
The death of Caroline Drewes also means the death of a kind of journalism we may not see again. The special qualities she brought to San Francisco newspapers for 60 years will surely and sorely be missed.
Caroline wrote with heart. As a writer and as a person, she cared deeply and wrote with intimacy, affection and truth. Never the hard-boiled news hound, her genteel artistry treated all with respect - including readers. When her stories had unpleasant or unsavory aspects, she skillfully avoided demeaning.
For Caroline, every story became an adventure into her subject, an adventure she could share with readers. Though warned against it, she became emotionally involved with everything she put to paper. At times, she wrote with tears streaming down her cheeks.
This was a lady of great words whose good heart, warm smiles and inner beauty enriched us all. Godspeed, Caroline. With you up there, heaven will be a lovelier place. Tom Eastham Vice president & Western director William Randolph Hearst Foundation (Former Examiner executive editor) San Francisco
Plea for supermarket
As Safeway stands up for applause on its record earnings to its stockholders ("Safeway earnings up 29% in quarter," Business section, Oct. 1), what about the Ocean Avenue and Ingleside neighborhoods that lost their only grocery store (yes, Safeway) in 1994? The large building on the corner of Jules Street and Ocean Avenue sits vacant and our neighborhood still has no supermarket.
Since Petrini's closed a year ago at Stonestown, the Park Merced area has also lost its neighborhood supermarket. Many people in this neighborhood do not drive. Can you imagine grocery shopping on the Muni?
Let's call on Safeway to reopen its Ocean Avenue store and provide free round-trip shuttle service to there from Park Merced. This would provide a wonderful service to our underserved areas. Barbara Commins San Francisco
Papal teaching in S.F.
Stephanie Salter's column, "If Jesus came to San Francisco" (Opinion Page, Sept. 17), suggests that in upholding certain teachings of the Catholic Church, Pope John Paul II is somehow at odds with the teachings of Jesus.
In so doing she ignores the words of Christ: "Thou art Peter, and upon this Rock I will build my Church." Thus did Jesus himself establish the pope as the vicar of Christ on Earth.
Salter is free to reject that fundamental Catholic teaching, as well as any other teachings of the church she chooses not to accept. She is not, however, empowered to rewrite church teachings to conform to her personal ideology. Rick Hinshaw Director of communications Catholic League for Religious and Civil Rights New York
Cops' faulty priorities
The bizarre attitude of the San Francisco Police Department regarding the relative threat posed by cars and bikes was never more apparent.
I work as a physician in the emergency department at St. Luke's Hospital. The other night a man ran his car into the back of a parked ambulance and passed out. He was brought into the emergency room and awoke, promptly becoming combative and biting a nurse.
Once he was restrained (no easy task), the police were called. They questioned him and he admitted having done PCP before setting out in his car. The police decided that no serious crime had been committed and left without citing or arresting the man. He was allowed to drive home once he sobered up.
This is shocking. This is outrageous. This is typical.
Any bicyclist in San Francisco can tell you that complaints to the police regarding dangerous drivers are rarely taken seriously or acted upon. It seems cyclists riding in Critical Mass are more dangerous than wildly intoxicated drivers.
I can only hope our patient that evening decides on his own not to drive while on PCP again. Certainly he hasn't had that advice from the police. Michael Treece, M.D. San Francisco
NATO and Serb atrocities
Does anyone remember NATO threatening Serbia a few months ago over Kosovo? What happened to those threats?
Serbia is just completing a mop-up operation on the Albanian population, business as usual murdering women, children and old men with bullets to the back of the head.
Every so often I hear the distant muttering of some NATO general venting indignation over "human rights" violations. Everything is fully documented by a team of international observers who remark daintily on another village full of blasted, sun-bloated corpses.
Does anyone remember that we stopped Slobodan Milosevic and his thugs in Bosnia with a few good aerial attacks? How can we let Serbian butchers repeat the same ethnic cleansing tactics three years later?
Have we lost all capacity for moral response or have Bosnia, Rwanda, Burma, Cambodia and our own butchery in El Salvador completely inured us to suffering and moral outrage? Bruce Skogen Oakland

IN PREDICTION SCIENCE, CHAOS IS THE ONLY CONSTANT: [SOONER EDITION]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/prediction-science-chaos-is-only-constant/docview/391589650/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Things have changed somewhat since then, even though predictions are as much in demand as ever, and even though we still send plenty of business to astrologers, tarot-card readers, numerologists, phrenologists, necromancers and psychics of all stripes. Scientific sages depend not on tea leaves or the positions of planets but on tools like mathematical modeling, statistical analysis, complexity theory, celestial mechanics, geology, economics and epidemiology. Needless to say, scientists discount the naive paradigms of fortune tellers, ancient and modern. The largest earthquake in four years - an 8.2-magnitude temblor centered under the ocean between Australia and Antarctica - caught seismologists completely off guard when it shook the sea floor last March 25, and geologists are still puzzled by the quake; it did not occur at the junction of tectonic plates where most quakes occur, but struck within a single plate.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=IN+PREDICTION+SCIENCE%2C+CHAOS+IS+THE+ONLY+CONSTANT&amp;author=&amp;issn=1068624X&amp;title=Pittsburgh+Post+-+Gazette&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-09-28&amp;spage=A.8&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Back during China's Shang dynasty, around 3,500 years ago, sages foretold the future by casting oracle bones - the clairvoyant equivalent of crap shooting.
Things have changed somewhat since then, even though predictions are as much in demand as ever, and even though we still send plenty of business to astrologers, tarot-card readers, numerologists, phrenologists, necromancers and psychics of all stripes.
Aside from the traditional soothsayers, we sometimes also heed forecasts based on observations, scientific synthesis and reasoning.
Scientific sages depend not on tea leaves or the positions of planets but on tools like mathematical modeling, statistical analysis, complexity theory, celestial mechanics, geology, economics and epidemiology. Needless to say, scientists discount the naive paradigms of fortune tellers, ancient and modern.
Alas, rational approaches to prediction also fail all too often, and it may be that there are some phenomena for which predictions will remain forever out of reach.
As the stock market lurches between the tugs of bulls and bears, financial analysts armed with statistics and the latest marketing theories throw up their hands in despair when asked to guess what will happen from one day to the next.
Picnic planners know better than to rely on short-term weather forecasts, and meteorologists offer little hope that truly accurate weather predictions for specific places and times will ever be possible.
Relief agencies get little useful help from experts as they try to brace for disasters that might, with equal likelihood, occur tomorrow or a thousand years from now. Understanding the mechanisms of events like earthquakes and volcanoes offers little help in predicting precisely when they will occur.
Geologists have a pretty good idea that earthquakes are caused by the movement of tectonic plates, yet the art of earthquake forecasting remains notoriously imprecise.
The largest earthquake in four years - an 8.2-magnitude temblor centered under the ocean between Australia and Antarctica - caught seismologists completely off guard when it shook the sea floor last March 25, and geologists are still puzzled by the quake; it did not occur at the junction of tectonic plates where most quakes occur, but struck within a single plate.
Future seismic surprises, including those causing catastrophic loss of life, seem inevitable.
The tsunami that roared over Papua New Guinea on July 17, killing more than 2,000 people, caught everyone by surprise. Experts excused their failure to warn the population on the ground that a giant tsunami materializes only when a scarcely noticeable wave spawned by an earthquake or some other event far out at sea reaches shallow coastal water. By then it is almost on top of some hapless coast line, leaving no time to prepare.
There have been recent reminders that even in principle, some things are impossible to predict.
Among the puzzles that have perplexed mathematicians and physicists at least since the time of Isaac Newton in the 17th century is the "N-body problem" (sometimes called the "Many-body problem").
One of Newton's monumental discoveries was that any two objects attract each other with a force proportional to their masses and inversely proportional to the square of their distance apart. But when three or more objects - the Sun, the Earth and the Moon, for example - are interacting gravitationally, exact solutions of the equations describing their motions generally remain beyond reach.
Fortunately, since Newton's day, some very good approximate solutions of N-body equations have been devised, and such solutions have allowed space scientists to send vehicles to the distant reaches of the solar system with astounding precision.
This month, Gregory R. Buck of Saint Anselm College, in Manchester, N.H., disclosed a new class of approximate N-body solutions based on the analogy of a closed loop of beads, in which the beads, evenly spaced, chase each other around the curves and tangles of the loop.
The system may help to work out the interactions of particles within a plasma, Buck suggested.
But none of the approximations now known exactly solve Newton's equations, and this means that the motions of asteroids and comets can be predicted only up to a certain point in the distant future. Beyond that, forecasting an impact by one of these objects on the earth may be intrinsically impossible.
A hit or miss may depend so sensitively on the minuscule "initial conditions" of all the objects involved that precise calculation becomes impossible. This is a property of a large class of systems scientists describe as chaotic.
Long-term foreknowledge of the hit or miss - a question of life or death for millions - is ruled out by the chaos inherent in the N-body system.
Scientists have yet to come to terms with chaos in all its manifestations. Steven Weinberg, winner of a Nobel prize in physics, once said he considered an understanding of chaotic turbulence in fluids as the single most intractable problem in physics. Until scientists reach a deeper understanding of turbulence, many physicists believe, the dynamics of climate change, the behavior of galaxies and many other phenomena can never be fully penetrated.
Chaos can also prevent reliable predictions of group decisions, including those that investors make. Group decisions by investors can cause chaotic price fluctuations of commodities that are intrinsically unpredictable, it seems.
In a report published Aug. 24 in the journal Physical Review Letters, David A. Meyer of the University of California at San Diego and Thad A. Brown of the University of Missouri presented formal proof that collective decisions can be chaotic, even when the views of all participants are known and a standard voting rule is strictly applied.
When a group of decision-makers must choose between three or more options by comparing two of them at a time, the collective outcome often depends on the order in which the choices are presented. The outcome can cycle chaotically, the mathematicians found.
Frustrated by the complex and chaotic behavior of the real world, some theorists have invented forecasting techniques based on little more than pure mathematics.
In the 1970s, Rene Thom, a French mathematician, developed an approach called Catastrophe Theory, which, for a time, enjoyed a considerable vogue among physicists, biologists and even sociologists. The theory is an application of topology, a field of mathematics that deals with the shapes of surfaces.
Thom's theory holds that many sequences of events can be represented as smooth trajectories along a saddle-shaped surface, at one point of which an abrupt discontinuity, or "catastrophe cusp," shunts them off to one side of the saddle or the other.
Thom and his followers proposed that mathematical models based on catastrophe cusps could be used to predict the reproduction of bacteria, the behavior of the stock market, heart attacks, biological evolution and the outbreak of war, among many other things.
To some, Thom's theory seemed to offer an explanation of almost everything, but many others condemned it as useless. Today, Catastrophe Theory is all but forgotten.
Predicting lifespans in the absence of detailed knowledge has long interested scientists as well as insurance statisticians. A forecast of life expectancy based on the average age at death of a person's four grandparents is a simple example of statistical forecasting. But a much more daring approach was devised a few years ago by J. Richard Gott III, a professor of astrophysics at Princeton University.
Gott's scheme is based on the "Copernican principle," which assumes that the odds are overwhelmingly against any particular place or time being "special." From this, Gott reasoned that the mere knowledge of how long something (or someone) has been around is sufficient to estimate how much longer it could last.
Based on this system, and the assumption that Homo sapiens appeared on earth about 200,000 years ago, Gott calculated that intelligent human beings are 95 percent certain to survive a minimum of 5,128 years more, and a maximum of 7.8 million years more.
There are those who contend that predictions like these are so vague that they not useful.
Scientists will never be able to answer all our questions about future events or to satisfy a deep-seated human yearning to foresee what's coming at us. Some scientific efforts at prediction will always be defeated by the nature of Nature.
Mystic oracles have never shed light on future events either, but even 1,398 years after Socrates' suicide, legions of people continue to visit palmists, astrologists and psychics. It's human to prefer something to nothing at all.
Illustration DRAWING; Caption: DRAWING: By James Hilston/Post-Gazette
Credit: MALCOLM W. BROWNE, THE NEW YORK TIMES

Title: IN PREDICTION SCIENCE, CHAOS IS THE ONLY CONSTANT:   [SOONER Edition ]

Publication date: Sep 28, 1998

Document type: NEWSPAPER

ProQuest document ID: 391589650

Science Squints at a Future Fogged by Chaotic Uncertainty

Author: Browne, Malcolm W

Full text: BACK during China's Shang dynasty, around 3,500 years ago, sages foretold the future by casting oracle bones -- the clairvoyant equivalent of crap shooting.

This month, Dr. Gregory R. Buck of Saint Anselm College, in Manchester, N.H., disclosed a new class of approximate N-body solutions based on the analogy of a closed loop of beads, in which the beads, evenly spaced, chase each other around the curves and tangles of the loop. Long-term foreknowledge of the hit or miss -- a question of life or death for millions -- is ruled out by the chaos inherent in the N-body system.
Scientists have yet to come to terms with chaos in all its manifestations. Dr. Steven Weinberg, winner of a Nobel prize in physics, once said he considered an understanding of chaotic turbulence in fluids as the single most intractable problem in physics. Until scientists reach a deeper understanding of turbulence, many physicists believe, the dynamics of climate change, the behavior of galaxies and many other phenomena can never be fully penetrated.
Chaos can also prevent reliable predictions of group decisions, including those that investors make. Group decisions by investors can cause chaotic price fluctuations of commodities that are intrinsically unpredictable, it seems.
In a report published Aug. 24 in the journal Physical Review Letters, Dr. David A. Meyer of the University of California at San Diego and Dr. Thad A. Brown of the University of Missouri presented formal proof that collective decisions can be chaotic, even when the views of all participants are known and a standard voting rule is strictly applied.
When a group of decision-makers must choose between three or more options by comparing two of them at a time, the collective outcome often depends on the order in which the choices are presented. The outcome can cycle chaotically, the mathematicians found. Even nonhuman decision-makers -- the computers that buy and sell commodities according to programmed rules, for example -- are subject to chaotic uncertainty, a situation in which prediction becomes impossible.
Frustrated by the complex and chaotic behavior of the real world, some theorists have invented forecasting techniques based on little more than pure mathematics.
In the 1970's, Rene Thom, a French mathematician, developed an approach called Catastrophe Theory, which, for a time, enjoyed a considerable vogue among physicists, biologists and even sociologists. The theory is an application of topology, a field of mathematics that deals with the shapes of surfaces.
Dr. Thom's theory holds that many sequences of events can be represented as smooth trajectories along a saddle-shaped surface, at one point of which an abrupt discontinuity, or ''catastrophe cusp,'' shunts them off to one side of the saddle or the other.
Dr. Thom and his followers proposed that mathematical models based on catastrophe cusps could be used to predict the reproduction of bacteria, the behavior of the stock market, heart attacks, biological evolution and the outbreak of war, among many other things. To some, Dr. Thom's theory seemed to offer an explanation of almost everything, but many others condemned it as useless. Today, Catastrophe Theory is all but forgotten.
Predicting life spans in the absence of detailed knowledge has long interested scientists as well as insurance statisticians. A forecast of life expectancy based on the average age at death of a person's four grandparents is a simple example of statistical forecasting. But a much more daring approach was devised a few years ago by Dr. J. Richard Gott 3d, a professor of astrophysics at Princeton University.
Dr. Gott's scheme is based on the ''Copernican principle,'' which assumes that the odds are overwhelmingly against any particular place or time being ''special.'' From this, Dr. Gott reasoned that the mere knowledge of how long something (or someone) has been around is sufficient to estimate how much longer it could last. Based on this system, and the assumption that Homo sapiens appeared on earth about 200,000 years ago, Dr. Gott calculated that intelligent human beings are 95-percent certain to survive a minimum of 5,128 years more, and a maximum of 7.8 million years more.
There are those who contend that predictions like these are so vague that they are scarcely more useful than the prophecies of the Delphic oracle in ancient Greece, which was consulted by Socrates, Oedipus and other luminaries of the day. The oracle (operated by a concealed priest or priestess) was so ambiguous it could nearly never be proved wrong.
Scientists will never be able to answer all our questions about future events or to satisfy a deep-seated human yearning to foresee what's coming at us. Some scientific efforts at prediction will always be defeated by the nature of Nature.
Mystic oracles have never shed light on future events either, but even 1,398 years after Socrates' suicide, legions of people continue to visit palmists, astrologists and psychics. It's human to prefer something to nothing at all.

Renewable energy sources needed: [2 Edition]

Author: SIMS, Ralph

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/renewable-energy-sources-needed/docview/314148675/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: In light of recent media reports that the Earth's oil reserves are dwindling, which will lead to drastic increases in oil prices, predicted to hit around the year 2005, we must consider the options available to a country like New Zealand that doesn't have sufficient oil supplies of its own. On a global level, displacing fossil fuels with renewable energy sources is one solution that has been suggested to reduce the rate of climate change. The Europeans aim to increase their supply of energy from these sources from 6 to 12 percent. In the United States, President Bill Clinton's science advisers have recommended huge increases in research funding into alternative energy. In Australia, the Government has advocated a 2 percent compulsory purchase by electricity retailers from Green Power generators.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Renewable+energy+sources+needed&amp;author=SIMS%2C+Ralph&amp;issn=11700394&amp;title=The+Evening+Standard&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-09-22&amp;spage=7&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: THE TIME has come for renewable energy sources.
In light of recent media reports that the Earth's oil reserves are dwindling, which will lead to drastic increases in oil prices, predicted to hit around the year 2005, we must consider the options available to a country like New Zealand that doesn't have sufficient oil supplies of its own.
On a global level, displacing fossil fuels with renewable energy sources is one solution that has been suggested to reduce the rate of climate change.
This was advocated at Kyoto, Japan, at the Climate Change conference in Decem ber 1997.
Other countries around the world are reading the signs and are investing in renewable energy supplies.
The Europeans aim to increase their supply of energy from these sources from 6 to 12 percent. In the United States, President Bill Clinton's science advisers have recommended huge increases in research funding into alternative energy. In Australia, the Government has advocated a 2 percent compulsory purchase by electricity retailers from Green Power generators.
The global insurance industry is also concerned at the increasing claims resulting from extreme weather events in recent years, such as drought, cyclones and floods, possibly a result of climate change caused by the consumption of these fossil fuels and the greenhouse gases they create.
It is also encouraging that international oil companies such as Shell and BP are diversifying into alternative energy sources by investing large sums in energy companies using sources of renewables, paving the way for future business.
But in New Zealand there has been a "disconnection" between recent energy-related policy decisions and our international obligations on greenhouse gas emissions and controls.
Cheaper car imports, resulting in more vehicle use and increased carbon dioxide emissions and a drastic cut in funding to the Energy Efficiency and Conservation Authority, are just two examples of New Zealand's backward stance.
Electricity reforms are also a backward step. Cheaper wholesale electricity prices in the short term, from increased competition, mean alternative sources of renewable energy may not be able to compete with gas and coal, until such time as the costs on society for emitting greenhouse gases are also included.
Oil and gas exploration is being encouraged and recent successes may replace the dwindling Maui reserves; Huntly may re convert from gas to coal; and the threatened carbon tax to industry is no longer on the Government's agenda.
Research funding in the energy sector continues to be dominated by the fossil fuel dinosaurs to the detriment of the sustainable energy mammals.
So, what is the answer? One controversial option could be just to continue to burn fossil fuels and plant more trees to absorb the CO2 until New Zealand is just one big forest again. Or better still, undergo a slow and painless transition from dependence on fossil fuels to more renewables while also encouraging energy efficiency. But to succeed we have to start now -- not put it off until 10 or 20 years' time.

Science Squints at a Future Fogged by Chaotic Uncertainty

Green stocks beat the Footsie: [FINAL Edition]

Author: GEOFFREY LEAN, Environment Correspondent

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/green-stocks-beat-footsie/docview/312724143/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: At a United Nations meeting of more than 200 financiers, the Environment Minister, Michael Meacher, presented figures showing that an index of stocks of firms, chosen for their environmental and social responsibility, had consistently outstripped the FTSE All- Share Index during the long bull market, and done twice as well since prices began to fall precipitously this summer. About pounds 2bn is invested in ethical and environmental funds in Britain - more than twice as much as in the rest of Europe. The NPI Social Index, compiled by the City firm, NPI Asset Management, measures the performance of the 150 British firms "assessed as being the most effective in managing their environmental and social impact". To join it, companies must have a good record, stretching from products offering environmental benefits, to charity donations and profit-sharing for employees. Household names in the index include Boots, BT, Iceland, Sainsbury, NatWest and General Accident.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Green+stocks+beat+the+Footsie&amp;author=GEOFFREY+LEAN%2C+Environment+Correspondent&amp;issn=09519467&amp;title=The+Independent&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-09-20&amp;spage=5&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: "GREEN-CHIP" ethical investments are far outperforming other stocks and shares, even in the stock-market slump, according to latest figures.
At a United Nations meeting of more than 200 financiers, the Environment Minister, Michael Meacher, presented figures showing that an index of stocks of firms, chosen for their environmental and social responsibility, had consistently outstripped the FTSE All- Share Index during the long bull market, and done twice as well since prices began to fall precipitously this summer.
Similar measurements for Asia and the Pacific - where the crash has hit hard - show that ethical stocks actually increased in value over the past six weeks, while shares as a whole fell by nearly 10 per cent.
This startling evidence that virtue need not be its own reward was one example of self-interest pulling in the same direction as the interests of the planet, discussed at a conference on "Profitability and Responsibility in the 21st Century" held last week at Queen's College, Cambridge.
The meeting, organised by the United Nations Environment Programme (UNEP), heard of big business opportunities in fighting pollution, discounted loans and "green" mortgages - and how global warming could threaten banks' and insurance firms' survival.
About pounds 2bn is invested in ethical and environmental funds in Britain - more than twice as much as in the rest of Europe. The NPI Social Index, compiled by the City firm, NPI Asset Management, measures the performance of the 150 British firms "assessed as being the most effective in managing their environmental and social impact". To join it, companies must have a good record, stretching from products offering environmental benefits, to charity donations and profit-sharing for employees. Household names in the index include Boots, BT, Iceland, Sainsbury, NatWest and General Accident.
The figures show that cumulative returns from the Social Index companies have risen by more than three-and-a-half times this decade, bettering a threefold increase by the FTSE All-Share Index, and doing better on almost all measures of share prices and dividends. And since 1 July this year, the price of its shares has dropped by only 5 per cent - less than half of the 11.05 per cent fall in shares as a whole.
Mr Meacher told the conference: "Too often, financial managers see ... ethical investment as a niche market. But perhaps these ethical investors have something to teach the broader investment industry." A good record on the environment was increasingly viewed by analysts as a sign of a well-managed company. "Fund managers are looking for efficient environmental management. 'Green chips' are as significant as blue chips."
There were massive opportunities for investment and profit in a fast- rising market for green products that was already worth about pounds 280bn a year worldwide. There was also a lot to lose through pollution and climate change.
By last week, 115 banks and other financial institutions, and 78 insurance companies worldwide had signed a UNEP init-iative, promising to incorporate environmental concerns into every aspect of their businesses.

`Crisis' in oceans may pose threat to human survival - commission report: [CITY EDITION]

Author: Siggins, Lorna

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/crisis-oceans-may-pose-threat-human-survival/docview/310423661/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The land pollutes the sea. Not a startling revelation, you might think, but it is one of the many detailed facts in the first report by the Independent World Commission on the Oceans which was published at Expo '98 in Lisbon, Portugal, last week. Published by the commission to mark the International Year of the Ocean, the report warns of an "unfolding drama" on the high seas, extending from territorial disputes which threaten peace and security and the effects of global climate change to over-fishing, habitat destruction, illegal trafficking, congested shipping lanes, piracy, terrorism and the disruption of coastal communities. The commission, which was formed under UN auspices in 1995, says it is no exaggeration to refer to a "crisis" in the oceans which may pose a threat to human survival. After all, in the words of the late Jacques Cousteau, "if the sea dies, man dies". The report presents much evidence to back up its case, including statistics on pollution levels, with both land and atmospheric sources of same; the growth of piracy and maritime violence, particularly in south-east Asia; detrimental effects of mineral exploitation and certain modern fishing practices; and the impact of El Nino.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=%60Crisis%27+in+oceans+may+pose+threat+to+human+survival+-+commission+report&amp;author=Siggins%2C+Lorna&amp;issn=&amp;title=Irish+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-09-07&amp;spage=15&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The land pollutes the sea. Not a startling revelation, you might think, but it is one of the many detailed facts in the first report by the Independent World Commission on the Oceans which was published at Expo '98 in Lisbon, Portugal, last week.
In fact, land-based sources account for some 77 per cent of marine pollution levels, according to the report which calls for a new global approach to ocean management and recommends that the issue of ocean affairs be integrated into all relevant UN bodies and agencies.
Published by the commission to mark the International Year of the Ocean, the report warns of an "unfolding drama" on the high seas, extending from territorial disputes which threaten peace and security and the effects of global climate change to over-fishing, habitat destruction, illegal trafficking, congested shipping lanes, piracy, terrorism and the disruption of coastal communities.
The commission, which was formed under UN auspices in 1995, says it is no exaggeration to refer to a "crisis" in the oceans which may pose a threat to human survival. After all, in the words of the late Jacques Cousteau, "if the sea dies, man dies". The report presents much evidence to back up its case, including statistics on pollution levels, with both land and atmospheric sources of same; the growth of piracy and maritime violence, particularly in south-east Asia; detrimental effects of mineral exploitation and certain modern fishing practices; and the impact of El Nino.
In relation to climate change, one of the most disturbing facts was gleaned during an analysis of whale catches since 1930 near the Antarctic ice edge. This has shown that the circumpolar ice edge retreated several degrees of latitude between 1954 and 1972, corresponding to a dramatic 25 per cent reduction in the area of ice cover, which probably has significant consequences for the biological productivity of the Southern Ocean. This major regional climatic change may signal a global one, it says.
The commission says it is unable to gauge to what extent climate change will be accompanied by an increase in the frequency and severity of storms or hurricanes - information which is critical to coastal engineering design and to the assessment of risk by the insurance industry. It believes oceans must be monitored to improve the ability to detect and predict the effects of climate change and the oceans' role as a "carbon dioxide sink".
It makes several recommendations aimed at allowing less developed coastal states like Ireland to build the capacity to take effective advantage - in a sustainable manner - of exclusive rights to use of resources in their areas, and to meet their obligations under the UN Law of the Sea Convention. However, high seas should be treated as a public trust, it stresses. Emphasising the need for a political will, it proposes that navies be deployed to the non-military task of protecting the marine environment. The maintenance of the freedom of the seas must be balanced against the claims of coastal states, it says.
"Efforts to reorientate naval responsibilities should include the creation and training of specialised naval units, mandated and equipped to undertake policing tasks supported, wherever possible, by the use of ocean observation satellites. Where navies are reluctant to depart from tradition, the capability of national coastguards and other security forces could be appropriately strengthened," it says.
The Ocean: Our Future is published by the Independent World Commission on the Oceans, Geneva, Switzerland, at e-mail: secretariat(at)world-oceans.org
Credit: Marine Correspondent

OTHER ISSUES: [FINAL EDITION]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/other-issues/docview/408730664/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: In 1996, a late rapprochement between President Clinton and congressional Republicans brought compromise on welfare and immigration reform, health insurance transferability for workers changing jobs and a minimum wage increase. That cast an aura of success over what had been a contentious Congress. It also helped the president and the House and Senate majorities to re-election. No such scenario seems likely this fall. President Clinton's wounded condition has Democrats on the defensive. House and Senate leaders feel emboldened to insist on their own priorities. That means showdowns could occur on a broad range of issues, including some that have not figured prominently in the campaign to date. Guns: The Brady law's five-day waiting period for handgun purchases, an achievement of Clinton's first term, expires in November. Democrats have proposed an extension, but the restrictions are unpopular with gunowners' rights advocates, who are influential with the majority Republicans in Congress. The issue gives both parties a chance to charge up hardcore supporters.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=OTHER+ISSUES&amp;author=&amp;issn=07347456&amp;title=USA+TODAY&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-09-04&amp;spage=13.A&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   POLITICS: ISSUES FOR THE FALL CAMPAIGN; Election '98
In 1996, a late rapprochement between President Clinton and congressional Republicans brought compromise on welfare and immigration reform, health insurance transferability for workers changing jobs and a minimum wage increase. That cast an aura of success over what had been a contentious Congress. It also helped the president and the House and Senate majorities to re-election.
No such scenario seems likely this fall. President Clinton's wounded condition has Democrats on the defensive. House and Senate leaders feel emboldened to insist on their own priorities. That means showdowns could occur on a broad range of issues, including some that have not figured prominently in the campaign to date.
Guns: The Brady law's five-day waiting period for handgun purchases, an achievement of Clinton's first term, expires in November. Democrats have proposed an extension, but the restrictions are unpopular with gunowners' rights advocates, who are influential with the majority Republicans in Congress. The issue gives both parties a chance to charge up hardcore supporters.
Farms: Farm state members have taken to the floor this summer to remind colleagues that agriculture has not shared in the nation's general prosperity of late. Amendments attached to the agriculture appropriations bill would give farmers emergency relief from the devastating consequences of drought, other bad weather caused by El Nino and plummeting commodity prices caused by topsy-turvy currency conditions in Asia. Without that emergency aid, incumbents from these states would be in jeopardy.
Trade: Congress defeated renewal of Clinton's "fast-track" trading authority in 1997. Fast-track allows the president to negotiate trade treaties for Congress to approve or disapprove without amendment. The bipartisan coalition that prevailed last year seems intact, and not many voters are clamoring for new trade agreements, especially given the rising tide of imports from Asia.
Minimum wage: Senate Democrats are pushing a vote to increase minimum wage. Although this issue brought the parties together in 1996, Republican leaders have never supported it in principle and see no special political pressure to relent on the issue now.
Gays: Clinton's effort to admit gays in the military was a potent issue when Republicans seized their first majority of Southern House seats in 1994. But this year, when an effort was made to rescind Clinton's executive order on gays in the federal civilian workforce, a substantial number of Republicans sided with most Democrats in rejecting it. Some candidates still will raise the issue of either "gay rights" or "the homosexual agenda," but usually not in competitive districts.
Juvenile crime: Crime bills were once a staple of election-year legislative agendas. But this year, with crime rates down, the major crime legislation focused on juvenile programs and penalties. Efforts to tie in school violence have not raised the bill to national prominence, however. A House-passed bill is pending in the Senate, where it's certain to face filibuster.
Environment: Democrats are eager to address climate change and global warming concerns, but Republicans question the reliability of the underpinning theories. Democrats also are seeking ways to make use of voter anxiety about overdevelopment and urban sprawl, while Republicans press the rights of property owners against government regulation.
