SECURITISATION Capital markets set to take on bigger role: [Surveys edition]

Author: Coppack, Lee

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/securitisation-capital-markets-set-take-on-bigger/docview/248734017/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Jacques Blondeau, the chairman and chief executive of French reinsurer SCOR, is a strong believer in catastrophe bonds. Says Mr Blondeau: "For a normal reinsurer to build up a huge fund of capital waiting for the big one does not make sense from an economic or financial perspective, since it would depress the return on equity. I think for this sort of activity, the capital markets will take over." The reluctance of reinsurers to carry peak risks is one of the main forces driving the development of the market for alternative forms of catastrophe finance. From the perspective of the worldwide insurance and reinsurance industry, there is an awareness of the imbalance between the supply of capital that can be raised and serviced and the potential exposure for catastrophe losses in the developed world alone, without even taking into account future demand from developing countries. The Catastrophe Price Index calculated by Paragon Reinsurance Risk Management Services Inc calculates relative US property/catastrophe prices from a sample of approximately 300 companies representing almost 1,000 treaties, and approximately 42 per cent of estimated industry premium. It started at 1.00 on January 1 in 1984 and fell to 0.93 by January 1 1989. Rates were already moving upwards when Hurricane Andrew occurred in September 1992. The index rose 60.2 per cent on the January 1 1993 renewals and peaked two years later at 2.47 following the Northridge earthquake. It has fallen steadily since and was down to 1.67 for renewals from January 1 1998.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=SECURITISATION+Capital+markets+set+to+take+on+bigger+role&amp;author=Coppack%2C+Lee&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-09-04&amp;spage=03&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: * by Lee Coppack
Capital markets set to take on bigger role
New forms of catastrophe finance are being used to spread risks
Jacques Blondeau, the chairman and chief executive of French reinsurer SCOR, is a strong believer in catastrophe bonds. Says Mr Blondeau: "For a normal reinsurer to build up a huge fund of capital waiting for the big one does not make sense from an economic or financial perspective, since it would depress the return on equity. I think for this sort of activity, the capital markets will take over."
The reluctance of reinsurers to carry peak risks is one of the main forces driving the development of the market for alternative forms of catastrophe finance. From the perspective of the worldwide insurance and reinsurance industry, there is an awareness of the imbalance between the supply of capital that can be raised and serviced and the potential exposure for catastrophe losses in the developed world alone, without even taking into account future demand from developing countries.
Favouring stronger capital market involvement is a demand for highly rated investments as the issue of sovereign debt from industrial countries declines and as a way of securing better performance than can be obtained from low-yielding government bonds. Catastrophe bond issues, such as the 1998 bond for Texas-based USAA, are still being priced at more than 400 basis points over three-month Libor.
Normally, after a large catastrophe, rates rise. Reinsurers can raise more capital to take advantage of the higher rates. Prices soon begin to flatten, however, as a function of increased supply of capital. The problem, as the Bermuda property/catastrophe companies have found even in a zero tax environment, is not raising the capital but servicing it when the rates slide down.
The Catastrophe Price Index calculated by Paragon Reinsurance Risk Management Services Inc calculates relative US property/catastrophe prices from a sample of approximately 300 companies representing almost 1,000 treaties, and approximately 42 per cent of estimated industry premium. It started at 1.00 on January 1 in 1984 and fell to 0.93 by January 1 1989. Rates were already moving upwards when Hurricane Andrew occurred in September 1992. The index rose 60.2 per cent on the January 1 1993 renewals and peaked two years later at 2.47 following the Northridge earthquake. It has fallen steadily since and was down to 1.67 for renewals from January 1 1998.
Two points emerge. Although these three catastrophes alone resulted in insured losses of around $34.4bn, according to Swiss Re's Sigma report , competition began pushing rates down from January 1 1994. At the same time rates remain materially above pre-Andrew levels, which suggests that reinsurers are comparatively conservative about the amount of capital they are prepared to commit to US catastrophe risks.
The demand for securitised catastrophe protection could be extensive because without even considering the potential loss from a Tokyo earthquake or a wind storm striking the length the US Atlantic coast, there is already a substantial gap between insured and economic loss in catastrophes.
Munich Re estimates that in 1996 and 1997 insured losses were only 15 per cent of the total. Insurance density is the key. Insurance density and insured values are very high in the US. The Kobe earthquake in Japan in January 1995 killed about 6,000 people and resulted in insured losses of $2.6bn, in 1996 price terms, but Munich Re estimates uninsured economic losses at about $97bn.
The growing world population and intensifying urbanisation do not just lead to an increase in insured exposures but can actually change the nature of risk. Urban centres are attractive targets for terrorists because of the impact they can have. The combination of populations under pressure in cities with valuable property is a volatile one; race riots in Los Angeles in 1992 figure among the 40 mostly costly loss events since 1970. The increased heat and exhaust gases associated with urbanisation and geological changes resulting from large building works can also affect risk. As Munich Re points out: "Big cities create their own climate, a problem that will be intensified by global climate change."
It also points out that more than 15 per cent of all insurance payments were for insurance of the person - life, health and personal accident. In terms of fatalities, the greatest deaths occurred in earthquakes and cyclones, but the list also includes three man-made disasters in India: a dam-burst in 1979 when 15,000 people died; the Bhopal chemical plant malfunction which resulted in 3,000 deaths and much disability which was not recorded; and a railway accident at Bihar in 1981 when about 2,500 people died. Copyright Financial Times Limited 1998. All Rights Reserved.

Beware of `experts' with one bright idea: [Final Edition]

Author: Riley, Susan

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/beware-experts-with-one-bright-idea/docview/353016867/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Indeed, Carl Sonnen of Informetrica, Ottawa-based economic consultants, believes ongoing cuts to education and health spending are contributing to the decline in productivity, not reversing it. His is a credible, if rarely heard, challenge to current orthodoxy. As Sonnen says, a productive workforce tends to be "well-educated and healthy." In underfunding social programs, the government may be blunting our competitive edge. Such complexities are lost amid the alarmist headlines, dire warnings and daily predictions of disaster. Never mind that stock markets, by their nature, rise and fall, or that the loonie's latest decline is an extension of a 15-year slide; caveats like these can ruin a good panic. And a panic, abetted by an always-eager media, provides a perfect opportunity for religious fanatics of all stripes to advance their own brand of salvation. Lately, it has been tax cuts. Pollster Frank Graves of Ekos Research says repeated soundings show Canadians think taxes are too high, but rank tax cuts below other priorities, like shoring up medicare and retiring the debt. For his part, Graves doesn't object to tax relief, but he rightly accuses its most ardent advocates of being "disingenuous" and "self- interested" when advancing broad-based cuts as an economic cure- all. "I'm surprised," he says, "they weren't suggested as a solution to climate change or El Nino."

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Beware+of+%60experts%27+with+one+bright+idea&amp;author=Riley%2C+Susan&amp;issn=11974397&amp;title=Kingston+Whig+-+Standard&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-09-03&amp;spage=7&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: All of a sudden, everything has come undone for Finance Minister Paul Martin (if not for the rest of us.) Stock markets are slumping, the loonie has reached historic lows, interest rates have gone up, growth is slowing and talk of global recession has dampened this spring's cheery predictions of yearly federal surpluses.
So far, Martin has responded with reassurances and a promise. In what has become a mantra, he insists that "the fundamentals are right" - that our declining dollar is the victim of nervous speculators spooked by the Asian crisis, and not a judgment on Canada's economic health. And he promises more funding for health, along with tax cuts for middle-class Canadians, in his February budget.
But are the fundamentals right? Not all economists think so; indeed, they point to evidence that productivity - the efficiency with which we produce and export goods - has fallen in the last five years as sporadic layoffs and downsizing continue. The right blames this on spoiled or undermotivated Canadian workers, allegedly coddled by free medicare and unemployment benefits that are still too rich. This powerful chorus calls for more cuts in government spending, more privatization, an end to minimum wage laws and other "labour market rigidities" designed to shelter people from abject misery.
There is another school, less popular with newspaper editorial boards, that argues that poor productivity is as much the result of uninspired management, bad investments and lack of funding for research as the fault of a malingering workforce. Another problem, some say, is a low dollar that makes our exports automatically attractive and removes incentives to Canadian industry to modernize.
Indeed, Carl Sonnen of Informetrica, Ottawa-based economic consultants, believes ongoing cuts to education and health spending are contributing to the decline in productivity, not reversing it. His is a credible, if rarely heard, challenge to current orthodoxy. As Sonnen says, a productive workforce tends to be "well-educated and healthy." In underfunding social programs, the government may be blunting our competitive edge.
But such complexities are lost amid the alarmist headlines, dire warnings and daily predictions of disaster. Never mind that stock markets, by their nature, rise and fall, or that the loonie's latest decline is an extension of a 15-year slide; caveats like these can ruin a good panic. And a panic, abetted by an always-eager media, provides a perfect opportunity for religious fanatics of all stripes to advance their own brand of salvation. Lately, it has been tax cuts.
The drumbeat started some years ago in the financial press, within the Reform Party caucus and in other redoubts of the right, and has now reached the finance minister's office. So eager is Martin to reward long-suffering Canadians with a tax break this winter that he is apparently ready to appropriate the Employment Insurance fund, with its tempting $6-billion surplus - enough, Martin hopes, to offset an expected $4-billion shortfall in anticipated federal revenues.
But will tax cuts stimulate a floundering economy, avert a recession and compensate for interest rate increases, as its champions suggest? That depends on how large the tax cut is and how widely shared. Across-the-board cuts, which attract most attention - for an ambitious finance minister, a crucial consideration - are also the most costly. In fact, they are so costly that Martin won't likely be able to afford anything more than a token gesture - "enough to buy a radio, but not a car," in the words of ministerial adviser Bob Brown.
Why waste money on an ineffectual tax cut? Martin has a sophisticated grasp of economics and politics; if he proceeds with a general tax cut, it will be the politician speaking, although he may not be saying what people, outside of his highly-paid advisers, want to hear.
Pollster Frank Graves of Ekos Research says repeated soundings show Canadians think taxes are too high, but rank tax cuts below other priorities, like shoring up medicare and retiring the debt. For his part, Graves doesn't object to tax relief, but he rightly accuses its most ardent advocates of being "disingenuous" and "self- interested" when advancing broad-based cuts as an economic cure- all. "I'm surprised," he says, "they weren't suggested as a solution to climate change or El Nino."
In the end, several apparently contradictory things are true. The economic situation isn't as bad as it seems and it could get much worse. Beware of the "expert" with one bright idea. And remember: the fundamentals are right. Or are they?

____________________________________________________________

Climate Change: As Gains on Equities Dissipate, So Does U.S. Economic Froth --- Party Atmosphere Darkens Despite a Rally; Jaguars And IPOs Are Affected --- A Test for Corporate Europe

Author: By staff reporters Michael Siconolfi, E.S. Browning and Patrick McGeehan

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/climate-change-as-gains-on-equities-dissipate-so/docview/308330508/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: What's left of that portfolio has since plunged. So when it came time for Mr. [Mark] Schwartz to finally buy the "restored and pristine" Jaguar of his dreams, he just couldn't do it. The lawyer from Bryn Mawr, Pennsylvania, figured he could make do with his 1988 Volvo. It wasn't just Mr. Schwartz's stocks that took a beating. So did his psyche. And it isn't just individuals and Americans who are affected. The 1990s' rising stock prices paved the way for a record crop of entrepreneurial businesses to tap the public markets for capital, primarily through initial public offerings. In addition, some of the world's largest corporate mergers -- witness the $48 billion trans-Atlantic deal between British Petroleum PLC and Amoco Corp. -- have taken place in the past few years, compliments of high stock prices that made deals easier to do. In corporate compensation, steadily rising share prices have made stock options a valuable part of millions of American pay packages, not just for executives, but also for some lower-level employees. While it is hard to say how profoundly a bear market would affect corporate practices -- so much would depend on how relentless the bear was -- it is notable that American IPOs have already been slammed, while some European IPOs have been postponed. An IPO well that stayed dry for a long time could, unbeknownst to the world, lead to the death of any number of innovative little companies that years from now might otherwise have become major players. The venture-capital seed money that funds those kinds of start-ups also would likely dry up.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Climate+Change%3A+As+Gains+on+Equities+Dissipate%2C+So+Does+U.S.+Economic+Froth+---+Party+Atmosphere+Darkens+Despite+a+Rally%3B+Jaguars+And+IPOs+Are+Affected+---+A+Test+for+Corporate+Europe&amp;author=By+staff+reporters+Michael+Siconolfi%2C+E.S.+Browning+and+Patrick+McGeehan&amp;issn=09219986&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-09-03&amp;spage=1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: What would a bear market be like?
U.S. share prices moved down again Wednesday, reminding investors of Monday's 6.4% drop and giving them a troubling hint of what it would mean to their lives and businesses if shares no longer were heading inexorably higher.
It was an experience new to many: The last 20% stock decline came nearly eight years ago, and the last grinding, relentless down market was 25 years back. With another long and deep decline in U.S. stocks no longer unimaginable, it is worth exploring some people's reactions to the recent chill winds.
Mark Schwartz's reaction, for instance. In last year's raging bull market, Mr. Schwartz felt so rich that he sold a $25,000 chunk of his portfolio of Israeli high-technology stocks to pay for his son's bar mitzvah.
What's left of that portfolio has since plunged. So when it came time for Mr. Schwartz to finally buy the "restored and pristine" Jaguar of his dreams, he just couldn't do it. The lawyer from Bryn Mawr, Pennsylvania, figured he could make do with his 1988 Volvo. It wasn't just Mr. Schwartz's stocks that took a beating. So did his psyche.
And the beatings are likely to continue. The Dow Jones Industrial Average sank 45.06 points to 7782.37 in late trading Wednesday, deflating some of the optimism behind Tuesday's 288-point rally -- and leaving investors in U.S. stocks about $2 trillion poorer than they were last week. One result is that many people are already feeling less well off, and some are starting to pull back from the types of spending commonplace in the bull market's heyday.
And it isn't just individuals and Americans who are affected. The 1990s' rising stock prices paved the way for a record crop of entrepreneurial businesses to tap the public markets for capital, primarily through initial public offerings. In addition, some of the world's largest corporate mergers -- witness the $48 billion trans-Atlantic deal between British Petroleum PLC and Amoco Corp. -- have taken place in the past few years, compliments of high stock prices that made deals easier to do. In corporate compensation, steadily rising share prices have made stock options a valuable part of millions of American pay packages, not just for executives, but also for some lower-level employees.
The great bull market has also helped draw thousands of European investors into stocks for the first time; a protracted bear market in the U.S. would test the nerve of these newcomers as never before. It would also test European corporate executives, many of whom are now saying that the current market gyrations won't prompt them to scale back their investment plans (see "Many European Concerns Aren't Rattled by Markets" -- WSJE Sept. 3,1998).
While it is hard to say how profoundly a bear market would affect corporate practices -- so much would depend on how relentless the bear was -- it is notable that American IPOs have already been slammed, while some European IPOs have been postponed. An IPO well that stayed dry for a long time could, unbeknownst to the world, lead to the death of any number of innovative little companies that years from now might otherwise have become major players. The venture-capital seed money that funds those kinds of start-ups also would likely dry up.
All this threatens to weaken the "entrepreneurial spirit" that has flourished in the 1990s, says Reena Aggarwal, associate professor of finance at Georgetown University. It would be particularly problematic for potential start-ups because, Ms. Aggarwal says, no other country in the world has any substantive new-issue market for young companies: "The U.S. is the only country where a Netscape and Yahoo! can go public."
Just one new issue came to market in the U.S. last week, down from 19 only four weeks ago. Other IPOs have been postponed or canceled. One casualty is Galacticomm Technologies Inc., a Florida-based software company that was slated to price an $11 million IPO Tuesday and begin trading Wednesday. Galacticomm plans to try for its IPO in a couple of weeks, provided the market stabilizes. If not, "you have to temper your ambitions," says Peter Berg, chief executive, and try tapping private sources of capital instead.
Similar casualties are cropping up across Europe. In Sweden, for example, the Stockholm Stock Exchange last year saw 54 IPOs and other share releases. This year, exchange officials are expecting a maximum of 40 -- or, if the current market volatility continues, as few as 30. In recent weeks, some five companies have put their plans to release shares on hold until the market quiets down, exchange officials say.
In the years since Americans last lived through a prolonged bear market -- 1973 and much of 1974 -- the world has become a very different place. For one thing, it is more sensitive to stock-market swings. In the mid-1970s, Silicon Valley was in its infancy, and many of its fabulous growth stocks didn't yet exist. Powerhouse companies that today drive an economy centered on technology -- behemoths such as Microsoft, Cisco Systems and Dell Computer -- hadn't been founded. It was nearly unimaginable that a young company could hire an earring-wearing engineer with a fat package of options, then see him jump off to found his own company and take it public in the stock market.
American retirement accounts those days tended to be run by faceless pension-fund executives with an attachment to bonds and predictable returns. Today, individuals often manage their own retirement accounts, shifting them into and out of a dizzying array of mutual funds. Indeed, individuals never have been more vulnerable to a stock downturn. Fueled by the bull market, households' net worth -- their financial and real assets less liabilities -- earlier this year hit a record six times their disposable personal income.
Many investors remain flush, of course, after nearly eight years of market gains. The full impact on the spending and other behavior of individuals and corporations tends to lag behind the market action. And, of course, the market correction could be over; stocks could continue Tuesday and Wednesday's broad rebound and earn back the losses. Indeed, optimism about a recovery led Gregory Hinckley, chief operating officer of Oregon-based Mentor Graphics Corp., to buy 5,000 more shares of his company's slumping stock to add to the 25,000 shares he already owned. "I'm a contrarian by nature," he says.
Yet many people suddenly "don't feel as wealthy as they did, and are less likely to splurge," says John Lonski, chief economist at Moody's Investors Service Inc., the credit-rating company. "Spending on bigticket items" will suffer, Mr. Lonski predicts. "What you're going to find is that consumer confidence will be much more sensitive" to a market swoon.
About a quarter of U.S. consumer spending is "due to the wealth from the stock market," estimates Sung Won Sohn, chief economist at Minneapolis banking group Norwest Corp. "A correction or bear market, which is what I think we are seeing, will have a significant impact on consumer spending," he says, "and therefore on all the companies selling to consumers. And two-thirds of the economy is driven by consumer spending." An America that consumes less also imports less, a troubling thought considering that the U.S. has long been the world's consumer of last resort.
Credit Suisse First Boston Corp., which had losses in Russia and other emerging markets, has told recruiters it won't be doing any more hiring for its bond, investment-banking and derivatives departments this year. A spokesman says those divisions at the firm, a unit of Credit Suisse Group, had already "met their head count" although "exceptions" are permitted. "This is routine business practice this time of year," he says.
Meanwhile, some analysts say a worsening market slump would threaten the long-anticipated November IPO of Wall Street giant Goldman, Sachs & Co. Goldman says its "plans remain unchanged."
If the stock market gives the nation a chill, New York City could face a frost. The largest U.S. city has become more dependent than ever on Wall Street in the past five years. Wall Street bonuses hit $12 billion last year, bringing budget surpluses to government, according to a report by state Comptroller Carl McCall. Securities firms incurring emerging-market trading losses are likely to cut bonuses and perhaps lay people off, surely affecting the region's financial health.
From his front-row view of the market's drama of recent weeks, Mark Greenstein had seen enough by the end of last week to lose his appetite for a bigger condo and a new car. A broker at a small Wall Street firm, Mr. Greenstein was in the midst of selling a one-bedroom apartment in Manhattan for $255,000, with his sights set on a two-bedroom priced at about $350,000.
He says he also had his eye on a new Mercedes to replace the 1983 Cadillac Coupe de Ville he inherited from his father. He has scrapped those plans, at least for now, because he thinks trouble in stocks could portend a weaker real-estate market down the road. "I'll just keep the old Caddy running," he says, and take the ribbing from his colleagues.
Corcoran Group, a real-estate brokerage firm in New York, has held workshops to coach brokers on "how to reassure your buyer." Among the tips: Remind them that "unlike most stock portfolios, a home is a long-term investment." Though they needed more hand-holding, most prospective buyers went ahead with their purchases -- until last weekend, says the firm's president, Barbara Corcoran. She says buyers started pulling out of deals, citing fears about the direction of stocks.
Silicon Valley, where scores of high-tech start-ups have enriched employees with lucrative stock options, also faces some cracks. Already, some tech companies have begun repricing options, which allow holders to buy shares at a set price. With the steep stock-price plunges, some of those options have become essentially worthless. Companies such as Quickturn Design Systems Inc. in San Jose, California, have "reloaded" them at lower prices, partly to keep employees from bolting. The company's "most valuable talent" was being wooed by rivals, and it needed to reprice the options to keep those employees "focused and content," says Raymond Ostby, chief financial officer.
Some area restaurants have felt a chill. Steve Koidal, general manager of Left At Albuquerque in Palo Alto, California, finds people still are drinking his margaritas and eating his salmon and fajitas. But business, instead of growing steadily as in the past, has become choppy. The restaurant will do record business one day, "and then some other days have been extremely slow for no apparent reason," he says.
Many tech stocks trading on the Nasdaq Stock Market have been in a slump since April. And even as overall California home sales rose 8.1% in July from June, sales in Silicon Valley slid 7.3%, according to G.U. Krueger, deputy chief economist at the California Association of Realtors. Lee Blansett put his Palo Alto house on the market in early August, hoping to make a quick killing after seeing a two-year buying frenzy in the area. He thought he would sell in two weeks. It took four.
"What ended up happening was that instead of getting multiple bids as we expected -- six or seven has been typical in Palo Alto -- we only got one," says Mr. Blansett, a market researcher with a Palo Alto health-care consultancy. Agents had told him to expect $540,000 to $550,000; he sold for $515,000, and to get that figure, the agent gave up part of the commission.
"What we are seeing is that Silicon Valley is very much driven by IPOs and by Nasdaq," Mr. Blansett says. "Nasdaq has been down, so everyone's options have been underwater. And the slowdown of the IPO market means that you don't have so many people wandering around with $500,000 in cash in their pockets. So we've had a lot less liquidity in the market."
Even the toniest areas back on the American Eastern seaboard are feeling pain. William Benedetto, an investment banker, a month ago looked to sell his waterfront summer home in Long Island's Hamptons for more than $1 million. But as the market has fallen off, so has the number of visitors. Last Thursday, as the Dow Industrials plunged more than 350 points, a potential buyer from Wall Street canceled a visit because, Mr. Benedetto says, he "couldn't leave" his computer screen. "Waterfront premiums had been the highest in 20 years," Mr. Benedetto says. "Now the premium may be shrinking because of the market."
For some, the pinch has been both personal and professional. Michael Cohen, a stockbroker at Robert W. Baird & Co. in Florida, planned to soup up his Harley-Davidson motorcycle, which would have cost just $1,500 to $2,000. But with his clients pulling back, Mr. Cohen sat tight on the bike. "It's the little things I might have done otherwise," he says.
What Gary Goldstein, president of Whitney Group, an executive-recruiting firm, decided to put off was a kitchen renovation of his 1790 Colonial home in Bedford, New York. The shares of his firm's parent, Headway Corporate Resources Inc., have plunged to $5.625 from $12.75 on Nasdaq, and Mr. Goldstein has a "big chunk" of his net worth in the stock. "I've lived there 10 years; there's no reason I can't wait another six months" to do the kitchen, Mr. Goldstein says.
The potential impact on corporate America is harder to quantify, but it's clear that a battered stock price, from whatever cause, disrupts a company's investment, development and, sometimes, hiring plans. Interneuron Pharmaceuticals Inc. in Lexington, Massachusetts, has faced such a battering, although it was due not so much to this weak market as to last year's withdrawal of Redux, the diet drug that Interneuron developed and American Home Products Corp. marketed. Having experienced a depressed stock price, Interneuron spokesman Bill Boni observes that "a general market downturn like this makes it difficult for small companies because it makes it difficult for investors to focus on the potential of individual products in your pipeline."
Meanwhile, the outlook has changed for some companies that have grown rapidly through acquisition, such as Conseco Inc. The financial-services and insurance company has taken over 20 companies since 1982. "We haven't been immune from this fallout," says Conseco Chairman Stephen Hilbert. "It also seems like anybody that's acquired anything has gotten killed as well."
Some acquisitive corporate executives see the decline as an opportunity. Mr. Hinckley, the Mentor Graphics COO, thinks the market upheaval will help his company's all-cash hostile takeover bid for rival Quickturn for $12.125 a share, which was recently rejected. With Quickturn at $9.8125 a share Monday on Nasdaq, Mr. Hinckley commented, "A few more days with the market going down 500 points, and it'll be looking more attractive." Quickturn, whose shares were trading at about the same price late Wednesday, declined to comment.
For some individuals, it is their company's stock that has hurt them most. Employees at Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc., who own 25% of the firm's 122 million shares outstanding, have had paper losses in the past several weeks totaling more than $1.38 billion, as their company stock -- which can make up much of their annual income -- has plunged by more than 50%. "My kids didn't have to go to college," joked William Ahearn, a Lehman spokesman, late Monday as Lehman's shares plunged 14%, or $6.3125, to $39.375, on the Big Board. By late Wednesday, the shares had clawed their way back to $43.1875.
Still, given the price at which people have been able to buy the stock, "everybody still has appreciation," Mr. Ahearn says.
At the end of a brutal Monday for the market and Lehman, Mr. Ahearn could still look on the bright side. He joked that he now would have to figure out how to pay for his recently completed vacation in West Virginia and added that with Lehman's annual stock-grant program nearing, "we'll get more shares at $45 than we would at $80."
---
Almar Latour contributed to this report.

Full text: What would a bear market be like?
Stocks came back smartly yesterday, but not before investors had gotten a troubling hint over the past few weeks of what it would mean to their lives and businesses if shares no longer were heading steadily, inexorably higher.
It was an experience new to many: The last 20% stock decline came nearly eight years ago, and the last grinding, relentless down market was 25 years back. With another long and deep decline in U.S. stocks no longer unimaginable, it is worth exploring some people's reactions to the recent chill winds.
Mark Schwartz's reaction, for instance. In last year's raging bull market, Mr. Schwartz felt so rich that he sold a $25,000 chunk of his portfolio of Israeli high-technology stocks to pay for his son's bar mitzvah.
What's left of that portfolio has since plunged. So when it came time for Mr. Schwartz to finally buy the "restored and pristine" Jaguar of his dreams, he just couldn't do it. The Bryn Mawr, Pa., lawyer figured he could make do with his 1988 Volvo, 135,000 miles and all. It wasn't just Mr. Schwartz's stocks that took a beating. So did his psyche.
Even yesterday's comeback still leaves investors as a whole about $2 trillion poorer. The Russell 2000 index of small stocks -- the kind many individual investors favor -- is off 29% since April. One result is that many people are already feeling less well off, and some are starting to pull back from the types of spending commonplace in the bull market's heyday.
And it isn't just individuals who are affected. The 1990s' rising stock prices paved the way for a record crop of entrepreneurial businesses to tap the public markets for capital, primarily through initial public stock offerings. In addition, some of the world's largest corporate mergers have taken place in the past few years, compliments of high stock prices that made deals easier to do. Finally, in corporate compensation, steadily rising share prices have made stock options a valuable part of millions of pay packages, not just for executives, but also for some lower-level employees.

The market downturn has affected Mr. Benedetto in other ways. When he offered to buy a car for his son, the third-year medical student asked for a BMW. Mr. Benedetto had another idea: a Saturn.
For some, the pinch has been personal and professional. Michael Cohen, a stockbroker at Robert W. Baird & Co. in Florida, planned to soup up his Harley-Davidson motorcycle, which would have cost just $1,500 to $2,000. But with his clients pulling back, Mr. Cohen sat tight on the bike. "It's the little things I might have done otherwise," he says.
What Gary Goldstein, president of Whitney Group, an executive-recruiting firm, decided to put off was a kitchen renovation of his 1790 Colonial home in Bedford, N.Y. The shares of his firm's parent, Headway Corporate Resources Inc., have plunged to $5.625 from $12.75 on Nasdaq, and Mr. Goldstein has a "big chunk" of his net worth in the stock. "I've lived there 10 years; there's no reason I can't wait another six months" to do the kitchen, Mr. Goldstein says.
The potential impact on corporate America is harder to quantify, but it's clear that a battered stock price, from whatever cause, disrupts a company's investment, development and, sometimes, hiring plans. Interneuron Pharmaceuticals Inc. in Lexington, Mass., has faced such a battering, although it was due not so much to this weak market as to last year's withdrawal of Redux, the diet drug that Interneuron developed and American Home Products Corp. marketed. Having experienced a depressed stock price, Interneuron spokesman Bill Boni observes that "a general market downturn like this makes it difficult for small companies because it makes it difficult for investors to focus on the potential of individual products in your pipeline."
Meanwhile, the outlook has changed for some companies that have grown rapidly through acquisition, such as Conseco Inc. The financial-services and insurance company has taken over 20 companies since 1982. "We haven't been immune from this fallout," says Conseco Chairman Stephen Hilbert. "It also seems like anybody that's acquired anything has gotten killed as well."
Some acquisitive corporate executives see the decline as an opportunity. Mr. Hinckley, the Mentor Graphics COO, thinks the market upheaval will help his company's all-cash hostile takeover bid for rival Quickturn for $12.125 a share, which was recently rejected. With Quickturn at $9.8125 a share Monday on Nasdaq, Mr. Hinckley commented, "A few more days with the market going down 500 points, and it'll be looking more attractive." Quickturn shares rebounded a bit yesterday to $9.875. The company declined to comment.
For some individuals, it is their company's stock that has hurt them most. Employees at Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc., who own 25% of the firm's 122 million shares outstanding, have had paper losses in the past several weeks totaling more than $1.38 billion, as their company stock -- which can make up much of their annual income -- has plunged by more than 50%. "My kids didn't have to go to college," quipped William Ahearn, a Lehman spokesman, late Monday as Lehman's shares plunged 14%, or $6.3125, to $39.375, on the Big Board. The shares rose 25 cents yesterday.
Still, given the price at which people have been able to buy the stock, "everybody still has appreciation," Mr. Ahearn says.
At the end of a brutal Monday for the market and Lehman, Mr. Ahearn could still look on the bright side. He joked that he now would have to figure out how to pay for his recently completed vacation in West Virginia and added that with Lehman's annual stock-grant program nearing, "we'll get more shares at $45 than we would at $80."

Are `the fundamentals' right, or the fundamentalists?: [Final Edition]

Author: Riley, Susan

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/are-fundamentals-right-fundamentalists/docview/240199347/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Are the fundamentals right? Not all economists think so; indeed, they point to evidence that productivity -- the efficiency with which we produce and export goods -- has fallen in the last five years as sporadic layoffs and downsizing continue. The right blames this on spoiled or undermotivated Canadian workers, allegedly coddled by free medicare and unemployment benefits that are still too rich. This powerful chorus calls for more cuts in government spending, more privatization, an end to minimum wage laws and other "labour market rigidities" designed to shelter people from abject misery. Indeed, Carl Sonnen of Informetrica, Ottawa-based economic consultants, believes ongoing cuts to education and health spending are contributing to the decline in productivity, not reversing it. His is a credible, if rarely-heard, challenge to current orthodoxy. As Sonnen says, a productive workforce tends to be "well-educated and healthy." In underfunding social programs, the government may be blunting our competitive edge. Pollster Frank Graves of Ekos Research says repeated soundings show Canadians think taxes are too high, but rank cuts below other priorities, like shoring up medicare and retiring the debt. For his part, Graves doesn't object to tax relief, but he rightly accuses its most ardent advocates of being "disingenuous" and "self- interested" when advancing broad-based cuts as an economic cure- all. "I'm surprised," he says, "they weren't suggested as a solution to climate change or El Nino."

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Are+%60the+fundamentals%27+right%2C+or+the+fundamentalists%3F&amp;author=Riley%2C+Susan&amp;issn=08393222&amp;title=The+Ottawa+Citizen&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-09-02&amp;spage=A.15&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The Citizen's Susan Riley writes here Wednesdays and Fridays.
All of a sudden, everything has come undone for Finance Minister Paul Martin (if not for the rest of us.) Stock markets are slumping, the loonie has reached historic lows, interest rates have gone up, growth is slowing and talk of global recession has dampened this spring's cheery predictions of yearly federal surpluses.
So far, Martin has responded with reassurances and a promise. In what has become a mantra, he insists that "the fundamentals are right" -- that our declining dollar is the victim of nervous speculators spooked by the Asian crisis, and not a judgement on Canada's economic health. And he promises more funding for health, along with tax cuts for middle-class Canadians, in his February budget.
But are the fundamentals right? Not all economists think so; indeed, they point to evidence that productivity -- the efficiency with which we produce and export goods -- has fallen in the last five years as sporadic layoffs and downsizing continue. The right blames this on spoiled or undermotivated Canadian workers, allegedly coddled by free medicare and unemployment benefits that are still too rich. This powerful chorus calls for more cuts in government spending, more privatization, an end to minimum wage laws and other "labour market rigidities" designed to shelter people from abject misery.
There is another school, less popular with newspaper editorial boards, which argues that poor productivity is as much the result of uninspired management, bad investments and lack of funding for research as the fault of a malingering workforce. Another problem, some say, is a low dollar that makes our exports automatically attractive and removes incentives to Canadian industry to modernize.
Indeed, Carl Sonnen of Informetrica, Ottawa-based economic consultants, believes ongoing cuts to education and health spending are contributing to the decline in productivity, not reversing it. His is a credible, if rarely-heard, challenge to current orthodoxy. As Sonnen says, a productive workforce tends to be "well-educated and healthy." In underfunding social programs, the government may be blunting our competitive edge.
But such complexities are lost amid the alarmist headlines, dire warnings and daily predictions of disaster. Never mind that stock markets, by their nature, rise and fall, or that the loonie's latest decline is an extension of a 15-year slide; caveats like these can ruin a good panic. And a panic, abetted by an always-eager media, provides a perfect opportunity for religious fanatics of all stripes to advance their own brand of salvation. Lately, it has been tax cuts.
The drumbeat started some years ago in the financial press, within the Reform Party caucus and in other redoubts of the right, and has now reached the finance minister's office. So eager is Martin to reward long-suffering Canadians with a tax break this winter that he is apparently ready to appropriate the Employment Insurance fund, with its tempting $6 billion surplus -- enough, Martin's hopes, to offset an expected $4 billion shortfall in anticipated federal revenues.
But will tax cuts stimulate a floundering economy, avert a recession and compensate for interest rate increases as its champions suggest? That depends on how large the tax cut is and how widely shared. Across-the-board cuts, which attract most attention - - for an ambitious finance minister, a crucial consideration -- are also the most costly. In fact, they are so costly that Martin won't likely be able to afford anything more than a token gesture -- "enough to buy a radio, but not a car" in the words of ministerial adviser Bob Brown.
Why waste money on an ineffectual tax cut? Martin has a sophisticated grasp of economics and politics; if he proceeds with a general tax cut it will be the politician speaking, although he may not be saying what people, outside of his highly-paid advisers, want to hear.
Pollster Frank Graves of Ekos Research says repeated soundings show Canadians think taxes are too high, but rank cuts below other priorities, like shoring up medicare and retiring the debt. For his part, Graves doesn't object to tax relief, but he rightly accuses its most ardent advocates of being "disingenuous" and "self- interested" when advancing broad-based cuts as an economic cure- all. "I'm surprised," he says, "they weren't suggested as a solution to climate change or El Nino."
In the end, several apparently contradictory things are true. The economic situation isn't as bad as it seems and it could get much worse. Beware of the "expert" with one bright idea. And remember: the fundamentals are right. Or are they?

Specious arguments don't reduce the real threat of global warming: [Final Edition]

Author: Bennett, John

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/specious-arguments-dont-reduce-real-threat-global/docview/240231986/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Mr. [Matthew Rees] then attempts to malign the source rather than refute the facts -- an ancient debating trick. The world's leading climate scientists, he suggests, are working with inadequate computers. It's a plot by bureaucrats and politicians to grab more power. It's promoted by "enviromantics" and "Luddites" -- heck, Al Gore's one of them. That softens up the audience for the next ploy, "the flawed half- study." The DRI study Mr. Rees quotes was designed to find the negative costs and did not look at the positive economic impacts of reducing greenhouse gas emissions. Of course, if we just raise the price of coal and oil and no one figures out how to be more efficient or how to take a advantage of worldwide boom in conservation and renewable technology etc., the economy will be devastated.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Specious+arguments+don%27t+reduce+the+real+threat+of+global+warming&amp;author=Bennett%2C+John&amp;issn=08393222&amp;title=The+Ottawa+Citizen&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-09-01&amp;spage=A.10&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Matthew Rees's Aug. 27 column, "What ever happened to global cooling?" should be clipped by debating coaches and used to help students learn how to present an argument when they draw the negative. He displays a real talent for false debate.
The question he asks about global cooling is specious, an attempt to throw the opposition off guard. Several hundred of the world's leading scientists have devoted years to studying the climate and have concluded climate change is a real threat, and that the world must act to reduce greenhouse gas emissions. Mr. Rees's sources are 20-year-old newspaper and magazine reports.
Mr. Rees then attempts to malign the source rather than refute the facts -- an ancient debating trick. The world's leading climate scientists, he suggests, are working with inadequate computers. It's a plot by bureaucrats and politicians to grab more power. It's promoted by "enviromantics" and "Luddites" -- heck, Al Gore's one of them.
That softens up the audience for the next ploy, "the flawed half- study." The DRI study Mr. Rees quotes was designed to find the negative costs and did not look at the positive economic impacts of reducing greenhouse gas emissions. Of course, if we just raise the price of coal and oil and no one figures out how to be more efficient or how to take a advantage of worldwide boom in conservation and renewable technology etc., the economy will be devastated.
There are plenty of other studies that show the cost of not acting on global warming is much higher than acting to reduce greenhouse gas emissions. Just ask the insurance industry. It reported last week that the Great Ice Storm of '98 cost four times more than any weather event in Canadian history. And just wait for the bills from Bonnie.
The next manoeuvre in a good debate is to go for the heart- strings of your audience. What would be better than cars and death? The "enviromantics" are going to stuff you into a tiny plastic car and kill you in a collision. He failed to mention that about 50,000 Americans already die in cars every year (4,000 in Canada) and about 2.5 million are injured (250,000 in Canada). Nor did he point out that reducing the size of cars is not the only way to make them more efficient and less polluting. (If a fact undermines the argument, leave it out.)
You can't leave the audience thinking about death and destruction. Say something uplifting at the end to put them in a good mood to judge the debate. So Mr. Rees tells us we are healthy, wealthy and (he implies) wise. What could be more uplifting?
He failed to mention that the air pollution is killing thousands of Americans and Canadians every year and making millions sick, and that cancer is rampant in both countries. We do suffer less from infectious disease but will we once the climate changes and subtropical diseases move north?
This "enviromantic" loves a good debate and Mr. Rees is a great debater. But the facts prove him wrong. Acting to reduce greenhouse gases will result in new economic opportunities, less air pollution, healthier people and better more livable cities. The healthy, wealthy and wise move.
John Bennett, director, energy and atmosphere, Sierra Club of Canada

Preparing for a hotter planet

Author: Thorsell, William

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/preparing-hotter-planet/docview/384601926/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: It may well get hotter still. According to the World Energy Council, annual global emissions of carbon dioxide (CO2) have risen by 9 per cent since 1990, and they will continue to rise as world population and economies grow. If we stabilized annual global CO2 emissions this year -- a very ambitious goal that is not even on the global agenda -- CO2 levels in the atmosphere would continue to rise.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Preparing+for+a+hotter+planet&amp;author=Thorsell%2C+William&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-08-29&amp;spage=D.6&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Preparing for a hotter planet
Saturday, August 29, 1998
Nineteen ninety-eight is said to be the warmest year globally in the past several centuries, with Canada's July the warmest in 50 years. Environment Canada reports that the first seven months of the year were the warmest on record in Canada (which is, however, only a short time geologically). Scientists now say that satellite measurements have understated high-altitude temperatures over the past two decades, because scientists failed to correct for orbit degradation over time. Clearly, it's warmer this year than normal and the past 20 years have been generally warmer than normal.
It may well get hotter still. According to the World Energy Council, annual global emissions of carbon dioxide (CO2) have risen by 9 per cent since 1990, and they will continue to rise as world population and economies grow. If we stabilized annual global CO2 emissions this year -- a very ambitious goal that is not even on the global agenda -- CO2 levels in the atmosphere would continue to rise.
Most scientists say that rising CO2 levels will contribute to global warming, which may well be under way for other powerful reasons. (The Earth is still emerging from an ice age, after all, and global temperatures are still well below geological averages.) The predictive quality of Earth scientists is open to dispute, but let us assume that additional CO2 will contribute to natural global warming, and that we are in for hotter times.
In 1995, the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) published a report called Impacts, Adaptation and Mitigation of Climate Change. Environment magazine examined the 728 pages of text and observed that only 32 pages were devoted to adaptation. That was perhaps understandable in a political context, where making the case for CO2 reductions was the primary goal. It is not enough now in the context of real world experience.
We need another IPCC-type study focused solely on adaptation, which could well be the pressing issue for humanity in the next 50 years. Actively preparing for global warming should not be resisted on the grounds that it suggests complacency about humanity's contribution to greenhouse gases. In fact, the more visibly we prepare for the possibility of global warming, the greater the political constituency may be for possibly mitigating it through actual CO2 reductions.
According to the sketchy 1995 IPCC report, the greatest impact is likely to be on agriculture in equatorial zones. This suggests an analysis of alternatives to current crops in hot climes, and the development of food supply systems for highly exposed peoples, most of whom are poor.
Rising sea levels caused by warmer water (which expands with heat) and melting glaciers would threaten many coastal areas, including places such as New York City, London and the Netherlands, not to mention Bangladesh and many tropical islands. Adaptation strategies would have to range from dike protection (as exists already across the Thames in London) to orderly abandonment (bye bye Greenwich Village). Insurance coverage, land-use regulation and environmental rules need to be reviewed. Should we allow people to build in newly identified flood-prone spots, or to buy insurance if they do?
The implications for human health range from expanding malaria territories to safer winter conditions in northern climes. Vaccination and sanitation programs will need to be reconsidered. Vast public-health information programs will be required if the planet really does heat up.
Warmer climates will lead to expanding ranges for certain flora and fauna. It has been suggested in North America that natural corridors be created to allow for the orderly migration north of tree species and animals. University agriculture faculties might well undertake research on the speedy conversion of boreal soils to food production, which would help offset agricultural losses in the tropics.
We need analyses of potential water and electricity use in light of warmer temperatures, even in places such as Canada which have an abundance of both. More irrigation, air conditioning and pressure to export water are likely in the face of hotter summers.
Some predict that tens of millions of refugees in the tropics will attempt to emigrate north, as their arable soil and the land itself erodes. This could lead to considerable conflict in neighbouring states and moral dilemmas for everyone else.
While much work is done on potential adaptation strategies, we also need much more work on the economic consequences and choices that come with serious commitments to CO2 reduction. It may or may not be relevant, but the vagueness about the effects of possible CO2 reduction strategies almost equals that about the scope of possible adaptations to much more temperate climes.
The intellectual work on what to do about what may be inevitable in global warming has barely begun.

  

E-mail: wthorsell@globeandmail.ca

New tax on energy could raise pounds 5bn: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Brown, Colin

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/new-tax-on-energy-could-raise-pounds-5bn/docview/312720855/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: A "GREEN" windfall tax looks set is to be levied on the gas and electricity generators, with the aim of raising prices to force industry to cut excessive energy use and cut harmful emissions. Some Whitehall insiders estimate the Chancellor could raise pounds 5bn in an energy tax which could be unveiled in the Chancellor's pre- budget statement in the autumn. Ministers said the aim of the tax was to reduce the waste of energy, not to raise revenue for the Treasury, but it could give the Chancellor a bonus for the general election campaign.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=New+tax+on+energy+could+raise+pounds+5bn&amp;author=Brown%2C+Colin&amp;issn=09519467&amp;title=The+Independent&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-08-25&amp;spage=4&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: A "GREEN" windfall tax looks set is to be levied on the gas and electricity generators, with the aim of raising prices to force industry to cut excessive energy use and cut harmful emissions.
Some Whitehall insiders estimate the Chancellor could raise pounds 5bn in an energy tax which could be unveiled in the Chancellor's pre- budget statement in the autumn.
Ministers said the aim of the tax was to reduce the waste of energy, not to raise revenue for the Treasury, but it could give the Chancellor a bonus for the general election campaign.
"It is big money and it is implementing our Kyoto targets on {carbon dioxide}, making industry more efficient and giving them incentives to cut waste in energy," said a ministerial source.
Whitehall sources said the Chancellor was planning to offset the extra burden on industry by offering tax breaks, but it will still mean that some companies will face higher charges, which they may have to pass on to their customers, unless stopped from doing so by the regulators.
The tax breaks could include cuts in National Insurance contributions for industries hit by the tax to make it cheaper to hire workers, and to prevent the move contributing to a rise in unemployment.
Big users of energy, such as the steel industry, who are already efficient, could be given special exemptions. Officials believe the main targets will be middle-range firms, heavy users of gas and electricity who have room for more efficiency.
The appointment of Sir Colin Marshall, the President of the Confederation of British Industry, to head the review of an energy tax, announced in last year's budget, was seen by the "green" lobby as a signal that big business would be able to head off the threat of a tax, when it was announced in the Budget by the Chancellor.
But although Sir Colin's task-force of Whitehall officials has yet to finish its report, Whitehall insiders have told The Independent that Sir Colin's review is expected to come down firmly in favour of an energy tax on industry.
"If we had appointed somebody like Jonathon Porritt from Friends of the Earth, business would never have bought it, but Sir Colin will be able to sell it to big business," said a senior ministerial source. "His appointment makes it more likely, not less likely, that there will be a big industry energy tax."
Ministers believe Mr Brown will go ahead with the tax in his next Budget in the spring, next year. It would be imposed on the generators, making it easy for the Government to collect, and automatically passed to big industrial users of energy. "The idea is to get industry to be much more efficient in the way it uses energy," said the source.
The Government could be accused of breaking its election pledge to make the windfall tax on the privatised a "one-off" to fund its welfare- to-work programme. But Mr Brown said in the Budget that the task-force, bringing together people from industry and Whitehall, "shows that the Government and the private sector can achieve more working together".
In June, Sir Colin launched a consultation paper on the idea of an energy tax, saying there was a "need for industry and commerce to make a contribution towards tackling climate change".
The closing date for submissions was the end of July. The task force is also looking at a trading scheme in energy or carbon emissions, which would enable firms to "buy" permits from competitors.
At the Kyoto conference last year, the European Union committed itself to a reduction in emissions of greenhouse gases of 8 per cent below 1990 levels by 2008 to 2012. The UK has its own target of a 20 per cent cut in carbon dioxide emissions on 1990 levels by 2010.

Leaving a Legacy: As Clinton Testifies, Another Jury Weighs His Place in History --- Scholars Have Become Kinder To Less-Activist Leaders, But Scandals Will Hurt --- A New Model: Ulysses Grant

Author: By Jackie Calmes

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/leaving-legacy-as-clinton-testifies-another-jury/docview/398693759/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Mr. Clinton has often looked to past presidents for models. At his first inaugural, he echoed Franklin Roosevelt's call for "bold, persistent experimentation." Two years later, confronted with the first Republican Congress in 40 years, he turned to "Give 'em hell" Harry Truman. By his re-election, he was recalling Theodore Roosevelt, who made his mark in a time of peace at the turn of the last century. Yet perhaps it is Mr. Clinton's fabled luck that historians these days seem to be taking a kinder view of chief executives who managed America rather than changed it. Like Dwight Eisenhower, or even "Silent Cal" Coolidge, the subject of a two-day re-examination at the John F. Kennedy Library in Boston last month. The irony of Mr. Clinton's quest for a legacy is that his best hope may be to take a place among these GOP presidents, remembered less for their activism than for their quiet handling of prosperous times. That's not to say Mr. Clinton hasn't been active. He helped balance the budget after three decades of red ink, promoted free trade, preached eloquently from the bully pulpit for racial healing, overhauled welfare and steered his party's politics to the middle. But experience and defeat, chiefly on health-care reform, chastened him. He has sought an activism within limits, and the FDR echoes gave way to Mr. Clinton's utterance that "the era of big government is over."

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Leaving+a+Legacy%3A+As+Clinton+Testifies%2C+Another+Jury+Weighs+His+Place+in+History+---+Scholars+Have+Become+Kinder+To+Less-Activist+Leaders%2C+But+Scandals+Will+Hurt+---+A+New+Model%3A+Ulysses+Grant&amp;author=By+Jackie+Calmes&amp;issn=00999660&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-08-17&amp;spage=A.1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: WASHINGTON -- Today, Bill Clinton makes history. But it isn't the kind of history he had hoped for.
Mr. Clinton has often looked to past presidents for models. At his first inaugural, he echoed Franklin Roosevelt's call for "bold, persistent experimentation." Two years later, confronted with the first Republican Congress in 40 years, he turned to "Give 'em hell" Harry Truman. By his re-election, he was recalling Theodore Roosevelt, who made his mark in a time of peace at the turn of the last century.
More recently, as the Monica Lewinsky affair swirled about him, he mused that scandal-tarred Ulysses S. Grant had gotten "a bum rap."
In advance of Mr. Clinton's grand-jury testimony today, some advisers intimated that he might revise his prior account of his relationship with Ms. Lewinsky, though his attorney dismissed that as "groundless speculation."
How will history view William Jefferson Clinton? Will he be remembered more for today's role as the first president to testify before a grand jury in a criminal case? Or will other accomplishments transcend the scandals that dog him? With two years left in his second term, few historians want to hazard a judgment. For any president, it's agreed, verdicts are best left to later generations.
But it is clear that Mr. Clinton hasn't been the Rooseveltian activist he once hoped to be. And history's judges have long been partial to doers and crisis managers. Peace and prosperity, ever the stuff of campaign promises, don't lend themselves to great deeds and legacy-building.
"Clinton's great problem is, he came in with high aspirations, [but he] is in a period where no one particularly wants to do very much, and he has a Republican Congress to deal with," says Arthur Schlesinger Jr., who, along with his late father, did much to define the activist yardstick by which presidents have been judged for a half-century.
Yet perhaps it is Mr. Clinton's fabled luck that historians these days seem to be taking a kinder view of chief executives who managed America rather than changed it. Like Dwight Eisenhower, or even "Silent Cal" Coolidge, the subject of a two-day re-examination at the John F. Kennedy Library in Boston last month. The irony of Mr. Clinton's quest for a legacy is that his best hope may be to take a place among these GOP presidents, remembered less for their activism than for their quiet handling of prosperous times.
That's not to say Mr. Clinton hasn't been active. He helped balance the budget after three decades of red ink, promoted free trade, preached eloquently from the bully pulpit for racial healing, overhauled welfare and steered his party's politics to the middle. But experience and defeat, chiefly on health-care reform, chastened him. He has sought an activism within limits, and the FDR echoes gave way to Mr. Clinton's utterance that "the era of big government is over."
For years, critics have complained of a liberal bias in scholars' presidential ratings, with insufficient credit given to those who simply managed things or stopped bad ideas. Historian Richard Norton Smith, a self-described Rockefeller Republican, and Herbert Hoover's friendly biographer as well as George Washington's, calls the notion of liberal bias "simplistic." But he says there has been a bias -- for "conflict and drama centralized in Washington." The 20th-century judges favor "the activist president, the visionary, the man who personalizes the office as he centralizes power, to overawe Congress and set the national agenda, and to use the media."
Mr. Smith longs for "a pre-New Deal presidential model," and suggests that "a new consensus may be emerging." After Vietnam and Watergate, historians began rethinking their romance with strong presidents. In Richard Nixon and Lyndon Johnson, they saw the dangers of the "imperial presidency." As the century wanes, scholars have developed more appreciation for the checks and balances provided by a strong Congress, and for the import of actions beyond Washington.
Still, in judging legacies, Mr. Smith and other conservatives generally come to the same bottom-line conclusions about past presidents' relative standing as have liberals like the Schlesingers.
"If a president doesn't do things, he doesn't make much of a mark," says Mr. Schlesinger. "That doesn't necessarily mean an extension of government or presidential authority. It means the ability to rise to a challenge."
First, of course, a president must face a challenge -- the bigger, the better. And in times like the present, the challenges may defy greatness. Lincoln biographer David Herbert Donald recalls that, during a 1962 visit to the White House, John Kennedy asked him "if it's necessary to have a war to be a great president. I was stunned, because he was after all the president and he wanted to be a great president."
Mr. Kennedy's predecessor, Mr. Eisenhower, has suffered as much as any president against the activist yardstick, despite his contemporary popularity and his two terms. Yet perhaps no president has so benefited from time's perspective, and from the yardstick's reassessment.
Contrast Mr. Schlesinger Sr.'s 1962 update of his pioneering presidential poll, which first appeared in Life magazine 50 years ago, with his son's update two years ago. Mr. Eisenhower went from 22nd among the 31 presidents rated in 1962, a year after leaving office, to 14th of the 39 presidents rated in 1996. In other recent surveys, he rates even better. His presidential papers had become available, showing historians a more engaged figure behind the scenes than the semi-retired First Duffer of public image.
Recently, the president and historians alike have paid new attention to the so-called forgotten men of the late 19th century. These caretaker presidents - -men like Grant and Rutherford B. Hayes -- are a blur between Lincoln and Theodore Roosevelt; most served one term or less, and were overshadowed by Congresses after the Civil War. But, of interest to Mr. Clinton, their end-of-century Gilded Age parallels the present for being a time of economic transition and melting-pot pressures.
Asked late last year about his own legacy, Mr. Clinton proposed this epitaph: "He had to make America work in a new world." The president predicted scholars would ask: "Did I help America transform itself so that we would still be the greatest nation in the world in a global economy" while uniting "the most . . . diverse population in our history?"
For many in the Clinton circle, the hand-lettered sign from his 1992 campaign headquarters -- "It's the Economy, Stupid" -- serves just as well as an answer to speculation about his legacy. History's judgment, of course, depends on a continued good economy, and on Mr. Clinton's getting credit for it.
His claim rests on the 1993 deficit-reduction package pushed through over unanimous GOP opposition, and the 1993 North American Free Trade Agreement approved over his own party's objections; his veto of the 1995 GOP plan for tax and social-spending cuts; and his negotiation of the 1997 budget compromise. Before Mr. Clinton, says political adviser Rahm Emanuel, "America had economically lost its zap and bounce. We had come to accept the deficit and debt as a fait accompli."
With the aim of helping Americans in the transition from a factory-based economy to the information age, Mr. Clinton has also sought, within severe budget constraints, to expand higher-education opportunities, put computers in schools and provide tax credits and familyleave policies to ease working families' pressures. The first president elected since the Cold War was won, Mr. Clinton also has taken a more interventionist role in foreign affairs than suggested by his 1992 campaign, during which he attacked President Bush as being preoccupied with foreign affairs.
In Mr. Schlesinger's introduction to his 1996 survey, he wrote that the president was poised between greatness and humiliation. "It's still a toss-up," the historian says. Since that survey, Mr. Clinton has won re-election and reached last year's balanced-budget agreement with the GOP Congress. The president who failed disastrously in 1994 to pass universal health insurance won laws expanding uninsured children's coverage and protecting insurance for some workers who lose or change jobs. As much as anything, that contrast illustrated Mr. Clinton's adjustment to a more limited activism.
But he will be hard-put to add to his achievements in his final two years. Second terms are traditionally frustrating, and the parties already are facing off for the 2000 election. He is distracted and weakened by scandal.
That has taken its toll on his agenda, despite the public's continued high job-approval ratings. Congressional Republicans have buried his major tobacco bill, which promised billions in future revenues for health and other domestic programs. They've ignored his proposal to expand Medicare for those approaching retirement age. And they are gunning for his domestic-spending initiatives.
What's more, the issues Mr. Clinton has staked out for the remainder of his term -- "saving" Social Security, and action against climate change -- are huge challenges.
Mr. Clinton's aides argue that his plans to overhaul Social Security and head off its insolvency when baby boomers retire could earn him a measure of greatness. "This would be a historic thing, to take a major problem like this and do something about it before it actually happens," says Martha Phillips, executive director of the nonpartisan budget watchdog Concord Coalition, which is helping coordinate Mr. Clinton's public-education effort on the issue. From top administration officials, Mrs. Phillips says, "I get every sense they're serious about this."
A serious effort against global warming would also be a major undertaking, requiring emissions reductions that could result in costly adjustments for industry and consumers. That is one reason most analysts believe the administration's globalwarming push will result more in rhetoric than action.
Moreover, the politics of either global warming or Social Security hold big risks for another of Mr. Clinton's hoped-for legacies-getting Vice President Al Gore elected, and thus extending the "New Democrat" coalition. Mr. Gore's victory is the necessary first step to proving whether Mr. Clinton's centrist formula -- picked up even by some moderate Republican governors and foreign leaders such as Britain's Tony Blair -- has staying power.
Whatever happens, as Mr. Clinton must know by his ruminations on Mr. Grant, scandal is always an albatross. In the Grant case, guilt was by association with corrupt cronies, not personal malfeasance. Mr. Nixon's disgrace, making him the only president to resign, is in a class by itself. Pop history already provides a warning to Mr. Clinton. Writer Nathan Miller, making changes as his book "Star-Spangled Men: America's Ten Worst Presidents" goes to paperback publication, weighed whether to include Bill Clinton in William Howard Taft's place. But he decided "it's premature. The jury is still out -- both literally and figuratively."
Mr. Clinton's case, with its unprecedented focus on sexual relationships, breaks uncharted ground. "There aren't any lessons from the past because, let's face it, we've defined scandal down," says Brookings Institution scholar Thomas Mann. "We don't have a clue as to whether, 30 years from now, people will look back on this as a host of trivial matters that were lent stature by a law for an independent counsel -- and by a political culture that acted in a crazy and irrational way -- or whether in fact something consequential comes out."
One Clinton legacy could be a counterreaction to the way the country approaches corruption allegations. Columbia University historian Alan Brinkley has decried "the institutionalization of scandal," owed to the post-Watergate law that triggers appointment of independent counsels and the permanence of Congress's self-appointed ethics committees. Many predict that the independent-counsel law will be altered or repealed.
However Washington's scandal culture might change, Mr. Clinton's record will have to stand on its own. It's "a credible record" so far, says Mr. Donald, the Lincoln biographer. "The question is whether he has so undercut his moral authority, or his enemies have, that he can no longer speak for the American people."
At work on another Lincoln book, Mr. Donald stays immersed in the life of the president most often deemed the nation's greatest, only to shift daily to the tawdry news from the current White House. But, he says, "I go through the New York Herald of the 1860s, and there's hardly a day you don't read Lincoln's a fool, a charlatan, a baboon, and worse."
So, as for Mr. Clinton, he says, "I like to reserve judgment."
