Commentary; COLUMN LEFT/ MOLLY IVINS; Debate Dries Up on Global Warming; The media got snookered into blaming only El Nino for the Texas heat and drought.: [Home Edition]

Author: Ivins, Molly

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/commentary-column-left-molly-ivins-debate-dries/docview/421414781/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: As Texas endures the slow, agonizing death of our entire agricultural sector by drought, a check of our media and political leaders shows we are also suffering from a bizarre silence on a topic that could be described as "the cause that dare not speak its name." Of the few articles on the subject, all are limited to the answer "El Nino," which is half right. According to climatologists, this is an El Nino drought: El Nino shifted the jet stream just enough to hold the high that normally sits over the Rockies east over Texas, so we are not getting the clouds and cooling that normally give us some relief. But the other half of the answer, global warming, has gotten little or no attention. The media are doing so poorly on this issue that it's an embarrassment to the profession, and we are being hoist partly by the petard of our infamous "objectivity." We continue to report global warming as though it were a "debate" among scientists. It is not.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Commentary%3B+COLUMN+LEFT%2F+MOLLY+IVINS%3B+Debate+Dries+Up+on+Global+Warming%3B+The+media+got+snookered+into+blaming+only+El+Nino+for+the+Texas+heat+and+drought.&amp;author=Ivins%2C+Molly&amp;issn=04583035&amp;title=Los+Angeles+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-08-16&amp;spage=5&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: As Texas endures the slow, agonizing death of our entire agricultural sector by drought, a check of our media and political leaders shows we are also suffering from a bizarre silence on a topic that could be described as "the cause that dare not speak its name."
Local newspapers have responded heroically to the heat wave that has now killed more than 120 Texans, unleashing a torrent of community efforts to help those most in peril, keeping people informed of water shortages and conservation plans. The one topic they have not addressed is: Why is this happening?
Of the few articles on the subject, all are limited to the answer "El Nino," which is half right. According to climatologists, this is an El Nino drought: El Nino shifted the jet stream just enough to hold the high that normally sits over the Rockies east over Texas, so we are not getting the clouds and cooling that normally give us some relief. But the other half of the answer, global warming, has gotten little or no attention.
The media are doing so poorly on this issue that it's an embarrassment to the profession, and we are being hoist partly by the petard of our infamous "objectivity." We continue to report global warming as though it were a "debate" among scientists. It is not.
(Actually, there is a debate among scientists on global warming, but it is over exactly how much and how fast it is happening, not whether it exists.)
What we mistake for a "debate" is actually a public relations campaign by the American Petroleum Institute, which has recruited and funded a few scientists who question the entire phenomenon. They, in turn, are given equal weight by the media, as though they were precisely as objective as the 2,500 scientists who work with the United Nations' Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change.
According to USA Today, when 14 energy industry lobbyists gathered in April to work out the details of a $6-million lobbying plan on global warming, they targeted Congress, the news media, the public and schoolchildren. "Informing teachers/students about uncertainties in climate science will begin to erect a barrier against further efforts to impose Kyoto-like measures in the future," says a memo from the meeting obtained by the National Environmental Trust.
The notion that the U.N. panel is some group of fear-mongering enviros is easily disproved by study of any of its cautious work or the testimony of its chairman, Robert T. Watson. On the other hand, the petroleum institute's PR campaign is designed, in the words of its own strategy documents, to "reposition global warming as theory rather than fact." And it has been quite successful.
In addition, a number of conservative think tanks have been churning out dubious studies allegedly proving that doing much of anything about global warming will cost each and every citizen a small fortune and "radically" affect all our lives. These studies have been given solemn coverage by the press.
Perhaps the funniest media blooper so far was to bite on what purported to be a petition sponsored by the Oregon Institute of Science and Medicine and signed by "16,000 scientists," all of whom agree that global warming is hooey. It turns out that the institute is a small outfit in Cave Junction, Ore., run by a biochemist who specializes in home schooling and nuclear shelters. The petition was put on the Internet; anyone who claimed to be a scientist could "sign." Among the 16,000 "scientists" were the real name of Ginger Spice of the Spice Girls, Dr. B.J. Hunnicut of "MASH" and a raft of other patently ridiculous names, none of which were checked.
Among the most important developments this year is the formation of a coalition of major companies--including Sun Co., 3M, British Petroleum, Lockheed, Maytag, United Technologies, Boeing--that not only accept climate change as a serious threat but also believe that action is necessary and can be taken without economic damage. Of course, the one industry with the largest stake in all this is insurance, which has taken the problem seriously for years. According to Ross Gelbspan, author of "The Heat Is On," insurance losses in the 1980s averaged $2 billion a year; in the 1990s, they have been averaging close to $12 billion a year.
Meanwhile, the Republican Party of Texas has adopted the flat statement: "We oppose the theory of global warming and the Kyoto agreement." That certainly takes care of that as far as Texas Republicans are concerned.
Credit: Molly Ivins is a columnist for the Fort Worth Star-Telegram

Subject: Global warming

Why Is Texas Drying Up? It's Global Warming, Stupid

Author: Molly Ivins FORT WORTH STAR-TELEGRAM

Full text:   AUSTIN, Texas -- As Texas endures the slow, agonizing death of our entire agricultural sector by drought, a check of our media and political leaders shows we are also suffering from a bizarre silence on a topic that could be described as "the cause that dare not speak its name."
Local newspapers have responded heroically to the heat wave that has now killed more than 120 Texans, unleashing a torrent of community efforts to help those most in peril, keeping people informed of water shortages and conservation plans, and praising the emergency and medical workers who are saving lives. In addition, a number of conservative think tanks have been churning out dubious studies allegedly proving that doing much of anything about global warming will cost each and every citizen a small fortune and "radically" affect all our lives. The petition was put on the Internet; anyone who claimed to be a scientist could "sign" and be counted. Enviros soon caught on, and among the 16,000 "scientists," they added, among others, the real name of Ginger Spice of the Spice Girls, Dr. B.J. Hunnicut of "MASH," actor Michael J. Fox and a raft of other patently ridiculous names, none of which were checked.

GLOBAL WARMING NEEDS ACTION; POWER COMPANIES MUST WORK TO SAVE ENVIRONMENT

Author: Freeman, S David

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-warming-needs-action-power-companies-must/docview/281790548/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: S. David Freeman is the general manager of the Los Angeles Department of Water and Power. Write to him in care of Daily News Opinions, P.O. Box 4200, Woodland Hills, CA 91365-4200. SMACK in the middle of the hottest days of summer, it is easy to dismiss the headline-stealing heat wave as just another blast of hot air. But mounting evidence suggests that the incredible thermometer readings may be a sneak preview of future weather patterns. Carbon dioxide, the principal greenhouse gas, is produced by the burning of fossil fuels. Carbon dioxide in the air does indeed create a greenhouse effect, and without it, the Earth would experience Ice Age-type conditions with an average temperature some 60 degrees cooler than it is today.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=GLOBAL+WARMING+NEEDS+ACTION%3B+POWER+COMPANIES+MUST+WORK+TO+SAVE+ENVIRONMENT&amp;author=Freeman%2C+S+David&amp;issn=&amp;title=Daily+News&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-08-16&amp;spage=V.3&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   S. David Freeman is the general manager of the Los Angeles Department of Water and Power. Write to him in care of Daily News Opinions, P.O. Box 4200, Woodland Hills, CA 91365-4200.
SMACK in the middle of the hottest days of summer, it is easy to dismiss the headline-stealing heat wave as just another blast of hot air. But mounting evidence suggests that the incredible thermometer readings may be a sneak preview of future weather patterns.
The phenomenon known as global warming is very real, and we need to recognize the danger and make out an "insurance policy" to cover the risk.
Warmer and extreme weather, a measured rise in the sea level, and increasing threats on natural ecosystems worldwide forecast a troubling future for the planet unless bold, affirmative steps are taken to reduce the emission of heat-trapping greenhouse gases into our atmosphere.
I call attention to this problem as a utility executive whose power system is part of the problem but is moving to "come clean."
Carbon dioxide, the principal greenhouse gas, is produced by the burning of fossil fuels. Carbon dioxide in the air does indeed create a greenhouse effect, and without it, the Earth would experience Ice Age-type conditions with an average temperature some 60 degrees cooler than it is today.
The concern is that we are making the greenhouse stronger and the earth hotter. Concentrations have increased 30 percent since pre- industrial times. Now, scientists using leading-edge computer models estimate that carbon dioxide emissions could double by 2100, producing the highest level of CO2 in 50 million years.
"Global warming is a relatively small change from year to year, but it is always pushing in one direction," Kevin Trenberth, one of the government's leading researchers studying climate change, said recently.
Studies show that the 20th century has been the warmest in 600 years - with the 1990s the warmest decade, and 1997 the single warmest year. With greenhouse gases blanketing Earth at current rates, the United Nations Inter-governmental Panel on Climate Change projects that global temperatures could rise 2 to 6 degrees Fahrenheit in the next 100 years - a change sufficient enough to trigger extreme conditions, including the melting of the huge west Antarctic ice sheer.
Other impacts could include reduced crop yields; an erosion of coastal land mass; increased health hazards from heat stress to respiratory illness; and degradation of the world's forests.
A worst-case glimpse of the potential future impact of global warming is this year's heat-related devastation in Texas. Three weeks of consecutive 100-degree days has caused nearly 100 deaths and over $1 billion in crop damage. In Florida, drought-fed fires ravaged the terrain, contributing to billion-dollar losses in that state. Agricultural officials are issuing disaster declarations throughout the southwest United States. Even in the usual temperature climate of the Pacific Northwest, thermometers have topped 108 degrees in places including Walla Walla, Washington.
No one is saying this is the smoking gun that proves global warming is a killer, but it sure looks like it could be. And the scientific evidence indicates that rising levels of carbon dioxide are causing bad weather to become worse and more frequent.
Global warming is a real risk that is here and now, and the responsibility for reversing the current course rests with us all. The utility industry, which is a major contributor to global warming, should take an active leadership role in promoting and implementing programs to protect the global environment. I intend to do just that.
For starters, the industry should make investments now to reduce its reliance on coal-fired generation plants in favor of green power alternatives such as solar, wind and hydropower. As the nation's largest municipal utility, drawing nearly 60 percent of its electricity from coal-fired power plants in the western U.S., the Los Angeles Department of Water and Power is poised to become a leader in the use of these more environmentally friendly generation options.
We are proposing a green power program for consumers to pay $3-$5 a month to fund new clean energy. This slight increase can be more than offset by cash credits which will be offered for customers who use efficient appliances.
Utilities should all step up their programs to promote energy efficiency. The "Cool Schools" program in Los Angeles is one such example. The DWP is planting 4,000 hardy, shade-producing trees around school buildings at 42 Los Angeles Unified School District campuses to reduce energy use for air conditioning. The environmental benefit is reduced carbon dioxide emissions and improved air quality.
The DWP also is distributing energy-efficient appliances, such as refrigerators and washing machines, to low-income residents and providing rebates to other customers who purchase these energy efficient products.
Utility executives should do more than just loosen their ties and oppose regulations in dealing with global warming. The industry can drive market demand for environmentally friendly products and programs, such as electric cars and buses, residential solar energy systems, highly advanced energy efficient appliances and widespread tree planting.
The industry has a responsibility to voluntarily phase down its use of polluting coal-fired generation plants. Utilities that oppose regulations to reduce global warming should be mandated to do so.
While these solutions do not have the immediate cooling effect of an ice-cold glass of lemonade on a hot summer day, they do represent leadership that will insure against the very real risk of damage that may be far greater than we can imagine.

Subject: Global warming

Global warming lost in Texas heat wave shuffle

Author: Ivins, Molly

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-warming-lost-texas-heat-wave-shuffle/docview/330990576/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Of the few articles on the subject, all are limited to the answer "El Nino," which is half right. But the other half of the answer, global warming, has gotten little or no attention. An unusual amount of hot air, say experts on both meteorology and politics." Heh-heh. Hot World, Cooler Growth Can humans progress and still preserve the home planet? A hot summer, oil merger, and Asian slump focus debate: [ALL Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/hot-world-cooler-growth-can-humans-progress-still/docview/405655866/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Econ- and Eco- are the sound-alike prefixes scholars and politicians love to affix to their new millennium pronouncements. Broadly, the two are set against each other. They're the perceived bookends of mankind's future. Economic growth (read improved living standards) supposedly causes ecologic decline (read hotter, less green, fewer species). Conversely, eco-preservation means econo- decline. Pop wisdom says you can have one or the other, not both.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Hot+World%2C+Cooler+Growth+Can+humans+progress+and+still+preserve+the+home+planet%3F+A+hot+summer%2C+oil+merger%2C+and+Asian+slump+focus+debate&amp;author=&amp;issn=08827729&amp;title=The+Christian+Science+Monitor&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-08-13&amp;spage=12&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Econ- and Eco- are the sound-alike prefixes scholars and politicians love to affix to their new millennium pronouncements.
Broadly, the two are set against each other. They're the perceived bookends of mankind's future. Economic growth (read improved living standards) supposedly causes ecologic decline (read hotter, less green, fewer species). Conversely, eco-preservation means econo- decline. Pop wisdom says you can have one or the other, not both.
We refuse to buy that equation.
This subject snaps into focus at the moment, not because of millennium chatter but because of three global events that demand more than usual attention:
1. The hottest summer month on record plus other climate anomalies.
2. Asian slump II. Spreading concern that Asia's slowdown is, after all, going to affect people and businesses everywhere.
3. The giant BP-Amoco oil company merger, and what it says about future fuel supplies, costs, greenhouse gases, and green energy alternatives.
The three, taken together, constitute a powerful argument for the mouth-full concept of "sustainable development." Those readers already well versed in what this means may skip a paragraph.
Sustainable development broadly describes an array of steps that would allow billions of hungry, resource-poor, shelter-deprived, education-poor, overworked or unemployed people to gain an acceptable standard of living through economic growth, while still preserving our planet's ecosystems in a self-sustaining balance.
In practical terms this means redesigning thousands of industrial processes to make them pollution reduced, if not pollution-free. It means designing transport (cars, motorcycles, tractors) whose exhaust is cleaner than their air intake. Ditto, heating and cooling. Ditto, chlorine-compound insecticides, herbicides, and household products.
Skeptics base their scenario of inevitable econ-eco collision on what they see as either (1) the technical impossibility of, or (2) the lack of human focus on, achieving sustainable development. We said we refuse to buy that skepticism. We do so because we have faith in mankind's ability to solve problems once fully alerted to them.
The hottest summer weather since the advent of record keeping and the spread of Asia's slump constitute a vigorous alert. BP's giant leap in influence on petroleum exploration and use is an interesting move on the sustainability front.
BP's CEO, Sir John Browne, made a splash two years ago when he announced at his alma mater, Stanford, that he was pushing his firm to do a billion dollars worth of business in solar energy devices by 2000. He continues to parley with Greenpeace and other environmental organizations in an effort to find common ground on sustainable, environmentally sound growth. And he's not the only captain of industry seeking to profit from shifting world industry to sustainable processes - in cooperation with governments, universities, and groups espousing both the environment and human betterment in food, housing, and work conditions.
Let's be blunt and realistic. Such sustainable development and better living standards will need petroleum and the steel, plastics, power, fertilizer, and the other output oil supports into the foreseeable future. But the uses to which such fossil fuels are put will have to be cleaned up as noted above. That means far greater efficiency of manufacturing, heat/cooling, transport, so as to curb toxic output and steadily reduce greenhouse gas buildup. It also means more urgency on shifting to alternative energy technology.
Is this hot-tin-roof summer certifiable evidence of global climate change? Not definitively. But growing more likely. Obviously climate records dating only from 1870 tell us little. But deep glacier samples, ancient tree rings, suboceanic core samples and other evidence warrant caution. And that caution should take the form of a sensible global insurance policy.
If we assume, as economic logic urges, that the Asian-slump spread is temporary and that the desire for a better life throughout the developing world persists, we must prepare for renewed worldwide growth.
That, surely, is a goad to speeding research on sustainable processes in every area of manufacturing, transport, construction, and general energy use. It surely is a goad, also, to leaders of industry, environmental groups, politicians, universities, and developing nations' human rights advocates to work cooperatively in the fashion gingerly explored by BP's Browne and Greenpeace's Lord Peter Melchett.
Many such leaders are already quietly engaged in cooperation based on research and global realities. That, not the political cliche, is the true bridge to the 21st century.
Growth, better standards of living, and an ecologically stable planet require rivals to cooperate.

Hot World, Cooler Growth Can humans progress and still preserve the home planet? A hot summer, oil merger, and Asian slump focus debate: [ALL Edition 1]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/hot-world-cooler-growth-can-humans-progress-still/docview/405655399/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: BP's CEO, Sir John Browne, made a splash two years ago when he announced at his alma mater, Stanford, that he was pushing his firm to do a billion dollars worth of business in solar energy devices by 2000. He continues to parley with Greenpeace and other environmental organizations in an effort to find common ground on sustainable, environmentally sound growth. And he's not the only captain of industry seeking to profit from shifting world industry to sustainable processes - in cooperation with governments, universities, and groups espousing both the environment and human betterment in food, housing, and work conditions. Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Hot+World%2C+Cooler+Growth+Can+humans+progress+and+still+preserve+the+home+planet%3F+A+hot+summer%2C+oil+merger%2C+and+Asian+slump+focus+debate&amp;author=&amp;issn=08827729&amp;title=The+Christian+Science+Monitor&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-08-13&amp;spage=12&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Econ- and Eco- are the sound-alike prefixes scholars and politicians love to affix to their new millennium pronouncements.
Broadly, the two are set against each other. They're the perceived bookends of mankind's future. Economic growth (read improved living standards) supposedly causes ecologic decline (read hotter, less green, fewer species). Conversely, eco-preservation means econo- decline. Pop wisdom says you can have one or the other, not both.
We refuse to buy that equation.
This subject snaps into focus at the moment, not because of millennium chatter but because of three global events that demand more than usual attention:
1. The hottest summer month on record plus other climate anomalies.
2. Asian slump II. Spreading concern that Asia's slowdown is, after all, going to affect people and businesses everywhere.
3. The giant BP-Amoco oil company merger, and what it says about future fuel supplies, costs, greenhouse gases, and green energy alternatives.
The three, taken together, constitute a powerful argument for the mouth-full concept of "sustainable development." Those readers already well versed in what this means may skip a paragraph.
Sustainable development broadly describes an array of steps that would allow billions of hungry, resource-poor, shelter-deprived, education-poor, overworked or unemployed people to gain an acceptable standard of living through economic growth, while still preserving our planet's ecosystems in a self-sustaining balance.
In practical terms this means redesigning thousands of industrial processes to make them pollution reduced, if not pollution-free. It means designing transport (cars, motorcycles, tractors) whose exhaust is cleaner than their air intake. Ditto, heating and cooling. Ditto, chlorine-compound insecticides, herbicides, and household products.
Skeptics base their scenario of inevitable econ-eco collision on what they see as either (1) the technical impossibility of, or (2) the lack of human focus on, achieving sustainable development. We said we refuse to buy that skepticism. We do so because we have faith in mankind's ability to solve problems once fully alerted to them.
The hottest summer weather since the advent of record keeping and the spread of Asia's slump constitute a vigorous alert. BP's giant leap in influence on petroleum exploration and use is an interesting move on the sustainability front.
BP's CEO, Sir John Browne, made a splash two years ago when he announced at his alma mater, Stanford, that he was pushing his firm to do a billion dollars worth of business in solar energy devices by 2000. He continues to parley with Greenpeace and other environmental organizations in an effort to find common ground on sustainable, environmentally sound growth. And he's not the only captain of industry seeking to profit from shifting world industry to sustainable processes - in cooperation with governments, universities, and groups espousing both the environment and human betterment in food, housing, and work conditions.
Let's be blunt and realistic. Such sustainable development and better living standards will need petroleum and the steel, plastics, power, fertilizer, and the other output oil supports into the foreseeable future. But the uses to which such fossil fuels are put will have to be cleaned up as noted above. That means far greater efficiency of manufacturing, heat/cooling, transport, so as to curb toxic output and steadily reduce greenhouse gas buildup. It also means more urgency on shifting to alternative energy technology.
Is this hot-tin-roof summer certifiable evidence of global climate change? Not definitively. But growing more likely. Obviously climate records dating only from 1870 tell us little. But deep glacier samples, ancient tree rings, suboceanic core samples and other evidence warrant caution. And that caution should take the form of a sensible global insurance policy.
If we assume, as economic logic urges, that the Asian-slump spread is temporary and that the desire for a better life throughout the developing world persists, we must prepare for renewed worldwide growth.
That, surely, is a goad to speeding research on sustainable processes in every area of manufacturing, transport, construction, and general energy use. It surely is a goad, also, to leaders of industry, environmental groups, politicians, universities, and developing nations' human rights advocates to work cooperatively in the fashion gingerly explored by BP's Browne and Greenpeace's Lord Peter Melchett.
Many such leaders are already quietly engaged in cooperation based on research and global realities. That, not the political cliche, is the true bridge to the 21st century.
Growth, better standards of living, and an ecologically stable planet require rivals to cooperate.

60 million people, 60 million rats ... there's probably one near you

Author: Vidal, John

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/60-million-people-rats-theres-probably-one-near/docview/245308989/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: When a Kettering woman went to the lavatory last month, she got a surprise. Just as she sat down, she heard a scratching and scrabbling beneath her. Alarmed, she found that a rat had negotiated the U-bend and was trying to climb out of the toilet bowl. Nor does her experience represent an isolated case of Rattus norvegicus, the common brown rat, making a dash from the sewers. A national survey of local authorities, published today, reports rat populations exploding across the country with increased risks to human health as they reach the surface. The culprits, says the Robens Centre for Public and Environmetal Health, are privatised water companies and local authorities that are neglecting to repair the crumbling sewerage systems which house almost all Britain's estimated 60 million rats.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=60+million+people%2C+60+million+rats+...+there%27s+probably+one+near+you&amp;author=Vidal%2C+John&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-08-13&amp;spage=003&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: WHEN a Kettering woman went to the lavatory last month, she got a surprise. Just as she sat down, she heard a scratching and scrabbling beneath her. Alarmed, she found that a rat had negotiated the U-bend and was trying to climb out of the toilet bowl.
Nor does her experience represent an isolated case of Rattus norvegicus, the common brown rat, making a dash from the sewers. A national survey of local authorities, published today, reports rat populations exploding across the country with increased risks to human health as they reach the surface.
The culprits, says the Robens Centre for Public and Environmetal Health, are privatised water companies and local authorities that are neglecting to repair the crumbling sewerage systems which house almost all Britain's estimated 60 million rats.
As cracks appear in the underground drainage systems, so the rats are coming out.
The study of more than 160 local authorities suggests that there have been 40,000 reported cases of rats in homes in the past three years. Real numbers could be far higher. Government research of places known to be infested suggests that only 40 per cent of people make official complaints.
Almost half the councils surveyed said that rat populations were now increasing considerably. This follows a Ministry of Agriculture report in 1993 that showed that rat populations had leapt by 40 per cent since the late 1970s.
The problem may be greater than anyone thouht with more than 300,000 kilometres of public sewerage system network and a similar length of private pipes going uninspected.
But Britain's sewers are now being dangerously neglected, says the report's author, environmental health consultant Stephen Battersby, who is accepted as one of Britain's authorities on vermin.
Few local authorities have sewerage maintenance agreements since the privatisation of the water companies. Some do not lay bait to control rats and only 8 per cent have revised their pest control strategies in the past two years, Mr Battersby says.
The water companies reply that they spend more than pounds 2 million a year dealing with rats.
Mr Battersby called on the Government to make water companies and local authorities take the threat to public health seriously and act now. The Government has no plans either to issue guidelines or review the laws, some dating back 50 years. But yesterday an official of the Department of the Environment said it planned to meet Mr Battersby to discuss his report.
Mr Battersby said: "We are seeing the beginnings of the disintegration of the sewerage system as a direct consequence of the situation being ignored for so long by everyone, from the Government down."
Rats flourish when they are out of the sewers, where they have little food, the report says. One pair can produce a colony of 2,000 rats in a year. Local authorities list climate change, more fast food outlets, and excessive litter as contributory factors to the rat population explosion.
The phenomenon is not just British. These are good times for rats around the world with uncontrolled expansion of cities, overflowing rubbish dumps, and a decline of public services following privatisation everywhere.
Rattus norvegicus carries foot and mouth disease, tuberculosis, rat-bite fever and cryptosporidium, among other diseases.
Joan Whalley, the Labour MP who chairs the Campaign for the Renewal of Older Sewerage Systems, has written to John Prescott, the Deputy Prime Minister, about the problem. She called yesterday for a government summit to address the public health issues posed by rats. "We face a desperate situation and have to take action now."
Rat facts
Rats live for about 18 months and breed three months from birth. One pair can produce a colony of 2,000 in a year. It would be impossible to eradicate them completely.
They can climb brickwork and guttering, swim along pipes, climb U bends in toilets and get into cavity walls.
US rats are thought to cause $10 billion damage a year. They can undermine foundations, chew through electric cabling and cause fires, destroy telecommunications junction boxes. British insurance companies usually exclude damage by vermin from policies.
Rats are expected to flourish in the next 10 years. Old Victorian sewerage systems are near the end of their lifespan and new sewers and drains are often in very poor condition. At the present rate of repair, some sewers will have to last more than 500 years before they are replaced.

U.S. Climate Center Sees Heat Records Break, A Trend That Has Multibillion-Dollar Effects

Author: By John J. Fialka

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/u-s-climate-center-sees-heat-records-break-trend/docview/398646423/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Because climatology is still more of an art than a science, the numbers aren't always tidy. But satellites give the analysts here, once confined to a network of 11,000 mostly volunteer weather observers in the U.S., the ability to scan the world. Their collection of weather data -- the world's largest -- allows them to "back-forecast" what the weather might have been in the year 1400. And their supercomputers generate models showing what future weather may be. Reports of raging wildfires in Florida, severe drought in Texas and epic floods in California all find a place here, because these are the people who declare new records and redefine what "normal" weather patterns are. Increasingly, that information has multibillion-dollar consequences for business, because it will soon show up in changed government weather norms used for building codes, highway construction, the utility industry, flood insurance and the nation's protective levees and dams. To be sure, global warming, and its suggestions of still more violent weather to come, has its skeptics. One of them used to be Mr. (Tom) Karl, who believed that some temperatures were being skewed upward by "urban heat sinks," or rapidly growing cities whose masonry and pavement tend to store heat. The theory fizzled. "It accounted for only a small percentage of the warming that is going on," he explains.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=U.S.+Climate+Center+Sees+Heat+Records+Break%2C+A+Trend+That+Has+Multibillion-Dollar+Effects&amp;author=By+John+J.+Fialka&amp;issn=00999660&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-08-12&amp;spage=1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: ASHEVILLE, N.C. -- It's summertime and the living here, in the cool of the Great Smoky Mountains, is easy. But not at the U.S. National Climatic Data Center, where experts are sweating over multiple records showing this may be the planet's hottest summer in 600 years.
Because climatology is still more of an art than a science, the numbers aren't always tidy. But satellites give the analysts here, once confined to a network of 11,000 mostly volunteer weather observers in the U.S., the ability to scan the world. Their collection of weather data -- the world's largest -- allows them to "back-forecast" what the weather might have been in the year 1400. And their supercomputers generate models showing what future weather may be.
Reports of raging wildfires in Florida, severe drought in Texas and epic floods in California all find a place here, because these are the people who declare new records and redefine what "normal" weather patterns are. Increasingly, that information has multibillion-dollar consequences for business, because it will soon show up in changed government weather norms used for building codes, highway construction, the utility industry, flood insurance and the nation's protective levees and dams.
"This is a pretty exciting time," says Tom Karl, a climatologist who directs the center. Lately he has been summoned to the White House on a monthly basis to help Vice President Al Gore explain what each new record means. He was on hand this week when Mr. Gore announced that July was the globe's hottest month on record.
To be sure, global warming, and its suggestions of still more violent weather to come, has its skeptics. One of them used to be Mr. Karl, who believed that some temperatures were being skewed upward by "urban heat sinks," or rapidly growing cities whose masonry and pavement tend to store heat. The theory fizzled. "It accounted for only a small percentage of the warming that is going on," he explains.
One who is still a skeptic is John Christy, from the University of Alabama in Huntsville. He sought to debunk global warming because 19 years of satellite data he monitors showed that lower levels of the earth's atmosphere were cooling slightly. This year, though, his data turned on him. "January through now is the hottest data we've ever seen," he says.
He still dismisses many of the U.S. weather disturbances as mere "regional variations," but his numbers show the globe's tropics are steaming, generating enough heat to slightly raise the earth's average temperature. What does it all mean? "It means we've improved our level of ignorance about the climate system," he says.
Robert Quayle, who heads the global climate laboratory here, found himself doubting a graph produced by the center that showed the average temperature of the planet's oceans had jumped half a degree. "I thought maybe our instruments were wrong," recalls Mr. Quayle. So he called officials in the United Kingdom, which runs a separate measurement system, and found they measured the oceans even warmer.
"This is pretty striking. Sea temperatures are pretty conservative sorts of things. They don't change rapidly like air temperatures do," he explains. While many weather forecasters blame much of the current heat wave on El Nino, a massive periodic warming effect in the Pacific Ocean, Mr. Quayle and other climatologists suspect something more fundamental is going on.
Lately officials here have been meeting with bankers, traders and insurance managers who are also concerned about what has been happening. To them, warming means money, because record-breaking weather will change the 30-year weather patterns that the center uses to define "normal" weather.
Climatologists say warming puts more water into the atmosphere, which, in turn, means record rainfall and more violent storms. That means insurance rates go up and that the definition of flood-prone areas, normally measured by record floods over a period of 100 years, will have to be enlarged. "These questions go to whether you can get a bank mortgage or not, or how high to build a bridge. It will change standards for heating and cooling systems in building codes," Mr. Quayle says.
The questions have also kicked off a new political tempest in Washington, where Ben McNitt, co-author of a study by the National Wildlife Federation, asserts that the Federal Emergency Management Agency's 100-year flood maps "are not accurate in a large number of cases." He also says the Army Corps of Engineers, which builds dams and levees, is working with out-of-date information. "We found no sense of awareness within the middle management of these agencies that climate change was relevant to their day-to-day operations," Mr. McNitt asserts.
Michael Armstrong, an associate director of FEMA, which has spent over $1 billion over the past 30 years developing 100-year flood maps for communities, concedes some of them are at least 10 years old. "Everyone will acknowledge here that we need to do some more work in this area," he adds, referring to the maps. He blames the lagging at FEMA on "inadequate funding."
As for the Army Corps of Engineers, Eugene Stakhiv, a chief planner there, says: "Look, the corps is not just sitting here being fat, dumb and happy." The corps is studying the impact of climate change on the upper Mississippi and trying to decipher conflicting computer models projecting the effects of global warming. "What they could criticize us for," Mr. Stakhiv offers, "is that it takes a long time to do this, and we are not well-coordinated with FEMA."
While many people see trouble in the climatologists' reports, some see opportunity. "We've been studying the measurement of weather very extensively," says one of the center's more frequent visitors, Tripp Dunman, portfolio manager for Aquila Energy, a subsidiary of UtiliCorp United Inc., a utility in Kansas City, Mo. Aquila's interest in the area was stimulated by losses the parent company sustained when it bought too much fuel to prepare for what turned out to be a warmish winter.
So Aquila came up with a trading instrument, a "weather derivative," that allows utilities and other companies to hedge against the prospect of an abnormal winter. If a company pays it a certain premium, Aquila will pay it a specified amount for each degree above or below what the center determines is normal weather. "It's different than an insurance policy. There are no disputes, no claims procedures," Mr. Dunman says.
Is this risky business? Not necessarily, insists Jack D. Cogen, president of Natsource Corp., a New York firm that also trades in weather derivatives. The trick is to spread out the risks, he says, pointing to a deal last winter when Natsource had a Midwest utility wanting to hedge against a warm winter and a Chicago builder that wanted protection against work stoppages caused by an unusually cold winter. "Everybody won," he says.
Credit: Staff Reporter

Letters Matthews oversteps bounds in expressing moral outrage

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/letters-matthews-oversteps-bounds-expressing/docview/412706265/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The Editors: Richard Matthews' July 30 column ("Where is this country's sense of shame?") begs for a response. I, too, would hope that the people of our country will be outraged by moral decadence, but I remember a time when the accused were presumed innocent. One may have an opinion that "credible accusations" are sufficient to demand that the president resign, but to be morally outraged when the American people do not concur is sheer arrogance. The American people have made a decision that until there is absolute evidence to the contrary, [President] Clinton, whom they twice elected, should be presumed innocent. Matthews is not entitled to decide for me that the accusations are credible. Anyone can make accusations, but neither their existence nor their number alone is sufficient to condemn. The president's detractors presume him guilty. His supporters, including those who have enjoyed six years of prosperity, cling to the hope that he is not. If his guilt is conclusively proven, I, too, will expect him to resign. Until then, he has my support and my prayers. The Editors: Once again, the newspaper printed an article on the depleted uranium used in the Gulf War and did not use the words "radioactive waste" ("Uranium exposure: Gulf war vets claim health issues ignored," National News, Tuesday). Radioactive waste is what depleted uranium is. Radioactive waste always carries the threat of cancer, birth defects, leukemia and immune system damage, but unless it is called by its correct name and carries appropriate warning, the public will be at risk.

Full text:   Today's News
The Editors: Richard Matthews' July 30 column ("Where is this country's sense of shame?") begs for a response. I, too, would hope that the people of our country will be outraged by moral decadence, but I remember a time when the accused were presumed innocent. One may have an opinion that "credible accusations" are sufficient to demand that the president resign, but to be morally outraged when the American people do not concur is sheer arrogance. The American people have made a decision that until there is absolute evidence to the contrary, Clinton, whom they twice elected, should be presumed innocent.
Matthews is not entitled to decide for me that the accusations are credible. Anyone can make accusations, but neither their existence nor their number alone is sufficient to condemn. The president's detractors presume him guilty. His supporters, including those who have enjoyed six years of prosperity, cling to the hope that he is not. If his guilt is conclusively proven, I, too, will expect him to resign. Until then, he has my support and my prayers.
JAMES H. MEEK, Tucker
Bringing us down
The Editors: Like most people, my family and I are suffering from an information overload brought on by the never-ending accounts of the "Oval Office Follies." Frankly, we are not surprised by President Clinton's actions. From his earliest appearance in big-time politics, he has exemplified the image of the somewhat smarmy politician who will say anything and do anything to raise money or to get elected.
What is dismaying is the attitude of the public when confronted with Clinton's shenanigans. We have become a society that confuses celebrity with heroics. We idolize athletes and celebrities, regardless of their morality or behavior. When the presidency becomes the subject of ridicule and salacious jokes, we all are diminished. ROBERT T. CROSBY, Dunwoody
A genuine threat
The Editors: Once again, the newspaper printed an article on the depleted uranium used in the Gulf War and did not use the words "radioactive waste" ("Uranium exposure: Gulf war vets claim health issues ignored," National News, Tuesday). Radioactive waste is what depleted uranium is. Radioactive waste always carries the threat of cancer, birth defects, leukemia and immune system damage, but unless it is called by its correct name and carries appropriate warning, the public will be at risk.
Radioactive waste is the great unsolved problem, not because it is worse than climate change and toxic pollution, but because it will last for centuries. Human technology has no way to reverse the process of radioactive decay created in a nuclear reactor.
JOAN O. KING, Sautee
No comparison
The Editors: Pentagon spokesperson Dian Lawhon compared exposure to depleted uranium with children's exposure to lead by licking the ends of their pencils ("Gulf war vets claim health issues ignored"). Pencil "lead" is composed of graphite, which is elemental carbon bound in hexagonal sheets, mixed with clay or other substances to change its hardness.
Perhaps the Pentagon should spend some of my tax dollars on a refresher chemistry course for its personnel. ANDREW J. LAMPKIN III, College Park
Keep the change
The Editors: The new gold-colored dollar will be the size of a quarter, and the point of interest is the design on its face? As was noted in a Journal news article, ("New coin inspired by Sacajawea," July 30) the problem with the Susan B. Anthony coin was not that people disliked the color, or Anthony, but that it was the size of a quarter. People want to feel a dollar's worth in a dollar coin, not a quarter's worth. Want to hear a prediction concerning the public's acceptance of the new dollar coin?
J.W. COLLINS, Lawrenceville
A busy month
The Editors: In a story headlined "Lawmaker breaks ranks with GOP" (National News, Wednesday) a reporter asserted that House Speaker Newt Gingrich (R-Ga.)has had "precious little" to say in public recently and has only granted interviews to a select few Capitol Hill publications. The speaker did cancel some public events after the tragic shooting deaths of two Capitol police officers, but in the two preceding weeks, he took part in nearly a dozen major news conferences, gave another half-dozen speeches, and conducted more than 20 interviews.
The story also claimed that Rep. John Linder (R-Ga.) and the speaker "are meeting resistance" in securing contributions from other Republicans to a national issue advocacy campaign. The campaign has raised more than $6 million in its first two weeks alone.
Finally, in citing health-care legislation as the GOP's "one big success" in the past month, the reporter ignored (among other accomplishments) IRS reform, the Coverdell education savings accounts, the partial-birth abortion veto override, emergency aid for America's farmers, and automatic mortgage insurance cancellation.
CHRISTINA MARTIN Christina Martin is press secretary to Rep. Newt Gingrich.

Premiers seek $6-billion hike in health funds: [Final Edition]

Author: Jim Beatty, Sun Legislature Bureau

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/premiers-seek-6-billion-hike-health-funds/docview/242840477/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: While the premiers often end their annual conference by making numerous demands on Ottawa, they reduced their demands this year -- deferring a call for a national transportation strategy -- in the hope that their health care demands won't be diluted by other requests. In B.C., the federal transfer cuts will mean a loss of $625 million this year. When the cuts between 1995 and 1997 are added, the B.C. cuts total nearly $1.8 billion. Because the money is transferred in a lump sum, it is difficult to determine how much specifically has been cut from health. "From a global warming perspective and from an air quality perspective, the last thing we need in almost all instances is more highways," he said in an interview from Saskatoon, where he was lobbying the premiers to discuss a strategy to reduce greenhouse gases.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Premiers+seek+%246-billion+hike+in+health+funds&amp;author=Jim+Beatty%2C+Sun+Legislature+Bureau&amp;issn=08321299&amp;title=The+Vancouver+Sun&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-08-08&amp;spage=A.1.FRO&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: SASKATOON - Canada's premiers demanded a cut in payroll taxes and a $6-billion increase in health care spending Friday in an effort to create jobs, improve medicare and increase investor confidence in Canada.
Wrapping up their 39th annual conference on the picturesque banks of the South Saskatchewan River, the provincial leaders said improved health care is the top priority for Canadians from coast to coast.
"This is our first priority," said Ontario Premier Mike Harris calling on the federal government to restore the $6 billion a year that has been cut from health care. "That could all be done by next budget year."
In an effort to spark the economy and boost jobs - particularly for young people - the premiers also called on Ottawa to cut Employment Insurance premiums paid by employers and workers.
By reducing EI premiums, and eliminating them for young people, the premiers say 200,000 new jobs could be created, one quarter of them for youth.
By the end of 1998, the EI fund is expected to show a $19 billion surplus.
"I think it's unconscionable that there is a large surplus there," B.C. Premier Glen Clark said. "That's a payroll tax. There should be either enhanced benefits or cuts in the payroll tax."
While the premiers often end their annual conference by making numerous demands on Ottawa, they reduced their demands this year -- deferring a call for a national transportation strategy -- in the hope that their health care demands won't be diluted by other requests.
"The priority has to be health care," Clark said. "That's got to be the message to Canadians. We're deferring any talks or requests for a national highways program until we get the health care situation resolved."
In B.C., the federal transfer cuts will mean a loss of $625 million this year. When the cuts between 1995 and 1997 are added, the B.C. cuts total nearly $1.8 billion. Because the money is transferred in a lump sum, it is difficult to determine how much specifically has been cut from health.
"They should restore it all," Clark said.
The deferred transportation plan was greeted as a minor victory by Gerry Scott, who heads the climate change program at Vancouver's David Suzuki Foundation.
"From a global warming perspective and from an air quality perspective, the last thing we need in almost all instances is more highways," he said in an interview from Saskatoon, where he was lobbying the premiers to discuss a strategy to reduce greenhouse gases.
"We would like to see the national government and all the provinces move forward on reduction strategies. For years there's been talk and study on global warming . . .It's time for every level of government and industry in this country to move forward in a serious way."
But Scott left disappointed, when the premiers failed to address the issue.

Fence Post: [Lake Edition 5]

Abstract: One hundred percent of the children buried in St. Mary's Cemetery didn't hurt anyone, were a joy to their families, were taken away too soon, and have since been forgotten by the public at large. Full text:   Let Nicole rest in peace
One hundred percent of the children buried in St. Mary's Cemetery didn't hurt anyone, were a joy to their families, were taken away too soon, and have since been forgotten by the public at large. I personally know the families of two children who were killed on the streets of Buffalo Grove, one of whom was taken following a Buffalo Grove Days Parade. For years, I gladly volunteered for what was called the "Race for Nicole" at my parish. I don't know anyone who passes the reservoir area without remembering that a young life was lost there.
But enough is enough about a memorial for Nicole Soccorso, and that is so sad for Nicole. Her name no longer instills a sadness in people's hearts, but rather stirs up controversy and resentment. Her family needs to let go of Nicole and accept that she is now in a better place, that her suffering was somehow God's will that only strong faith can explain. Anyone who visits her grave at St. Mary's can attest to the fact that the family is missing the big picture. It's time they move on and live as Nicole would want them to. If the world memorialized every death, there wouldn't be any time to live.
It was upsetting to learn that a group of mothers from Arlington Heights is banding together to push for a memorial at the park. If they succeed, it would be an insult to all the children who have died an accidental death. Though their intentions are indeed inspiring, this was an unfortunate accident caused by vandalism, however terrible it was.
Death should be shared among those who really knew the person, grief should be shared among those who really loved the person, and memories should be shared among those who were fortunate enough to have been loved by the person.
Angels have no need to be preserved in memorials.
Mary Depcik
Buffalo Grove
Prevent global warming
Since the international climate change negotiations in Kyoto, Japan last December, many conservative politicians and pundits have developed a suspiciously consistent mantra: "Oil and coal, good. Global warming treaty, bad." Cynical and misinformed local media outlets have added "Don't worry, be happy" to the chant.
The treaty opponents are quite willing to twist the facts about global warming to "spin" their own political agenda. The narrow assumptions underlying their Business As Usual recommendations overlook emerging technologies, the strength of the American business community to use change as an advantage, and the consequences of being wrong.
Predictions of economic doom-and-gloom are mostly bellowing from the fossil fuel industry, and their converts in Congress and on talk radio. How else to interpret the op-ed pages and commentaries claiming that action to curb climate change will break the bank? However, a new report by the Union of Concerned Scientists reveals that these claims are pure fiction. UCS surveyed the most widely respected studies from the past 10 years to determine what cost, if any, the U.S. might pay for tackling global warming. The report looked at independent analyses from the U.S. government's national energy laboratories, the National Academy of Sciences, and the Office of Technology Assessment, and private energy research organizations. A much more encouraging force emerged.
The Kyoto climate treaty calls for U.S. emissions of heat-trapping gases to be cut by only 7 percent below 1990 levels by 2010. These studies show that the U.S. has great technological potential for significantly reducing emissions of carbon dioxide - the most critical global warming gas. In many cases, the resulting economic savings, in terms of reduced energy bills, would outweigh the cost of implementing the measures needed to tap this potential. Moreover, the same measures that would cut these emissions would also improve local air quality by reducing releases of dangerous air pollutants, a key consideration in the Chicagoland region.
To meet the treaty's goals, the United States needs to give priority to policies and measures that encourage more efficient energy use and a more rapid shift to cleaner, renewable energy sources, like wind and solar power. The alarmist economic predictions ignore the potential contribution of these clean technologies and assume that the only ways to reduce emissions are through costly and shortsighted means that actually would slow economic growth. By contrast, well-chosen policies and measures can overcome the market barriers that hinder widespread commercialization of these advanced and clean technologies, as well as stimulate further technological innovation.
Opponents of action also ignore the potentially enormous economic costs of global warming. Climate change could cause many social, demographic and political disruptions. The costs of some of these would be characterized by this summer's fires, floods and heat waves: sea level rise and coastal flooding; more intense rainfall, floods and droughts. Others are sometimes more difficult to quantify economically: accelerating loss of ecologically important species. Human health could be threatened not only by more frequent heat waves, but by spread of tropical diseases to Illinois latitudes.
There is an overwhelming consensus among the world's climate scientists that these threats are real and require immediate action. If the U.S. does not act, Americans could lose out on the rapidly expanding international market for advanced energy technologies. We'd be left to face the harsh impacts of a warmer, less stable climate.
Our political and business leaders must see through the doom and gloom rhetoric of do-nothing advocates, and scrutinize the billions of federal dollars going annually to subsidize the fossil fuel industry. In contrast, the Kyoto climate treaty is a very cheap insurance policy against the substantial risks of global warming. Future generations deserve no less than this small price of protection.
Evan Craig, Chair of the
Woods & Wetlands Group
of the Sierra Club
Vernon Hills
Alden Meyer, Director of Government Relations at the Union of Concerned Scientists
Washington, D.C.
Open space costs less
There were only a few surprises at the monthly Libertyville Township Board meeting July 16. Jack Martin and his sidekick, Pat Connors, were there in their usual role, threatening a lawsuit if the town board didn't do their bidding, and in the same breath implying their only motivation was to protect the taxpayers. There were a few present who were afraid they will be driven out of their homes by high taxes. That may be, but not because of Open Space - but because of development and the need to provide for new schools, libraries and roads.
Thus the purpose of this letter to the editor. Down through history, the rich and powerful have used their money and position to promote their own self-serving agendas, and all too often they have succeeded. The only method the masses ever had was to band together, and equally important, to stick together, to ensure they got a fair shake.
So it is with the threatened lawsuit by Martin and Connors to stop the Open Space program once again. They used the same tactics in 1989, and were highly successful in those efforts to deceive the public, and to cause the change of political leadership both at the township and county level. Most people know now the costly results of that change in direction.
The issues are over-development, traffic congestion, and more schools, which always mean higher taxes. More Open Space always means stabilized taxes and greatly improved property values and quality of life. The question the public must answer: Is it worth fighting for? The public will pay either way, but overdevelopment will cost several times more.
Edmund Burke once wrote: The only thing necessary for the triumph of evil is for good men to do nothing.
F.T. "Mike" Graham
Libertyville Township Supervisor
Libertyville
Violating right to choose
Recently the House of Representatives voted 223-202 to prohibit the FDA from testing, developing or approving a drug that would chemically induce an abortion, including RU-486 (Mifepristone). A woman's health-care provider administers RU-486, and it is used to safely terminate a pregnancy up to nine weeks. This drug has been used in Europe since 1981 as a safe and effective alternative to surgical abortion procedures. So why are American women being denied this option? The answer is simple.
As the 1998 elections approach, conservative lawmakers need to ensure the support of social conservatives. What better way to do that than to attack a woman's right to choose and further assault a woman's right to choose her own reproductive options.
In seeking to ban the FDA from approving medical abortion drugs, anti-choice lawmakers are subjecting the already rigorous drug- approval process to a highly political screen. They also are setting a dangerous precedent whereby scientific advancement must first pass political muster to move forward.
Abortion is legal in this country, and a woman has a constitutional right to choose. The Coburn amendment effectively bans a highly effective method of early pregnancy termination and is therefore unconstitutional. Congress should spend more time developing programs that address the causes of unintended pregnancy and thus reduce the need for abortion than on pandering to the wishes of a socially conservative minority.
Rupali Kumar
Chicago
Grow up, Granger
Mr. Granger:
It's understandable when an adolescent has to "act cool," bolstering his/her insecurity by mocking the rules and making fun of those who try to abide by the rules.
It's pitiful and ridiculous when an adult has to "act cool" by aligning with a recognized cool person. I read your article this morning and thought, "Grow up!" (Please, no articles on accepting the child in ourselves!)
We all know that people of outstanding ability or looks can and do get away with breaking the rules. Sometimes they break the rules in an amusing way. While I do not "delight" in Rodman's antics, neither do they disturb me; I see his charm sometimes; I was amused with him when he smiled in amazement at his free throws. But Rodman is no different drummer in the deeper sense. It takes great courage to really stand up against what is popular. I spent my career teaching high school students, and I can tell you that the true different drummer is the kid who has the guts to follow the rules - not out of fear, but out of principle. That kid usually suffers. Rodman is certainly being true to himself, but his actions are easy - what's a couple thousand in fines? He's a celebrity!
What depressed me most about your article was not your defense of Rodman, but your nasty attack on Jerry Sloan, and by implication, his team of "uncool" types. You mock Sloan and all who abide by the rules, stereotyping them as uptight, intolerant, out of it, while people like you and your son are open-minded and with it.
Rodman should be insulted by your condescending remarks. You and your son would never dress like him, but you both "delight" in his "antics."
A few, rare people of exceptional talent have the humility to try to live by the rules.
Patricia Sell
South Bend, Ind.
Congrats on 60 years
I was fortunate enough to recently attend a luncheon to celebrate Robert Y. Paddock's 60th anniversary with Paddock Publications. At this event were gathered representatives from the Boy Scouts, the Rotary, the Clearbrook Center, the village of Arlington Heights, High School District 214 Education Foundation, the Arlington Heights Central Business District, the Northwest Community Hospital Foundation and myself, representing the American Red Cross. All of these organizations have benefited from the generous efforts of Mr. Paddock over the years, and we were all there to show our deepest gratitude and congratulate Mr. Paddock on his anniversary.
Following the luncheon, a reception was held at the Daily Herald building, where Mr. Paddock was greeted by a rousing round of applause from the staff of the Daily Herald. He also was presented a plaque of recognition from Mayor Arlene Mulder of Arlington Heights, recognizing his outstanding work in the community.
As for the American Red Cross, we just wanted to take this opportunity to thank Mr. Paddock for his generous contributions to our organization. Once a year, over a period of several years, Mr. Paddock would swim one mile, and collect pledges from his colleagues around the community based on how far he swam. In that period of time, Mr. Paddock raised approximately $50,000 to help the American Red Cross meet its mission, to help people prevent, prepare for and respond to emergencies.
Mr. Paddock, congratulations on your anniversary with Paddock Publications. We hope you have many more happy and healthy years with the company. Your work with Paddock Publications and your community should be a model for us all to follow.
Donald M. Mountz
American Red Cross
of Greater Chicago

Goodale refuses global warming debate Regulating greenhouse gas emissions can't wait for argument to be solved, minister tells critics

Author: Jang, Brent

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/goodale-refuses-global-warming-debate-regulating/docview/384752397/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Calgary -- Canada's Natural Resources Minister struck back yesterday at oil-industry criticisms of Ottawa's acceptance that global warming exists, saying the skeptics are wrong to dismiss the effect of greenhouse gases on the planet's climate.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Goodale+refuses+global+warming+debate+Regulating+greenhouse+gas+emissions+can%27t+wait+for+argument+to+be+solved%2C+minister+tells+critics&amp;author=Jang%2C+Brent&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-07-15&amp;spage=A.10&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Regulating greenhouse gas emissions can't wait for argument to be solved, minister tells critics
Goodale refuses global warming debate
Regulating greenhouse gas emissions can't wait for argument to be solved, minister tells critics
Wednesday, July 15, 1998
Alberta Bureau
Calgary -- Canada's Natural Resources Minister struck back yesterday at oil-industry criticisms of Ottawa's acceptance that global warming exists, saying the skeptics are wrong to dismiss the effect of greenhouse gases on the planet's climate.
Ralph Goodale said he refuses to be "paralyzed by analysis," and urged the nay-sayers to stop complaining and start supporting the federal government's plan to reduce emissions of greenhouse gases.
"If the world waits for absolute scientific certainty, then the world will have waited too long," Mr. Goodale said after meeting with provincial energy ministers.
"The preponderance of international scientific opinion very clearly suggests that greenhouse gases are having an effect on the climate. If unchecked, those gases will lead to very serious consequences for the environment."
Many of Canada's major energy companies oppose Ottawa's goal of cutting emissions by 2012 to levels 6 per cent below those recorded in 1990. Among the most vocal critics are Imperial Oil Ltd., Talisman Energy Inc., Alberta Energy Co. Ltd. and Ipsco Inc.
Mr. Goodale said the federal government will press ahead with its emissions-reduction strategy but it won't be easy to win over those who question the science of climate change.
At the Kyoto conference on global warming last December, Ottawa agreed to set new emissions targets, promising to begin implementing its plan by early 2000.
Mr. Goodale acknowledged that some dissenting scientists have argued vigorously that there are huge flaws in studies that indicate greenhouse-gas emissions create excessive global warming and hurt the environment.
"This is a big issue. The solutions will not be simple. But this is a time to be pro-active. You cannot buy the insurance coverage you should have had after the fact."
Besides trying to get oil and gas companies to co-operate, Ottawa realizes that it also must persuade energy-producing provinces, especially Alberta and Saskatchewan, to back plans to reduce emissions, Mr. Goodale said.
Alberta Energy Minister Steve West declined to enter the debate over the science of global warming, but he said the Kyoto pledge won't be ratified by Alberta unless Ottawa lowers its targets.
"We have polluted over the years . . . but we're asking the federal government for maximum flexibility and further discussion," Mr. West said.
Saskatchewan Energy Minister Eldon Lautermilch said that although Ottawa has the power to move unilaterally, the reality is that provincial support will be needed to carry out the federal proposals.
David Manning, president of the Canadian Association of Petroleum Producers, said it's time for the oil patch to work more closely with Ottawa, even though some of his member companies are expressing basic doubts about global warming.
However, Mr. Manning said he's worried about the potentially harmful economic impact of limiting greenhouse gases.
He said a recent survey commissioned by the association indicated that of 2,000 Canadians polled, environmental issues such as water pollution and toxic waste easily emerged as more worrisome problems than climate change.
