Bill Clinton's week light on scandal: [SOUTH PINELLAS Edition]

Author: Dahl, David

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/bill-clintons-week-light-on-scandal/docview/263298713/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: President Clinton's week started with a baby boomer's lament on the passing of Roy Rogers. It began to wind down with a high-roller dinner at the garish Miami home of Sylvester Stallone. As witnesses parade in and out of the federal courthouse here, Clinton has remained focused on what he repeatedly says is the job the American people hired him to do. The strategy seems to fit perfectly with the waning public interest in the never-ending Kenneth Starr investigation of Clinton. By offering no further explanation for his conduct in months, Clinton has avoided fueling the story that once threatened to engulf him.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Bill+Clinton%27s+week+light+on+scandal&amp;author=Dahl%2C+David&amp;issn=&amp;title=St.+Petersburg+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-07-12&amp;spage=1.A&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: President Clinton's week started with a baby boomer's lament on the passing of Roy Rogers. It began to wind down with a high-roller dinner at the garish Miami home of Sylvester Stallone.
Along the way, he debated race relations on public television and announced plans to educate the poor about Medicareand to travel to Russia. He raised money for Democrats, praised firefighters in Florida, launched an anti-drug advertising campaign and delivered the medal of honor to a Vietnam War hero from Jacksonville.
Did someone say Monica Lewinsky? Not the president.
As witnesses parade in and out of the federal courthouse here, Clinton has remained focused on what he repeatedly says is the job the American people hired him to do. The strategy seems to fit perfectly with the waning public interest in the never-ending Kenneth Starr investigation of Clinton. By offering no further explanation for his conduct in months, Clinton has avoided fueling the story that once threatened to engulf him.
Six months after the scandal broke, this much is true: The predictions of Clinton's political demise appear much exaggerated.
He remains the top draw for Democrats in their latest quest for campaign cash, and is his party's chief spokesman in drawing the battle lines for this year's congressional races.
Just off his successful trip to China, he jumped back into a week of media events and announcements, often in Ronald Reagan-style settings that tap into Americans' pride and the country's satisfaction with its economic success and swelled mutual funds.
No wonder, then, that Stallone delivered a pair of red boxing gloves to the president at a fund-raiser Thursday night.
"I can use them," Clinton said. "I think I have established that I can take a punch. Now the time has come for me to deliver a few."
Consider these scenes from last week's White House script:
Monday: the Rose Garden
Under a beating morning sun, Clinton strides out of the Oval Office, clapping his hands together a couple of times as he approaches the podium. He has been back from China less than two days, but he exudes energy.
He begins by urging the Republican Congress to "choose partisanship" or what he calls "progress" by passing some remnants of his legislative agenda that Capitol Hill has virtually ignored this year. Aware that his hands are tied with a Congress in the hands of the other party, he announces that he will act on his own to reach out to 3-million low-income people who do not know they are eligible for Medicare. And then he takes a few questions from the assembled press corps. One is about trade legislation. Another about a newspaper report on poor people losing out on health care.
But none about Monica Lewinsky.
As he leaves, someone shouts: "Your thoughts on Roy Rogers?"
Clinton does not let that opportunity pass, and returns to the podium to wrap himself in the warm memories of the deceased movie cowboy. "Like most people my age, I grew up on Roy Rogers, Dale Evans and Trigger and Gabby Hayes. I really appreciate what he stood for, the movies he made, and the kind of values they embodied."
The presidential condolences passed along, he departs for his office.
Tuesday: scandals surface
Not a good day for Team Clinton, as two of his scandals poke their heads up once more.
An appeals court opened the way for Secret Service agents to testify before the grand jury investigating Clinton's relationship with Lewinsky. The federal court rejected claims by the Secret Service - and supported by the Justice Department - that testifying would endanger the president's life.
On a second front, Democratic fund-raiser Maria Hsia is indicted on federal tax charges. Her attorney says prosecutors are trying to force her client to plead guilty on campaign finance charges.
Both developments remind Washington's reporters, analysts and, presumably, the president himself, that any one of several scandals that have plagued his administration could still cause serious trouble. But the White House virtually ignores the criminal investigations, and talks about health care.
Staging yet another media event, Clinton slams insurance companies for failing to guarantee sick and hard to insure people more access to medical care, as required by a 1996 law. Returning once more to his limited power as the chief executive, he orders the Office of Personnel Management to consider canceling contracts of insurance carriers for federal employees if the companies are ignoring the law. It was a good try by the president, but this time the scandal got the big headlines the next morning.
Wednesday: race debate
It is a day of still more announcements from the White House.
Clinton confirms that he is going to Russia again to visit with Boris Yeltsin. He appears with lawmakers and gun control advocates to endorse a bill that would make parents criminally responsible for not safely storing fire arms. And he takes care of some political business, rewarding former Florida Democratic Party chairman Simon Ferro with an ambassadorship to Panama. It is unclear, though, whether the Republican Senate will get around to confirming the Ferro nomination.
But the main public event is Clinton's taping of a public television debate on race.
Loose and chatty, Clinton arrived at a suburban Washington studio full of minutia about Ulysses S. Grant. Having recently read a book about the Civil War general and former president, Clinton concluded that he was a "brilliant man," the best horseman in the Army, and someone who had gotten a bad rap.
There is "not a single solitary shred of evidence that he was ever drunk on the job," Clinton said, though he did concede that Grant was inebriated twice - off duty.
That little piece of history aside, the taping began and Clinton and a panel of columnists and experts began discussing his initiative on race. In the year since he began it, the effort has been held back by dissension on the panel he named and complaints that Clinton is not fully involved in the "dialogue" he tried to foster.
He and the panel spend an hour hashing over the feelings, successes and failures of Native Americans, Chinese Americans and African Americans.
Ever empathic, he notes that his grandmother is one-quarter Cherokee and that he grew up in a segregated South that has shaken off many of its old ways. He cites diversity success stories from California and Fairfax County, Va., but acknowledges Indians may face a future as bleak as their past.
"We're just getting warmed up," he says when moderator Jim Lehrer says it's time to end.
Lehrer was one of the first reporters to ask Clinton about his relationship with Lewinsky, months ago. This time, it didn't come up.
Thursday: down the coast
In a campaign-style trip down the east coast, Clinton hits three cities to raise political money and sell his agenda. His day begins at 7 a.m., when he leaves the White House aboard Marine One, to catch Air Force One at Andrews Air Force Base. His 747 touches down at Andrews at 1:40 a.m. Friday, some 18 hours later.
In Atlanta, Clinton shares the stage with House Speaker Newt Gingrich, his chief Republican nemesis, as well as Attorney General Janet Reno, who has made Clinton's life miserable by appointing several independent prosecutors and investigating fund-raising irregularities in Clinton's 1996 campaign.
He has warm words for both Reno and Gingrich, as he launches an anti-drug advertising campaign. Then, he goes to a local hotel to raise money for a Democratic Senate candidate in Georgia.
Twelve hours later, after a stop to visit with firefighters and victims of Florida's fires in Daytona Beach, Clinton is in a huge white tent on the 12-acre estate owned by Stallone.
Located on Biscayne Bay, the estate is a shrine to Stallone's ego and his wealth. A cardboard cutout of the actor as Rocky can be seen from a windowed weight-lifting room en route to the tent where the dinner is held. Most of the media is kept in a guardhouse decorated with a life-size photograph of Rocky and a heavy-handed oil painting by Stallone of the boxer.
Stallone is hoping to sell the home he calls "Casa Rocko" for $27- million, but this night is about raising money for Democrats, not for the actor. The evening will fetch $850,000 for Democrats, who want to retake the House and topple Gingrich, the man Clinton had greeted in Atlanta that morning. What would Newt say about all that money for Democrats? More importantly, what would Reno say?
But Clinton glides over the contradictions of the day, and adds to them. In his remarks to the crowd at Stallone's house, Clinton butters up his host by recalling that Stallone had shared his wisdom about global warming. Stallone had seen the effects of climate change, Clinton said, because the tanned actor spends so much time outside.
Clinton reminded the audience that his administration and the Democrats in Congress want to find a solution to global warming, along with wanting to "save" Social Security and adding money to lower class sizes and improve public education.
He adds that Democrats want to restore the Everglades, too. Last year's bipartisan budget agreement increased money for the Everglades, he pointed out, but this year Republicans have cast votes that "imply they are going to walk away from the commitment made last year."
By this point members of the crowd are yawning. The wife of U.S. Rep. Bob Wexler of Fort Lauderdale, trying to stay warm in the frosty air conditioning, drapes a coat over her shoulders. A few people - including Clinton's brother-in-law - sneak out of the huge white tent to find their way out of Stallone's estate.
So Clinton begins to wrap it up, zigzagging back to a course that portrays him as a president who will work with both parties, if only the Republicans would be reasonable and pass his calls for more money for the Everglades.
"I say this to you because I wasn't a particularly partisan person when I came to Washington. I was a Democrat by heritage, instinct and conviction, but I wanted people to work together," Clinton insisted. "The atmosphere in Washington is too partisan, and we have blinders on - some of the decision-makers not doing what is plainly in the long term best interest of this country."
After bashing Washington, Clinton heads back to the place.
Friday: winding down
It was the end of a slow news week, in which Clinton was able to dominate the headlines. He closed it with the most Reaganesque of events.
On Friday, he awarded the medal of honor to Jacksonville resident Robert Ingram for his bravery as a Navy corpsman in Vietnam 32 years ago. In his remarks, Clinton thanked the members of Congress who had worked to approve the medal - both Democrats and Republicans - and recalled that Ingram suffered severe wounds while tending to dying Marines.
"His vital signs dropped to the point that he was tagged 'killed in action' and placed in a dead pile," Clinton said. "But as you can see, he did not die."
Said Clinton: "We are all proud to call you an American."
Once more, there were no more Monica Lewinsky questions. Clinton had survived another week.

IN MEDIA GLARE, OVERHEATED WEATHER SPAWNS A MAD SERIES OF CATASTROPHES

Author: Borenstein, Seth

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/media-glare-overheated-weather-spawns-mad-series/docview/1842446412/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=IN+MEDIA+GLARE%2C+OVERHEATED+WEATHER+SPAWNS+A+MAD+SERIES+OF+CATASTROPHES&amp;author=Borenstein%2C+Seth&amp;issn=08856613&amp;title=Philadelphia+Inquirer&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-07-12&amp;spage=E.3&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   The succession of photos of burning forests, flooded streets, and tornado-torn mobile homes suggests that the weather has gone stark raving mad.
But there's more at work than Mother Nature and her problem child, El Nino.
The effects of a record hot global atmosphere are being magnified by a more overheated media that beams each catastrophe into living rooms, and by a real estate boom that has placed growing numbers of people in disaster-prone areas, experts in several fields say.
``It's a combination,'' National Weather Service Director John J. Kelly said. ``It's been a busy year. I don't think the world is going nuts. Fires have always been there. Think of life before CNN.''
Even without the hype, the weather this year has lurched from extreme to extreme.
The federal government has declared 35 disasters in 1998 - with eight more disaster declarations pending - and the year is barely half over. With 34 disasters declared before July 1, the number of catastrophes is 26 percent higher than recent midyear averages. Although this year's 35 are - for now - far short of the 1996 record total of 75, there are already more disasters this year than in either 1993 or 1995.
First flooding, then drought and then wildfires bedeviled Florida. Disastrous floods hit Vermont, Massachusetts, Ohio and West Virginia. Early in the year, rainstorms bludgeoned California.
``It has been wild,'' said National Climatic Data Center meteorologist Richard Heim, supervising editor of the Climate Variation Bulletin. ``Anytime we've had extremes like the one-two punches like we've had in Florida, it's something to make you sit up and notice.''
So far this year, the insurance industry has paid out more than $4 billion in disaster claims - not including Florida fires and most flooding. That is substantially more than the $2.6 billion paid in all of 1997, said Gary Kerney, assistant vice president of Property Claim Services, which tracks catastrophes for the insurance industry. In 1992, the industry paid out a record $23 billion, and in 1994, it paid $17 billion.
The last six weeks are ``probably as busy as I've seen since I've been working in the catastrophe area,'' said Dan Carrigan, at State Farm, a 15-year veteran of the disaster business.
El Nino, the warming of part of the Pacific Ocean that is the weather scapegoat of the year, cannot be blamed for this summer's floods or fires, said one of the leading experts on the subject, Nicholas Graham, director of experimental forecasts at the International Research Institute for Climate Prediction in La Jolla, Calif. El Nino faded by May; since then, a dozen weather disasters have plagued the nation, according to the Federal Emergency Management Agency.
Nor has El Nino's flip side, La Nina, a cooling of waters in the central Pacific, started yet. That brings Graham to one of the first rules of talking about the weather: Normal doesn't exist.
There may always be extreme weather, but Heim, who tracks climate oddities, said that this year there have been an extreme number of extremes.
In recent years, global warming has created stronger and more frequent El Ninos than in the past, said Kevin Trenberth, head of climate analysis at the National Center for Atmospheric Research. In each of the first five months of 1998, the average global temperature set a new high, the National Climatic Data Center found.
The Earth, Trenberth said, is sweating much like an overheated human body attempting to cool itself. The sweat in this case is more moisture in the atmosphere at some points and less in others. He said there had been a significant increase in the number of downpours of more than 2 inches of rain, while other places are completely cut off from the flow of moisture. For example, the downpour-to-drought switch that took place in Florida has a 1-in-1,000 chance of happening naturally, said David Easterling at the National Climatic Data Center.
``Something real is going on here,'' Trenberth said. ``Certainly this relates to the whole idea of climate change.''
Nonsense, said William Gray, a noted hurricane prognosticator at Colorado State University. ``I don't think the case can be made that there's more extremes of weather now than in the past,'' Gray said. ``We've always had unusual weather - floods, droughts and everything.''
He said that some environmentalists are playing up recent extreme weather events and that they have been joined by the media, which focus on individual tragedies and miss the big picture.
Even experts who disagree on global warming agree that people are paying more attention to weather than they used to.
When he got into the business in 1981, said Kerney, the insurance expert: ``There could be tremendous damage, death and injuries caused by severe weather events in Kansas, for example, and you never heard about them in the media reports.''
Real estate development near flood-prone lakes and rivers only makes matters worse.
``These are popular places to put real estate,'' said Bob Hirsch, chief hydrologist for U.S. Geological Survey, ``but are also areas that are most prone to danger.''
Though tornado damage and the number of twisters are very high this year, that could also be the result of better reporting, Kelly said. In the past, tornadoes could have popped up in rural areas and gone unnoticed, but now the population has spread out. Add to that relatively new radar and more storm-chasing thrill-seekers, Heim said, and few twisters go unreported.

Credit: By Seth Borenstein, INQUIRER WASHINGTON BUREAU

U.K., China to Sign Agreement That Will Extend Air Services

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/u-k-china-sign-agreement-that-will-extend-air/docview/308325728/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The U.K. and China will sign a new air-services agreement that will give British carriers direct routes to Shanghai for the first time, U.K.'s Deputy Prime Minister John Prescott said. The agreement will also bring additional services between London and Beijing for U.K. airlines, Mr. Prescott told reporters at a news conference.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=U.K.%2C+China+to+Sign+Agreement+That+Will+Extend+Air+Services&amp;author=&amp;issn=09219986&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-07-07&amp;spage=2&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: BEIJING -- The U.K. and China will sign a new air-services agreement that will give British carriers direct routes to Shanghai for the first time, U.K.'s Deputy Prime Minister John Prescott said.
The agreement will also bring additional services between London and Beijing for U.K. airlines, Mr. Prescott told reporters at a news conference.
The new services, which are expected to begin in the spring or summer of next year, will be offered by only one airline initially with more airlines to be added later, Mr. Prescott said.
Other details, such as which airlines will be chosen and how many routes will be added, are still being worked out, he said.
Mr. Prescott, who is accompanied by a trade mission of water and aerospace companies, said he and business leaders met with Chinese officials to discuss possible future agreements in the water-treatment, aviation, finance, and insurance sectors.
Chinese officials also brought up the country's continuing plans to build a 100-seat passenger plane with European consortium Airbus Industrie, Mr. Prescott said.
Recent reports that various parties involved in the airplane deal are threatening to pull out have raised speculation that the project may be scrapped. China hopes the project will help the country develop its own aircraft manufacturing industry.
Mr. Prescott also met with Premier Zhu Rongji and other senior Chinese leaders to discuss international economic and political issues, including efforts to combat climate change.

Life's complexity makes us vulnerable

Author: Meadows, Donella

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/lifes-complexity-makes-us-vulnerable/docview/330872224/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: That statistic is quoted over and over by people who want us to think about how distant we are from our basic sustenance, how vulnerable our lives have become, how dependent on systems we can't control or even understand. These people intend to scare us in the same way the people who are yammering about the Y2K problem intend to scare us. Y2K is the computer world's label for a bug that apparently infests computer programs everywhere. Y2K stands for "year 2000," K being the scientific abbreviation for "kilo," which means thousand. As you have undoubtedly heard, programmers, back when computer memory was scarce, entered only the last two digits for years - "73" for 1973 - never thinking of the moment when the clock would turn over to "00." Now some credit cards are already bouncing because a computer assumes that "00" means the card expired in 1900. One chemical company, testing for Y2K problems by artificially advancing the date throughout its automated factories, discovered a computer chip that would have shut down a cooling system at midnight on Dec. 31, 1999, probably causing an explosion. That problem got fixed, but, the experts say, half of large corporations and 75 percent of small businesses are unprepared for Y2K, and the government is especially far behind.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Life%27s+complexity+makes+us+vulnerable&amp;author=Meadows%2C+Donella&amp;issn=&amp;title=The+Charleston+Gazette&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-07-06&amp;spage=4.A&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   THE average bite of food eaten by an American travels 1,300 miles from field to mouth.
That statistic is quoted over and over by people who want us to think about how distant we are from our basic sustenance, how vulnerable our lives have become, how dependent on systems we can't control or even understand. These people intend to scare us in the same way the people who are yammering about the Y2K problem intend to scare us.
Y2K is the computer world's label for a bug that apparently infests computer programs everywhere. Y2K stands for "year 2000," K being the scientific abbreviation for "kilo," which means thousand. As you have undoubtedly heard, programmers, back when computer memory was scarce, entered only the last two digits for years - "73" for 1973 - never thinking of the moment when the clock would turn over to "00."
Now some credit cards are already bouncing because a computer assumes that "00" means the card expired in 1900. One chemical company, testing for Y2K problems by artificially advancing the date throughout its automated factories, discovered a computer chip that would have shut down a cooling system at midnight on Dec. 31, 1999, probably causing an explosion. That problem got fixed, but, the experts say, half of large corporations and 75 percent of small businesses are unprepared for Y2K, and the government is especially far behind.
Social Security and Medicare payments could be held up for nobody knows how long, as computers deduce that someone born in 1930 is minus-30 years old.
Here's a typical list of computer-dependent entities that could go haywire: air traffic and railroad control systems, banks, stock exchanges, insurance companies, electric utilities, major manufacturing installations. One consultant estimates that there are 50 million chips embedded in hospital equipment, burglar alarms, oil refineries, power stations, elevators, all of which could shut a machine down if they think it was last serviced in 1900.
The National Federation of Independent Businesses estimates that the Y2K bug will bankrupt 330,000 small businesses.
I have listened to this scare talk with a mixture of disbelief, fear, amusement - and the sort of smugness that doubters can't resist when technology goes awry. Ha! Those smart-aleck computers that are supposed to save us time are now demanding enormous amounts of time just to fix a stupid mistake. (State Farm Insurance has been grappling with the Y2K problem since 1989 and still has 100 employees working on it full time.)
Furthermore, I can see in the shortsightedness of the programmers an apt metaphor for other social shortsightedness. (Such as oil wells or groundwater wells running dry, which will happen about as far ahead of us now as the Y2K problem was in 1970.) But surely this Y2K thing is exaggerated, right?
Then a few weeks ago I was talking with Ray Anderson of Interface carpet company, who told me he had spent $20 million on reprogramming within his firm and feels he's on top of Y2K. But he can't say as much for his suppliers, banks, shippers, electricity sources or customers. Then he told me about a dinner party with a high-ranking military officer at which he asked whether it might be possible that a Y2K glitch could send off a missile.
The general turned white, admitted he'd never thought of that and said he'd find out the very next day. "He won't," said Amory Lovins, who was listening in on our conversation. "It'll take at least six months."
So now I'm taking Y2K seriously. I don't plan to spend December 1999 panicking and hoarding (which, if everyone did it, would create a Y2K problem even if the computers work fine). I'm just going to plant more potatoes and squash and dry beans than usual and lay in extra firewood.
But what about that average bite of food, which presumably could be stuck on a stalled train or hung up by a bounced credit card or a missing Social Security check somewhere on its 1,300-mile journey to the average American?
Thinking about that, I asked my brilliant research assistant Diana Wright to track down where the 1,300-mile number came from. She found it in - believe it or not - a Defense Department study conducted in 1969, right about the time the programmers were creating the Y2K problem.
The Office of Civil Defense tried to calculate how to get food to surviving Americans after a nuclear attack. The analysts looked at where food was grown (mainly the Midwest) and where people lived (mainly the East), made some wild assumptions (such as ignoring fruits and vegetables) and came up with a 1,300-mile average shipping distance. (If it's any comfort, they concluded the distance would be less post-attack, because East Coast populations would be so reduced.)
The number was too low then and must be way too low now, given the globalizing market, lamb from New Zealand, oranges from Israel, apples from Chile, and 50 million more Americans than we had 30 years ago. The point about vulnerability holds, however, enhanced now by our increased dependence on foreign oil and on those millions of buggy computers mediating our transactions.
We may have gotten past the threat of full-out nuclear attack (not sure). We may get past the Y2K meltdown (far from sure). But there are other threats ahead, predictable and unpredictable, probable and improbable, from climate change to terrorists to toxic spills to ebola virus to those asteroids the movies are so obsessed with.
Why do we go on making our life support systems ever more interdependent and unresilient?
Meadows is an adjunct professor of environmental studies at Dartmouth College.

WASHINGTON - With every photo of flooded streets, burning forests

Author: Borenstein, Seth

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/washington-with-every-photo-flooded-streets/docview/332307471/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The federal government has declared 35 disasters in 1998 - with eight more disaster declarations pending - and the year is barely half over. With 34 disasters declared before July 1, the number of catastrophes is 26 percent higher than recent mid-year averages. While 1998 isn't near the 75 disasters declared in 1996, there are already more disasters this year than in either 1993 or 1995. "It has been wild," said National Climatic Data Center meteorologist Richard Heim, supervising editor of the Climate Variation Bulletin. "Anytime we've had extremes like the one-two punches like we've had in Florida, it's something to make you sit up and notice." El Ni o, the warming of the Pacific Ocean and weather scapegoat of the year, can't be blamed for this summer's floods or still-festering fires, said one of the top El Ni o gurus, Nicholas Graham, director of experimental forecasts at the International Research Institute for Climate Prediction in La Jolla, Calif.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=WASHINGTON+-+With+every+photo+of+flooded+streets%2C+burning+forests&amp;author=Borenstein%2C+Seth&amp;issn=&amp;title=Sunday+Gazette+-+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-07-05&amp;spage=13.A&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   WASHINGTON - With every photo of flooded streets, burning forests and tornado-torn mobile homes, it seems that the weather has gone stark raving mad.
But there's more at work than Mother Nature and her problem child, El Nino.
The effects of a record hot global atmosphere are being magnified by a more-overheated media beaming each catastrophe into living rooms, and a real estate boom that has placed growing numbers of people in disaster-prone areas, experts in several fields say.
"It's a combination," National Weather Service Director John J. Kelly said. "It's been a busy year. I don't think the world is going nuts. Fires have always been there. Think of life before CNN."
Even without the hype, the weather this year has lurched from extreme to extreme.
The federal government has declared 35 disasters in 1998 - with eight more disaster declarations pending - and the year is barely half over. With 34 disasters declared before July 1, the number of catastrophes is 26 percent higher than recent mid-year averages. While 1998 isn't near the 75 disasters declared in 1996, there are already more disasters this year than in either 1993 or 1995.
Flooding morphed into drought and then into fires to bedevil Florida. Vermont, Massachusetts, Ohio and West Virginia are awash in disastrous floods.
"It has been wild," said National Climatic Data Center meteorologist Richard Heim, supervising editor of the Climate Variation Bulletin. "Anytime we've had extremes like the one-two punches like we've had in Florida, it's something to make you sit up and notice."
So far this year, the insurance industry has paid out more than $4 billion in disaster claims - not including Florida fires and most flooding - which is way more than the $2.6 billion paid in all of 1997, said Gary Kerney, assistant vice president of Property Claim Services, which tracks catastrophes for the insurance industry.
In 1992, the industry paid out $23 billion, and in 1994 it paid $17 billion.
The past six weeks have driven insurers nuts.
"It's probably as busy as I've seen since I've been working in the catastrophe area," said Dan Carrigan, at State Farm, a 15-year veteran of the disaster business.
El Ni o, the warming of the Pacific Ocean and weather scapegoat of the year, can't be blamed for this summer's floods or still-festering fires, said one of the top El Ni o gurus, Nicholas Graham, director of experimental forecasts at the International Research Institute for Climate Prediction in La Jolla, Calif.
El Ni o faded by May and since then, a dozen weather disasters have plagued the nation, according to the Federal Emergency Management Agency.
Nor has El Ni o's flip side, La Ni a, a cooling of waters in the central Pacific, started yet. That brings Graham to one of the first rules of talking about the weather: Normal doesn't exist.
"We're kind of hypersensitive because the weather during the winter was really strange," Graham said. "The weather's always strange. How often do you see an article saying the weather around the country has sure been normal?"
There may always be extreme weather, but Heim, who tracks climate oddities, said this year there has been an extreme number of extremes.
Global warming has created stronger and more frequent El Ni os than in the past, said Kevin Trenberth, head of climate analysis at the National Center for Atmospheric Research. In each of the first five months of 1998, the average global temperature set a record high, the National Climatic Data Center found.
Trenberth said the Earth is sweating much like an overheated human body attempting to cool itself. The sweat in this case is more moisture in the atmosphere at some points and less in others.
He said there's a significant increase in the number of downpours of more than 2 inches of rain, while other places are completely cut off from the flow of moisture.
For example, the downpour-to-drought switch that took place in Florida has a 1-in-1,000 chance of happening naturally, said David Easterling at the National Climatic Data Center.
"Something real is going on here," Trenberth said. "Certainly this relates to the whole idea of climate change."
Bunk, said William Gray, a noted hurricane prognosticator at Colorado State University.
"I don't think the case can be made that there's more extremes of weather now than in the past," Gray said. "We've always had unusual weather - floods, droughts and everything."
Some environmentalists are playing up recent extreme weather events and they are joined by the media, which focuses on individual tragedies and misses the big picture, Gray said.
Even experts who disagree on global warming agree that people are paying more attention to weather than they used to.
When he got into the business in 1981, said Kerney, the insurance expert, "There could be tremendous damage, death and injuries caused by severe weather events in Kansas for example and you never heard about them in the media reports."
As a Washington television meteorologist, Bob Ryan, told disaster experts last week, "If you think you've seen a lot of El Ni o, wait until La Ni a gets into 'Dateline {NBC}' this winter."
Real estate development near floodprone lakes and rivers only makes matters worse.
"These are popular places to put real estate, but are also areas that are most prone to danger," said Bob Hirsch, chief hydrologist for U.S. Geological Survey.
Though tornado damage and the number of twisters are way up this year, that could also be attributed to better reporting, Kelly said.
In the past, tornadoes could have popped up in rural areas and gone unnoticed, but now the population has spread out.
Add to that relatively new radar and more storm-chasing thrill- seekers, Heim said, and few twisters go unreported.
Even the weather victims believe this is just a freak thing, but that's of little comfort. Jimmy Mutter, 16, had to flee his Mims, Fla., house at 9 p.m. Wednesday when the junkyard down the road exploded as one of the dozens of daily drought-stoked fires approached.
"It's been bad," Mutter said. "Mother Nature is just taking its course."
Unfortunately for Mutter, that course is right through his house.

A Hot (and Cold) New Investment Opportunity; Weather-Sensitive Firms Can Hedge Their Bets With Derivatives Based on Mother Nature's Whims: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Walsh, Sharon

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/hot-cold-new-investment-opportunity-weather/docview/408390898/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: What's more unpredictable than the weather? So now, some companies are sheltering themselves from adverse weather with something new -- weather derivatives. Potential players in this market include just about any company that could face financial ruin from a sudden change in the weather -- theme parks hedging against excessive summer rainfall that keeps park attendance down, beer companies worried about a summer too cool for drinking ice-cold beer, thermal-underwear retailers anxious about a mild winter, school districts preparing for an icy winter that means higher heating bills, and newly deregulated energy companies protecting themselves from unfavorable temperatures. Recently, a retail catalogue company that sells thermal underwear expressed interest in a weather hedge with Enron. The company, whose name (Lynda) Clemmons declined to disclose, sent out its catalogue in November and found through market research that people would throw the catalogue away if November was mild, suspecting they wouldn't need long underwear that winter. But customers would likely place an order if November was cold.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=A+Hot+%28and+Cold%29+New+Investment+Opportunity%3B+Weather-Sensitive+Firms+Can+Hedge+Their+Bets+With+Derivatives+Based+on+Mother+Nature%27s+Whims&amp;author=Walsh%2C+Sharon&amp;issn=01908286&amp;title=The+Washington+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-07-04&amp;spage=C.12&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Global warming, greenhouse gases and the most extreme El Nin~o since the 1800s are contributing to severe swings in climate and sending companies scurrying to find ways to protect themselves against the vagaries of Mother Nature.
For years, businesses have hedged against economic losses with financial products based on interest rates, currencies, commodities, even the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index. These products, cooked up with computers and often amazingly complicated, are called derivatives because they are financial instruments derived from different bets on the future movement of just about anything that can't be predicted with certainty.
What's more unpredictable than the weather? So now, some companies are sheltering themselves from adverse weather with something new -- weather derivatives.
Potential players in this market include just about any company that could face financial ruin from a sudden change in the weather -- theme parks hedging against excessive summer rainfall that keeps park attendance down, beer companies worried about a summer too cool for drinking ice-cold beer, thermal-underwear retailers anxious about a mild winter, school districts preparing for an icy winter that means higher heating bills, and newly deregulated energy companies protecting themselves from unfavorable temperatures.
Two years ago, Enron Corp., a Houston-based energy company, started looking at ways to hedge weather risks, said Lynda Clemmons, director of Enron Capital and Trade Resources Corp.
"We were looking at our own exposure," Clemmons said. "We wanted to start building a diverse portfolio of weather risks."
Since last July, Enron has become a major player in that market, taking a position in about 170 weather-derivatives transactions with a maximum exposure of $5 million to $10 million each.
Recently, a retail catalogue company that sells thermal underwear expressed interest in a weather hedge with Enron. The company, whose name Clemmons declined to disclose, sent out its catalogue in November and found through market research that people would throw the catalogue away if November was mild, suspecting they wouldn't need long underwear that winter. But customers would likely place an order if November was cold.
"They wanted to hedge against potential lost sales if the dates they sent out the catalogues were warm," Clemmons said.
The company could take a position that would allow it to protect itself from mild weather by buying a contract that specified it could collect a certain amount of money for each day in November when the temperature is above a certain level.
Unlike insurance against bad weather, the company buying a weather derivative would not have to prove that it actually lost money, only that the temperature was above a certain strike point in the contract. Enron or some other company would take the opposite position, betting that the temperature would not hit the specified levels.
Weather derivatives have been traded for only a little over a year, but experts think the market is set to take off and could reach $70 billion to $100 billion in just a few years, in part because the 1997-98 weather has been so unusual.
"El Nin~o has been an overwhelming factor, causing one of the warmest, wettest winters on record," said Andy Ertel, a principal at Natsource Inc., a broker in the weather derivatives market. "People have seen dramatic effects on their businesses."
In the Manhattan trading room of Natsource, Ertel and his staff preside over weather data on 8,000 cities, following heat, cold, rain and snow.
"We had a major snow-blower maker do a hedge based on a basket of 10 different cities," Ertel said. "He got killed last winter because there was so little snow in New York."
There are few reliable studies about how much money businesses lose each year because of the weather. But a study by Yale economists said that about 5 percent of the gross national product of the United States is affected by climate change -- a broader measure than weather. That's about $350 billion a year.
Natsource held its first conference on weather derivatives here in April. About 150 companies showed up -- from agricultural firms to trading houses to energy companies.
The big players right now in the weather derivatives business are companies such as Koch Industries, Enron and Natsource. Natsource is a broker between the two principals in any derivatives deal. Enron and Koch are both power companies that take a position on one side of a derivatives contract, essentially betting that the weather will be better -- or worse -- than the company betting on the other side.
One party in the deal, for instance, agrees to pay the second party if temperatures fall below an agreed-on level. If the mercury rises above a specific point, the other party pays.
Contracts can be based on degree days, minimum and maximum temperatures, rain and snowfall levels, wind speed, sunshine and humidity indexes.
A few mainstream brokerage firms are getting into the act. Merrill Lynch & Co., for example, is using weather derivatives as another way to manage risks for its clients.
"Last year was a great year to get the business started," said Eric Tell, vice president of global new derivatives at Merrill Lynch. "El Nin~o really demonstrated to companies that earnings would take a hit because of a warm winter. . . . We take this very seriously."
The industry that will likely be the biggest user of weather contracts is the utility business. More than a dozen states have deregulated utilities, allowing customers to choose their provider of gas or electricity. That competition -- and the entry of new energy providers into the market -- makes it more important for utilities to be able to hedge their risks with the weather.
One of the country's largest providers of energy, Consolidated Edison Inc., is already considering a spinoff of weather derivatives.
"We think new market participants will be looking for volume hedges," said Charlie Weliky, chief operating officer of Con Edison Energy, a subsidiary of Con Ed that is part of the restructured wholesale energy market. "If a company predicts that customers will use X amount of energy and they don't, the company won't earn as much. Con Ed Energy is planning to buy weather hedges and reformat them specifically so energy companies will want to buy them . . . in addition to buying them for ourselves."
For example, new energy companies that supply energy directly to Con Ed's retail customers must now forecast on an hourly basis the customers' energy use. Con Ed will offer a service that will allow those companies to hedge their risk in case their forecasts are wrong.
The hedges are not risk free for those taking a position in weather derivatives. "You have to have financial staying power," Weliky said. "It's just like Las Vegas. Is 100 years of weather data enough to hedge against? . . . The real downside is if you haven't done the math correctly."
With all the interest in weather, there needs to be a reliable source for weather information. That's where the U.S. government comes in.
Allan Eustis, chief of the National Weather Service's office of industrial meteorology, has become a popular figure on the weather derivatives conference circuit as companies realize they can't just stick a temperature gauge in the window to come up with an official temperature.
The National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration is the quality control because it keeps the official highs and lows that are used in legal proceedings. NOAA creates an index using heating degree days and cooling degree days. Each of those terms measures the departure of the average temperature of any day from 65 degrees -- which is considered the temperature at which most people need neither heat nor air conditioning.
"I was worried about the government weather folks being able to make or break a market," Eustis said. "But we're just the baseline forecaster against whom they bet."

Disasters aren't all nature's handiwork/ Weather's extremes magnified by media reports

Chagall has arrived, but don't ask how Vanessa Thorpe on the secrecy and danger that surround the transport of art: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Thorpe, Vanessa

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/chagall-has-arrived-dont-ask-how-vanessa-thorpe/docview/312687974/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: IN ONE of the most complicated fine art transport operations ever carried out, more than eight separate agencies have combined to bring a series of large Chagall murals to Britain for the first time. The perilous six-day journey began two weeks ago at the murals' permanent home in Moscow's Tretyakov Gallery. Each work was painstakingly packed in specially made Swedish crates and then moved by crane. From there, they travelled in convoy by road and sea, following a secret route and under the devoted guardianship of a team of Russian curators and restorers, until they reached the Royal Academy in London. The unique Chagall paintings, which are watercolour and gouache on canvas, at one time decorated the walls of Moscow's Yiddish Theatre and show a colourful assortment of Jewish entertainers. Neglected for years as the Yiddish tradition dwindled under Communism, the recently restored works will be on display at the Royal Academy of Arts from Thursday. Each mural has been positioned around the main hall of the academy's Sackler Gallery in a close copy of the positioning originally intended by the artist.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Chagall+has+arrived%2C+but+don%27t+ask+how+Vanessa+Thorpe+on+the+secrecy+and+danger+that+surround+the+transport+of+art&amp;author=Thorpe%2C+Vanessa&amp;issn=09519467&amp;title=The+Independent&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-06-28&amp;spage=9&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: IN ONE of the most complicated fine art transport operations ever carried out, more than eight separate agencies have combined to bring a series of large Chagall murals to Britain for the first time.
The perilous six-day journey began two weeks ago at the murals' permanent home in Moscow's Tretyakov Gallery. Each work was painstakingly packed in specially made Swedish crates and then moved by crane. From there, they travelled in convoy by road and sea, following a secret route and under the devoted guardianship of a team of Russian curators and restorers, until they reached the Royal Academy in London.
The intricate transport operation surrounding the murals is new proof of the way modern technology is defeating the multiple hazards of moving international works of art. Hurdles such as customs, climate change and security have all now become negotiable. The price tag though, remains high.
Government indemnity schemes such as those currently underwriting exhibitions in Great Britain and the United States have helped to make insurance less expensive, but the cost of hiring lorries and equipment can still be prohibitive for all but the biggest name galleries. The fee for the hire of the climate- controlled vehicles used on the murals' journey from Moscow, for example, was around pounds 12,300.
The unique Chagall paintings, which are watercolour and gouache on canvas, at one time decorated the walls of Moscow's Yiddish Theatre and show a colourful assortment of Jewish entertainers. Neglected for years as the Yiddish tradition dwindled under Communism, the recently restored works will be on display at the Royal Academy of Arts from Thursday. Each mural has been positioned around the main hall of the academy's Sackler Gallery in a close copy of the positioning originally intended by the artist.
The largest painting, 284x787cm, faces what would have been the entrance to the original Yiddish theatre and features, among many other characters, the image of Chagall himself being carried by the theatre manager towards the figure of the proprietor. On the right hangs a group of individual panels: one showing a fiddler, one a dancer and one a cantor.
The curtains that Chagall designed to hang around the stage have long since disintegrated, but bore the image of a goat's head, the artist's own shorthand symbol for Judaism. An accompanying ceiling painting has also been lost, but the Royal Academy's Sue Compton believes it portrayed a loving couple rather like the pair of entwined, translucent dancers on the mural which now hangs at the back of the make-believe auditorium.
When the rolls of canvas arrived in London at the beginning of last week, they were slowly stretched out over wooden frames and then left overnight to settle on a table that took up most of the gallery.
"Unfortunately our chief restorer, the man who breathed life back into these canvasses, is now dead," sighed the Tretyakov's curator of 19th- century and early 20th-century art, Alla Loukanova, as she watched over the unpacking of her babies. "All the same, it is very good indeed for me to see them here."
The restored murals were first shown in the new wing of her gallery in Moscow seven years ago. "There were still people around in Moscow who remembered them," said Miss Loukanova, "although, of course, only in the second Jewish Theatre building - a larger one, which was in turn closed down in 1949 when the tradition was destroyed."
Visitors to the academy's Chagall exhibition this week should be mindful of the expense and effort which have gone into its staging, according to the former freelance curator Michael Tarantino, the man now in charge of organising exhibitions at Oxford's Museum of Modern Art.
"It is so difficult to stage an important foreign show these days," he said. "The hire fees are very high, unless you can work in collaboration with other institutions to reduce the cost of catalogues, and even then, when you add the transport to the total, it shoots up."
The newest technology does not come cheap, and Helen Wilson, exhibition coordinator for Wingate and Johnston, the Peckham-based company that brought in the Chagalls, argues that cutting edge equipment is vital because it reduces the risk of damage or theft. For her the worrying part about the murals' transportation was coping with Russian bureaucracy.
"It was hard to find out what the regulations were and what was going on," she said. "Although, to be fair, there is now a transportation company which is allied to the State Russian Museum and that allows you to have some confidence in the vehicles provided."
Ms Wilson refuses to discuss the route the convoy took and confirms that her staff are instructed not to talk about their precious cargo during the journey. "We keep things very low key, except in countries where a police escort is provided, such as Italy or Spain," she said.
"It is usually an indemnity condition that the vehicle is never left alone. So this time the driver slept in the cab."
To reduce the risk of theft, customs officials now rarely examine fine art and simply accept signed authorities from the museums. Any unnecessary opening of containers could result in theft and could destroy the controlled atmosphere. And it is damage, says James Emson of the Art Loss Register, which is the chief enemy.
"Insurance is high because of the dangers of breaking or knocking the work of art in transit," he said. "There was frightful damage, for example, nine years ago at Heathrow when fire ravaged a depot."
Theft still poses a problem. There are currently 355 stolen Picassos, 109 Andy Warhols and 121 Rembrandts unaccounted for somewhere in the world and, while it might seem unlikely that anyone would attempt to walk off with a crate full of Chagall murals, Mr Emson points out that, even when a work is too well known to be sold, a ransom can be demanded.
On two separate occasions in the past decade, masterpieces, including two Picassos and a Meijer de Haan, have been temporarily snatched as they languished in customs at JFK airport in New York.
"Security is only ever as good as the people you have," said Mr Emson. "That is always the weak link, and works of art are always vulnerable in transit."
Illustration Caption: The Chagall murals leave Moscow's Tretyakov Gallery (left) for their heavily protected six-day journey to the Royal Academy of Arts in London (right) PHOTOGRAPHS BY YURI GRIPAS/NEVILLE ELDER

House GOP Boosts Antidrug Programs Amid Effort to Limit Fallout on Tobacco

Author: By David Rogers

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/house-gop-boosts-antidrug-programs-amid-effort/docview/398637172/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: House Republicans are pumping billions into antidrug programs that have also become a front line of defense for the GOP in the political wars over tobacco this election year. From the Customs Service to the Pentagon and even the Small Business Administration, appropriations bills this week are heavy with spending items ordered up by a leadership drug task force. About $2 billion in add-ons are being sought, running from $120,000 for four Toyota Land Cruisers for drug agents along the Bolivian border to hundreds of millions for Coast Guard cutters and Customs Service planes. Just as the White House paints the GOP as serving big tobacco companies at the expense of children, Republicans counter that President Clinton has been lax in fighting drugs. Increasingly, GOP leaders speak of tobacco in the same context as drugs, and the party's teenage-smoking bill will be packaged with antidrug legislation next month.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=House+GOP+Boosts+Antidrug+Programs+Amid+Effort+to+Limit+Fallout+on+Tobacco&amp;author=By+David+Rogers&amp;issn=00999660&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-06-26&amp;spage=1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: WASHINGTON -- House Republicans are pumping billions into antidrug programs that have also become a front line of defense for the GOP in the political wars over tobacco this election year.
From the Customs Service to the Pentagon and even the Small Business Administration, appropriations bills this week are heavy with spending items ordered up by a leadership drug task force. About $2 billion in add-ons are being sought, running from $120,000 for four Toyota Land Cruisers for drug agents along the Bolivian border to hundreds of millions for Coast Guard cutters and Customs Service planes.
Budget restraints have so far prevented funding of the most costly new hardware, but Speaker Newt Gingrich suggested yesterday that a "year-end deal" with the White House could yet yield extra money for drug interdiction.
The speaker made the drug war an early priority this year. But it has taken on greater political significance now with the collapse of major tobacco legislation.
Just as the White House paints the GOP as serving big tobacco companies at the expense of children, Republicans counter that President Clinton has been lax in fighting drugs. Increasingly, GOP leaders speak of tobacco in the same context as drugs, and the party's teenage-smoking bill will be packaged with antidrug legislation next month.
The Federal Trade Commission would be given increased power to police tobacco ads aimed at underage smokers. And a new Republican-backed national advertising campaign against teenage smoking would also target drug use and be run through an existing antidrug office in the government.
Melding the two issues may be good politics, but the leadership itself still struggles with the message. "Tobacco is a drug," said House Majority Whip Tom DeLay (R., Texas) flatly. Yet Ohio Rep. John Boehner, chairman of the House GOP conference, took a more resigned tone. "Teenagers are going to smoke," he said. "It's one of those rites of passage."
Tobacco wasn't the GOP's only worry as lawmakers went home for the July Fourth recess yesterday. Conservative plans to kill funding for the National Endowment for the Arts were upended in a surprise vote in the House Appropriations Committee. And on the House floor, 155 Republicans joined an almost equal number of Democrats to trounce a leadership-backed resolution governing debate on a Treasury and general government spending bill.
Election-year fears in the Farm Belt prompted a Capitol news conference by Mr. Gingrich promising "to lead on the issues that matter to American farmers and ranchers." The House GOP was embarrassed last month by its handling of popular crop-insurance legislation, but Mr. Gingrich pledged action on trade and the long-delayed debate on financing for the International Monetary Fund.
The trade votes are expected in September, but the IMF issue could be joined as early as next month when the House takes up the foreign-aid budget for the fiscal year that begins Oct. 1.
Following is a summary of major spending legislation yesterday:
House and Senate Appropriations Committees approved competing versions of a $13 billion-plus natural-resources bill funding the Interior Department and arts agencies. The NEA emerged triumphant, after a 31-27 vote restoring $98 million for the coming fiscal year. But the Clinton administration's wildlife and environmental initiatives took a beating on both sides of the Capitol.
The House bill would terminate a Columbia River basin environmental-planning project covering millions of acres in six Western states. The Senate measure adds dozens of pages of its own affecting the management of federal lands and forests as well as the rights of Indian tribes. Oil interests won a one-year moratorium on new regulations governing royalty payments owed the government for oil produced on public lands. Ranchers blocked the introduction of grizzly bears into Idaho and Montana, and Appropriations Chairman Ted Stevens added a provision to allow construction of a gravel road in the Izembek National Wildlife Refuge, a protected wilderness area in Alaska.
The chairman has fiercely defended the amendment, entitled a "health and safety act" for the small hamlet of King Cove, which would be served by the road. But the proposal has infuriated environmentalists, and Interior Secretary Bruce Babbitt says the sacrifice of habitat is "incalculable."
The White House won at least a partial victory in its efforts to raise the ceiling on government-insured home mortgages. Despite misgivings by the GOP leadership, the House Appropriations panel endorsed a move allowing the Federal Housing Administration to insure mortgages up to $109,000 nationwide -- an increase of $23,000. For high-cost areas, the ceiling would jump to $197,000 from $170,000.
The decision is in line with a similar Senate plan and fits into a $93.5 billion bill funding not only housing but also veterans, science and environmental programs. As part of the same bill, auto-industry allies have added sweeping new language intended to bar the Environmental Protection Agency from educational and outreach activities related to climate change and last year's Kyoto Protocol global-warming pact.
-- A $33.2 billion budget for the departments of Commerce, State and Justice won approval from the Senate Appropriations panel after fierce, angry exchanges between Chairman Stevens and Sen. Slade Gorton (R., Wash.,) over a fishing-rights dispute in West Coast waters. Mr. Stevens, who is waging a war with Norwegian-financed American Seafood Inc., based in Mr. Gorton's home state, agreed to back down on major points. But the final bill would still bar three large trawlers owned by the Seattle company from returning to U.S. waters until the fall of 1999. The measure also sets a standard that individual fishing quotas issued after Sept. 30, 2000, will go only to U.S. citizens or entities that are 75%-owned by Americans.
Credit: Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal

Prairie wildlife will likely suffer in global warming: [Final Edition]

Author: Struzik, Ed

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/prairie-wildlife-will-likely-suffer-global/docview/254267502/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Disease and habitat loss will take a major toll on the millions of birds and animals that make use of the Prairies if the climate warms as predicted, says a study commissioned by the World Wildlife Fund. Droughts are a fact of life on the Prairies regardless of greenhouse gas emissions. A serious drought generally strikes the Prairies once every 30 to 50 years. The last significant drought in 1988 led to net income losses for farmers that reached as high as 78 per cent in Saskatchewan. More than $1.3 billion in drought, crop insurance and special drought assistance was paid out to mitigate the damages that occurred that year. Scientists asked by the department to look at global warming scenarios are predicting warmer temperatures, generally drier conditions and less severe winters for the Prairies.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Prairie+wildlife+will+likely+suffer+in+global+warming&amp;author=Struzik%2C+Ed&amp;issn=&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-06-13&amp;spage=H.6&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Disease and habitat loss will take a major toll on the millions of birds and animals that make use of the Prairies if the climate warms as predicted, says a study commissioned by the World Wildlife Fund.
The year-long study commissioned scientists to use sophisticated computer modelling programs to simulate potential changes in the environment based on the rise in greenhouse gases.
Predictive studies on climate change have been done before, but this one was unique in that it focused on the impact on vegetation and habitat.
University of Toronto scientist Jay Markham says such changes can be predicted by looking at climatic conditions that favour different types of plants and then by comparing them with the various climate scenarios in the future.
"Whichever scenario you use, and there are a number of them out there," says Markham, "the end result is that the Prairies end up being particularly hard hit."
Malcolm says he was initially surprised by the findings which he arrived at with Adam Markham of World Wildlife Fund International in Washington.
"I thought that we would see the tree line rising quickly up the Rocky Mountains," he says. "And that is the case. But the pace of change on the Prairies was most pronounced."
Looked at more closely, the results aren't so difficult to explain, says Malcolm.
The Prairies are home to hundreds of thousands of shallow potholes and wetlands that provide critical habitat for millions of shorebirds, ducks, and geese, which in turn are an important food source for predators.
Even under so-called normal conditions, the slightest variation in precipitation and temperatures can significantly alter the size of these wetlands in a given year.
"Many kinds of migratory birds take shelter in temporary marshes that form on the prairie early in the year," says Malcolm.
"As the climate warms, these marshes will dry up earlier. Farmers will be tempted to use these dry conditions to cultivate earlier. And this will force birds to fly farther north for acceptable nesting sites."
Any trend towards further warming will likely dry up many of these wetlands altogether, irrespective of the fact that global change may bring more precipitation, says Malcolm.
Droughts are a fact of life on the Prairies regardless of greenhouse gas emissions. A serious drought generally strikes the Prairies once every 30 to 50 years. The last significant drought in 1988 led to net income losses for farmers that reached as high as 78 per cent in Saskatchewan. More than $1.3 billion in drought, crop insurance and special drought assistance was paid out to mitigate the damages that occurred that year.
The World Wildlife Fund study falls into line with many of the predictions of Environment Canada.
Scientists asked by the department to look at global warming scenarios are predicting warmer temperatures, generally drier conditions and less severe winters for the Prairies.

Water everywhere - except in the tap: It costs more to buy a house beside a river or the sea - for now. Changes in climate could alter all that. Anne Spackman reports: [Surveys edition]

Author: Spackman, Anne

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/water-everywhere-except-tap-costs-more-buy-house/docview/248715205/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Homeowners still counting the cost of Easter's freak rains in Britain might be interested to hear what is happening to flood victims in America. Residents in exposed areas of the US - in particular, Florida - are discovering there is a limit to the pity and the pay-outs their plight engenders. Some Florida homeowners have already seen their flood insurance premiums quadruple. As the state's weather becomes more volatile, the fear is that cover may be withdrawn completely from those living in the most vulnerable areas. Insurers in the UK insist flood cover will continue to be provided as part of a standard insurance package. (In this respect the UK is unusual, as in most European countries flood protection is either an extra or not offered at all.) However, coastal erosion is not an insurable risk and tends to affect properties in the same vulnerable areas - particularly East Anglia.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Water+everywhere+-+except+in+the+tap%3A+It+costs+more+to+buy+a+house+beside+a+river+or+the+sea+-+for+now.+Changes+in+climate+could+alter+all+that.+Anne+Spackman+reports%3A&amp;author=Spackman%2C+Anne&amp;issn=03071766&amp;title=Financial+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-06-13&amp;spage=05&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Homeowners still counting the cost of Easter's freak rains in Britain might be interested to hear what is happening to flood victims in America. Residents in exposed areas of the US - in particular, Florida - are discovering there is a limit to the pity and the pay-outs their plight engenders.
Some Florida homeowners have already seen their flood insurance premiums quadruple. As the state's weather becomes more volatile, the fear is that cover may be withdrawn completely from those living in the most vulnerable areas.
For now, houses on the seafront in the UK are expected to sell for a 20 per cent premium; in the US, with its far sunnier coastline, the premium is even higher. But if the evidence is that global warming is changing weather patterns for good, buyers will have to balance great views against great risks.
Figures from the Insurance Information Institute show that 21 of 25 of the biggest weather-related insurance pay-outs in the US have occurred in the past 10 years. Hurricane Andrew, which hit south Florida in August 1992, was the event that changed attitudes.
It produced a bill for damages of $15.5bn. Only one previous incident had exceeded the $1bn barrier. "If a major hurricane like that were to hit a city like Miami, the damages claims could exceed $50bn," says the institute's Loretta Worters.
"All coastal areas are high-risk, whether they are in Florida, Long Island or New Jersey. It's a question of how close the individual property is to the water."
She says the size of the insurance pay-outs, coupled with increasingly sophisticated systems of risk analysis, mean homeowners' "catastrophe loading" has risen fourfold since Hurricane Andrew. Now, insurers are questioning whether the system can continue in its present form. "There is a reluctance to continue to pay out," she says.
By comparison, the UK's weather-related claims are tiny. In 1997 they cost the insurance industry £377m, compared with £393m for subsidence (also a weather-related issue), £247m for fire and £513m for domestic burglaries.
Insurers in the UK insist flood cover will continue to be provided as part of a standard insurance package. (In this respect the UK is unusual, as in most European countries flood protection is either an extra or not offered at all.) However, coastal erosion is not an insurable risk and tends to affect properties in the same vulnerable areas - particularly East Anglia.
The Association of British Insurers dismisses reports that surfaced after the floods which suggested that areas such as East Anglia and the Northamptonshire flood plain might be blacklisted by insurance companies. But they admit insurance costs could well rise.
"If flooding has occurred more than once, an insurer might refuse cover until certain protection works have been carried out," says Vic Rance of the ABI. "The company might increase the premium or impose a high excess in case of flooding, but it would be exceedingly rare for cover to be withdrawn."
It is possible that insurers will adopt the postcode system to identify areas of highest flood risk, as they do when setting premiums for subsidence and burglary. "Insurers may start to differentiate on flood risk in the long term," Mr Rance says, "but so far flooding has been so erratic, it seems unlikely".
In Florida and California, insurers are most concerned about damage to coastal property caused by hurricanes, mudslides and earthquakes. In the UK, insurers say they are "relaxed" about the coastline. They believe coastal defences will withstand the expected increase in sea levels in East Anglia, of 38cms by 2050.
However, if climate change intensifies and sea levels rise more quickly, the industry would have to reassess that risk. As a result, a number of UK insurers, led by General Accident, have signed up to a United Nations initiative on environmental responsibility.
"If there is an increase in severe weather, it is the insurance industry which will bear the brunt initially," David Crichton of GA points out. "We are lobbying governments to do something about climate change and committing ourselves to behave in an environmentally responsible way."
In the short term, the UK industry is far more worried about river, rather than sea flooding. It is worried that planners are approving housing developments in areas prone to flooding.
"With the pressure on available land we are concerned that building may take place against the advice of the Environment Agency," says Vic Rance of the ABI. There may be a shift towards the system which exists in Scotland, whereby developers must check that insurance cover will be available before they start new housing schemes.
There is one other fear associated with climate change and new housing developments: it is not that too much water will come through the door, but that not enough will come out of the taps.
Michael Sayer, a Norfolk farmer, is also chairman of the local branch of the Country Landowners Association. His family have been measuring rainfall on their land since the day his father returned from war in 1945. For 30 years their recordings barely varied. Since then, they have become increasingly volatile. April was the wettest month ever recorded but there have also been periods of serious drought.
He says: "The basic theory of climate change is that as the hydrologic cycle intensifies, you get an increase in overall rainfall, but the distribution changes. There are more flash floods, more run-off and more erosion, but there are longer periods of drought. Our figures would suggest this is happening."
Such were the problems with water supply caused by drought in his part of Norfolk that a new borehole had to be sunk on Sayer's land to cope with the demands of a huge new housing development on the outskirts of Norwich, called Thorpe Marriott. "They simply ran out of water," Sayer says.
Five years on, building is continuing. But Sayer fears his water supply is not growing at the same rate. The CLA is opposing Norfolk's plans for a further 61,000 new homes on the basis that there is no evidence of sufficient water to supply them.
While the US faces up to a potentially wet and stormy future, it seems that homeowners in the UK may be in danger of facing the ancient mariner's dilemma: "Water, water, everywhere, nor any drop to drink". Copyright Financial Times Limited 1998. All Rights Reserved.

The best bet on global warming: [Final Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/best-bet-on-global-warming/docview/269916536/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: In recommending early action rather than more study, [Stuart Smith] noted that if the worries about global warming prove premature, Canadians will have time to adjust their response. He put needed perspective on the cost issue by reminding Canadians of the important side benefits of boosting pollution control and energy efficiency: cleaner, healthier air, and a more efficient and therefore productive economy.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=The+best+bet+on+global+warming&amp;author=&amp;issn=11899417&amp;title=The+Spectator&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-06-08&amp;spage=A.8&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: A non-partisan panel of distinguished Canadians is offering prudent advice in urging government, business and individuals to take action against global warming now. As the advisory group says, Canada needs to reduce greenhouse gas emissions if only as insurance to protect itself against the potential worst effects of artificial climate change.
Coming from 25 Order of Canada recipients who approached the problem in objective fashion, the panel's report has considerable credibility. All regions and sectors of the economy were represented in the three-month study, including business, labour, environmental organizations and First Nations. The group reached a consensus that makes good sense.
Although they acknowledged that uncertainty surrounds the science of climate change, the panelists concluded there's enough evidence to suggest that rising temperatures pose potentially calamitous consequences.
They made a compelling argument that unanswered questions and concerns about the costs of better pollution controls can't be used to justify inaction. Former Ontario Liberal leader Stuart Smith, the panel moderator, made a telling point in urging Canadians to heed the lessons of the Titanic. "If we think those icebergs are out there, we'd better start steering now, because if we wait until we're 100-per-cent sure, we won't be able to steer away fast enough."
In recommending early action rather than more study, Smith noted that if the worries about global warming prove premature, Canadians will have time to adjust their response. He put needed perspective on the cost issue by reminding Canadians of the important side benefits of boosting pollution control and energy efficiency: cleaner, healthier air, and a more efficient and therefore productive economy.
Those benefits need to be considered along with the costs of controls -- and the costs of extreme weather events which could be caused by global warming. A new Environment Canada report suggests that warmer temperatures pose the danger of a rise in the number of extremely hot summer days; more heavy rainfalls, flash floods and hail storms; and fewer light rainfalls. Problems such as these would put severe strain on municipal sewers.
The panel's message should kick-start the federal and provincial governments to begin living up to the commitments they've made on global warming. At the Kyoto climate change summit last December, Canada agreed to reduce greenhouse gas emissions by six per cent below 1990 levels as of 2012. But instead of proceeding, federal and provincial politicians already seem to have lost any sense of urgency. In fact, at a federal-provincial meeting in April, environment and energy ministers put concrete action on hold. They decided to spend the next 18 months studying the economic impact of emission cuts. That's a far cry from a call to action.
The lack of leadership in Ottawa is especially disappointing. All talk, and no action, is an invitation to failure and a gamble that simply isn't worth taking.

Title: The best bet on global warming:   [Final Edition ]
