Meacher allays no-go insurance area fears: [CITY Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/meacher-allays-no-go-insurance-area-fears/docview/324447416/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The Government yesterday moved to avert fears that some parts of Britain could become "no-go" areas for insurance companies, who fear huge bills through flooding due to global warming. Environment Minister Mr Michael Meacher urged the public not to "leap to conclusions" after a warning from a climate action group that one firm had stopped cover in high-risk areas.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Meacher+allays+no-go+insurance+area+fears&amp;author=&amp;issn=&amp;title=Birmingham+Post&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-06-06&amp;spage=2&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: The Government yesterday moved to avert fears that some parts of Britain could become "no-go" areas for insurance companies, who fear huge bills through flooding due to global warming.
Environment Minister Mr Michael Meacher urged the public not to "leap to conclusions" after a warning from a climate action group that one firm had stopped cover in high-risk areas.
Mr Walter Menzies, chief executive of Sustainability North West, said insurance companies in the low-lying Fylde area of Lancashire feared the costs of rising sea levels could run into millions.
"One insurance company has stopped cover in high-risk areas, so we have clear implications," he said.
Mr Menzies spoke out at a Department of the Environment, Transport and the Regions news conference in London on the threat to the economy in every part of the UK as the effects of greenhouse gases on the atmosphere take hold.
Mr Meacher, who was unveiling the Government's plans for the world's first integrated inquiry into the impact of climate change, said: "I don't think we should leap to the conclusion that the Fylde coast is a no-go area for future development."

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/ministers-insurance-message/docview/323078951/se-2?accountid=10226

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Minister%27s+insurance+message&amp;author=&amp;issn=&amp;title=Evening+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-06-06&amp;spage=4&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: THE Government is trying to to avert fears that some parts of Britain could become "no-go" areas for insurance companies which fear huge bills through flooding due to global warming.
Environment Minister Michael Meacher urged the public not to "leap to conclusions" after a warning from a climate action group that one firm had stopped cover in high risk areas.
Walter Menzies, chief executive of Sustainability North West, told a news conference that insurance companies in the low-lying Fylde coastal area of Lancashire feared the costs of rising sea levels could run into millions.
Mr Menzies said: "The North West is lucky enough to have the partnerships in place to fight and limit the damage which climate change may wreak upon the region and to make a real contribution to slashing the nation's greenhouse gas emissions.
"If an industrialised, heavily-developed region like ours can get its act together, so can the rest of the UK."
Mr Meacher, who yesterday5 unveiled the Government's plans for the world's first integrated inquiry into the impact of climate change, said: "I don't think we should leap to the conclusion that the Fylde coast is a no-go area for future development.
"But it is precisely this attempt to quantify the consequences of climate change that we need to look at.

The salting of the earth: [1ST Edition]

Author: Derbyshire, David

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/salting-earth/docview/320964763/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: HALF of Britain's most fertile farmland could be barren within 100 years because of global warming, Government scientists warned yesterday. All land lying less than 15ft above sea level will be at risk from salt contamination as the oceans slowly rise, they said. For the next two years, scientists will study the likely effects of climate change on East Anglia and the North-West. A full UK study will follow.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=The+salting+of+the+earth&amp;author=Derbyshire%2C+David&amp;issn=03077578&amp;title=Daily+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-06-06&amp;spage=24&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   HALF of Britain's most fertile farmland could be barren within 100 years because of global warming, Government scientists warned yesterday.
All land lying less than 15ft above sea level will be at risk from salt contamination as the oceans slowly rise, they said.
That would devastate the agricultural industry and force Britain to rely more and more on imported food.
The predictions came as the Government launched the big- gest study yet into how global warming will change our lives.
For the next two years, scientists will study the likely effects of climate change on East Anglia and the North-West. A full UK study will follow.
Although a handful of scientists still refuse to accept that greenhouse gases from industry and cars are warming the planet, almost all agree global temperatures are rising.
The world is predicted to become 3.5c warmer over the next century, causing ice caps to melt and sea levels to rise by 20 inches.
The higher waters will flood parts of Britain's low-lying coastlines and put the fenlands and farms of East Anglia at risk.
'Salt intrusion could be an even bigger problem than rising seas themselves,' according to a report from the Department of Environment, Transport and the Regions published to mark World
Environment Day. 'Half the UK's Grade One agricultural land is below the five-metre contour and much of it could become saline.' Scientists at Oxford University's Environmental Change Unit will draw up climate predictions for the entire UK.
East Anglia, with its flood risk, and the North West, with its flat Lancashire plain and high rainfall, will be the first areas examined.
Launching the scheme, Environment Minister Michael Meacher said Britain had agreed to cut its carbon dioxide emissions by 20 per cent of the 1990 level within the next 11 years.
'However, we mustn't forget too that climate change may also offer opportunities for businesses, such as the opportunity to grow crops previously suited to sunnier climes such as olives, grapes and sunflowers,' he said.
'Whether climate change represents a threat or an opportunity, there will certainly only be advantages to be had in being ahead of the game when it comes to being best placed to deal with it.' Some experts fear the drought of 1995-97 and April's records floods are the first signs of climate change.
Last year, one insurance company stopped offering cover to firms starting up in high-risk flood and storm zones. However, it has since changed that policy.

Title: The salting of the earth:   [1ST Edition ]

Canadians urged to reduce consumption of energy: [Final Edition]

Author: Duffy, Andrew

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/canadians-urged-reduce-consumption-energy/docview/349123567/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: He left Wednesday, along with the 24 other distinguished Canadians brought together to assess the threat posed by climate change, convinced that the government must take immediate action. The panel, however, warned Canadians to prepare themselves for higher energy costs since any action to seriously reduce greenhouse gases will likely raise the cost of fuel. "Even if we have tradable greenhouse gas emission permits, that will have the effect of putting a cost on the release of carbon dioxide. So one way or another, anything that releases carbon dioxide will have to cost more." [Stuart Smith] said Canadians concerned about the cost of taking action should heed the lesson of the Titanic.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Canadians+urged+to+reduce+consumption+of+energy&amp;author=Duffy%2C+Andrew&amp;issn=08373434&amp;title=The+Standard&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-06-04&amp;spage=A.5&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: OTTAWA - Physicist Colin Franklin joined the National Forum on Climate Change as a skeptic, believing the dangers of global warming to be greatly exaggerated.
He left Wednesday, along with the 24 other distinguished Canadians brought together to assess the threat posed by climate change, convinced that the government must take immediate action.
"I guess I got converted," Franklin said in an interview. "There's a lot of evidence that the majority of scientists throughout the world think this is a problem.
"And from the point of view of precautionary action, from the point of view of insurance, I was convinced there's a need to take action now."
The citizens' panel, composed mostly of Order-of-Canada recipients, issued a declaration Wednesday that calls for urgent action by government, industry and individuals to reduce greenhouse gas emissions.
"Canadians have little to lose and everything to gain by taking action immediately. Each of us can and must make a difference," the declaration says.
The panel, however, warned Canadians to prepare themselves for higher energy costs since any action to seriously reduce greenhouse gases will likely raise the cost of fuel.
When burned, coal, oil and gasoline produce carbon dioxide, a greenhouse gas that most scientists believe is responsible for trapping heat inside the earth's atmosphere.
Natural Resources Minister Ralph Goodale told reporters he remains unconvinced that energy prices will have to rise during the fight against greenhouse gases, provided the country uses market instruments to keep costs low.
But Dr. Stuart Smith, chair of the National Round Table on the Environment and the Economy, said higher energy prices are inevitable if climate change is to be addressed.
"Even if we have tradable greenhouse gas emission permits, that will have the effect of putting a cost on the release of carbon dioxide. So one way or another, anything that releases carbon dioxide will have to cost more." Smith said Canadians concerned about the cost of taking action should heed the lesson of the Titanic.
"If we think those are icebergs out there, we'd better start steering now," he said, "because if we wait until we're 100-per- cent sure, we won't be able to steer away fast enough."
The panel urged Canadians to reduce energy consumption by taking public transit and better insulating their homes.

People: Franklin, Colin

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Distinguished+Canadians+call+for+%60action+now%27+on+climate+change&amp;author=Andrew+Duffy+Southam+Newspapers&amp;issn=0839203X&amp;title=Sault+Star&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-06-04&amp;spage=A.5&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: OTTAWA -- Physicist Colin Franklin joined the National Forum on Climate Change as a skeptic, believing the dangers of global warming to be greatly exaggerated.
He left Wednesday, along with the 24 other distinguished Canadians brought together to assess the threat posed by climate change, convinced that the government must take immediate action.
"I guess I got converted," Franklin said in an interview. "There's a lot of evidence that the majority of scientists throughout the world think this is a problem.
"And from the point of view of precautionary action, from the point of view of insurance, I was convinced there's a need to take action now."
The citizens' panel, composed mostly of Order-of-Canada recipients, issued a declaration Wednesday that calls for urgent action by government, industry and individuals to reduce greenhouse gas emissions.
Canada agreed in Kyoto, Japan, to reduce its emissions by six per cent over 1990 levels by 2008-2012.
The panel called that commitment a "crucial first effort to move the economy in the right direction."
"Canadians have little to lose and everything to gain by taking action immediately. Each of us can and must make a difference," the declaration says.
The panel, however, warned Canadians to prepare themselves for higher energy costs since any action to seriously reduce greenhouse gases will likely raise the cost of fuel.
When burned, coal, oil and gasoline produce carbon dioxide, a greenhouse gas that most scientists believe is responsible for trapping heat inside the earth's atmosphere.
Natural Resources Minister Ralph Goodale told reporters he remains unconvinced that energy prices will have to rise during the fight against greenhouse gases provided the country uses market instruments to keep costs low.
But Dr. Stuart Smith, chair of the National Round Table on the Environment and the Economy, said higher energy prices are inevitable if climate change is to be addressed.
"Even if we have tradable greenhouse gas emission permits, that will have the effect of putting a cost on the release of carbon dioxide. So one way or another, anything that releases carbon dioxide will have to cost more."
The citizens' panel said important uncertainties remain about the science of climate change, but those cannot be used to justify inaction.
Smith said Canadians concerned about the cost of taking action should heed the lesson of the Titanic.
"If we think those are icebergs out there, we'd better start steering now," he said, "because if we wait until we're 100-per- cent sure, we won't be able to steer away fast enough."
The round-table brought together 25 Canadians from diverse backgrounds -- people like dancer Frank Augustyn, Olympian Linda Thom and country singer Hank Smith --to examine for three months the issue of climate change from the vantage point of the average citizen.
They heard from climate experts, business executives and dissenting scientists.

People: Franklin, Colin

Full text:   Physicist Colin Franklin joined the National Forum on Climate Change as a skeptic, believing the dangers of global warming to be greatly exaggerated.
He left Wednesday, along with the 24 other distinguished Canadians brought together to assess the threat posed by climate change, convinced that the government must take immediate action.
"I guess I got converted," Franklin said in an interview. "There's a lot of evidence that the majority of scientists throughout the world think this is a problem.
"And from the point of view of precautionary action, from the point of view of insurance, I was convinced there's a need to take action now."
The citizens' panel, composed mostly of Order-of-Canada recipients, issued a declaration Wednesday that calls for urgent action by government, industry and individuals to reduce greenhouse gas emissions.
Agreed to reduction
Canada agreed in Kyoto, Japan, to reduce its emissions by six per cent over 1990 levels by 2008-2012.
The panel called that commitment a "crucial first effort to move the economy in the right direction."
"Canadians have little to lose and everything to gain by taking action immediately. Each of us can and must make a difference," the declaration says.
The panel, however, warned Canadians to prepare themselves for higher energy costs since any action to seriously reduce greenhouse gases will likely raise the cost of fuel.
When burned, coal, oil and gasoline produce carbon dioxide, a greenhouse gas that most scientists believe is responsible for trapping heat inside the earth's atmosphere.
Natural Resources Minister Ralph Goodale told reporters he remains unconvinced that energy prices will have to rise during the fight against greenhouse gases provided the country uses market instruments to keep costs low.
But Dr. Stuart Smith, chair of the National Round Table on the Environment and the Economy, said higher energy prices are inevitable if climate change is to be addressed.
"Even if we have tradable greenhouse gas emission permits, that will have the effect of putting a cost on the release of carbon dioxide.
"So one way or another, anything that releases carbon dioxide will have to cost more."
The citizens' panel said important uncertainties remain about the science of climate change, but those cannot be used to justify inaction.
Smith said Canadians concerned about the cost of taking action should heed the lesson of the Titanic.
"If we think those are icebergs out there, we'd better start steering now," he said, "because if we wait until we're 100-per- cent sure, we won't be able to steer away fast enough."
Diverse backgrounds
The round-table brought together 25 Canadians from diverse backgrounds -- people like dancer Frank Augustyn, Olympian Linda Thom and country singer Hank Smith -- to examine for three months the issue of climate change from the vantage point of the average citizen. They heard from climate experts, business executives and dissenting scientists.
Although at times overwhelmed by the complexity of the issue, the panel decided on what Smith calls a balanced, pragmatic, Canadian- type approach:
"They're saying we don't know for sure, but we have enough evidence that we have to start acting upon it now."
Both environmentalists and oil industry representatives applauded the citizens' efforts.
Elizabeth May, director of the Sierra Club of Canada, said she was pleased unbiased Canadians heard all the evidence.
Dee Parkinson-Marcous, president of Ensyn Energy Corp., said the energy industry accepts the careful, balanced that is being advocated by the panel.

____________________________________________________________

Urgent action needed on climate, national forum says Governments urged to ignore skepticism, work to avert effects of global warming

Author: Greenspon, Edward

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/urgent-action-needed-on-climate-national-forum/docview/384644640/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The group characterizes Canada's commitment at the Kyoto, Japan, conference on climate change last December to reduce greenhouse gas emissions in 2010 by 6 per cent below 1990 levels as "a crucial first effort to move the global economy in the right direction." Canada is already emitting far more greenhouse gases than it did in 1990 so the real reduction will be somewhere between 20 per cent and 25 per cent, the group says.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Urgent+action+needed+on+climate%2C+national+forum+says+Governments+urged+to+ignore+skepticism%2C+work+to+avert+effects+of+global+warming&amp;author=Greenspon%2C+Edward&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-06-03&amp;spage=A.3&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Governments urged to ignore skepticism, work to avert effects of global warming
Urgent action needed on climate, national forum says
Governments urged to ignore skepticism, work to avert effects of global warming
Wednesday, June 3, 1998
Parliamentary Bureau Chief
OTTAWA -- A forum of eminent Canadians brought together in the winter and spring to examine the climate-change issue will release a declaration today calling for immediate and urgent action.
The so-called national forum on climate change, largely composed of Order-of-Canada recipients, says it has been convinced that the balance of scientific evidence supports the view that global warming poses a real threat.
Its declaration acknowledges the existence of scientific and economic uncertainty and states that skeptical voices could be correct and must continue to be heard. The group expresses its own concerns about limitations in the computer models used to predict the seriousness of climate change and the costs of action.
But it rejects these uncertainties as an excuse for inaction.
If the challenge is as serious as many scientists believe, the group says, early action will help build momentum for the difficult adjustments that may be necessary.
On the other hand, if concerns about climate change turn out to be overstated, Canadians can adjust their response down the road. They will have taken out a measure of insurance and will reap side benefits, such as reduced pollution, better urban air quality and a more efficient economy.
"Canadians have little to lose and everything to gain by taking action immediately," the declaration says.
The group characterizes Canada's commitment at the Kyoto, Japan, conference on climate change last December to reduce greenhouse gas emissions in 2010 by 6 per cent below 1990 levels as "a crucial first effort to move the global economy in the right direction." Canada is already emitting far more greenhouse gases than it did in 1990 so the real reduction will be somewhere between 20 per cent and 25 per cent, the group says.
It suggests that many immediate steps can be taken to respond to the Kyoto commitment. "However, action to fully meet the terms of the agreement should be taken after thorough economic cost analysis and should be coupled with policies that address any negative impacts on Canadian communities."
The group concedes that an effective climate change response will likely mean higher energy costs for Canadians. It says that any increases to government revenues from these higher costs must be used to reduce the effects of global warming.
The declaration calls on federal and provincial governments to create a National Atmospheric Fund to provide seed money for climate change research, education and action, including support for the development of new energy technologies.
The declaration says that the group's 25 members came to the issue with an open mind.
While climate change could lead to some benefits, such as longer growing seasons, the forum finds the dangers more compelling.
It lists some of these as more frequent and severe storms along the lines of those leading to the Saguenay floods in July of 1996, Red River flooding in spring of 1997 and the 1998 ice storms; longer and more severe summer heat waves, and changes in precipitation patterns that could exacerbate droughts on the Prairies.

Top scientist denies threat of global warming : A former head of the U.S. National Academy of Sciences is making waves by arguing that climate change may be beneficial -- so the U.S. should reject proposals for cuts in greenhouse gas emissions.: [Final Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/top-scientist-denies-threat-global-warming-former/docview/242881326/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: He's the former head of the U.S. National Academy of Sciences who, earlier this year, circulated a petition urging the U.S. government to reject the Kyoto Protocol of the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change, which calls for major reductions in greenhouse gas emissions in the next 15 years. The U.S. National Academy of Sciences also entered the fray. The most influential organization of scientists in the U.S. issued a news release expressing concern over "confusion caused by a petition being circulated via letter from a former president of this Academy." "The NAS Council would like to make it clear that this petition has nothing to do with the National Academy of Sciences and that the manuscript was not published in the Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences or in any other peer-reviewed journal," the NAS says. "The petition does not reflect the conclusions of expert reports of the Academy."

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Top+scientist+denies+threat+of+global+warming+%3A+A+former+head+of+the+U.S.+National+Academy+of+Sciences+is+making+waves+by+arguing+that+climate+change+may+be+beneficial+--+so+the+U.S.+should+reject+proposals+for+cuts+in+greenhouse+gas+emissions.&amp;author=&amp;issn=08321299&amp;title=The+Vancouver+Sun&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-06-03&amp;spage=B.2&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Who is Frederick Seitz and why is he causing such a commotion over global climate change in the U.S.?
He's the former head of the U.S. National Academy of Sciences who, earlier this year, circulated a petition urging the U.S. government to reject the Kyoto Protocol of the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change, which calls for major reductions in greenhouse gas emissions in the next 15 years.
The petition claims there is no convincing evidence that climate will be catastrophically altered " in the foreseeable future," and that there is substantial evidence that greater carbon dioxide concentrations are beneficial. By late April 1998, his supporters claimed more than 15,000 scientists had signed the petition.
Unfortunately for Seitz and his supporters, some of the names on the mail-in petition turned out to be bogus. They included Perry Mason, Michael J. Fox, Drs. Frank Burns, Honeycutt and Pierce, the trio from the TV show M*A*S*H, and even Geraldine Halliwell, better known as Ginger Spice, a member of the Spice Girls music group until recently.
The U.S. National Academy of Sciences also entered the fray. The most influential organization of scientists in the U.S. issued a news release expressing concern over "confusion caused by a petition being circulated via letter from a former president of this Academy."
The academy pointed out that the mailing included a "manuscript in a format that is nearly identical to that of scientific articles published in the Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences.
"The NAS Council would like to make it clear that this petition has nothing to do with the National Academy of Sciences and that the manuscript was not published in the Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences or in any other peer-reviewed journal," the NAS says. "The petition does not reflect the conclusions of expert reports of the Academy."
Referring to a 1991-92 NAS study on this issue called Policy Implications of Greenhouse Warming, the academy said "even given the considerable uncertainties in our knowledge of the relevant phenomena, greenhouse warming poses a potential threat sufficient to merit prompt responses. Investment in mitigation measures acts as insurance protection against the great uncertainties and the possibility of dramatic surprises."
-
Did you know that the hottest temperature ever recorded on earth was 57.7 C (136 degrees F) at El Azizia, Libya on Sept. 13, 1922? Or that the hottest temperature ever recorded on Antarctica was only 15 C (59 degrees F) on July 21, 1983 at Vostok?
All these extreme temperatures and more about severe weather can be found at <http://www.ncdc.noaa.gov/ol/climate/severeweather/ severeweather.htm l>, the web site for the U.S. National Climate Data Center.
Canada gets two entries on the list of climate extremes by continent.
The coldest recorded in North America was --63.3 C (--81.4 degrees F) at Snag, Yukon on Feb. 3, 1947 and the highest average annual precipitation for North America is at Henderson Lake where during the past 14 years, it has averaged 650.24 cm (256 inches) of rain.
The driest place on earth is Arica, Chile where, on average for each of the past 59 years, only .07 cm (0.03 inches) of rain has fallen annually.

ELIMINATE ROAD RAGE BY CLEARING THE AIR (POLLUTION): [VALLEY EDITION]

Author: Gregory Wright Local View

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/eliminate-road-rage-clearing-air-pollution/docview/281781626/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Gregory Wright lives in Sherman Oaks and is a member of the Campaign on Auto Pollution & Efficiency, headquartered at the International Center for Technology Assessment, Washington, D.C. Readers can write to him in care of Daily News Opinions, P.O. Box 4200, Woodland Hills, CA 91365-4200. HERE are some ways to reduce road rage (Daily News Drive section, May 11). (Bonus: Many of these measures will also reduce America's disproportionately high per capita contribution to global greenhouse gas emissions. Increase the fuel economy of all U.S.-manufactured and imported vehicles by raising the corporate average fuel economy (C.A.F.E.) standards immediately, thereby ending the trend toward "faster and bigger" cars rightly fingered as a major source of the phenomenal recent increase in aggressive driving and road rage. Such driving, not surprisingly, stirs up in other drivers. The ridiculously overpowered cars on the road today are an automatic major source of the road rage phenomenon.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=ELIMINATE+ROAD+RAGE+BY+CLEARING+THE+AIR+%28POLLUTION%29&amp;author=Gregory+Wright+Local+View&amp;issn=&amp;title=Daily+News&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-05-27&amp;spage=N.13&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Gregory Wright lives in Sherman Oaks and is a member of the Campaign on Auto Pollution & Efficiency, headquartered at the International Center for Technology Assessment, Washington, D.C. Readers can write to him in care of Daily News Opinions, P.O. Box 4200, Woodland Hills, CA 91365-4200.
HERE are some ways to reduce road rage (Daily News Drive section, May 11). (Bonus: Many of these measures will also reduce America's disproportionately high per capita contribution to global greenhouse gas emissions.
Increase the fuel economy of all U.S.-manufactured and imported vehicles by raising the corporate average fuel economy (C.A.F.E.) standards immediately, thereby ending the trend toward "faster and bigger" cars rightly fingered as a major source of the phenomenal recent increase in aggressive driving and road rage. Such driving, not surprisingly, stirs up in other drivers. The ridiculously overpowered cars on the road today are an automatic major source of the road rage phenomenon.
Support the long-proposed global "carbon tax" to provide incentive to reduce fossil-fuel use and help fund the many measures needed to transition the world from the fossil-fuel age and avert the worst consequences of global warming.
Institute a pay-at-the-pump state auto insurance program as a major supplement to (if not eventual replacement of) private auto insurance. This innovation will require all drivers automatically to pay for their car insurance, or some portion of it, and will require the drivers who drive "larger and faster" and more miles to pay more, in proportion to their increased gasoline consumption. (Out-of-state visitors also hereby will help foot the California car insurance bill.)
Encourage "green driving" to reduce fuel consumption, atmospheric and local air pollution, and high-fuel-consumption aggressive driving (all that speeding and rapid acceleration uses a lot more gas). That is, teach new and old drivers to drive moderate, fuel-efficient speeds (around 55 mph, the people's commitment to mitigating the U.S. contribution to global climate change) and to avoid excessive, pointless acceleration on surface streets and freeways. Also, set lower speed limits on sustained uphill runs on highways and freeways.
Experiment with "variable speed limits" to be read out on roadside digital speed limit signs that will temporarily and specifically lower urban-freeway speed limits, within a range of reduced speeds, when traffic has slowed further on. If cars do not approach jammed traffic at high speeds, the backward-spreading rapid braking of numerous cars can be avoided and the traffic can continue to move as a managed, slower-speed continuous "stream" (instead of driver- and car-racking stop-and-go), conferring easier driving, earlier arrivals at successive off-ramps and less pollution.
Enact laws mandating the placement of speed- and acceleration-reducing governors on the engines of the cars of convicted speeders and reckless and aggressive drivers. Consider mandating speed-reducing governors on all cars driven by more-accident-prone young drivers (under, say, age 25). And fund research into the perfection of speed- and acceleration-reducing technology for these purposes.
No doubt some readers will feel some road (-related) rage at my suggestions. But the alternative is a tremendous increase in the travail and danger of driving as our population mushrooms in the years ahead - and in the terrible consequences of the relentless accumulation of carbon dioxide and other pollutants in the global atmosphere, conditions like the climate-change-driven disruption of agriculture and the food supply, the spread of tropical diseases, hotter heat waves, super storms, and the destruction of natural beauty. Not the world I want to live the rest of my life in.

Subject: Road rage

Environment report slams Ottawa: An excerpt from conclusions of the report by the Standing Committee on the Environment and Sustainable Development: [Final Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/environment-report-slams-ottawa-excerpt/docview/269899434/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Of course, this estimate was based on perceived need for the 1994- 1995 period. Since then, more regulations and laws, as mentioned earlier, have been added to the roster of enforcement duties, and still more are to come. . . Also mentioned earlier in this report was the extent to which operating budgets have been reduced. . . The operating budget (minus salaries) for 1997-1998 in the Quebec Region was $115,000; in the Atlantic Region, it was $150,000. Given the extremely limited enforcement budgets and the unacceptably low number of enforcement officers which must cope with an ever-increasing workload, it is not surprising that Environment Canada is favouring voluntary approaches and the downloading of federal responsibilities on the provinces and territories. As the Supreme Court of Canada noted, protecting the environment has become "one of the major challenges of our time." It also stated that CEPA's provisions respecting the regulation of toxic substances served a public purpose of "superordinate importance." It is imperative, in the committee's view, that the enforcement program be given additional resources.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Environment+report+slams+Ottawa%3A+An+excerpt+from+conclusions+of+the+report+by+the+Standing+Committee+on+the+Environment+and+Sustainable+Development%3A&amp;author=&amp;issn=11899417&amp;title=The+Spectator&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-05-27&amp;spage=A.11&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Throughout this report the committee has stressed the importance of effectively enforcing Canada's environmental laws. Based on the evidence presented, the committee concludes that this is not happening.
Lack of political will might be partly to blame, but the major cause, in the committee's view, is the lack of adequate resources.
In (the committee's) opinion, the enforcement program is woefully understaffed.
There are in total 60 enforcement personnel in the regions and a further 22 supporting personnel at the national office of enforcement.
In contrast, an internal study, prepared in 1993, conservatively estimated that over 300 staff (full-time equivalents) would be needed for effective enforcement.
Of course, this estimate was based on perceived need for the 1994- 1995 period. Since then, more regulations and laws, as mentioned earlier, have been added to the roster of enforcement duties, and still more are to come. . . Also mentioned earlier in this report was the extent to which operating budgets have been reduced. . . The operating budget (minus salaries) for 1997-1998 in the Quebec Region was $115,000; in the Atlantic Region, it was $150,000.
As mentioned earlier, the one for the Pacific and Yukon Region, at $87,100, dropped by 72 per cent from the last to the current fiscal year due principally to the termination of the Fraser River Action Plan on April 1, 1998! The committee finds these figures alarming.
VACANCIES UNFILLED
Also alarming is the fact that enforcement staff vacancies have been left unfilled for so many years on end in some regions -- a situation that may be perpetuated in the years to come if, as is being done in the Pacific and Yukon Region, jobs are being sacrificed so that the savings on salaries can be converted into desperately needed operating funds.
Given the extremely limited enforcement budgets and the unacceptably low number of enforcement officers which must cope with an ever-increasing workload, it is not surprising that Environment Canada is favouring voluntary approaches and the downloading of federal responsibilities on the provinces and territories.
The committee wishes to stress that it is not against work-share agreements with the provinces and territories. Some have worked out very well.
One example, cited by the Canadian Institute for Environmental Law and Policy (CIELAP) as a very good model of co-operative government action, is the Canada-Ontario Agreement respecting the Great Lakes Basin Ecosystem (COA).
Noting, however, that the success of this agreement was limited recently by budget cutbacks and the restructuring of the Ontario Ministry of Environment and Energy, CIELAP observed that even efficient, effective cooperative government action fails without adequate funding and coherent staffing policy. The committee agrees with this observation.
Adequate funding and coherent staffing policy are fundamental elements if the job is to be well done.
In the committee's opinion, the purposes served by effectively enforcing environmental laws like the Canadian Environmental Protection Act (CEPA) and the pollution prevention provisions of the Fisheries Act cannot be over-emphasized.
We are talking about providing Canadians with safe, clean air that they can breathe without being placed at risk.
We are talking about providing Canadians with safe, clean water that they can drink and in which they can swim without being placed at risk.
We are talking about providing Canadians with a safe, uncompromised atmosphere in which they can live without being placed at risk.
On a less critical but nonetheless important level, we are talking about providing Canadians with healthy, green spaces, rich in biodiversity, that they can admire and enjoy and hand over to subsequent generations for the latter's benefit and enjoyment.
If toxic and other harmful substances are not adequately regulated and kept in check . . . all of these vital needs and interests are imperilled and might be irretrievably lost.
That such might be the outcome must be resisted at all costs.
The public interest demands no less.
COST-ANALYSIS STUDIES
The committee deplores the fact that the cost-analysis studies prepared by the department do not fully take into account, in a quantifiable way, the
full benefits, whether health, social, ecological or economic, of environmental protection regulations for Canadians.
We appreciate that methodologies for conducting such studies might not be fully developed. On the other hand, we believe that, at present, too much emphasis is placed on the short-term economic costs of implementing environmental legislation, and not enough on the overall medium- and long-term benefits.
The committee can think of a number of factors that should be counted as benefits, for example:
- Benefits to public health and improved quality of life.
- Benefits in terms of achieving long-term "sustainable development" commitments and practices.
- Benefits to the economy generally (increased employment, research and development and export opportunities).
- Benefits to the regulated industries (increased productivity, decreased litigation costs, improved public image, level playing field, export opportunities).
- Benefits to insurance companies and consumers (decreased liability, greater risk management).
- Benefits to all levels of government and local communities (lower health costs, increased tourism and recreation activities, increased yields and decreased disruptions for resource-based industries such as fisheries);
- Benefits to all levels of government (improved international reputation, decreased vulnerability to environmental litigation).
Unless these and other benefits of regulatory action are considered, the committee fears that the emphasis will continue to be placed exclusively on the short-term economic costs.
This situation must be remedied.
The committee recommends that the Minister of the Environment direct Environment Canada to include the full economic benefits of regulatory action in all cost/benefit analysis made in relation to the development and implementation of regulatory solutions to environmental problems.
It is clear that increased funding is required for Environment Canada's enforcement program. Adequate resources are lacking at present; the problem will only be magnified in the future if Environment Canada is serious about fulfilling its statutory mandate to preserve and enhance the quality of the natural environment.
Environmental protection is of paramount importance to Canadians.
As the Supreme Court of Canada noted, protecting the environment has become "one of the major challenges of our time." It also stated that CEPA's provisions respecting the regulation of toxic substances served a public purpose of "superordinate importance." It is imperative, in the committee's view, that the enforcement program be given additional resources.
Having reached this conclusion, the committee was undecided about whether, in light of the existing fiscal restraint, the additional funding should come from reallocations within the department, or whether new, additional funding should be sought.
In the end, the committee decided that new funding for enforcement was needed.
The committee considers that the other core activities within the department, such as science, wildlife protection, biodiversity, meteorology and climate change, are too important in their own right and should not be emasculated in order to subsidize the enforcement program.
he department has lost over one- third of its budget in recent years.
All programs have been pared to the bone. The public finances, however, are now under control and on the road to recovery.
It is time for the Minister of the Environment to seize the opportunity, reaffirm her commitment to environmental protection and seek new money for the enforcement program.
To read the report in its entirety, it can be found on the Internet (http://interparl.parl.gc.ca/InfocomDoc/ENSU/Studies/ Reports/ensurp0 3/06-rep-e.htm).
ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT
- The issue: A Liberal-dominated Commons environment committee released a report calling for an overhaul of the system, citing serious oversights, insufficient staffing and resources, and intergovernmental bungling. It recommends that the auditor-general take a look at the situation, that an independent enforcement agency be created, and that the environment minister publish an annual report of any findings.

Can't blame global warming for weather: [1 Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/cant-blame-global-warming-weather/docview/437773277/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: This is particularly so over western Canada where El Nino (warm phase) brings milder and drier winters, while La Nina (cold phase) brings colder and wetter winters. The super El Nino of 1997-98 produced one of the warmest (and driest) winters over western Canada (there were no major blizzards this past winter over western Canada) while the La Nina of 1995-96 produced severe winter and heavy snowfall over the British Columbia and Alberta border, leading to a major disruption in the transportation of grain and oil.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Can%27t+blame+global+warming+for+weather&amp;author=&amp;issn=03190781&amp;title=Toronto+Star&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-05-14&amp;spage=1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Re Weather worse than it used to be: Scientists (May 8).
The contention of Dr. Henry Hengeveld of Environment Canada that "severe weather events like hail, floods, blizzards, etc. have increased in frequency in recent years in Canada" is completely baseless.
As a retired Environment Canada research scientist, I can say that there is not a single study so far published in reputable peer- reviewed scientific journals that clearly documents such an increasing trend.
Hengeveld's assessment of severe weather events based on increased insurance claims can be very misleading, since more people have been buying insurance in recent years.
Furthermore, lifestyle changes have prompted more people to live near lakeshore (or seashore) areas, which are prone to floods and related natural hazards.
In a couple of recent studies, appearing in the peer-reviewed journal Atmosphere-Ocean, published by the Canadian Meteorological and Oceanographic Society, my co-workers and I have clearly documented that the warm and the cold phases of El Nino have definite impact on the Canadian climate.
This is particularly so over western Canada where El Nino (warm phase) brings milder and drier winters, while La Nina (cold phase) brings colder and wetter winters.
The super El Nino of 1997-98 produced one of the warmest (and driest) winters over western Canada (there were no major blizzards this past winter over western Canada) while the La Nina of 1995-96 produced severe winter and heavy snowfall over the British Columbia and Alberta border, leading to a major disruption in the transportation of grain and oil.
These dramatic changes in weather patterns from the 1995-96 winter to 1997-1998 winter were in no way related to "global warming" as claimed by Hengeveld.
Hengeveld should first publish his results in peer-reviewed scientific journals before making any claims of climate change due to global warming.
Madhav L. Khandekar
Unionville

`Porno-weather' is our obsession, climatologist says: El Nino, disasters of '98, inspire hyperbole: [Final Edition]

Author: Hughes, Graham

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/porno-weather-is-our-obsession-climatologist-says/docview/240162687/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: As for El Nino, Mr. [Dave Phillips] said, "we blame El Nino for everything, even things that happen indoors. Even the demise of the Toronto Maple Leafs. It's unfair. El Nino doesn't cause specific weather events, it causes a condition, a kind of a circulation upon which storms can be blamed. ... El Nino gets blamed for everything and the world is getting warmer, so the public connects global warming and El Nino, he said. But scientists are only beginning to understand El Nino, although it has been known since the 1500s. Black & White Photo: Rod MacIvor, The Ottawa Citizen / Climatologist Dave Phillips told the forum on weather disasters that people in other countries are enjoying Canadians' favourite hobby: talking about the weather. ;

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=%60Porno-weather%27+is+our+obsession%2C+climatologist+says%3A+El+Nino%2C+disasters+of+%2798%2C+inspire+hyperbole&amp;author=Hughes%2C+Graham&amp;issn=08393222&amp;title=The+Ottawa+Citizen&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-05-08&amp;spage=A.5&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Weather defines Canadians, says one of the country's best-known weather scientists.
Dave Phillips, senior climatologist with the Atmospheric Environment Service of Environment Canada, said yesterday that "getting Canadians to talk about the weather is like asking them to breathe."
But, after recent weather-related disasters, "people around the world are doing the Canadian thing -- they're watching the skies and they're talking about the weather, which is second nature to Canadians.
"The media loves to cover extreme weather," Mr. Phillips told his audience. "I call it porno-weather. It lends itself to hyperbole and exaggeration."
The author and creator of the Canadian Weather Trivia Calendar, was speaking at an Environment Canada forum on understanding and dealing with weather disasters.
According to Environment Canada, weather causes an average 220 fatal car accidents and more than 11,500 injuries a year. But, said Mr. Phillips, "We live in one of the healthiest countries in the world. More Canadians die falling out of bed each year than from weather."
Location is also a key element in weather-related disasters. The January ice storm wouldn't have been such a disaster, perhaps, if it hit 200 kilometres further east or west, said Henry Hengeveld, senior science adviser on climate change with the Atmospheric Environment Service. But it hit one of the most populated areas in North America and made headlines because it affected 15 per cent of Canadians.
He said the greatest weather disasters are often linked to a combination of unusual weather features.
The ice storm, he said, resulted from a series of moist air masses from the Gulf of Mexico, a warm air mass and an unusual atmospheric block in the east that kept these around for a prolonged period.
Last year's Red River flood was the result of a wet fall that saturated the ground, a high snowfall in the river's headwaters, a major snowfall in Manitoba in April and a moderate melt rate.
The number of individual factors involved in such major events increases the odds against them, he said.
The media play a part in public perception that weather is getting worse, Mr. Phillips said.
There's also "the CNN effect." When Galveston, Texas, was destroyed by a hurricane in 1900, between 6,000 and 12,000 people died, but it took two weeks for news of the tragedy to reach New York, he noted.
"That wouldn't happen today with instant news," Mr. Phillips noted. "This will be the third year in a row where the No. 1 news story will be a weather story."
He said when people view scenes on television like the Saguenay River floods, the Red River flood and the ice storm, they think climate is dramatically changing.
Scientists don't know whether this heralds a trend toward more chaotic, turbulent weather, Mr. Phillips said. "Or is it just some bad weather luck, a cluster of extremes that will go away and we'll be back to cold winters and warm summers in the future? We just don't know."
But, he said, "We're warming the planet at a rate faster and greater than at any time in the last 600 years."
As for El Nino, Mr. Phillips said, "we blame El Nino for everything, even things that happen indoors. Even the demise of the Toronto Maple Leafs. It's unfair. El Nino doesn't cause specific weather events, it causes a condition, a kind of a circulation upon which storms can be blamed. ...
"I do know one thing for sure. The number of weather-related disasters has increased, and that's because not only are there possibly more extremes, but people are causing disasters."
He noted that in the 1940-1960 period the eastern seaboard of the United States was hit by 18 hurricanes. In 1970-80, there were only two, so people felt safe in developing coastal and off-shore properties. Then came the hurricanes of the 90s and the resulting multibillion-dollar disasters as the new developments were hammered.
The costs of disasters are rising, he said. There's more property damage, people are living in more vulnerable exposed areas and so these weather hits are big blows today.
"So those extremes our grandparents lived through are now not just little weather hits, they're weather blows or disasters because of the society and infrastructure that we have and where we're living, in more exposed and more marginal areas."
El Nino gets blamed for everything and the world is getting warmer, so the public connects global warming and El Nino, he said. But scientists are only beginning to understand El Nino, although it has been known since the 1500s.
"You warm up the world, you don't necessarily get more El Ninos," Mr. Phillips said. "Scientists have a gut feeling that there is a connection. But they need the proof to have 99 per cent evidence that warming the planet brings more El Ninos."
Science can't say yet whether we're experiencing more extreme weather, Mr. Hengeveld said. "The world is warming, but we need another 10 or 15 years to say if there are more extremes, like floods and heat waves."
He said he believes the climate is changing. The world is warming and measures must be taken to do something about it.
"We have to say to policy makers that we can't definitely say this is going to happen, but there is a high probability and we should take out some insurance.
"We know the global climate is changing, regardless of what is causing it. We know it's changing from what we've seen over the last 100 years, and that is indicating we should expect weather to change.
"It's not a question of waiting to see what's happening and then change things. Society takes a long time to change, the time to start taking measures is now."
Climate change will change the pattern of weather around the globe and we should prepare ourselves today to deal with weather we're not used to, he said.
"Studies to date, although not necessarily conclusive, suggest the probability that big storms in Canada will increase."
Illustration Black & White Photo: Rod MacIvor, The Ottawa Citizen / Climatologist Dave Phillips told the forum on weather disasters that people in other countries are enjoying Canadians' favourite hobby: talking about the weather. ;

Insurers cool on global weather

Author: Corcoran, Terence

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/insurers-cool-on-global-weather/docview/384616604/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The industry's war on fossil fuels peaked in July, 1996, when 57 firms signed a United Nations Position Statement on Climate Change. It was a slippery document that hyped the global warming scare, downgraded market forces and pushed for "political initiatives." Man-made climate change "will lead to shifts in atmospheric and oceanic circulation patterns. This will probably increase the likelihood of extreme weather events in certain areas.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Insurers+cool+on+global+weather&amp;author=Corcoran%2C+Terence&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-05-05&amp;spage=B.2&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Insurers cool on global weather
Tuesday, May 5, 1998
THROUGH much of this decade, the world's insurance industry has been fanning the fires of global warming. At the extreme, some of the largest firms in the business have sounded like Greenpeace campaigners bent on spooking governments into taking drastic action. Proffering statistics that appeared to show a dramatic increase in floods and storms, along with evidence of massive insurance losses, the industry mounted a major effort in support of the United Nations' greenhouse gas control program.
New numbers suggest the campaign was based mostly on hot air, although the early warnings appeared ominous. Climate change "could bankrupt the industry," said Franklin Nutter, president of the Reinsurance Association of America. Gerhard Berz, head of Munich Reinsurance's geoscience research group and one of the industry's leading climate hawks, said he feared climate change "will lead to natural disasters of unprecedented severity and frequency . . . so somebody better do something."
The industry's war on fossil fuels peaked in July, 1996, when 57 firms signed a United Nations Position Statement on Climate Change. It was a slippery document that hyped the global warming scare, downgraded market forces and pushed for "political initiatives." Man-made climate change "will lead to shifts in atmospheric and oceanic circulation patterns. This will probably increase the likelihood of extreme weather events in certain areas. Such effects carry the risk of dramatically increasing property damage, with serious implications for property insurers and reinsurers." To prevent that risk, the statement invoked the treacherous precautionary principle (there's no proof, but let's do something anyway to be on the safe side) and called on the world's governments to "achieve early, substantial reductions in greenhouse gas emissions."
Greenpeace was never far from the insurance industry initiative. Greenpeace International's climate change director, Jeremy Leggett, played a major role co-ordinating industry thinking and disseminating apocalyptic scenarios. Mr. Leggett, whose main objective is a world covered with solar power panels, wrote a 1993 Greenpeace report called Climate Change and the Insurance Industry that foretold of insurance disasters as the world's climate warmed. The report was widely endorsed by insurance industry officials. "Greenpeace makes a good case," Mr. Nutter said.
The latest figures, however, throw cold water on the industry's alarmist forecasts. Insurance industry losses from natural disasters have plunged over the past couple of years, according to Swiss Reinsurance. After peaking at $26.3-billion (U.S.) in 1992 -- the year of Hurricane Andrew -- losses from natural catastrophes fell back to $4.1-billion in 1997, a level that's not out of line with recent history. Swiss Reinsurance commented, moreover, that there is "no evidence of the influence of climate change. To date, there is no general scientific evidence of any significant change in the frequency of events."
The spikes in the early 1990s were caused by an unpredictable number of high-cost weather and earthquake events that the insurance industry was simply not prepared for. But even Swiss Reinsurance said these events do not amount to a trend. "The observation period of 20 years is much too short to make any conclusive statements, given the extremely volatile claims burden and the big impact that single events exert."
It is now also clear that a dramatic increase in natural disasters reported over the past decade may be a statistical development rather than a weather development. The increase in the number of events over the past decade can be explained by increased reporting in the media of storms and floods, greater population densities, industrialization and other factors unrelated to climate. As the chart shows, the number of man-made disasters -- fires, plane crashes and shipping mishaps -- increased at about the same rate during the same period. That suggests a change in the climate for collecting information, rather than climate change.

Finding the funds for cleaner air: [City Edition]

Author: Paul R. Epstein and Lewis Milford

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/finding-funds-cleaner-air/docview/405216786/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Next week Secretary of State Madeleine Albright embarks on a mission to convince developing nations to sign the Kyoto Protocol, a treaty negotiated by more than 150 countries to combat global climate change. Unfortunately, she goes without the one thing that could make it happen: resources. Climate is exhibiting increasing instability, and delaying our withdrawal from fossil fuel dependence may be extremely costly -- for our well-being and for our economy. In Kyoto, the community of nations agreed that burning fossil fuels an felling forests were major causes for changes in climate. Now we must focus on solutions and how to fund them. The Kyoto Protocol created a Clean Development Fund to stimulate renewable energy enterprises and energy-efficient technologies. But the fund is empty. Filling it would provide the incentive for developing nations to sign the treaty and enable development with new energy sources. With participation of developing nations, the US Congress could be convinced to support the treaty. This fund could be used by nations to help jump-start "infant industries" until they reach "economies of scale" and create demand for their products and services. A large fund will be needed to move clean technologies into the global marketplace. But big problems warrant bold solutions.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Finding+the+funds+for+cleaner+air&amp;author=Paul+R.+Epstein+and+Lewis+Milford&amp;issn=07431791&amp;title=Boston+Globe&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-04-25&amp;spage=A.17&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Dr. Paul R. Epstein is associate director of the Center for Health and the Global Environment at Harvard Medical School. Lewis Milford is director of the Clean Energy Group in Montpelier, Vt.
Next week Secretary of State Madeleine Albright embarks on a mission to convince developing nations to sign the Kyoto Protocol, a treaty negotiated by more than 150 countries to combat global climate change. Unfortunately, she goes without the one thing that could make it happen: resources. Climate is exhibiting increasing instability, and delaying our withdrawal from fossil fuel dependence may be extremely costly -- for our well-being and for our economy. In Kyoto, the community of nations agreed that burning fossil fuels an felling forests were major causes for changes in climate. Now we must focus on solutions and how to fund them.
The Kyoto Protocol created a Clean Development Fund to stimulate renewable energy enterprises and energy-efficient technologies. But the fund is empty. Filling it would provide the incentive for developing nations to sign the treaty and enable development with new energy sources. With participation of developing nations, the US Congress could be convinced to support the treaty.
This fund could be used by nations to help jump-start "infant industries" until they reach "economies of scale" and create demand for their products and services. A large fund will be needed to move clean technologies into the global marketplace. But big problems warrant bold solutions.
Can the magnitude of funds necessary be raised? Recently more than $100 billion was raised for East Asian financial bailouts, much in the form of guarantees that may never be used. The purpose is to create confidence. Why can we not raise a comparable fund for a problem that could have dire consequences for all?
There are several options for building a Clean Development Fund. One proposal is to levy the approximately $1.5 trillion exchanged daily on the world's currency markets. A 0.1 percent tarriff -- one dime set aside for each $100 exchanged -- would generate some $300 billion annually. Several pennies on each $100 -- a small fee for using the global commons -- might be a pilot.
Yale University economist and Nobel winner James Tobin proposed a 1 percent tax on financial transactions in 1972 ($18 billion daily at the time) to reduce short-term speculation and stabilize currency markets. The "Tobin tax" -- the burden spread across the financial landscape -- could serve to limit the kind of speculation that contributed to Asia's financial ills, and generate a public goods fund and global insurance policy for our common future.
Are there models for such funds? Yes, existing clean energy mechanisms are in place. Already the World Bank has established the Solar Development Corp. and the Renewable Energy and Energy Efficiency Fund to finance new, less polluting power projects in the developing world.Will an international fund threaten national sovereignty? Not if funds are disbursed through established national credit agencies and nations set their own expenditure priorities, within agreed upon global objectives.
A new international financial framework -- a network of existing credit institutions -- is needed to manage the economic mechanisms that drive the energy transition. Combining private financial institutions for implementation with public accountability is key.
In Massachusetts, a renewable investment fund of $150 million over 5 years will be managed by the Massachusetts Technology Collaborative. This fund -- using an economic development approach to clean power -- can help companies seeking local and export markets for new technologies. Such local funds can be part of utility restructuring or create as stand-alone initiatives.
Nationally, President Clinton has proposed a $6.3 billion "Credit for Early Action" program over 5 years, including substantial tax incentives (e.g., a $3,000 break for buying an energy efficient car). The Big Three automakers have called for new subsidies and government programs, such as electric-car charging stations to aid the process.
The full spectrum of local, national and international funds can help overcome barriers to developing, distributing and using low carbon emitting technologies, and generate a historic opportunity to establish the first links in a necessary chain to create clean economic growth. Coordinating these funds could lead to a "proof-of- concept" for managing the Kyoto Fund.
Moreover, individuals and institutions can examine their own energy use -- their sources of power and their efficiency. Switching from oil and coal to natural gas -- and wind, solar and fuel cells if possible -- will strengthen the demand for clean energy, while increasing energy-efficiency can generate immediate savings.
Eventually there must be an energy transition; the question is how soon it can be achieved. No process of transformation can rely on penalties alone. Incentives are needed to push and pull the process, to overcome the built-in inertia and incentives for nations to continue to abuse their environments.
Secretary Albright will undoubtedly hear from her hosts that financial support is needed to meet the new international goals. Building the Clean Development Fund called for in the Kyoto Protocol -- while using existing funds to finance early stage development of technologies that lower greenhouse gas emissions -- can catalyze a climate change solution with full and fair global participation, the economic benefits of which may surprise us all.

Subject: Treaties; Alternative energy sources; Greenhouse effect; Funding; Emission standards

RED FLAGS ON GREEN ISSUES: TIME TO WARM UP TO GLOBAL WARMING

Author: Thomas Gale Moore

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/red-flags-on-green-issues/docview/1842147325/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=RED+FLAGS+ON+GREEN+ISSUES&amp;author=Thomas+Gale+Moore&amp;issn=08856613&amp;title=Philadelphia+Inquirer&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-04-22&amp;spage=A.13&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Pundits, politicians and the press have argued that global warming will bring disaster to the world, but there are good reasons to believe that if warming occurs, we will like it.
Where do retirees go when they are free to move? Not to Duluth. People like warmth. When television weather reporters say, ``It will be a great day,'' they usually mean it will be warmer than normal.
The weather can be too warm, of course, but that is unlikely to become a major problem. The Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (the U.N. body that has been studying this possibility for more than a decade) has forecast that the world's climate will be about 3.6 degrees Fahrenheit warmer and precipitation about 7 percent higher by the end of the next century. Most of the warming will occur at night and during the winter, the panel predicts.
Temperatures are expected to increase the most toward the poles. Thus, Minneapolis should enjoy more warming than Dallas. But even the Twin Cities should find that most of their temperature increase occurs during their coldest season, making their climate more livable. Over this century, summer highs have actually declined while winter lows have gone up, records show.
Warmer winters will produce less ice and snow, facilitating commuting and making snow-shoveling less of a chore. Families will have less need to invest in parkas, bulky jackets, earmuffs and boots. Department of Energy studies show a warmer climate would reduce heating bills more than it would boost outlays on air conditioning. If we now enjoyed the weather predicted for the end of the next century, heating and cooling expenditures would be cut by about $12.2 billion annually.
Most economic activities would be unaffected by climate change. Manufacturing, banking, insurance, retailing, wholesaling, medicine, education, mining, finance and most other services can be carried out in cold or hot climates. Some weather-related or outdoor services would be affected. Transportation would benefit generally from a warmer climate, with fewer slippery or impassable highways. Airlines would experience fewer weather-related delays in the winter.
Doomsayers have predicted that a warmer world will inflict tropical diseases on Americans. They neglect to mention that those diseases - including malaria, cholera and yellow fever - were widespread in the United States in the colder 19th century. Their absence here today is attributable to modern sanitation and the American lifestyle, which prevent the microbes from getting a foothold - not to a climate unsuitable to their propagation. It is actually warmer along the Gulf Coast, which is free of dengue fever, than on the Caribbean islands where the disease is endemic.
My research shows that a warmer world would be healthier for Americans and would cut the number of deaths in the United States by about 40,000 per year - roughly the number killed on highways.
According to climatologists, the villain causing a warmer world is the unprecedented amount of carbon dioxide we keep pumping into the atmosphere. As high-school biology teachers emphasize, plants absorb carbon dioxide and emit oxygen. Researchers have shown, moreover, that virtually all plants will do better in an environment enriched with carbon dioxide than in the current atmosphere, which contains only trace amounts of their basic food.
Warmer winters and nights would mean longer growing seasons. Combined with higher levels of carbon dioxide, plant life would become more vigorous, providing more food for animals and humans. Given a rising world population, longer growing seasons, greater rainfall and an enriched atmosphere could be just the ticket to stave off famine and want.
A slowly rising sea level constitutes the only significant drawback to global warming. Oceans will rise about 2 inches per decade, scientists say. The cost to Americans of building dikes and levees to mitigate the damage would be less than $1 billion per year - an insignificant amount compared to the likely gain of more than $100 billion for the American people as a whole.
Let's not rush into costly programs to stave off something we might like if it occurs. Warmer is better; richer is healthier; acting now is foolish.
Thomas Gale Moore is a senior fellow at the Hoover Institution and the author of Climate of Fear: Why We Shouldn't Worry about Global Warming.

Credit: By Thomas Gale Moore

(Uncaptioned) (DEAN ROHRER)

____________________________________________________________

Atlantic not getting rougher - expert: [CITY EDITION]

Author: Siggins, Lorna

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/atlantic-not-getting-rougher-expert/docview/310448161/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: El Nino and global warming may be changing our climate, but there is no conclusive evidence to suggest the Atlantic is getting systematically rougher, an international ocean weather conference heard in Dublin yesterday. Current conditions in the north-eastern Atlantic may be no worse than they were a century ago, Mr Vincent Cardone, a US weather expert, told representatives of the major oil companies, scientists and researchers at the Fifth Annual Metocean Workshop in Dublin Castle. Opening the workshop, the Minister for the Marine and Natural Resources, Dr Woods, said several studies indicated that significant hydrocarbon potential existed in the Rockall Trough.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Atlantic+not+getting+rougher+-+expert&amp;author=Siggins%2C+Lorna&amp;issn=&amp;title=Irish+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-04-21&amp;spage=5&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   El Nino and global warming may be changing our climate, but there is no conclusive evidence to suggest the Atlantic is getting systematically rougher, an international ocean weather conference heard in Dublin yesterday.
Current conditions in the north-eastern Atlantic may be no worse than they were a century ago, Mr Vincent Cardone, a US weather expert, told representatives of the major oil companies, scientists and researchers at the Fifth Annual Metocean Workshop in Dublin Castle.
Opening the workshop, the Minister for the Marine and Natural Resources, Dr Woods, said several studies indicated that significant hydrocarbon potential existed in the Rockall Trough.
He recently launched a petroleum infrastructure programme, funded by the 16 companies awarded licences in the trough last June. As part of this programme, a budget of nearly (pounds) 5 million had been earmarked by a Rockall studies group - a reflection of confidence in the exploration work.
Paying tribute to the "spirit of co-operation" among the offshore sector, the Minister said he welcomed the development of a metocean or weather ocean strategy for the Rockall area.
Dr Woods said he expected a high level of interest in exploration licences under the South Porcupine Frontier licensing round, which will be awarded next year. Under the round, which closes on December 15th, some 40,000 square kilometres are available in a water depth varying from 2,000 metres in the east to more than 2,500 metres in the north-west.
The two-day workshop, which is sponsored by BP, Statoil and Shell, is being hosted for the first time in Ireland by the Department of the Marine and Natural Resources, the Marine Institute, and the Galway-based consultancy, MCS International.
Considerable research into climate change and the effect on the Atlantic ocean during the last decade had not come up with incontrovertible evidence, Mr Cardone of Oceanweather Incorporated suggested. This research had been prompted by growing concern within the northern European insurance industry, the offshore exploration sector and the media and general public about an increase in storminess.
Similar concerns had been expressed in North America in response to measurements of extreme waves made by calibrated instruments on data buoys, moored off the east and west coasts of the US and Canada. Much of the research had culminated in a project known as Wave and Storms in the Atlantic, which was now complete, he said.
One of the big problems was the variability of data, he said. It was virtually impossible to come up with a homogeneous marine data bank for wind and waves. Historical weather maps had creeping inconsistencies, and there was also a lack of consistency in marine reports of wave heights.
Most of the information recorded on ships was concentrated in the southern part of the North Atlantic, due to the location of the busiest shipping lanes, and was hampered by the fact that some vessels used anemometers and others used Beaufort systems for measuring wind speeds, he said.
Observational and storm proxy studies indicated that the storm and surge climate of north-west Europe had not systematically worsened in the past hundred years. Certain data also suggested that the climate now was no worse than earlier this century.
However, there is evidence of inter-annual and inter-decadal variability in the wave climate of the Atlantic margin, he said. Improvement in climate models would yield better information, but more studies were required.
Credit: Marine Correspondent
