Germany -- Campaign '98 --- Animal House: Will German Greens Be the Kingmakers Or Liability to SPD? --- Poised for Linchpin Role In Toppling Kohl, Party Rankles Nearly Everyone --- Marsh Birds vs. 4,000 Jobs

Author: By Greg Steinmetz and Caecilie Rohwedder

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/germany-campaign-98-animal-house-will-german/docview/308321480/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Now, the party that fights for animals is poised for its biggest success yet. If the Social Democratic Party gets more votes than Chancellor Helmut Kohl's Christian Democrats in the federal election in September, the SPD will likely turn to the Greens to form a coalition government. The latest poll by Germany's Forschungsgruppe Wahlen, a polling group in Mannheim, shows the SPD leading with 43% while the Greens have 8%, giving the two a majority. Mr. Kohl's party and its ally, the small Free Democratic Party, have a total of 40%. The rise of the Greens -- from protest movement to kingmaker -- has profoundly changed Germany's political landscape in recent years. Now the country's third-largest political force, the Greens share power in five of Germany's 16 federal states, including its richest, Hesse, and its most populous, North-Rhine Westphalia. On a national level, their ability to rally behind a Social Democratic chancellor could be decisive in the opposition's hopes of ending Mr. Kohl's 16-year reign. But in recent weeks, the Greens have alienated the SPD, German business and even the public with an election program that raises doubts about the Greens' political judgment and, indeed, the likelihood of a stable partnership with the SPD. The agenda the Greens issued last month demands, among other things, a dissolution of NATO, a halt to German participation in United Nations peacekeeping missions and a tripling of gasoline prices over 10 years.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Germany+--+Campaign+%2798+---+Animal+House%3A+Will+German+Greens+Be+the+Kingmakers+Or+Liability+to+SPD%3F+---+Poised+for+Linchpin+Role+In+Toppling+Kohl%2C+Party+Rankles+Nearly+Everyone+---+Marsh+Birds+vs.+4%2C000+Jobs&amp;author=By+Greg+Steinmetz+and+Caecilie+Rohwedder&amp;issn=09219986&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-04-21&amp;spage=1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: MAGDEBURG, Germany -- To save an endangered bat, Germany's Green Party blocked construction of a police station here. The Greens stopped a housing development in Hamburg to rescue a rare bird, and held up construction of a road along a Frankfurt riverbank for the benefit of some toads.
Now, the party that fights for animals is poised for its biggest success yet. If the Social Democratic Party gets more votes than Chancellor Helmut Kohl's Christian Democrats in the federal election in September, the SPD will likely turn to the Greens to form a coalition government. The latest poll by Germany's Forschungsgruppe Wahlen, a polling group in Mannheim, shows the SPD leading with 43% while the Greens have 8%, giving the two a majority. Mr. Kohl's party and its ally, the small Free Democratic Party, have a total of 40%.
The rise of the Greens -- from protest movement to kingmaker -- has profoundly changed Germany's political landscape in recent years. Now the country's third-largest political force, the Greens share power in five of Germany's 16 federal states, including its richest, Hesse, and its most populous, North-Rhine Westphalia. On a national level, their ability to rally behind a Social Democratic chancellor could be decisive in the opposition's hopes of ending Mr. Kohl's 16-year reign.
But in recent weeks, the Greens have alienated the SPD, German business and even the public with an election program that raises doubts about the Greens' political judgment and, indeed, the likelihood of a stable partnership with the SPD. The agenda the Greens issued last month demands, among other things, a dissolution of NATO, a halt to German participation in United Nations peacekeeping missions and a tripling of gasoline prices over 10 years.
In the land of no speed limits, the plan to boost gasoline prices to five German marks ($2.77) a liter -- from about 1.53 marks now -- sparked an immediate outcry. Public hostility was so strong that the SPD leaders feared the Greens could seriously damage their election prospects. Indeed, the Greens' agenda seemed designed to abet Mr. Kohl's campaign strategy of fanning fears of a so-called red-green coalition.
"The current talk of a grand coalition only aims to conceal that a Social Democratic victory would mean Greens in government," Mr. Kohl told a news conference Monday.
Fast to sense the danger of being linked with Green radicalism, the Social Democrats dismissed the ideas of their likely coalition partner as "nonsense." Their candidate for the chancellor's job, Gerhard Schroeder, declared the Greens "unfit to govern." And at a party convention Friday, he pledged that a government led by him would stay in the North Atlantic Treaty Organization, continue to send German troops abroad and "prevent gasoline prices from becoming a source of new social injustice." The SPD is aiming to put more money in the pockets of low-income families and therefore cringes at the thought of a big tax increase affecting rich and poor equally.
Even the Greens themselves are now backpedaling on their most controversial idea. They plan to publish a new, trimmed-down version of their manifesto that will still include the goal of higher energy prices but not mention the disputed five-mark threshhold.
German industrialists, never big fans of the party, have grown increasingly anxious about the prospect of Greens in government. "The Greens are dreamers who have as much understanding of business as a tree frog has of algebra," says Ignaz Walter, president of the powerful German Industry Federation, or BDI.
A look the Green record would certainly frighten many a Chamber of Commerce member. The party currently is holding up plans to expand airports in Dortmund, Duesseldorf and Frankfurt. In Hamburg, the Greens are opposing plans to enlarge an aircraft factory that could bring 4,000 new jobs to a city with 13.3% unemployment.
To build a new plane for the European consortium Airbus Industries, Dasa, the aerospace arm of Daimler-Benz AG, wants to enlarge its existing Hamburg factory into the adjoining marsh. But the Greens argue that the site, a habitat for 200 species of birds and a rare aquatic plant, must be spared. In January, Hamburg's government of Greens and Social Democrats designated the area a nature reserve.
"The Social Democrats alone certainly wouldn't have done this," says a proud spokesman for the Greens in Hamburg.
The city says it is looking for alternatives. But Dasa says only the marsh area is feasible, and the Airbus consortium says it has other sites being offered by Seville in Spain, Toulouse and Saint Nazare in France, and the eastern German town of Rostock. Observers say the Greens' move makes it unlikely that Hamburg will win the bid for the plant expansion when a decision is made at the end of this year.
Two years ago, a factory in Dormagen belonging to chemicals maker Bayer AG set up a self-policing mechanism to safeguard against environmental problems. It was the sort of system permitted in other German states and endorsed by the European Union. But local authorities in North-Rhine Westphalia, where the Greens share power with the SPD, have refused to scale back inspections. Bayer and others complain of "administrative overkill" that stands in the way of competitiveness. They also dislike a continuing debate on new special charges for waste-producing companies.
"The discussion about extra charges and the extra financial, managerial and organizational burdens send the wrong signals, especially to foreign investors," the chemical industry group said last month.
One of the biggest problems for companies is the uncertainty that often results from political haggling between Greens and their coalition partners. In January, the state government in North-Rhine Westphalia nearly collapsed after a long festering row over the planned extension of a brown-coal, or lignite, mine known as Garzweiler II. Environmentalists are alarmed at the mine's impact on carbon-dioxide -- brown coal is considered a particularly dirty substance -- and its impact on local nature reserves. Proponents of the projects, which include the state's Social Democratic economics minister, say the Garzweiler extension will maintain 50,000 jobs and is strategically vital: Brown coal generates a third of Germany's electricity.
"The worst is the uncertainty," says an executive close to Rheinbraun AG and RWE AG, the two companies involved in the project. "If politicians banned brown coal, or slapped a hefty tax on it, then we'd know what we're dealing with. The problem is the uncertainty and the fighting."
The Greens' plans for governing also give industry little cause for comfort. Wolfgang Piech, chairman of Volkswagen AG, strongly denounced Green plans to raise the price of gasoline to five marks a liter. "That's an idea for the rich in big cities," he told the German magazine Die Woche, suggesting that urban drivers use less fuel and have more money to pay for it. "The majority of the population will be punished for where they live."
German airlines and tour operators are outraged by a recent Green demand to reintroduce taxes on jet fuel. A Green parliamentarian said Germans should be content to take a vacation by airplane every five years. That rankled the public as well as the businesses.
So far, the Greens are proposing only a modest increase of 10 pfennig a liter of kerosene, with a gradual increase to 50 pfennig a liter. "Ten pfennig would be conceivable, but anything more would lead to a massive losses in the industry," says Michael Iwand, environmental director for TUI, one of Germany's biggest package-tour operators.
Worse, Mr. Iwand says, the environmental merits of the tax are far from proven. The income from the tax would likely be used for budget gaps rather than environmental projects. Third World countries that greatly depend on tourism would suffer. And introducing the tax in Germany alone would backfire: To avoid German fuel prices, planes would fly detours or stop for fuel in other countries, boosting costs and harming the environment rather than helping it.
But Juergen Trittin, one of the Green leaders, says the party's program is far from being the "economic dictatorship" that industry makes it out to be. Industry opposes the Greens, not because they would make less money, but because the Greens would bring change and force them to adopt new policies. "They complained about catalytic converters, too," he says.
Even the vilified gas tax is grounded in economic arguments, he says. The proceeds raised from increasing pump prices can be used to cut employer contributions to social benefit programs. Currently, for every mark employers pay in wages, another mark goes to insurance, retirement and vacation pay. Employers' high contributions are one of the problems often cited in Germany's inability to make a dent in unemployment.
If the Greens spark so much doubt, anxiety and even outrage, why do German voters seem bent on handing the party a share of federal power? One factor often cited is Germany's political system, where a party needs only 5% of the vote to gain representation in Parliament. In the U.S., by contrast, "it's winner take all," says Betty Wood, who coordinates information for the Green Party USA. "If we had proportional representation, other people besides Democrats and Republicans would have a chance to win something."
In addition, the German Greens, despite their recent bouts of radicalism, have grown more pragmatic. Party leader Joschka Fischer stunned his own ranks a couple years ago when he abandoned the party's traditional pacifism and supported the deployment of German troops in the former Yugoslavia. Such concessions to Realpolitik, combined with an organization that has ensured at least a Green voice, if not always a victory, in state and federal politics, have helped put the party solidly within the political mainstream.
"Throughout the 1980s, the Greens have managed to build up a large group of regular voters," says Juergen Falter, a political scientist at Mainz University. "And through their relatively modest course in various state governments, they have won additional supporters, largely from the center."
Fundamental to the party is the widespread concern in Germany for the environment, even if it means paying extra for cleaner and healthier surroundings. The signs of this are everywhere. At a McDonald's in Frankfurt, drinks are served in reusable plastic cups. Cologne, Munich, Heidelberg and other cities have vast downtown areas off limits to cars. Bicycle lanes are ubiquitous. Public transportation is well-developed. A nationwide recycling programs takes in not only bottles, cans and newspapers but plastic packaging material.
The most famous example of German environmental fervor came in 1995 over Royal Dutch/Shell's plan to sink the Brent Spar oil-storage platform. While environmental activists elsewhere could muster only limited protests, German consumers -- fired up by Greenpeace -- boycotted Shell gas stations and forced the company to find an alternative. Indeed, a third of Greenpeace's world-wide income comes from Germany, whereas only 16% comes from the U.S.
Germans are said to care more about the environment because they can afford it. With their basic needs assured by a paternalistic welfare state, Germans can look beyond material issues. "People focus on what's highest up in their needs. In America, that's food, clothing and shelter and not climate change," says Kalee Kreider, an official with Greenpeace in Washington.
Perhaps the leading source of the Greens' strength is their strong roots among the country's baby boomers, the now middle-age generation that protested in the streets in 1968, formed Germany's peace movement in the early 1980s and embraced environmentalism as part of an effort to do the world more good than its parents had.
Formed in 1980, Germany's Greens first entered Parliament in 1983 on a strictly ecological, pacifist and antinuclear platform. Their speaker, Joschka Fischer, caused a scandal when he took his oath of office in tennis shoes. Beards and sandals were other trademarks of party members in the legislature. Protest badges were common, bras less so, and some members knitted and tended to children during legislative sessions.
Both pacifism and the environment were popular causes in the affluent West Germany of the 1980s when the Greens prospered, and they still are for large parts of Germany's first postwar generation. In addition, the Greens' idealism and their uncompromising views on issues ranging from nuclear weapons to nuclear energy are falling on fertile ground in a nation known for its perfectionism and dislike of political deal-making.
To many Germans -- whose parents' Nazi past turned them early against politics -- the Greens' ideals seem worthy and pleasantly removed from the political mainstream. In addition, the Greens attracted that portion of more radical, left-leaning voters who in other European countries vote for Communist parties. But in Germany, where real-life communism was ever-present across the infamous Berlin Wall, Communist parties never stood a chance, and the Greens benefited.
"Without the Iron Curtain, the Greens wouldn't exist in their current strength," says Mr. Trittin, the party leader.
At the same time, analysts say Germany has changed since the days of the Iron Curtain, and the Greens must still prove that they have changed also. At a time of economic hardship, analysts say, job creation now tops voters' agenda, not environmental concerns. The Greens aren't widely associated with having answers to the jobless crisis. They played hardly any role in the debate last year over tax and market reforms. And the end of the Cold War has given way to far more complex conflicts in areas from Rwanda to Yugoslavia that don't allow for the clear-cut positions of the 1980s.
"Many things that helped the Greens' ascent in the 1980s are beginning to hurt them in the changed climate of the 1990s," wrote Renate Koecher of the renowned Allensbach Institute, a private polling institute, in a recent study of the Greens. "Their distance from business, their skepticism toward technological progress, their distrust of state power, their educational zeal and their support for a militant protest culture."
In the U.S., those concepts never made big inroads in the first place. The American Greens can claim a municipal judge in New Mexico, a few county commissioners and a handful of other officials, most of which are in California. In the northern Californian city of Arcata, population 15,000, Greens control the city council.
In the 1996 presidential race, the Green candidate, consumer advocate Ralph Nader, won a mere 0.6% of the popular vote. Mr. Nader refused to run on the Green platform and turned down offers of campaign support from Green officials. But the Greens considered the campaign a success because at least they had made it onto the ballot, albeit in only 22 states.
Says Carl Lankowski, research director for the American Institute for Contemporary German Studies in Washington: "Germany has the Greens, America has the Sierra Club."
Credit: Staff Reporter

Insurers, Risk and the Global Climate: [Interview]

Author: Dubrow, Robert

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/insurers-risk-global-climate/docview/430958400/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: JOEL GORDES, an energy consultant, believes that Connecticut's multibillion-dollar insurance industry has a choice: face possible financial ruin from losses due to more frequent and severe storms, droughts and wildfires or commit some of the industry's considerable resources to efforts to stem global warming. Mr. Gordes is a consultant to Dr. Jeremy Leggett, formerly of Greenpeace International, and now of Solar Century in Britain. Dr. Leggett began the discussions about climate change between insurers and environmentalists in Europe. Mr. Gordes now engages in similar discussions with Connecticut and other United States insurers, proposing products he feels would both increase insurers' bottom lines and reduce emission of climate-changing gases.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Insurers%2C+Risk+and+the+Global+Climate&amp;author=Dubrow%2C+Robert&amp;issn=03624331&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1998-04-19&amp;spage=14CN.3&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   JOEL GORDES, an energy consultant, believes that Connecticut's multibillion-dollar insurance industry has a choice: face possible financial ruin from losses due to more frequent and severe storms, droughts and wildfires or commit some of the industry's considerable resources to efforts to stem global warming.
Mr. Gordes is a consultant to Dr. Jeremy Leggett, formerly of Greenpeace International, and now of Solar Century in Britain. Dr. Leggett began the discussions about climate change between insurers and environmentalists in Europe.
Mr. Gordes now engages in similar discussions with Connecticut and other United States insurers, proposing products he feels would both increase insurers' bottom lines and reduce emission of climate-changing gases.
Mr. Gordes has been involved in energy-related work since 1975, when he was hired by a solar energy company in Waterbury. He was vice chairman of the Connecticut General Assembly's energy committee when he served as a State Representative from 1987 to 1991.
A lifelong Connecticut resident, Mr. Gordes, 51, lives in Colebrook. In a recent interview, he discussed global warming and the insurance industry. Following are excerpts from that conversation.
Q. Why should the insurance industry be concerned about global warming?
A. The insurance industry is based upon stability of weather within certain types of boundaries.
There has to be an actuarial experience to be able to say, ''This is what the premium should be for this property in this place.'' If climate is changing, the probability of risk changes, and that's not good. There could be more flooding, hail storms, intense wind storms, intense precipitation events.
Some recent research from the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration says that climate change may make hurricanes worse. Brushfires could become urban firestorms if they come off the ridges and go into Los Angeles.
It's going to be erratic and volatile, and that's not a great situation to base insurance premiums on.
Q. What is the magnitude of losses from these kinds of events?
A. If Hurricane Andrew had hit a more populated and wealthy area, like Miami, it would have been a $50 billion loss rather than the $16.5 billion loss it was. If a similar event hit metropolitan New York, New Jersey and then went into Connecticut, it could be as much as $110 billion.
Q. Have insurance losses been increasing in the 1990's?
A. Of the 25 largest weather-related insurance losses in the United States, 21 have occurred in the past decade. There has been a remarkable increase in billion-dollar catastrophic losses. Two primary reasons are greater wealth and people building more in harm's way. However, even when you normalize for that, it appears there are other factors at work.
Q. With all the uncertainty involved in projecting the future of global climate, why should the insurance companies take this seriously?
A. Absence of certainty does not mean absence of risk. If you wake up at the end of the day and say, ''What if I'm wrong, what if there really is climate change?'' it may be too late by then to do anything about it. The insurance industry is the first in line to be bankrupt if climate change is real.
Q. What should the insurance industry do about climate change?
A. European insurers usually have staff climatologists on board. The United States industry, with the exception of Employers Reinsurance of Kansas, generally does not.
They need to start looking at the probabilities of what could happen. Then they can ask what they can do to help mitigate climate change that at the same time will increase their bottom line -- for instance, making their own facilities more energy efficient. They own tremendous amounts of property. It will make it lower cost to maintain, at the same time they are reducing emissions.
The other thing is reducing property-casualty losses through energy types of upgrades.
A few winters ago we had all the huge icicles, called ice dams, forming along the edges of roofs. They are caused by heat escaping from poorly insulated ceilings, melting the snow on the roof from below, the water running down over the unheated eaves, refreezing, working its way back under the shingles and causing water damage inside the home. Insulating the ceiling correctly and ventilating the air above it stops ice dams.
So here's a case where saving energy, which is saving emissions, saves future claims for the insurance industry. The insurance industry could say, ''We're going to give you 10 percent off your insurance for three years, if you will insulate your ceiling.''
Q. Did the insurance industry have a presence at the December global warming conference in Kyoto?
A. The European and Asian insurers certainly did, and they came out with some very, very strong statements calling for emission reductions. Even as far back as 1995, Lloyd's of London, Munich Reinsurance and Swiss Reinsurance were calling for reductions. The American industry has not been there.
Seventy-one insurers have signed a United Nations accord, pledging to consider the issues of climate change and other environmental problems in all business practices. Employers Reinsurance is the only United States company to sign.
Q. Have the European and Asian insurers been involved in encouraging energy efficiency?
A. They've gone even further. They've started to invest in renewable energy. Swiss Reinsurance has put money into a fund to help people in the third world with photovoltaics. There are a number of English insurers joining the campaign called Solar Century.
Q. Why can't insurance companies simply not insure vulnerable areas instead of trying to mitigate global warming?
A. Then you don't have much of a business. And you are going to have to have the insurer of last resort take over, which is usually the government. In the long run that usually doesn't work too well. Somebody has to pay somewhere. If you're an insurer, you calculate the risks and do your business. There are a lot of other tools they are developing to manage those risks, financial tools mostly, but that ignores what may be the biggest threat.
Q. Why can't insurance companies simply raise their rates as their response to global warming?
A. They have been doing that -- in Florida, in certain other prone areas. But remember, it's a regulated business, and the minute you start raising rates, people start squawking to their state senators and representatives and insurance commissioners. It's a politically driven process.
Q. Are there precedents for the insurance industry advocating social policy changes that would reduce risks?
A. Absolutely. What about the support they've given to Mothers Against Drunk Driving? What about seat belt safety and air bag safety? In one case they took some pension funds and invested them in businesses that created jobs for union members from whose pension funds the money came.
Why should insurance companies, if there is a solid link to climate change from fossil fuel emissions, be investing in fossil fuel projects? It's like a health insurance company investing in tobacco.
Q. What has been the response of United States insurance executives to your arguments?
A. They are, as insurers usually are, very cautious. They want to see more ironclad links before they begin to take action. However, a few are starting to make some statements, one that will be putting something in their annual report.
We've come up with certain reasons why the United States insurers have not followed in the footsteps of the European Community. The Europeans have more global exposure to losses. The Europeans do not have a massive disinformation campaign going on by their fossil fuel industry, as we do.
United States companies don't want to scare their stockholders and customers by saying climate change is imminent. Many also sell auto insurance. And one of the things we're going to have to do, if we want to stop emissions, is get a lot of people out of their autos.
Q. How important is this issue for Connecticut?
A. Connecticut's economy is still heavily invested in the insurance industry. Some statements were made by Republican State Chairman Chris DePino about how Democratic Senator Joseph I. Lieberman's support for climate change legislation and the Kyoto treaty holds a risk for him. I don't think the Republican chairman has reckoned with the threat that climate change could present to the insurance industry in his home state. So I think he's barking up the wrong tree.
Photograph Joel Gordes, at home in Colebrook, is trying to convince insurance companies that it will indeed pay to pursue alternate energy sources. (Don Heiny for The New York Times)

Insurers, Risk and the Global Climate

Author: By ROBERT DUBROW

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/insurers-risk-global-climate/docview/109989393/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: JOEL GORDES, an energy consultant, believes that Connecticut's multibillion-dollar insurance industry has a choice: face possible financial ruin from losses due to more frequent and severe storms, droughts and wildfires or commit some of the industry's considerable resources to efforts to stem global warming.
